Halfway down the Rue Saint-Denis near the Rue du Petit-Lion, a weathered house helped historians reconstruct old Paris: dilapidated walls exposed timber and cracked plaster, a warped triangular roof overhung the street, and overlapping upper planks lightened the fragile structure.On a rainy March morning a young man, cloaked like an antiquary, stood across studying its peculiarities. He ignored narrow first-floor windows and inspected the third-floor sash windows of crude green-tinted glass, glancing often at the shop below. A large wooden beam, supported by four pillars, bore many paint layers and a whimsical painting of a cat with an oversized racket; a faded sign read “Guillaume, Successor to Master Chevrel.”Dressed as if from a ball—stylish cloak, white silk stockings, white gloves, disheveled hair—his pale face and dazzling cravat drew shopkeepers’ eyes. Three apprentices at the dormer laughed and sprinkled him with shaving cream; he brushed it off as a girl's Raphael-like face flashed and vanished. The “Cat and Racket” shutters opened, a manservant displayed the cloth sign, and behind iron bars many brown-linen packages appeared. Monsieur Guillaume, old-fashioned in appearance, was reputed the city’s best-stocked, honest cloth merchant, though rivals grumbled about his notary’s heavy discounts.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The old merchant, Monsieur Guillaume, awakens at his Rue Saint-Denis shop and watches a stranger loitering outside. A staunch traditionalist with a shrewd, patient face, he enforces a strict routine on three apprentices who fear his keen gaze. When work begins, Guillaume notices the stranger’s anxious looks; the eldest apprentice, curious, steps out, thinks he sees Mademoiselle Augustine hurrying away, and hails a cab—thwarting a prank aimed at her.Guillaume reprimands the youths for idleness, recalling his own faster youth, and insists on rigorous labor despite the apprentices’ comfortable homes and board payments. The spotless shop and frugal household—apprentices even barred from the family dessert—follow strict tradition: Mass together, modest wages for long service. Joseph Lebas, the eldest apprentice, earns a small wage and is seen as a possible match for Virginie, though he secretly loves Augustine. Virginie is devout and severe; Augustine, eighteen, is delicate, bookish, and yearns for passion. Guillaume worries about his daughters’ prospects in changing times.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################From that day the old merchant, grieving his eldest daughter’s decline, recalled marrying Mademoiselle Chevrel under circumstances like Joseph Lebas’s and Virginie’s; marrying off his daughter would repay a debt to an orphan benefactor. Joseph Lebas, thirty-three, aware of the fifteen-year gap with Augustine and Monsieur Guillaume’s rule that the elder sister marry first, suffered in silence. Warm-hearted, tall, and broad-chested, he kept his love concealed.Months earlier a young painter paused outside the dark Cat and Racket, transfixed by a scene any artist would envy: a cave-like shop opening to a lamp-lit dining room that evoked Dutch interiors. The family, Augustine among them with a chubby maid, formed a modest, peaceful tableau. Augustine, pensive and barely touching her food, seemed almost angelic in the lamp’s glow; the sight overwhelmed the painter, who left unable to eat or sleep.Determined to capture it, he returned repeatedly—sometimes in disguise—studying Augustine. For eight months he lived in love and art, neglecting friends and routine. His friend Girodet, seeing two masterpieces intended for the Salon, recognized their depth and said love had inspired them, though he warned against public display. The paintings were nevertheless shown and caused a sensation: The Interior launched a new genre, and the portrait became unforgettable, winning acclaim and offers the artist declined.Madame Guillaume mentioned the exhibition; with her cousin’s aid Augustine saw the Louvre. Trembling before the portrait she recognized herself; the artist whispered, “You see how love has inspired me,” and she was stunned. Fleeing the crowd, she wept, exchanged a brief nod with him, and felt guilt and exhilaration. She feigned a headache to avoid talk, longed for the elegance of a genius’s wife, and next day learned the painter had removed the pictures—an absence that revealed her instinctive sensitivity.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The morning after the ball, Theodore de Sommervieux—who had won Augustine’s heart—found it hard to declare his love because her household constrained them. Augustine sat in the counting-house between Mademoiselle Virginie and Madame Guillaume, closely watched by her mother, while Theodore, fearing rivals among the assistants, bribed a maid to arrange discrete meetings; they agreed to meet during Mass at Saint-Leu.At the Cat and Racket the family busied itself with stock-taking under Monsieur Guillaume, ignoring Augustine’s longing. After the day's work they relaxed—attending Cendrillon and letting the apprentices enjoy a night out. The next morning Guillaume resumed routine and summoned his assistant Joseph Lebas, offering him partnership. Overwhelmed, Joseph blurted he loved Augustine; Guillaume at first misheard Virginie's name, then, once clear, suggested Joseph accompany Augustine to Mass.At breakfast tension rose; Madame chastised Augustine and sent her away, prompting a family discussion about her future. Augustine declared her love for Theodore and vouched for his respectable, prosperous background. Madame Roguin arrived with news of Theodore’s honors and fortune, swaying the parents. By evening Guillaume consented, Joseph was informed, Theodore presented a painting of the Cat and Racket, and the household embraced a joyful future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A few months after that memorable Sunday, Saint-Leu’s high altar witnessed two contrasting weddings. Augustine and Theodore shone with happiness while Virginie, simply dressed, followed her sister. Monsieur Guillaume had wanted Virginie married first, but the priests attended the more elegant bride. Neighbors praised Virginie’s sensible match and envied Augustine for marrying an artist of rank; an old fan-maker warned such extravagance would bring ruin. After a splendid ball and supper the Guillaumes returned home and the Lebas family resumed their old residence. The ecstatic artist carried Augustine to their richly adorned apartment.Their first year was blissful: Theodore lavished affection and Augustine, lost in love, believed her devotion would always enchant him. As pregnancy approached, Theodore felt the pull of work, reconnected with friends, and frequented the Duchesse de Carigliano’s salons, pleased to be seen with a beautiful wife. Augustine liked society but felt out of place among artists; her admiration was sincere but superficial, and an intellectual disconnect grew. Studio life only exposed her discomfort; at gatherings her innocence clashed with freer manners. Gradually Theodore cooled, and three years into marriage Augustine sank into sorrow as his involvement with the Duchesse deepened.She tried to educate herself in poetry and art to reconnect, but her efforts only highlighted her inadequacy. Seeking solace at her childhood home—where Virginie and Joseph Lebas ran a steady drapery—brought no comfort. Augustine returned to her husband’s home, burdened by uncertainty and longing, like a desperate patient testing remedies for a fractured marriage.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The elderly Guillaumes welcomed their daughter warmly; her visit was a refreshing break from four years of monotony spent in reminiscence—especially over the Lecocq bankruptcy. Monsieur Guillaume checked on his shop, the Cat and Racket, and chatted with former competitors, while Madame Guillaume, placed on the Army’s clothing consulting board through her son-in-law, filled their home with extravagant, tasteless decor. Their nostalgic stagnation struck Augustine as a stark contrast to her turbulent life, leaving her both proud and appalled.Augustine displayed her accomplishments and aired marital grievances about her husband Theodore, a painter, whom her mother judged for late nights and eccentric habits. When she mentioned divorce, her parents were shocked; Monsieur offered practical support, but Augustine recoiled, realizing only those who had suffered like her could understand her isolation.Determined to change course, she sought the Duchess de Carigliano—not to compel Theodore but to learn to captivate him. The Duchess counseled that one must project strength, self-control, cultivate mystery, and never reveal the whole measure of affection. She even offered Augustine a portrait Theodore had painted to rekindle him. Theodore’s jealous fury at this provoked a violent quarrel; the next morning Augustine was found in despair amid the wreckage and died young. Her tragic end stands as a poignant reminder of the struggle women face pursuing love amid the complexities of artistic genius; her grave’s inscription reflects this sorrow.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Comte de Fontaine, a royalist who fought in La Vendée, was ruined when his estate was confiscated. He married Mlle de Kergarouet, noble yet penniless, and moved his family to Paris. Royal honors briefly raised hopes, but new aristocrats overshadowed him. Nearly returning home, he reentered service in 1815 and became Louis XVIII's trusted adviser. The family prospered: sons won posts, daughters married advantageously, if modestly.Emilie, youngest, spoiled and socially central, rebuffed suitors with snobbery, rejecting matches for petty faults. As the King's health failed, the Comte pressed to find her a husband to secure their standing, but her winter disdain caused repeated refusals and left their future uncertain.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Nature had endowed Emilie de Fontaine with every quality needed to play the part of Célimène. Tall and slender, she could assume dignity or playfulness at will; her long neck and arched brows suggested scorn and impertinence, her fine black hair and melodious voice could charm or silence. Her alabaster complexion betrayed little except fleeting emotion. Many young men she scorned accused her of acting; she said she merely inspired desire to subject it to her whims.Monsieur de Fontaine realized too late that home adoration had spoiled his daughter. Constant admiration inflated a pride and selfishness that his gentle counsels could not pierce; affection from her often took a condescending tone, like a child demanding attention. Emilie sometimes showed fondness but frequently withdrew, jealous of shared family affection and blaming fate for her solitude, blind to the inward source of happiness. Determined to resist her sisters’ marriages, she nevertheless envied their happiness; her mother suspected she might be a little mad.This pride, the narrator suggests, was common among beautiful young women of affluent families: feeling misunderstood by mothers, imagining maternal envy, nursing secret tears, and rebelling against perceived tyranny. They build elaborate ideals of the perfect partner, only to learn that experience rarely matches fantasy.Emilie’s standards were precise and unyielding: her husband must be a peer of France, a soldier wearing an Order so they could be respected; he must be slim, for she despised corpulence and equated slenderness with romance. Her disdain for inadequate suitors was plain, and Monsieur de Fontaine feared ridicule and the risks of delay.During the waning months of Charles X’s reign he hosted gatherings to attract matches, but her whims persisted. One morning he summoned her, urged a wise choice, and warned that mutual respect, not rank alone, makes marital happiness. Moved, Emilie feigned affection yet kept her pride.At a family gathering she again insisted she would only marry a peer; an uncle joked she should wait for the Duc de Bordeaux. Later, at the Bal de Sceaux, she noticed a striking young man apart from the throng—aloof, elegant—and, seeing him watch a pale dancer named Clara, followed out of jealousy. Learning he was not the noble she hoped, she pressed for an introduction; her imagination and infatuation deepened, still ignorant of love’s complexities.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next morning, before Emilie awoke, her uncle hurried to Chevreuse. Recognizing the young man he’d insulted the day before, he approached with courtly civility. “I’m a vice-admiral,” he said, apologizing for his drunken outburst and preferring a handshake to family quarrel. The young man accepted, and they rode into the woods, where the Count, half-jesting about duels, fired a pistol into a birch and invited the young man to shoot near his mark. The man obliged and won the admiral’s approval.During the ride the Count tested the young man about gentlemanly codes—debts, pay, mistresses—and teased his own past recklessness. He promised to introduce the young man at the Pavillon Planat and urged him to dinner to meet his nephew, the Comte de Fontaine. The newcomer—Longueville—found the old man odd but affable and accepted.When the house party gathered and “Monsieur de Longueville” was announced, everyone rushed to appraise him. Simply but elegantly dressed, he moved comfortably in the Receiver-General’s luxury. His polished manners and apt answers—on naval architecture and the arts—suggested a worldly, educated man; some suspected École Polytechnique training. Longueville accepted flattery but declined dinner, saying he was “Hippocrates” to his delicate young sister—an explanation that provoked ironic speculation about his profession.Mademoiselle de Fontaine, who had dressed to attract him, felt slighted by his apparent indifference. Emilie, usually charming with newcomers, adopted a simpler, more natural manner that puzzled the family; some thought she was withholding to dazzle later. Longueville pleased everyone without revealing himself, prompting the Count to call him “a cunning rascal.” A sarcastic remark at dinner and Mademoiselle de Fontaine’s warning—that universal perfection must hide a defect—kept conjecture alive.Despite attempts at indifference, Emilie’s feelings soon betrayed her. Longueville’s modesty and discretion deepened her anxiety: he spoke little of himself or his family yet revealed connoisseurship in painting and music. Once they sang a duet, their intimacy touched a private chord even as his social status remained mysterious.Her uncle and family schemed to learn more and to meet Longueville’s sister, Clara, arranging an informal dance. Emilie welcomed another person to care for and grew gentler, a change read as love transforming selfishness. On the day of the dance, Clara and Maximilien were principal guests. Clara charmed Emilie with graceful ease—“I’ve heard so much about you from Maximilien; I wanted to know you out of affection for him”—putting her at ease. Believing him well born and seeing his grace meet her ideal, Emilie glittered with satisfaction.As autumn deepened, the couple surrendered to feeling. Small shared incidents—aimless conversations masking deeper affection, admiring sunsets, gathering daisies, singing duets—knit them closer, each casual exchange confirming the mutual love they carefully concealed.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################At a particular twilight that inspires reflection, a young painter finishing a large canvas falls from his ladder, strikes his head, and loses consciousness. He is awakened by a beautiful young woman and her marquise-like mother, who tend him with gentle, reserved care and withdraw without seeking acquaintance. The porter’s wife later identifies them as Mademoiselle Leseigneur and her mother, long-time residents of the building.The painter, Hippolyte Schinner, has just moved into the attic studio and is gaining recognition, though he keeps his affairs private to secure a better life for his own mother. During convalescence he replays the encounter, especially the girl’s image, and soon visits them, feeling a kinship born of imagined shared misfortune.Their modest home shows poverty but also an elegant bearing: worn finery kept meticulously clean and traces of past luxury. After awkward conversation turns to art, Madame Leseigneur reveals a decaying portrait of her late husband, a naval officer; Hippolyte offers to repaint it, hoping to deepen their acquaintance. Adelaide returns with candles to brighten the room, and a visitor arrives—an older, aristocratic-looking gentleman whose presence stirs Hippolyte’s jealousy and hints at complex social histories. He leaves torn between admiration and the fragile boundary between their worlds.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A remarkable, lean, aged man stood among the ragged remnants of the Monarchy—“Louis XIV’s light infantry”—a faded echo of his former self: worn coat, powdered hair dulled, intellect diminished. Rivarol’s quip about Champcenetz seemed apt. He puzzled the painter Chevalier; no one addressed him. His relation to the gallant man nearby was unclear, and his presence hinted at a possible past link to the gallant’s deceased wife without answering anything.One of the least dilapidated men kissed Madame de Rouville’s hand and sat beside her; another bowed and took a place nearby. Adelaide leaned over the old gentleman’s chair in a pose recalling a famous painting, though his familiarity irritated her. “Are you sulky with me?” he asked, glancing shrewdly at the painter Schinner.Madame de Rouville introduced Hippolyte, a celebrated painter. The old man noticed Hippolyte’s red ribbon and contrasted it with his own honors. Hippolyte, captivated by Adelaide’s beauty, was briefly blind to the squalor around them; the old gentleman lamented his losing streak at cards. Adelaide offered help; he insisted she stay where he could see her. When the game ended, the gentleman complained of losses and the silent figure only echoed the hour, prompting Hippolyte to take his leave, already smitten.The next day Hippolyte returned for the Baron’s portrait Adelaide sent him to fetch, their exchange charged with modesty and promise. Over the following days their relationship deepened—laughter, shared thoughts, and quiet mutual trust. Schinner learned piquet and often lost; Hippolyte grew jealous when the old Count showed familiarity with Adelaide. Within two months Hippolyte visited daily, the old gentleman’s visits faded, and Hippolyte took his place at the card table.Worried about Madame de Rouville’s finances, Hippolyte had spent two months on the Baron’s portrait and puzzled over the Baroness’s silence about payment. When Adelaide visited his studio, awed by the work, Hippolyte declared his desire to marry her. That evening, after a night of turmoil, his purse was missing and he suspected Adelaide and her mother of theft. Ashamed by her pallor and sadness when confronted, his doubt began to waver. During a tender card game Adelaide quietly placed a new purse before him; overwhelmed, he vowed to marry her. The old vice-admiral soon returned, congratulated the portrait, announced a promotion, and offered financial support, dispelling remaining doubts. The story follows these characters through love, mistrust, and reconciliation toward renewed unity.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In October 1800 Bartolomeo di Piombo, a distraught sixty‑something foreigner, stood at the Tuileries with his wife and young daughter, handed his dagger to his wife, and said he would confront the Bonapartes. A soldier detained him until Lucien recognized him and led him to Napoleon. Bartolomeo sought asylum, confessing he had killed the Portas—the family that burned his vineyard and murdered his son—avenging his child by slaying all seven. Bitter at perceived Bonaparte indifference and noting he had once protected their family, he appealed for mercy. Bound by law, Napoleon could not condone private vengeance; he acknowledged Corsican vendettas, offered lodging and financial aid, and warned Paris would not tolerate private justice. Bartolomeo accepted and pledged loyalty. Lucien later found them penniless, helped them, and noted their Corsican property could not sustain life in Paris. Fifteen years later these choices proved significant.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Servin, a respected artist of about forty, was the first to open a studio specifically for young girls who wished to paint. Morally strict and devoted to art, he was married to a general’s daughter with no dowry. Initially mothers stayed with their daughters but soon left them alone, reassured by Servin’s principles. To avoid social disputes and maintain rigor, he limited pupils to young ladies from wealthy families and refused those intent on professional careers. His careful method made him a byword for feminine art—Servin as Herbault was for bonnets and Leroy for gowns—and any mother who suspected talent in her daughter recommended him.The studio sat in an attic above his apartment, reached by a small spiral stair and sealed from below: a large, light-filled room with panes shaded by green linen, dark-gray walls hung with caricatures and plaster models, a purposeful mix of order and disorder.On a bright July day the girls worked at their easels. Two cliques had formed—wealthy merchants’ daughters and aristocrats led by the vain Amelie Thirion, and the rest. Ginevra Piombo, modest and anxious, provoked their jealousy with her talent and beauty; Amelie pushed her easel aside amid whispers. Political divisions sharpened matters: Ginevra was a Bonapartist, scorned by royalist families. A faint sound revealed Servin was sheltering a fugitive—an officer of the Imperial Guard. Ginevra chose proximity to protect him. When Madame Servin arrived, hinting at her husband’s absence yet aware of the hidden man, Ginevra quietly hummed a Corsican song while sketching his head to mask his breathing. Servin praised her work; the others, including Amelie, were drawn in. Amelie’s probes failed, and the shared secret forged a tacit bond: Ginevra resolved to keep the officer hidden and help his escape as turmoil brewed outside.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When Servin and Ginevra thought themselves alone, Servin admitted a young Imperial officer with his arm in a sling. He recoiled at first until Servin introduced Ginevra as the daughter of a loyal friend of the Emperor, the Baron di Piombo; recognizing her patriotism, the soldier relaxed. Ginevra noticed his wound, but cries from the street—“Condemned to death!”—made him pale at the name “Labedoyere.” He collapsed, anguished that Labedoyere faced death for their cause while he hid and felt useless. Ginevra urged him to wait out the danger, offered her savings and her father’s influence, and pressed him to consider escape and further service. Her maternal compassion moved him; he called her “an angel of goodness,” yet spoke of avenging Labedoyere and his devotion to Napoleon.As sunset lit his face, Ginevra felt a sudden, near-magical bond. The next day he—now Luigi—recounted the retreats from Moscow and Waterloo. They spoke Corsican, deepening their intimacy, and Ginevra listened with growing admiration. Her absences from the studio sparked rumors: Mademoiselle Thirion withdrew her class and attendance fell. Laure, sent away by her mother because of the scandal, left saddened; Ginevra felt the loss.When accused, Servin dismissed gossip and focused on his exhibition. Luigi arrived, jokingly claiming responsibility for the missing students; Servin teasingly suggested marriage. Their silence acknowledged mutual love. Servin encouraged them, and Ginevra pledged to use her father’s influence to seek Luigi a pardon.Meanwhile her parents fretted over her late returns. Bartolomeo, relieved when she came home, embraced her; the family’s warmth underscored that Ginevra and Luigi’s love, forged in adversity, united them and embodied the resilience of their shared ideals.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Here she is, my Ginevra… mia Ginevra bella!” cried the old baron, embracing his daughter. Ginevra protested, laughed it off, and reassured her mother. The baroness revived; Piombo and servants breathed with courtly satisfaction.At dinner Piombo teased Ginevra about her studio hours, hinting jealousy of painting. The tone shifted when Ginevra, eyes downcast, confessed she loved a young man—no prince, but a man without fortune, a comrade of Labedoyere who had been proscribed and hidden by Servin. Piombo, affronted, declared a daughter should love only her father; Ginevra answered that love was not a matter of will. She reminded him she had not reproached his devotion to Napoleon and said she wanted a husband to protect her. Accusations followed: Piombo’s possessive pride versus her plea to choose. She insisted she would not break her word—she loved him truly.Piombo raged that if the man truly loved her he would kill him; if not, Piombo himself would. Ginevra maintained filial and romantic love were not comparable; she felt both, perhaps the latter stronger. The baroness tried to soften things. Shaken, Piombo asked to think. Ginevra implored him to love her for herself and promised never to abandon him. Their tenderness cooled his anger; he forgave her, and they briefly returned to earlier affection.Days later Ginevra grew more solicitous, singing and saying their nocturnes needed a man’s voice. A week on, her mother signaled triumph: she had persuaded Piombo to receive the suitor. That day Ginevra arrived supported by Luigi, an officer who had reappeared after hiding and been restored to the list thanks to Ginevra’s appeals to the Duc de Feltre. Luigi’s pale earnestness deepened her love. Piombo sat cold and stern.Ginevra presented Luigi as a soldier who had fought beside the Emperor at Mont-Saint-Jean. Piombo inspected him and sarcastically noted he bore no decoration; Luigi said he no longer wore the Legion of Honor. The baroness remarked Luigi resembled someone from Corsica—Nina Porta. Luigi answered Nina had been his sister and, when pressed, confirmed he was Luigi Porta.The name staggered Piombo. He and the baroness left in stunned silence; Ginevra stood pale and speechless. The revelation unmasked a vendetta: the Portas had allegedly killed Ginevra’s brother and burned her house; Piombo had massacred the Portas in revenge. Luigi, bewildered, recounted being bound in the burning house, then taken to Genoa and raised by an old man named Colonna, who warned of enemies and told him to assume the name Luigi. How he survived remained unclear.Horrified, Ginevra told Luigi he was safe for the moment in her father’s house but warned that once he left he would be hunted by Corsicans in Piombo’s service. She smiled sadly and said their love must be pure and resolute to endure what lay ahead. He squeezed her hand; they accepted their sealed destiny. She escorted him to his modest lodging, determined rather than cowed.Back home a chill fell. Piombo fumbled for words and ordered a fire; both father and daughter faced private torment they could not bridge. On Ginevra’s birthday, the baroness summoned them to reconcile in Piombo’s study. Two notaries arrived with witnesses to fulfill the law regarding Ginevra’s marriage to Luigi Porta. Piombo bristled, furious at what he saw as a violation of paternal authority and demanded whether French laws could tear a daughter from her father.When the notary persisted, Piombo seized a dagger and rushed toward Ginevra; witnesses restrained him. Ginevra knelt and begged for mercy. The sight unnerved him; he flung the dagger away and, ashamed, pushed her aside. Then he pronounced his cruelest judgment: “Go. The wife of Luigi Porta cannot be a Piombo. I have no daughter. My Ginevra Piombo is buried here.” He renounced her publicly and irreversibly.Ginevra fled to Luigi’s humble home. She confessed the loss of parental blessing and their lack of fortune; Luigi flung aside material cares. “Then I am richer than kings!” he exclaimed, vowing to love her as her parents no longer would. Thus remained: Ginevra’s candid love, Piombo’s possessive rage and violent renunciation, Luigi’s return through Ginevra’s appeals, and the catastrophic revelation that Luigi was a Porta—binding them to a vendetta that had destroyed Ginevra’s family and left her only Luigi as shelter.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The day after Ginevra was expelled from her father’s house she took refuge with Madame Servin, who received her coldly and blamed her husband for the trouble. Shocked but proud, Ginevra rented a room near Luigi, who devoted himself to her and painted a bright future.One morning trunks from her mother arrived with a sorrowful letter pleading reconsideration. The letter moved her briefly, but Luigi’s presence dissolved her resolve to go back; she buried her grief for their love.On the wedding day she was essentially alone: Luigi brought ordinary men as witnesses while joyful processions passed, underlining their different circumstances. At the mayor’s office the clerk noted her parents’ absence and the ceremony felt like mere bureaucracy. After signing the registers they left with mixed relief and sadness. An old priest married them in a modest chapel; his remarks about absent parents stung, but a sergeant’s simple blessing comforted Ginevra.They moved into a modest apartment Luigi called their true wedding, thoughtfully arranged with artistic supplies. They soon worked: Ginevra copied paintings and gained clients; Luigi parlayed his handwriting into a copying business and hired assistants. Love blended with labor became their happiest period. Evenings were shared; though she sometimes regretted her old family life, she marked an anniversary with a self-portrait for Luigi and passed another contented year.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By early 1819 picture-dealers urged Ginevra to produce originals instead of copies as competition grew. Madame Porta regretted not turning earlier to genre painting, and though Ginevra tried portraiture she faced stiff rivalry. Despite modest savings, Luigi and Ginevra grew anxious about their future.That winter Luigi labored relentlessly in a crowded, less lucrative writing market. He could no longer afford help and had to work longer for the same pay. Ginevra painted commendable works, but dealers shunned lesser-known artists and low prices brought few sales.Their household became dire; love and happiness were overshadowed by poverty. They concealed worries from one another: Ginevra lavished affection while Luigi hid his fears. One night Luigi left to work late and Ginevra, moved, resolved to help by coloring lithographs; they feigned sleep at dawn to spare each other distress. On another night, feverish Luigi saw Ginevra’s lamp across the courtyard and, confronting her, learned she could not idle while he suffered. They watched the dawn together with tentative hope.Joy followed briefly with the birth of their son, Bartolomeo, but soon they sold possessions and jewels. Poverty stripped them of comfort and pride. Ginevra, frail, often starved herself to feed Luigi while tending the sickly child. Desperate, Luigi sold himself as a military substitute to get an advance. He returned home to find Bartolomeo cold and lifeless; Ginevra, delirious, professed her love and died in his arms. Her parents, awaiting news, were shattered—another life consumed by the same cycle of suffering, leaving only memories and grief.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY. MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Some human experiences demand a delicate, almost anatomical rendering; portraits need a soul, and some truths arise only through subtle harmonies of time, circumstance, or moral inclination.Such revelations are necessary for this simple story, intended for grave, reflective readers who live on tender emotions. If the writer, like a surgeon at a dying friend’s side, reveres the subject, the reader should share that feeling. The narrative speaks to those who know private grief—the luxury of tears, the silent pangs of memory, regret for loss, and smiles for past joys. Others may put the book down; those who have never buried a beloved will not grasp these pages. The writer assures the tale is true and worth the reader’s feeling.In contemporary France, each speaker reshapes a person. Ask a Practical man about Madame Firmiani and he lists facts: a fine house in the rue du Bac, well-furnished salons, an income of a hundred thousand francs, and a husband once receiver-general. A Lounger praises her social standing; an Egotist dismisses her salons as trivial. A Student calls her enchanting; a Fop laments faded beauty yet admits charm. A Connoisseur praises her paintings; a Spiteful woman brands her a dangerous siren. Others—an Attache, a Distinguishing observer, a Duke, gossipy old ladies, an Original, an Observer, a Contradictor, an Envious critic—each tell a different tale.Madame Firmiani thus emerges through many lenses, each account reflecting the observer as much as the woman, and pointing to deeper emotional truths.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In January 1824 opinions about Madame Firmiani diverged so widely that no clear portrait emerged: widow or wife, virtuous or otherwise, rich or poor, beautiful or plain. The variety of interpretations showed how society distorted a reputation, like lithographic blocks producing different images from slight variations.That winter Monsieur de Bourbonne, an elderly magnate from Touraine, grew intrigued. Shrewd and honorable, he cherished his nephew Octave de Camps. When Octave sold an estate without consulting him and a distant relative claimed Octave had squandered his fortune on Madame Firmiani and was reduced to teaching mathematics, Bourbonne refused the worst and went to Paris to investigate.Using his faubourg Saint‑Germain connections under the name de Rouxellay, he met Madame Firmiani while Octave was absent and confirmed his nephew’s distress. A former member of the King’s guard, he knew society’s manners. Though he had come with pity and anger, the salon’s atmosphere and Madame Firmiani’s presence—said to be twenty‑five but nearer twenty‑eight, married at sixteen, gifted in beauty, wealth, music, intelligence, and wit—softened him. Her acclaim had bred malicious, persistent gossip, revealing the hazards of societal suspicion and her complex character.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The former guardsman remained after the other guests left; Madame Firmiani found him still seated as the clock struck two. He announced bluntly that he was Monsieur Octave de Camps’s uncle and had heard a rumor that his nephew had ruined himself for her, living in a garret while she enjoyed wealth. Madame Firmiani, visibly affected, told him to stop: she knew the rumors and scorned money, having married without fortune. She insisted she welcomed Octave only as a friend she esteemed, and that her guardian angel kept her from dishonesty. When the uncle, trembling and almost a father to Octave, swore on his honor and asked one question—did she love Octave?—she refused to answer directly. “To confess love is the lover’s reward,” she said, implying such a declaration belonged to the lover alone, then withdrew.The next morning Monsieur de Bourbonne found Octave in his garret and reproached him for squandering the estate the uncle meant to leave him. When he asked if Octave had ruined himself for Madame Firmiani, Octave admitted it and produced a letter she had given him. He read aloud a passage that revealed both the depth of her feeling and a grave moral test she had set before him: the letter began, “My beloved Husband,” and went on to explain that she felt dishonored by the fact that Octave’s father had unjustly deprived others of their property. She could not respect a man who knowingly kept ill-gotten gains; love for a woman implies boundless confidence and reverence, and she would love him the more if he came to her poor. She did not demand legal restitution but urged him to consult his conscience and perceive his faults himself.The letter stunned them. Octave said it had sparked a revolution in him: haunted by conscience, every pleasure felt like theft. He rushed to Madame Firmiani; together they calculated what was due to the Bourgneufs, victims of his father’s injustice. Though his means were insufficient, Octave condemned himself to pay interest—three percent—and to make what restitution he could. Madame Firmiani, practical from having lived prudently after losing proof of her own husband’s death and will during the unrest in Greece, offered her savings and he accepted.Octave explained he had been teaching mathematics to earn his keep rather than live on others. The act of restitution transformed him: finding the Bourgneufs wretched—a father who had been an agent, daughters keeping accounts and household, a sick mother—he felt immense joy in bringing relief, as if he were Providence. He called Madame Firmiani “my dear conscience”; her moral insistence had given him a delicacy of feeling he had lacked, and he hoped to become independent by his own industry.As they spoke, the sound of a carriage announced Madame Firmiani. She entered somewhat annoyed to find the uncle but smiling, and explained she had come to entreat her husband humbly to accept her fortune. The Austrian Embassy had sent a document proving Monsieur Firmiani’s death and the will, which his valet had kept safe. Octave could accept her fortune, she said, but she added that he was richer already because of the treasures of the heart—her love—which no one could add to. She laid her head on his breast in a moment of unrestrained happiness.Monsieur de Bourbonne, moved, reflected that whereas in his day men “made love,” in theirs they “love,” and he praised women as the best in humanity, innocent even of their faults, which come through men.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Rue du Tourniquet-Saint-Jean, a dark, winding lane by the Hôtel de Ville that ended at the Rue Martroi, was closed by a turnstile until 1823. Less than six feet wide at its broadest, it held pooled gutter water and stayed perpetually muddy; sunlight barely touched it and dampness lingered. Residents lit lamps at five in June and kept them burning through winter. Approaching the Marais felt like walking through cellars; the corner house at Rue du Tourniquet and Rue de la Tixeranderie still bore iron rings from chains once used for public safety.Built to combat humidity, the house had raised cellars and an entrance reached by three steps; the door formed a closed arch with a keystone carved as a female head. Three high, iron-barred windows lit a small dwelling where two beds draped in green serge and an old woman tending a brazier—preparing a porter’s-wife stew—were visible. The kitchen-dining room was sparse but kept with evident cleanliness and thrift.The old woman’s pale, wrinkled face seemed part of the house; she sat by the hearth with mischief mingled with resignation while her daughter, Caroline, bent over an embroidery frame. Despite poverty, Caroline’s rosy cheeks and bright eyes showed an undimmed spirit. One August day in 1815 the mother noticed a new passerby—a tall, thin man in black, solemn, perhaps a clerk at the Préfet’s office—and awaited his return. When he passed again Caroline, stirred by a trace of suffering on his face, felt an unfamiliar sympathy and began to hope he might change her life.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For two months the “Black Gentleman” behaved erratically, no longer passing the Rue du Tourniquet at the same hour. Madame Crochard noticed his distracted manner—often lost in thought and seldom acknowledging her or daughter Caroline. One mid-September morning Caroline’s lively presence caught his eye; their fleeting glances suggested a silent understanding.Weeks later he began passing twice daily, and their brief, wordless encounters became a cautious routine: Caroline sensed his troubles, he noticed her worries, yet both feared deeper intimacy. Madame Crochard, frustrated by Caroline’s indecision, turned on the Gentleman, who had once earned her mild approval. Overhearing a frank conversation about their dire finances, he was moved and secretly left money; Caroline found it and a silent gratitude passed between them.In early May Caroline proposed a trip to Montmorency. Madame Crochard pretended to sleep, giving Caroline and the Gentleman—now called Roger—a chance for revealing talk. The countryside and their easy exchange lifted his spirits; sharing family hardships and sacrifices deepened their bond. The day brimmed with laughter; as evening fell Caroline confessed her happiness. Roger, contemplating lasting joy, playfully held her hand before they left—an unspoken promise of hope.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Roger and Caroline are in Paris. Caroline has lived a month in a second-floor flat on Rue Taitbout, furnished with gray hangings, a writing table and a bronze clock. On a September day in 1816 she wears a green gown and waits on the balcony, heart racing. After fifteen minutes impatience turns to joy when Roger arrives; they embrace. He must attend a meeting with the Attorney‑General, which disappoints her; she objects to his theatre outings and declares her devotion. Perched on his lap she would be Caroline de Bellefeuille and vows to use her earnings for the poor. She plays a sonata as he departs and watches him drive away, longing.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Five years after settling in her Rue Taitbout home, Mademoiselle Caroline de Bellefeuille, now twenty-four, is content with her four-year-old son Charles and infant daughter Eugenie. Embracing motherhood, she favors simple pleasures, values goodness and beauty, and shuns society’s expectations and luxury. Rocking Eugenie, she recalls joyful visits to Bellefeuille with Roger; their love has deepened in a quiet, uncomplicated life, yet his secrecy troubles her.On May 6, 1822—the anniversary of a pivotal day—Roger visits, bringing a document outlining Eugenie’s marriage portion. He argues Charles must learn self-sufficiency while Eugenie deserves a fortune. Their evening is warm and lively, capped by a magical lantern show for Charles, but Roger departs at night, promising a brief respite; his duty and secrecy leave Caroline with familiar pain.Soon after she hurries to her mother, the ailing Madame Crochard. Unrecognized by the widow’s acquaintances, Caroline finds women scheming for advantage and a priest summoned for last rites. Caroline tends her mother tenderly; Madame Crochard, lamenting their estrangement, reveals that the priest forced a confession about Caroline’s benefactor—hinting at complex secrets to come.The narrative then returns to November 1805, when young barrister Monsieur de Granville, urged into a proposed marriage with Angelique Bontems to secure his future despite her family’s stigma, visits Bayeux. At church he glimpses Angelique, rekindling a childhood affection and setting in motion events that entwine love, duty, and social obligation.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For two weeks after the Vicomte de Granville’s engagement to Mademoiselle Bontems he watched her in a dreary drawing-room as she quietly prepared her trousseau. She never argued religion; when he teased her rosary she calmly returned it to its bag. Firm in the Church’s teachings, Angelique insisted a mother must have religion and questioned how one could judge between disbelievers and God. Impressed by her conviction, Granville sometimes mistook desire for love and even felt tempted to adopt her faith. Madame Bontems extracted from him a promise to respect Angelique’s practices and allow her freedom of conscience; Angelique’s gentle look and the Abbe Fontanon’s approval sealed the marriage. After the wedding they moved to Paris, where Granville became public prosecutor.Angelique persuaded him to take a ground-floor apartment in the Marais near a church and chapel for the sake of future children; Granville, preferring a fashionable quarter, yielded. He set up his study and left furnishing to Angelique, who created a cold, solemn home reflecting her austere upbringing. He tried to excuse her ways and praised her love and gentleness, but grew frustrated by the lack of warmth.For months they seemed harmonious: Granville was absorbed in work and overlooked her strict observances until November 1808, when the Canon of Bayeux, Angelique’s spiritual director, arrived in Paris alarmed at what he saw as her moral decline and set her on stricter practices. Misunderstandings followed as Angelique avoided social obligations; Granville, irritated, pressed her to fulfill her role. She appealed to the Pope, who replied that a wife should accompany her husband even at some personal cost. Rather than reconcile them, the decision widened the rift; Angelique and her director dismissed it.Granville began asserting his preferences—ordering meat daily despite her objections—and withdrew from society. The household became oppressive: Angelique’s austerity dimmed her warmth and beauty, their children suffered under rigid rules, sons were sent to school early, and daughters hurried toward marriage. Increasingly isolated, Granville sought refuge in work and a legal book while Angelique’s efforts to reconvert him deepened their estrangement; the home was a rigid, joyless place rather than one of companionship.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between 1806 and 1825 the de Granville household showed little outward drama, but Madame de Granville was inwardly unhappy. After losing her husband's affection she prayed for reconciliation while Granville, now a prominent judge, lived in the entresol to avoid her. Each morning a housekeeper brought messages; he refused breakfast, citing urgent court business.At thirty-five and prematurely aged, she grew bitter. When Granville declined the presidency of a provincial court, friends and her confessor suspected an affair—an idea that mortified her. During Lent in 1822 her health worsened from stress and fever, compounded by his indifference and her pious efforts that undermined intimacy.Recovered in May, she consulted the Abbe Fontanon, who revealed Granville had lived seven years with a concubine, fathering two children and dissipating their fortune. Shocked, urged to forgive, she resolved to confront him. After a scene in which others blamed her and she fainted, she did confront Granville. He accused her of sacrificing his happiness to piety, admitted his long neglect and refusal to restore their marriage. The exchange made clear their marriage had failed; love could not be commanded, and she was left devastated.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Early one December night in 1833 a white‑haired man whose face showed grief more than years paced the Rue Gaillon and stopped before a modest two‑story house with a dim attic light. A young doctor, Horace Bianchon, came out; recognizing him, he offered his arm and bantered about the hour and the slippery pavement. The visitor—Comte de Granville—replied with weary pride that at fifty‑five he was still hale and often walked home from the Courts or the club.Bianchon teased that a man of his station was courting robbery. Granville shrugged off the danger with melancholy and confessed, half amused, that he had been spying on a shadow in the third‑floor window—the garret had a morbid attraction for him. When Bianchon offered to tell him who lived there, Granville refused: he would not pay to learn whether the shadow was man or woman, happy or miserable. Nine years earlier he had ceased to be surprised that old men took pleasure in growing flowers; life had taught him to distrust human affection. “I grew old within a few days,” he said. He would attach himself to nothing living—only “unreasoning animals, or plants, or superficial things” appealed to him; even Taglioni’s grace moved him more than most people. He abhorred life and insisted nothing could interest him.Bianchon probed: did he have children? Granville answered bitterly, listing them—an eldest daughter, the Comtesse de Vandenesse; another daughter married suitably through her sister; sons with successful legal careers: a viscount who had been public prosecutor at Limoges and was now President of the Court at Orléans, and a younger son who was public prosecutor in Paris. Each had their cares; none had devoted themselves to him. If one had tried to fill his void it would have ruined that child. Perhaps they now awaited his money. Bianchon protested; Granville, sarcastic and self‑aware, explained his philanthropy as self‑indulgence: he paid for sensations as he gambled, seeking illusions to make his heart glow while expecting no gratitude. He would see Bianchon die without blinking and asked the same indifference in return. Life’s events had flowed over his heart like lava over Herculaneum—the town remained, dead.Unable to leave him in that mood, Bianchon reluctantly described the garret’s occupant. It was a stark picture: a woman of about thirty whose love had become fanatical. She adored a young man of pleasing appearance but every vice—Solvet—a gambler given to wine and women, guilty of acts punishable by law. This woman had abandoned a comfortable life and an adoring husband, the father of her children, for this man. She worked day and night; he had stolen from her even funds saved for the children’s food and clothes. Only three days earlier she had sold her hair—the finest Bianchon had ever seen—and when the lover returned she could not hide the gold quickly enough. For a smile or a kiss she would give away the price of a fortnight’s subsistence. The toil had hollowed her cheeks; the children’s cries had broken her heart; she lay ill on her wretched bed that night. When Bianchon visited, the family had nothing to eat; the children were so weak they could not even cry.Despite himself Granville put his hand in his waistcoat pocket as if to help, but Bianchon begged him to pity the woman and help cure her passion. Granville recoiled: how could he pity a misery whose joys he might once have prized with his whole fortune? Thousands of dead and sick men would barter everything for a return of youth and feeling. At the name Bianchon murmured—“Poor Caroline!”—Granville shuddered, gripped the doctor’s arm, and asked hoarsely whether she was Caroline Crochard. On confirmation his reaction was violent: the name had pierced him with the worst pain his heart could endure, an emotion he called a gift from hell. He knew how to pay such debts.They reached the Rue de la Chaussée d’Antin where a shriveled night scavenger stood by the curb. Granville asked if the scavenger ever found thousand‑franc notes and, hearing that sometimes he did, handed him such a note with a grotesque injunction: spend it in the wine shop, get drunk, start fights, beat your wife, blacken your friends’ eyes—give work to the watch, the surgeon, the druggist, perhaps to the police, public prosecutor, judge, and prison wardens. “Do not try to do anything else, or the devil will be revenged on you sooner or later,” Granville said. He pointed out the scavenger’s corrupt face to Bianchon and declared he had thus “squared accounts with hell.” As for Caroline Crochard, he pronounced coldly that she might die of hunger and thirst listening to her starving children; he would not give a sou to rescue her. Because Bianchon had helped her, he said, he would see him no more.Granville left Bianchon stunned and walked to the Rue Saint‑Lazare where a carriage waited. A servant informed him that his son, the attorney‑general, had been that afternoon and was waiting in his bedroom. Granville dismissed the servant, then confronted his son—in a tone at once imperious and unexpectedly tender—about coming after being ordered not to visit unless summoned.Eugène, the younger man, spoke with tremor: that afternoon a very young man arrested in the house of a friend he had robbed had appealed to Granville, claiming to be his son. “His name?” Granville asked. “Charles Crochard,” Eugène answered. Granville waved him down: that sufficed. He stood a moment, then, speaking as a father, accepted the claim. He handed his son a considerable bundle of banknotes and authorized him to use it as he saw fit in the affair.The mood shifted. Granville’s last words of counsel were almost tender: he warned Eugène about the weight of marriage—the most important act society requires—and urged him to choose a partner carefully and to consult him. He promised to write on the subject from Florence. Then, with quietness suggesting a final parting, he asked his son to kiss him and announced he would beg dismissal from the King and go to Italy. He would keep his dignity as a father and a former presiding officer.The scene leaves two images of Granville: in the street a man hardened into cruelty and misanthropy, giving money to be squandered so social machinery may be set in motion on petty moral wreckage; in his bedroom a father handing money and last counsels to his son, preparing to depart. Between those moments stands Bianchon’s account of Caroline Crochard—a portrait of sacrifice and degradation—and the Comte’s icy rejection of any duty toward her, even as the name awakens a torment proving he is not wholly dead to feeling. Granville resolves to quit Paris and to cut off a friend who had awakened his pity, while secretly acknowledging familial ties he will attempt to leave behind with money and admonition.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The incident occurred in late November 1809, at the height of Napoleon’s empire. Wagram’s shadow still haunted Austria as peace talks with the Coalition went on. Kings and princes orbited Napoleon, who staged Parisian entertainments around his marriage to an Austrian archduchess. The French aristocracy’s new opulence—diamonds and gold on women and uniforms—contrasted with the Republic’s earlier austerity; military leaders flaunted wealth gained through the sacrifices of countless soldiers.Many adopted a carefree, Louis XV–style hedonism. Both sexes embraced pleasures that seemed to foreshadow doom. A craze for military men suited the Emperor, since frequent calls to arms made every peace temporary; women’s affections shifted rapidly, often from mistress to widow in weeks.Soldiers’ appeal rested on prospects of early widowhood, claims to historical glory, or the romance of courage; women imagined passions buried on the battlefield. This bred a culture that prized bravery and glitter—diamonds, fireworks, and jeweled men like Murat.Comte de Gondreville (formerly Citizen Malin) delayed a grand entertainment to outshine rivals and please Napoleon. Ambassadors and princes filled his drawing-rooms expecting the Emperor, but a private quarrel with Josephine, hinting at divorce, kept Napoleon away. Paris’s most beautiful women and the financial elite—eager to rival military splendor and trade wealth for favor—crowded the event, where smiles masked rivalry and mistrust.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################These remarks are necessary to explain the incidents surrounding the little imbroglio that is the subject of this study, reflecting the tone prevalent in Paris drawing-rooms at the time.At a brilliant ball in Madame de Gondreville’s salon a small group of men debate the identity of a pale, delicate young woman seated by a candelabrum, her chestnut hair arranged a la Chinoise and bluebells at her breast. Hidden in a shadowy corner yet unmistakably the fairest complexion in the room, she leans forward, her almond-shaped blue eyes seeming made for tears. The men—Montcornet, a Colonel of Cuirassiers; Martial de la Roche-Hugon, a young Maitre des Requetes; and other acquaintances—exchange the usual teasing hypotheses: prude or coquette, an aristocrat or a humble companion, a widow, the daughter of a German prince. Each theory reveals more about the gossips than about the woman: one insists she must be married, another that she is simply too youthful to be so; one proposes she’s the widow of a gambler—a common occurrence since the peace; another detects in her expression an intention to charm this very evening and wagers she is already in love.Montcornet delights in provoking Martial, arguing that Parisians jealously keep unknown beauties to themselves and that the ladies surrounding the stranger would conspire, by glances and silences, to keep men from taking her out. The group’s banter is punctuated with bursts of comic gallantry and professional rivalry. Martial, favored by Napoleon and of winning manners, is mocked as a lover of conquest despite his promising diplomatic career; Montcornet, a tall, vigorous soldier with an officer’s air of superiority, is admired for his shape and bearing in the fashionable white kerseymere breeches and the cuirassier’s uniform. Their friendly raillery ranges over social ranks and motives: is she an aristocrat or a companion in fine lace and sapphires? Montcornet even halts a portly, sharp-eyed man—Comte de Gondreville—asking if he knows the lady; Gondreville only suggests she is some friend of his wife’s, or at most one of the Comtesse’s whimsical invitations.Montcornet then warns Martial that Madame de Vaudremont has been watching him with ominous attention and might divine their whispered scheming. Martial, conscious of his role as the fiancé-designate of the young, wealthy Countess de Vaudremont, teases and defies the Colonel’s warning. He will not surrender his liberty, he laughs, though the Colonel reminds him he risks losing a secure match if he allows himself to be a seducer. The lawyer’s complacent insouciance—his polished thumb-nail clicked under a tooth—reveals his ease with such juggling of attachments; he is careful to hide his ambition beneath a cultivated mediocrity. Montcornet taunts him with the absurdity of playing the Lovelace while betrothed to a widow of twenty-two with four thousand napoleons a year and a glittering diamond on her finger.This little drama in the foreground of the party is soon overshadowed by the grand entrance of the most admired woman in Paris: Madame de Vaudremont, who makes it a rule to arrive only when a party has reached its highest pitch—never showing herself when flowers are drooping or faces are tired. She appears at about eleven with the Comte de Soulanges, a celebrated officer of artillery and a favorite of the Emperor. Her arrival halts conversation and draws a pressing circle of curiosity. Women whisper, men hurry in from inner rooms; the salon brightens as everyone looks to see how her dress and bearing outshine the rest. Madame de Vaudremont is tall, dignified, with dazzlingly white skin and a gracious manner that promises beauty fulfilled. Soulanges, in his thirties, is slender and pale, with black eyes full of vivacity but taciturn in society—a man whose oratorical gifts would later appear in political life.Martial watches them approach with anxious energy. Though the Countess acknowledges Soulanges with a coldly polite smile and sits near Madame de Gondreville, Martial feels a prick of jealousy at the Count’s nearness and the thought of being displaced. He posts himself near the fireplace, where he can observe the Countess and Soulanges without drawing notice. Yet his composure is strained; when Soulanges remains standing after Vaudremont sits, an air of awkwardness passes between them, and the Comte’s pale face suddenly flushes scarlet. Without a word, Soulanges moves away and, melancholy and embarrassed, passes Martial to shelter himself in a cardroom. The company interprets this as the Count’s retreat before a possible public ridicule; Martial, emboldened, raises his head and looks toward the pale stranger by the candelabrum.As Martial takes a seat near Madame de Vaudremont, he is so absent-minded that he misses her low-spoken plea about his ring—she asks him, behind her fan, not to wear the ring he took from her, saying she will explain later and requesting his arm to go to another hostess’s salon. He answers distractedly, and Vaudremont’s vexation shows; but the conversation between the men and the whisper of rivalry continues. The little blue lady becomes the magnet of renewed curiosity, attracting the eyes of the Colonel, Soulanges, Martial, and the Countess.While Martial dances a brilliant quadrille with the Countess, half-lost in the turmoil and scents and trappings of the ball, Colonel Montcornet crosses the room, from group to group, in pursuit of information about the unknown woman. Finding a temporary opening of empty chairs near the candelabrum, he presses forward through the palisade of shawls and wraps, making his way close to her. Tactful and deliberate, he begins with a vapid conversation to the left-hand neighbor to provoke some response; the plan is to coax the stranger into speech. But the lady receives his bland compliments in silence. At last he addresses her directly, gently asking if she is married. She answers simply, “Yes, monsieur,” and says her husband is present. When asked why she remains in the shadow if she aims at attracting attention, she smiles sadly and declines to dance: “I do not intend to dance, monsieur.”Her curt refusal discourages the Colonel enough to retire, though Martial, who has glimpsed the exchange, smiles and lets his diamond glitter. Vaudremont, noticing Martial’s distraction, scolds him for his inattentiveness; but the Baron is inwardly amused at the Colonel’s rebuff. The stranger, watching the Countess, comments on Martial’s diamond and asks the Colonel if the Baron is a fickle man and if he is on good terms with Madame de Vaudremont. When the Colonel replies that Martial is indeed close to the Countess, the unknown woman turns pale and admits she had thought Vaudremont long devoted to Soulanges. At mention of Soulanges, she says that she saw him earlier; in that moment the Colonel decides she must be in love with that “lucky devil” Martial.This confession, or the look that prompted it, quickens the wagerings of men. Montcornet withdraws to the window-bay with Martial. Montcornet, a man of honor, refuses to court a woman who will not be his; he prides himself on decency and tells Martial he will not pursue one he cannot marry. Martial, with the coxcomb’s audacity, bets a hundred napoleons to Montcornet’s gray horse that he will dance with her that evening. The Colonel accepts for sport, and, still curious, says he will look for Soulanges, whom the stranger seemed interested in and who might therefore know her. Martial laughs, declaring Montcornet has lost already because his eyes have met hers and he knows her meaning if anyone does.Montcornet indeed follows the instinct to find Soulanges. In the cardroom he finds the Count at a bouillotte table, amid a circle of men. There is little intimacy between the two soldiers beyond professional comradeship, but Montcornet is alarmed: Soulanges, prudent by nature, is playing with a frenzy that threatens ruin. The table bears heavy piles of gold and banknotes; men rise, cleaned out by the game. Others watch silently, speaking only in terse bets. As Montcornet approaches he sees Soulanges sweeping in a quantity of gold and notes—winning—but his face is more melancholy and scornful than joyful. He collects his stakes with a lip that curls in bitter defiance of fortune rather than with gratitude. The scene leaves Montcornet uneasy: the Count appears to gamble not for gain but to satisfy some inward tumult.Thus the evening’s scenes intertwine: a pale, sorrowful unknown under the candelabrum who refuses to dance; a famous beauty, Madame de Vaudremont, whose late entrance compels and judges the assembly; Martial, the vain but ambitious diplomat torn between conquest and a prosperous engagement; Montcornet, the honorable cuirassier who resents seduction in absentia; and Soulanges, the taciturn, brilliant soldier whose sudden agitation and retreat to the cardroom mark him with a mystery that may link him to the stranger. The salon’s playful conjectures, wagers, glances, and jealousies create the invisible bonds that will knot these characters together, making the night more than a round of quadrilles and triumphs of dress—a small drama of passion, reputation, and social ambition played out against the blaze of chandeliers and the murmurs of fashionable Paris.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Courage,” said the Colonel. “Courage, Soulanges!” He tried to pull him from the gaming table. “Come with me. I have good news, but on one condition.”“What is that?” asked Soulanges.“That you will answer a question.”The Comte de Soulanges rose, gathering his winnings into his handkerchief; his face was so fierce no one stopped him. The players seemed relieved at his departure.“Those soldiers are always as thick as thieves at a fair!” a diplomat remarked, and a tired face returned, “When we say military men, we do not mean civil, Monsieur le Ministre.”“My dear fellow,” Montcornet said, leading Soulanges aside, “the Emperor praised you this morning, and your promotion to field-marshal is certain.”“The Master does not love the Artillery.”“No, but he adores the nobility, and you are an aristocrat. He said that men who married in Paris during the campaign are not in disgrace. Well?”Soulanges looked confused.“And now I hope you will tell me if you know a charming woman sitting under a huge candelabrum----”At the mention of the mysterious lady the Count’s face lit up; he seized the Colonel’s hand. “My dear General,” he said, voice transformed, “if anyone else had asked me that, I would have cracked his skull with this gold. Leave me. I feel more like blowing out my brains tonight than----I hate everything I see. This gaiety, this music, these stupid faces, all laughing, are killing me!”“My poor friend!” Montcornet replied gently. “You are too vehement. What would you say if I told you that Martial is thinking so little of Madame de Vaudremont that he is quite smitten with that little lady?”“If he speaks to her,” Soulanges shouted, trembling with rage, “I will thrash him flat, even if he’s in the Emperor’s lap!”He sank into a chair; Montcornet, seeing him inconsolable, withdrew.Back in the ballroom Montcornet noticed Madame de Vaudremont, agitated, and sat beside her.“I wager something vexes you?” he said.“A mere trifle, General. I want to go to the Grand Duchess of Berg’s ball, but first to the Princesse de Wagram’s. Monsieur de la Roche-Hugon amuses himself by flirting with the dowagers.”“That’s not the whole secret. I bet a hundred louis you will stay here the whole evening.”“Impertinent man!”“You are afraid of seeing Martial at the feet—”“Of whom?” she exclaimed, feigning surprise.“Of that candelabrum,” he replied, glancing at the pale stranger and studying the Countess.“You have guessed it,” she said, hiding her face. “Old Madame de Lansac, malicious as an old monkey, told me Monsieur de la Roche-Hugon is flirting with that stranger, who sits there like a skeleton at a feast. I would rather see a death’s head than that face, so cruelly beautiful and pale. She is my evil genius. Madame de Lansac, who comes only to watch while pretending to sleep, has made me miserable. Martial shall pay dearly for this trick. Urge him, since he is your friend, not to make me unhappy.”“I just left a man who promises to blow his brains out if he speaks to that lady. And he is a man, madame, to keep his word. But I know Martial; such threats encourage him. Besides, we have wagered—” The Colonel lowered his voice.“Can it be true?” asked the Countess.“On my word of honor.”“Thank you, my dear Colonel,” she said, invitingly. “Will you do me the honor of dancing with me?”“Yes; but the next quadrille. During this one, I want to find out what will come of this little intrigue and ascertain who the little blue lady is; she looks intelligent.”Pleased, Montcornet retired to watch. At such entertainments the older women like Madame de Lansac gather to witness young people’s affairs. The Duchess of Lansac, a shrewd, mischievous dowager from an earlier generation, read hearts as if reading a book: a frown, a quiver, the slightest curl of the lips revealed the soul’s impressions. From her large armchair she admired the young coquette who disguised annoyance and grief; Madame de Vaudremont felt as much sorrow as she feigned cheerfulness. She had believed Martial a man of talent who could adorn her life with power; now she saw her mistake. Her sudden passion caused acute pain, like the amputation of a healthy limb.The old Duchess seized the opportunity to interfere. Her sardonic glance, like a torch in a tragedy’s climax, terrified Madame de Vaudremont, who suspected the dowager’s hand in the stranger’s presence. Tall, tastefully dressed, diamonds and rouge worn but eyes promising epigrams, Madame de Lansac’s politeness softened a mischievous mind. Her triumphant look across the room brightened the pale stranger under the chandelier; the alliance between the Duchess and the unknown did not escape Madame de Vaudremont’s practiced eye, and she resolved to uncover the mystery.The Baron de la Roche-Hugon, having questioned the dowagers without learning the blue lady’s name, turned to the Comtesse de Gondreville, who said the lady had been introduced by the “ancient” Duchesse de Lansac. By chance he caught the intelligence in the Duchess’s look and, despite previous coldness between them, approached. The Duchess’s smile and ironic air revived Martial’s hopes.“If the old witch pretends to be friendly,” thought the Baron, “she will play me a spiteful trick.” He addressed her: “Madame, you have, I am told, undertaken the charge of a very precious treasure.”“Do you take me for a dragon?” said the old lady. “But of whom are you speaking?” she asked sweetly.“Of that little lady, unknown to all, whom the jealousy of these coquettes has imprisoned in that corner. You, no doubt, know her family?”“Yes,” said the Duchess. “But what concern have you with a provincial heiress, married some time ago, a woman of good birth, whom you men do not know; she goes nowhere.”“Why does she not dance? She is such a pretty creature. May we conclude a treaty of peace? If you will tell me all I want to know, I promise to urge a petition for the restitution of the woods of Navarreins by the Commissioners of Crown Lands on the Emperor.”The Baron’s shameless suggestion to petition for an estate—an appeal to the Duchess’s past influence—was trickery revealing his ambition. The younger branch of Navarreins bears the arms of Lansac; the duchess’s past favors had once secured her husband a title. With deceptive gravity she answered, “Monsieur, bring the Comtesse de Vaudremont to me. I promise to reveal the mystery of the interesting unknown. You see, every man in the room has reached the same curiosity as yours. All eyes are turned towards the corner where my protégée has modestly placed herself; she reaps all the homage the women wished to deprive her of. Happy the man she chooses for her partner!” Fixing her eyes on Madame de Vaudremont she added, with a look that plainly said, “We are talking of you. I imagine you would rather learn the stranger’s name from the lips of your handsome Countess than from mine.”There was such provocation in the Duchess’s tone that Madame de Vaudremont rose, approached and took the chair Martial had placed for her. Without acknowledging him she said, “I can guess, madame, that you are talking of me; but I admit my lack of perspicacity; I do not know whether it is for good or evil.”Madame de Lansac pressed the young woman’s hand and replied low, “Poor child!”The women looked at each other. Madame de Vaudremont understood that Martial was in the way and dismissed him with an imperious “Leave us.” The Baron, displeased to see the Countess under the spell of the dangerous sibyl, gave one of those decisive looks men use—potent over a blinded heart but ridiculous to a woman beginning to judge the man who attracted her.Martial, a man of the world with wit and acumen, would not risk breaking with a woman favored at court whom the Emperor wished to see married. He counted on jealousy to discover the secret of her coolness and withdrew willingly as a new quadrille began.The Baron leaned against a marble console, watching the two ladies talk. Comparing the Countess with the new beauty—made attractive by mystery—he fell prey to the common self-interest of adventurous lovers, hesitating between a fortune within reach and indulging his caprice. The light threw his anxious face against white silk hangings, making him resemble an evil genius; observers might have said, “There is another poor wretch who seems to be enjoying himself!”Meanwhile the Colonel, in the doorway between ballroom and cardroom, laughed under his moustache. He enjoyed watching the dance’s turmoil, reading the Countess’s and Martial’s agitation, and wondering about the connection between Soulanges’ gloomy looks and the fair unknown’s face, which shifted between hope and dread. Montcornet, like a king of the feast, received inviting smiles; as Colonel of the Imperial Guard he was a fine match for society.It was nearly midnight. Conversation, gambling, dancing, flirtations, rivalries and scheming reached a fervor that made a young man exclaim, “A fine ball!”“My sweet little angel,” Madame de Lansac said to the Countess, “you are now at an age when I made many mistakes. Seeing you endure a thousand deaths, I thought I might give you some charity. To go wrong at two-and-twenty means spoiling your future; it is like tearing the gown you must wear. My dear, it is not much later that we learn to go about in it without crumpling it. Go on, making clever enemies and friends without sense of conduct, and you will see what a pleasant life you will lead!”“Oh, madame, it is very hard for a woman to be happy, do you not think?” the Countess eagerly exclaimed.“My child, at your age, you must learn to choose between pleasure and happiness. You want to marry Martial, who is not wise enough to be a good husband, nor passionate enough to remain a lover. He is in debt, my dear; he is the man to squander your fortune; still, that would be nothing if he could make you happy. Do you not see how aged he is? He must have been ill; he is making the most of what is left him. In three years, he will be a wreck. Then he will be ambitious; perhaps he may succeed. I do not think so. What is he? A man of intrigue, who may have business acumen and be able to gossip agreeably, but he is too presumptuous to have any real merit; he will not go far. Besides—look at him. Is it not written on his brow that, at this very moment, what he sees in you is not a young and pretty woman, but the two million francs you possess? He does not love you, my dear; he is calculating you as if you were an investment. If you are bent on marrying, find an older man with an assured position who is halfway in his career. A widow’s marriage should not be a trivial love affair. Is a mouse to be caught a second time in the same trap?
