A new alliance should be a good speculation on your part, and in marrying again, you ought at least to hope to be addressed as Madame la Maréchale!”Her advice was practical and pitying: seek rank and security rather than a romantic match with Martial, whom she painted as indebted, aging, and opportunistic. The Countess listened, torn between feeling and worldly counsel. Around them the salon’s intrigues continued—wagers, furtive glances, jealous men and calculating women—while the mystery of the pale stranger and the designs of the Duchess, the Colonel, the Baron, and Martial rotated like the dance itself, each player seeking advantage among love, ambition, and social maneuvering.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As she spoke, both women turned to Colonel Montcornet’s handsome face.The Duchess bluntly warned the Countess against flirting instead of marrying. She argued that brief vanities can destroy virtuous women and wreck families, urging genuine affection over fleeting passions. Citing her own regret and the Duc d’Albe’s remark that “one salmon is worth a thousand frogs,” she urged restraint and discretion: if one must toy with men, choose those with unsettled lives who have no duties. Then, pointing to a specific case, she accused the Countess of having ruined Soulanges’s happiness. For fifteen months the Countess had made him lovesick and neglectful of his wife; now the wife lived in tears and Soulanges felt remorse greater than any pleasure the affair had brought. “You’ve affected his whole life,” the Duchess said, and invited the Countess to witness the consequence.Leading Madame de Vaudremont to the card-room door, the Duchess revealed a pale, trembling figure under the candlelight—her grandniece, the Comtesse de Soulanges—who had at last come at the Duchess’s persuasion, leaving the sorrowful room where even her child offered little consolation. The Countess looked away, struck by what happiness once was and what it had become. The sight of Soulanges himself—pale, rigid in an easy chair, his brow tense—confirmed the Duchess’s indictment. Players passed him as if he were dead; the image of husband and weeping wife separated amid festivity made the Countess fear for her own future. She pressed the Duchess’s hand in a childlike thanks.Madame de Lansac, who had brought the niece, insisted that women could repel a man’s attentions as easily as they attracted them. She suggested the Countess could help restore the couple: choose a good husband and shut your door to her nephew. “A wife cannot accept her husband’s heart as a gift from another woman; she is far happier believing she has won it back,” the old lady lectured. By bringing her niece here she hoped to give the Comtesse a chance to regain Soulanges’s affection; all she asked of the Countess was to “play the Colonel.” She pointed to Montcornet; the Countess smiled at the implied role.Left alone, Martial (the Baron) asked whether the Countess knew the unknown woman’s name; she confirmed it playfully. Believing she loved him, Martial affected charm and asked if she would be annoyed if he seemed to care about that name. She warned him that her lingering affection might make her refuse to tell him, and begged him not to approach the young lady—“It could be dangerous.” Martial insisted losing her favor would be worse than losing his life and pressed to dance with the Comtesse de Soulanges himself, taunting Montcornet with the paradox that the Colonel would leave alone the man who had taken another’s love yet would fight for his wife. The Countess retorted that Soulanges’s wife loved her husband, and Martial took the obstacle as a challenge. Their banter mixed jest and bitterness—he promised to desist and then, joking, to go win the other woman and return more devoted than ever.Unaware of Madame de Vaudremont’s deeper designs and the ironic alignment of desires, the men were drawn together by mutual attraction. Meanwhile the lawyer approached the pale Comtesse de Soulanges; her husband entered the ballroom in a flash of anger. The Duchess, ever watchful, intercepted her nephew, took his arm, and pretended boredom to avoid a scene, giving her niece a knowing glance that seemed to signal revenge. Madame de Vaudremont, sensing duplicity in the Duchess’s moralizing, felt a surge of pride and curiosity that unsettled her; in her distraction the Colonel misread her embarrassment and became more ardent. Old diplomats watched, amused by so many concurrent intrigues—the passions of the two couples showed plainly in the crowded rooms and on surrounding faces.Finally the Baron seated himself beside the Comtesse de Soulanges and admired her beauty—the fresh neck, the graceful figure emphasized by fashion. She replied coldly that she never danced at parties and was little known, unaware her aunt had signaled the Baron to engage her. At the same moment Martial, adjusting a diamond on his hand, caught the Countess’s eye and felt hope. She blushed and, when he asked if she enjoyed dancing, confessed she did. He offered to be her partner for the next quadrille; despite a shy protest that she had already refused a tall cavalry colonel, she accepted. Her tone hinted at a deeper emotion that touched Martial’s weary heart. Standing beside her as the quadrille formed, he intended to show all his charms; for him, as for many men, dance was a way to express and win a woman’s heart.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################He led his conquest to the quadrille, where the most brilliant women prized dancing above all. As the orchestra played, the Baron watched the ladies form the square. Madame de Soulanges’ dress rivaled Madame de Vaudremont’s; men murmured their admiration and the blush that followed heightened her charm. When she raised her eyes she sought her partner’s opinion, blending innocence, vanity, and the budding affection of youth. Modest and inexperienced, she nevertheless knew when to glance at him.A new figure by the dancer Trenis brought Martial face to face with the Colonel. The latter joked, “I have won your horse.” Montcornet retorted, “Yes, but you have lost eighty thousand francs a year!” Martial waved it off: “What do I care? Madame de Soulanges is worth millions!”Whispers spread. Less attractive women moralized about Martial and the Comtesse; the more beautiful admired her ease. The men, puzzled by Martial’s luck, did not find him especially attractive. A few sympathetic women warned that a single glance or graceful dance could compromise a young wife. Only Martial grasped his happiness. During the last figure, as the ladies formed the moulinet, he clasped the Countess’s hand and felt her response through the thin glove. “Madame,” he said as the dance ended, “do not return to that corner where you’ve been hiding. Is admiration all you get from your jewels? Come and enjoy the scene — and yourself.”Yielding to him, Madame de Soulanges moved through the rooms with Martial, hesitating at each doorway until he assured her, “He is not here.” They reached a grand picture gallery and later an oval boudoir overlooking the garden, draped with flowers and blue hangings. The noise faded. Startled at first, the Countess resisted, then sat on an ottoman and, reassured by a mirror, relaxed.“This room is charming,” she said. “All here is love and delight!” Martial, moved, watched her: unease, modesty, eagerness—an expression that captivated him. She smiled and, flirtatious, took his left hand and slipped the ring from it: “What a fine diamond!”Intoxicated by her touch, Martial urged, “Wear it, in memory of this hour, and for the love of—” He broke off, caught by her rapture. “You give it to me?” she asked. “I wish I had the whole world to give you!” he replied. “Will you accept only my diamond?” She pressed, “You will never take it back?” “Never,” he assured her. She placed the ring on her finger. As he moved to embrace her, she suddenly rose and said clearly, “I accept the diamond, monsieur, with less scruple because it belongs to me.”Martial was taken aback. “Monsieur de Soulanges took it from my dressing-table and told me he had lost it,” she said. Martial protested that it had been given to him by Madame de Vaudremont. “Precisely,” she smiled. “My husband borrowed it, gave it to her, and she gifted it to you. This ring has made a little journey.” She touched a spring; a lock of M. de Soulanges’ hair appeared. Stunned, she fled into the crowded rooms, leaving Martial confounded.He saw the Colonel and Madame de Vaudremont laughing nearby; the Colonel teased, “Will you have my horse to chase after your prize?” Martial took it in stride, grateful they kept silent about the evening’s events, feeling he had exchanged his horse for a young wife.Driving home across Paris to the Faubourg Saint‑Germain, the Comtesse was consumed by anxiety. At the Hotel Gondreville she had failed to find her aunt and husband, who had left without her. She had long witnessed her husband’s torment since Madame de Vaudremont ensnared him and had hoped for his repentance; now she feared she had been wrong to follow her aunt’s scheme.Crossing the Pont Royal, she discarded the hair from the diamond—the only token of pure affection—and wept, pondering how women sustain devotion when they do not love. At home she found her husband by the fire. He scolded her for attending balls alone; she softened him, saying only that her aunt had taken her and she had been happy. His smile and her tenderness reconciled them. He kissed her hand gratefully; gratitude, she thought, often accompanies love.General Montcornet did not marry Madame de Vaudremont; she later perished in the fire that marred the ball hosted by the Austrian ambassador for Napoleon’s marriage to Joseph II’s daughter.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In September 1835 Clémentine du Rouvre, daughter of a spendthrift marquis, married Comte Adam Mitgislas Laginski, a Polish exile. Settlements from her mother's family secured her income. Adam, not supported by France, faced Parisian prejudice—Polish nobles were scorned and the émigré community divided—so he was mocked.He bought a house on rue de la Pépinière in 1834 and established himself in Paris; the home was lavish.Clémentine noticed Captain Paz, a discreet factotum who ran the household and stables yet remained invisible. A poor veteran relation of Adam’s, Paz became a close friend; his refusal of a gift revealed loyalty and equality. As they prepared to go out, Clémentine's affection for both men grew, showing ties could transcend social norms.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Thaddeus lived reclusively in Paris until the Comtesse Clémentine Laginska was seen riding with him in the Bois de Boulogne and insisted he dine with her and Adam. He dressed for the occasion but acted like a subordinate, answering little while listening to Clémentine; Adam urged him to be livelier. Though outwardly dull, Thaddeus was acutely aware of his love for Clémentine and struggled to conceal it.At the Opera he remained distant; Clémentine felt slighted and Adam excused him as disagreeable. His reserve sprang from unrequited love, deepened after her return from a trip. He took a bittersweet pleasure in being indispensable to their household—knowing it would falter without him even as he suffered inwardly. Clémentine, newly confident and intent on her Parisian standing, left little room for him, though once she noticed him while driving home and invited him along—an invitation that flustered him. Their interactions were charged with unspoken emotion: he felt gratitude and longing but kept his distance, finding solace in managing their affairs and reflecting on his pain.When Clémentine hosted a reception his absence went unnoticed; he retreated into silent devotion. Her constant struggle for attention, rivals, and the strain of maintaining status led her to increasingly neglect Adam. In May, preparing to visit her uncle’s estate, she saw Thaddeus again and insisted he join dinner. He engaged more; her interest grew, and after dinner she confessed dissatisfaction with Adam and a desire for passion. Torn between love and loyalty, Thaddeus almost confessed but instead invented a romance with Malaga, a circus performer, as a deflection. He provided for Malaga and visited her without real intimacy; misunderstandings followed until her despairing letter longing for his companionship reminded him of the constancy of his devotion to Clémentine amid his sacrifice.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Clementine returned home more beautiful. After dinner they discussed Thaddeus and Malaga. Adam feared Thaddeus’s misfortune would harm his standing by linking him to Malaga; Clementine scorned such women, while Adam defended Thaddeus’s sacrifices.Paz, who had asked Adam for money, was seen as a threat; he felt Clementine’s contempt, sank into despair, yet continued to admire her, care for the household, and fell ill.When Adam lost heavily gambling he asked Thaddeus to cover the loss by blaming Malaga; Thaddeus complied reluctantly. Clementine, angered by his generosity to Paz, questioned his loyalty; overwhelmed, Thaddeus taught her household management. In spring he nursed Adam through a grave illness; Adam recovered and praised him. Tormented by unrequited love, Thaddeus left for the Caucasus after sending a confession Clementine and Adam never grasped. Years later she remembered him as a bittersweet, unfulfilled love.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY — central section, framed by decorative separators.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Marquise de Listomere personifies the Restoration spirit, blending religious devotion with worldly elegance. Married seven years to the unremarkable Marquis—whose safe, fault-prone, uncharismatic conduct as a silent deputy has earned him the nickname “dull weather”—she sustains a virtuous façade to support his political prospects until he reaches the peerage.Reserved and principled, she commands respect and sometimes disdain from would-be flirts, replying with contempt yet conversing gracefully with suitable men. The narrator admires her: not plain, with animated eyes that reveal depth and an unforced charm.This emerges in a recent meeting with Eugène de Rastignac, a clever, modestly vivacious young man. At a ball they converse from opera to women’s duties; he leaves abruptly. Next morning Rastignac, distracted, imagines romance while writing letters until Horace mentions an expedition to Morea. The marquise then reads Rastignac’s affectionate letter, initially tempted to discard it but ending fatigued by its impertinence and the emotional tension it implies.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Madame de Listomere forbade her porter to admit Monsieur de Rastignac after finding his insolence intolerable; she even burned a letter he’d sent. Caroline relayed the gossip: “Madame received a fine love‑letter.” That evening the marquise waited at the Marquis de Beauseant’s party for Rastignac until two in the morning, then realized he would not come—Stendhal called her hardening “crystallization.”Four days later Rastignac scolded his valet Joseph for misplacing letters; one had been for Madame de Listomere. He laughed when he learned the lost note was not for Madame de Nucingen, aware his affections had strayed. His mistakes began with a foolish attempt to amuse the marquise by discussing the blunder and grew worse when he stayed away for several days, allowing her displeasure to harden.When he finally called, the porter said she was out but the marquis admitted him. Social awkwardness mounted: the marquise blushed, then recovered with dignity. Eugene tried to smooth things; she promised to forget the incident yet kept a reserve. Mention of the letter provoked feigned offense and suppressed amusement; a remark about Madame de Nucingen changed her tone—she rang for wood to signal his departure and coldly accused him. Embarrassed, he left. Days later she confirmed his explanation of the recipient and withdrew from society—her husband blamed a stomach ailment, her physician a nervous condition. The episode exposes social misunderstandings, emotional pride, and rigid romantic roles.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In the middle of the story. MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In Paris, balls often split into two parts: a formal round of pleasantries and a later, intimate meeting where wit, genuine conversation, and camaraderie flourish in salons hosted by women like Madame d’Espard and Mademoiselle des Touches. At one such evening, the Minister de Marsay recounted an early passionate affair with a woman six years his senior: a secret liaison disguised by outward indifference and private signals sent through the flowers she carried. When illness forced him to cancel a meeting, jealousy drove him to visit; he found her with another man. Her calm tenderness briefly soothed him, but the sight ignited a storm—an end to innocence and a bleaker view of love. He wrestled with revenge but concluded true love must transcend vengeance. His story stirred the salon, prompting reflection on love, jealousy, social expectation, and fragile trust.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Cured of his cold and disillusioned with pure love, the narrator begins an affair with a charming heroine whose beauty contrasts with a previous “angel.” He avoids quarrels with this clever woman, suspecting that practiced deceit may rival virtue. A visit to a hair artisan in the Rue Boucher, who displays hair mementos and mentions passing fashions, confirms his suspicions when he recognizes a handkerchief the artist has worked on. Convinced of women’s inauthentic affections, he resolves to pursue pleasure skeptically.Two months later, in the heroine’s boudoir, he tests Charlotte’s devotion. She professes exclusive love and claims he is the only man in her life, yet when he bluntly asks, “When are you to marry the Duke?” her startled reaction betrays guilt. He suggests she accept the Duke—rich, devout, and proper. She falls to her knees swearing before God that she loves only him; he presses her, recalling seeing her attend mass with the Duke. She dismisses his jealousy, alternates supplication and defiance, and laments that if he had spoken sooner they might have been happy. He insists she has gone too far and urges her to show devotion to avoid the Duke’s suspicions. When she asks what will become of him, he admits having made advances to another woman. Charlotte accuses him of unfaithfulness; he retorts that women cannot truly be loved in society, likening serious attachment to romanticizing an actress. She denounces his betrayal; he feigns submission and questions whether she will remember their grievances once she is a duchess. Her lingering bitterness convinces him he has successfully distanced himself—he believes he has mastered the art of withdrawal.In the salon, others sympathize for Charlotte while De Marsay smiles. Conversation shifts to the erosion of social distinctions: Lady Dudley notes marriages aren’t always seen as inconstancy; De Marsay laments the aristocracy’s decline under the Civil Code, which leveled hierarchies and created a new class of women. Vandenesse and Lord Dudley add that unsettled property hastens the fall; Joseph Bridau and Daniel d’Arthez bemoan the loss of women who once shaped society.They debate the modern “perfect lady”: tasteful, poised, and graceful but lacking the grandeur of past great ladies. Madame de Nucingen argues worth now lies in character and presentation; Blondet defines her as effortlessly elegant, commanding respect by bearing and demeanor. The dialogue highlights modern femininity’s complexity—women who combine charm, beauty, and intellect may compensate for vanished aristocratic splendor or simply reflect a society where merit and presentation replace inherited greatness.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“You will not talk for half an hour with a bourgeoise without her mentioning her husband,” Blondet began, painting the modern lady with grave satire. Married, she keeps her husband nearly invisible: at a ball a middle-aged man with a decoration bows and goes; she orders her carriage and departs. You are not the rose, but you have been with the rose; you sleep under a golden canopy of a delightful dream that may linger.At home she admits no one before four and keeps callers waiting. Her house is tastefully arranged; luxury is for daily use and regularly renewed—no dusty shades or forgotten wrappings. The staircase is warm, flowers abound—accepted only from chosen people, for they symbolize a promise. Fashionable trifles are everywhere but not museum pieces. She sits by the fire without rising to greet you. At the ball she was a creditor; here she owes you the pleasure of her wit. She seeks a man to widen her circle, an object for her attentions. To draw you into her drawing-room she will be charming. All this shows how isolated women are and why they want a little world to shine in. Conversation there lives on generalities.De Marsay agreed: the epigram of the age—brief wit—no longer attacks enduring traits but momentary incidents, fading quickly. Blondet continued: a lady’s intelligence, if she has any, lies in doubting everything. Compare two women: the townswoman is virtuous; the lady hesitates and contends with her virtue. This uncertainty is one of the last graces left her. She rarely goes to church but will discuss religion; if you express free thought she will try to convert you, slipping into familiar moral phrases—“For shame! I thought you had too much sense to attack religion. Society is tottering, and you deprive it of support. Religion means you and me; it is property and the future of our children! Let us not be selfish! Individualism is the disease of the age, and religion is the only remedy”—drifting into a neo-Christian, quasi-political, muddled discourse neither Catholic nor Protestant but very moral.The women laughed at Blondet’s satire. He told Count Adam the portrait showed that the “perfect lady” embodies intellectual and political confusion, surrounded by flashy but fleeting products of an industry that constantly replaces its output. She feigns ignorance to learn everything, probing your heart while seeming delicately to ask your secrets. There are things she never knows, even when she does; you alone feel uneasy, knowing nothing of her heart. The great ladies of old flaunted affairs; today’s lady arranges passions like a musical score, with pauses and accents. A weak woman fears to compromise love, husband, or children’s future, for name, position, and fortune no longer shield her. The aristocracy no longer protects. Unlike the great lady of the past, she lacks the power to crush; she must master Jesuitical compromises, moving between propriety and anonymous passion. She keeps servants and appearances in check like an Englishwoman fearing a divorce trial. So free at a ball and charming on a walk, she is a slave at home; she is never independent except in privacy or theoretically. Her task is to preserve her position as a lady.Blondet added that a woman repudiated by her husband, reduced to a meager allowance without carriage, luxury, or box at the opera, is neither wife, maid, nor townswoman; she is adrift, a chattel. The Carmelites would not accept a married woman; it would be bigamy. Would her lover still want her? That is the question. Thus the perfect lady may provoke calumny but never slander.“This sketch,” he concluded, “places today’s woman between English hypocrisy and eighteenth-century frankness—a bastard system symptomatic of an age of transition where nothing that emerges truly resembles what has vanished. Transition leads nowhere: everything is a matter of degree; distinction becomes purely personal. I am convinced no woman, even near a throne, can, before twenty-five, acquire the encyclopedic trifles, the practice of maneuvering, the small but important arts—tones, silences, angelic mischief, innocent cunning, speech and silence, wit and obtuseness, diplomacy and ignorance—that made the perfect lady.”Mademoiselle des Touches asked where the female author would fit. Blondet replied: when she has no genius, she is a woman “comme il n’en faut pas,” casting a look that suggested praise for Camille Maupin; the epigram, he allowed, was not his but Napoleon’s.Canalis deflected the barb: Napoleon had his literary jealousy, but who can depict him? He was paradox itself: a concentrated force who willed everything, conquering on impulse, a genius for action who spread armed civilization without fixing it. He could do everything because he willed everything; he was a phenomenon of will, conquering illness with battle yet destined to die in bed. He wielded code and sword in his brain, foresaw everything but his fall, was capricious yet decisive, made empires, codes, and even romance. He aimed to make all Europe French and, after changing laws and customs, left things poorer in some ways. He was a man of thought and action who understood his lieutenants, both despot and dispenser of justice when necessary—a true king in times.De Marsay and others exulted: what a pleasure to digest such conversation; wit’s charms are priceless. Joseph Bridau remarked that conversational flashes, like music, have no second performance. The Princesse de Cadignan, smiling at the women, defended contemporary female greatness. Under a regime that makes everything small, we adopt small dishes and small rooms—does that prove women have become smaller? Passions remain the same. There is beautiful devotion and sublime suffering without the old publicity. Contemporary figures—Marquise d’Espard, Madame Doublet, Madame du Deffant—have their counterparts now; Taglioni matches Camargo; Malibran the old singers. If the Grocers who govern us have stifled a distinct style, the Empire had its own stamp; sciences have not retreated. The Comte de Vandenesse agreed: our age contains many women who, like Madame Récamier, will appear to posterity as finely proportioned as any of the past. He urged that Blondet’s portrait of ephemeral fascinations masks the capacity for heroism. Where feelings are illegal, Providence often overwhelms what seems light.A playful exchange ensued: Madame de Vandenesse and others teased the Count to preach; the Baronne noted his preaching matched his example. General Montriveau, turning serious, offered a drama of conscience and will—an involuntary cause of a terrible disaster he had witnessed in the 1812 campaign that might interest Doctor Bianchon, since it concerned both body and mind.He recounted the Beresina retreat. Discipline had dissolved in the mixed, starving army. Wandering from the marshes of Zembin, hungry and without shelter, he found at dusk a wretched Polish farm: a single-room peasant house with a partition for forage. Inside, a few officers and a woman were eating potatoes, horseflesh, frozen beetroots; straw promised a bed. The general, an extraordinary Italian colonel, sat at the table: tall, powerfully built, with abundant black curls, a white complexion, small hands, an aquiline nose, and a temper like a thunderbolt—air that made him both beloved and feared. His force was paradoxical: tender in repose, ferocious in rage.The colonel had once, in a violent outburst on the road where battery wheels clashed, split an opponent’s skull in seconds—proof of irresistible and terrifying fury. The captain whose skull was split had a Sicilian wife, Rosina, who attracted the colonel’s attention. In the hovel opposite the colonel sat that captain and Rosina. Rosina was dark, thin, sun-browned, still possessing grace despite rags; her husband was a Piedmontese gentleman of ironic simplicity that hinted at cruelty. Conversation at the farmhouse was muted. After eating, the colonel bade goodnight, shot a black look at Rosina and called, “Rosina?” She made an expression that mixed offended dignity and helpless submission; a minute later the colonel called again, more brutally. Rosina rose, passed behind the comrades, and went to him. The company sat in silence; Montriveau, despite himself, laughed, and the others followed.The husband, perhaps sensing something, accused Montriveau of laughing. Montriveau professed contrition and at once all made themselves to sleep. Next morning they set off, selfishness and misery governing each man. Soon they converged again. From a knoll they saw the farmhouse barricaded and in flames; the roar and a woman’s scream rose above the smoke. The captain approached and, answering their unspoken curiosity, pointed to his chest and waved toward the fire, saying simply, “Son’io” (“It was I”). The group passed without comment. Montriveau left the scene as a terrible emblem of private revolt and the cruel smallness of human behavior in the rout.“There is nothing more terrible than the revolt of a sheep,” de Marsay observed. The image haunted some listeners; Madame de Montcornet feared she would dream of it.Conversation swung back to lighter jest—Lord Dudley’s quip about English foils, Blondet’s retort—but then de Marsay called on Dr. Bianchon, who had seen a dying woman’s end. Bianchon described, with his usual tenderness, the death of a duchess he and the Duke had watched. Pulmonary consumption in its final stage had reduced her; she had taken the sacrament. The Duke had fallen briefly asleep; waking and seeing his wife, he sat by her side. The dying woman, with a delicate serenity and a pity that seemed boundless, took his hand and murmured, “My poor dear, who is left to understand you now?” She died looking at him. The doctor’s story left a deep, sweet impression.Mademoiselle des Touches rose, and the scene closed on that lingering note of gentle pathos.PARIS, June 1839–42.ADDENDUMCharacters who recur elsewhere in Balzac’s cycle (selected): Bianchon, Horace; Blondet, Émile; Blondet, Virginie (Madame Montcornet); Bridau, Joseph; Canalis, Baron de; Dudley, Lord; Marquise d’Espard; Comte Adam Laginski; Henri de Marsay; Duchesse de Maufrigneuse; General Montriveau; Baron Nucingen and Baronne Delphine; Vicomtesse Portenduere; Eugène de Rastignac; Marquis de Ronquerolles; Comtesse de Serizy; Mademoiselle Félicité des Touches; Comte Félix de Vandenesse.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY repeated with hash-line separators.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################“Ah! madame,” said the doctor, “I have some appalling stories, but each has its proper hour in conversation—” “But it is two in the morning, and the story of Rosina has prepared us,” the hostess replied.“Tell us, Monsieur Bianchon!” they cried.The doctor bowed and began.About a hundred paces from Vendôme, on the bank of the Loir, stands an old brown house with very high roofs, solitary and utterly neglected—no tannery or tavern near it. A garden slopes to the river where once-clipped box shrubs now run wild; willows in the stream form a half-hidden fence. Weeds have taken the bank; fruit trees, neglected for ten years, bear no fruit, their suckers forming a thicket; espaliers look like a little copse. The paths are overgrown. From the hilltop where the ruins of the Dukes of Vendôme cling, one can see that this spot must once have been the pride of a country gentleman devoted to horticulture. An arbor’s wreck and a partially standing table testify to that bygone care. To complete the mournful air, one wall bears a sundial with the motto “Ultimam cogita” (think on your last).The house itself is dreadfully dilapidated: the roof leaking, shutters always closed, swallows nesting on balconies, doors shut. Grasses grow in the flagstones; ironwork is rusty; paint has peeled and boards warped. Silence is broken only by birds, cats, polecats, rats, and mice; to the observer an invisible hand has inscribed one word over it all: Mystery.From the street you see a large gate with a round-arched top, full of children’s holes; the gate had been blocked for ten years. Through breaches you view the courtyard: paving-stones choked with weeds, cracked walls, blackened coping laced with pellitory, disjointed stone steps, a rotten bell-cord, broken gutter-spouts. What fire from heaven, what decree, has struck this dwelling? The empty, deserted house is a vast enigma.It was formerly a small fiefed estate called La Grande Bretèche. During the doctor’s stay in Vendôme—where he had been left to attend a rich patient—this odd dwelling became one of his keenest pleasures. Better than a ruin, it carried a secret, an unrevealed thought: the slow destruction of a standing house seemed like an avenging hand. Many evenings he climbed the wild hedge surrounding the enclosure, braved scratches to enter the ownerless garden, and lingered for hours in its disorder. He wove romances and indulged in melancholia there; had he known the reason for the neglect, the charm would have been gone. To him the place represented various phases of human misfortune: sometimes a graveyard without the dead, sometimes a lepers’ home, sometimes the house of the Atridae—but above all provincial life, contemplative and like an hour-glass. He often wept there; he never laughed.Occasionally he felt involuntary terrors at the dull hum of a passing wood-pigeon. The damp earth required watching for lizards, vipers, and frogs; a cold would seem to settle on your shoulders like the Commendatore’s hand on Don Giovanni’s neck. One evening a weathercock creaked like a cry from the house as the doctor finished a gloomy drama to account for this enormous air of woe. He returned to his inn in a pensive mood. After supper the hostess came into his room with a mysterious air to announce, “Monsieur, here is Monsieur Regnault.”“Who is Monsieur Regnault?” the doctor asked.“What, sir, do you not know Monsieur Regnault?—Well, that’s odd,” she said and left.A tall, slim man in black appeared: hat in hand, receding forehead, small pointed head, colorless face like dirty water—an usherly look. His coat was old, worn at the seams, but he wore a diamond in his shirt frill and gold rings in his ears.“Monsieur,” the doctor asked, “whom have I the honor of addressing?”The man sat by the fire, rubbed his hands and answered, “Dear me, it is very cold. I am Monsieur Regnault.”“My curiosity is piqued,” the doctor said.“I am notary at Vendôme,” Regnault replied. He quickly informed the doctor that the latter’s habit of walking in La Grande Bretèche’s garden constituted a misdemeanor since the estate was enclosed property, and as executor under the will of the late Comtesse de Merret he begged the doctor to desist. He insisted he meant no offense and would be glad to allow free access if he were not bound by the testatrix’s instructions: he had never set foot in the house since the will was read. They had done nothing there but verify doors and windows to assess taxes; administering Madame de Merret’s estate had been the most important event of his life. His account was long and voluble, but the doctor pressed him for the reason that La Grande Bretèche was left to ruin.

Augustine Guillaume's cousin's tale is worth mentioning. 
Élodie-Rose, the widow of Maelien, lived a very miserable life. 
She was born in the same city, Paris, in the same year as Augustine, and grew up happily alongside her, until she got married. 
She married very early at 16, to a skinny man exactly twice her age with the same hair color and height, 
and moved to Albi with him immediately afterward. 
The last time Augustine and Élodie-Rose saw each other was at Élodie-Rose’s wedding. 
Élodie-Rose became single again very shortly after and had to continue her life in a town 
far from her family. She endured a life full of hardship and pain. 
The six feet tall lady aged very quickly—her dark black hair turned gray, 
she developed stage 4 glioblastoma, the worst type of brain cancer, 
and her beautiful hazel eyes grew sad. She lived in Albi for two decades, most of it alone,  
and died there a lonely death.


Regnault delighted in relating his own history and the tale of Madame de Merret. He had been head-clerk in a Paris office that went bankrupt; unable to buy a practice in Paris in 1816, he came to Vendôme, bought his predecessor’s business, and married into local relations. Three months after being licensed, just before his marriage, he was sent for to the Chateau of Merret by Madame la Comtesse. The Count had gone to Paris and died two months previously after falling into dissipation. On the day he left, Madame la Comtesse had quitted La Grande Bretèche, having dismantled it; some said she had burned the furniture and hangings. Regnault had been summoned: the Countess was very ill and seemed unlikely to survive the night, having received the Sacrament. The maid who met him at the door was now a servant in the inn.When Regnault reached the chateau about eleven, he climbed the great staircase, crossed large, lofty, dark rooms—cold and damp—and reached the state bedroom where the Countess lay. Rumors had painted her as a coquette; reality confounded that image. The enormous, old-fashioned room, papered with brown and filled with dust, had only an old Argand lamp for light. The bed was antique, the tester hung with flowered chintz; on a small table lay an Imitation of Christ and the lamp; a deep armchair for the maid and two small chairs formed the whole furnishing; there was no fire. If you had seen that vast room you would have felt transported into a romance; it was icy, funereal.Madame de Merret lay propped up, waxen and narrow-faced. Her hair, abundant but white as linen, was shown by a lace cap; her large black eyes, dimmed by fever, hardly moved beneath bony brows. Her forehead was clammy; her fleshless hands looked like bones covered with soft skin; veins and muscles showed—she must have once been striking, but disease had consumed her to a phantom. Her lips, pale-violet, scarcely moved when she spoke. Though Regnault’s work had exposed him to many deaths and last wishes, this solitary figure in her vast chateau was the most affecting: no sound, almost no breath, yet an immense presence. When her eyes moved, she tried to raise a hand, then, with difficulty, drew from under her pillow a sealed paper. “I place my will in your hands—Oh! God! Oh!” she uttered, then clutched a crucifix, kissed it, and died. Regnault remembered a joy stamped on her last glance, an expression that made him shudder, and he took away the will: she had appointed him her executor.The will proved extraordinary. She left most of her property to the Vendôme hospital, with a few legacies. As for La Grande Bretèche, she ordered that, for fifty years from her death, it should remain precisely in the state it was at her death: nobody, whoever he might be, should enter the apartments, no repairs were to be made, and watchmen were even to be paid if necessary to secure this injunction. At the expiration of the term, if the will had been dutifully executed, the house would become the executor’s heirs’ property; otherwise it would revert to the heirs-at-law under terms set in a codicil not to be opened for fifty years. The will had not been contested. Regnault’s account thrilled him and he enjoyed the legal triumph.The doctor, vivid imaginings spinning into a gothic romance, pressed Regnault for the specifics of the Countess’s eccentric injunctions, but the notary affected comical reticence. Eventually he regaled the doctor further with town gossip—diffuse, contradictory opinions that nearly put the doctor to sleep. Regnault’s monotone and self-satisfaction were too much for curiosity, and the notary took his leave, teasingly suggesting that many would wish to live fifty years longer, and hinting cryptically about age.After Regnault left, the doctor, still stirred, sat in his armchair when his landlady, Madame Lepas, entered. A buxom, florid Flemish woman with the keen scrutiny of an innkeeper, she had overheard the notary’s history. The doctor repeated the story, and Madame Lepas leaned close, an instinctive mix of constable, spy, and dealer in her look.When asked about Monsieur de Merret, she described him as extremely tall, a good gentleman from Picardy, taciturn and hot-tempered but generous in paying his debts, and well-liked by the common folk despite his haughty manner. He and Madame de Merret had once been a handsome couple; she was pretty, sweet, and wealthy—about twenty thousand francs a year. They were a splendid match and their wedding had filled the town. Were they happy? “So-so,” Madame Lepas said—the common folk were not intimate with their life. Madame de Merret was kind and pleasant but must sometimes have endured her husband’s temper.Yet the hostess then confided what she knew. During the Emperor’s time, when Spaniards and prisoners of war were sent here, she lodged a young Spaniard sent to Vendôme on parole. Despite the parole, he had to present himself daily to the sub-prefect. He was a Spanish grandee with a fanciful name—Bagos de Férédia or something like it. For a Spaniard he was handsome though short—no more than five feet two or three—but well made, with small, fastidious hands; he kept his hands, she said, as carefully as a woman keeps brushes. He had thick black hair, a flame in his eye, a somewhat coppery complexion, and the finest linen she had ever seen—finer than anything even princesses had worn at her inn. He ate little but had such polite, amiable ways that he was impossible to dislike; he spoke scarcely at all.He was devout: he read his breviary like a priest and went to mass regularly. Most notably, he always took his station within two yards of Madame de Merret’s chapel. At his first attendance there no one suspected intent; he never raised his eyes above his book. In the evenings he walked the hill among the ruins of the old castle—a pastime that must have reminded him of Spain, which, they said, is all hills.Thus the scene closes on La Grande Bretèche, the Countess’s strange last will that sealed the house for half a century, and the shadow of a Spanish exile whose daily position near the Countess’s chapel and solitary walks among the ruins suggest a link between foreign passion and domestic catastrophe—an implication the doctor, Regnault, and Madame Lepas allow the listener to supply, while the dilapidated house stands mute and enigmatic by the Loir.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Shortly after he arrived, he stayed out late one evening and did not return until midnight. We were used to his whims; he had the key, and we did not wait. He lived in a house we owned on Rue des Casernes. One of our stable-boys thought he had seen the Spanish Grandee swimming in the river. When he did return I warned him about the weeds; he seemed annoyed at being seen.One morning he had not come back at all. Searching his things, I found a note in his drawer, fifty doubloons—about five thousand francs—and a sealed box of diamonds worth ten thousand francs. The note said that, if he did not return, he left the money and diamonds to pay for masses in gratitude for his escape.My husband went searching and came back with the Spaniard’s clothes, found under a stone on a breakwater near la Grande Bretèche. He had come early, unseen. After reading the letter my husband burned the clothes and, in keeping with Count Férédia’s wishes, we announced that he had escaped. The sub-prefect sent the constabulary after him, but he was never caught. Lepas thought the Spaniard had drowned. I suspected otherwise, believing him implicated in Madame de Merret’s affair; Rosalie had told me the crucifix her mistress cherished and was buried with was ebony and silver, and early in his stay Monsieur Férédia had owned a similar crucifix that I never saw again.“My hostess asked, with a curious mixture of greed and conscience, whether she ought to feel remorse for the Spaniard’s fifteen thousand francs. Aren’t they truly mine?” I said, “Certainly. But have you ever questioned Rosalie?” She had tried; Rosalie was like a wall. The thought of la Grande Bretèche, its overgrown grass, shuttered windows, and deserted rooms, filled me with vague sinister curiosity and religious dread. I resolved not to leave Vendôme without learning the house’s secret. Rosalie, who seemed central to the affair, became my preoccupation.She presented an odd mixture of bloom and reserve—obedient, simple, clumsy at times, yet with an inner sorrow or hope. I decided to make advances if necessary to learn the truth. Calling her one evening, I teased about suitors and asked why she had become an inn-servant after leaving Madame de Merret; though the mistress had left her an annuity, Rosalie preferred her present post. Her evasiveness convinced me she knew more. After a fortnight I pressed her trust and she agreed to tell me the story on condition of secrecy.Rosalie’s account, between the notary’s gossip and Madame Lepas’s version, I will summarize. Madame de Merret’s bedroom at la Grande Bretèche was on the ground floor; a small, four-foot-deep wall cupboard served as a wardrobe. Three months before the evening in question Madame de Merret had been seriously ill; her husband had begun sleeping on the first floor. One night he returned late from the club. He had used Rosalie to tell him if his wife was in bed; that evening he decided to check, perhaps to share his troubles or seek comfort. He entered thinking he heard a closet door shut; Madame de Merret stood by the fireplace, anxious.“You’re very late,” she said huskily. Monsieur de Merret remained silent as Rosalie entered, which startled him. He began pacing. “Have you received bad news, or are you unwell?” his wife asked while Rosalie helped her undress. He did not answer.“You can go, Rosalie,” Madame de Merret said; once she was gone—or lingering in the passage—Monsieur confronted his wife: “Madame, there’s someone in your cupboard!” She calmly replied, “No, monsieur.”Her “No” pierced him. She appeared more saintly than ever. He moved to open the closet, but Madame de Merret took his hand and, with strange emotion, said: “Remember, if you find no one there, everything must end between us.” Her dignity disarmed him. “No, Josephine,” he said; “I won’t open it. I know your soul’s purity.” Then, producing an ebony-and-silver crucifix she owned, he made her swear on it that there was nobody in the closet. She swore without flinching.Observing the crucifix, he remarked it was fine work; she said she had bought it from Duvivier, who had bought it from a Spanish monk when a troop of Spanish prisoners passed through Vendôme. Then he rang for Rosalie and, in the presence of his coachman Jean and others, gave directions: he wanted everyone to sleep and for Jean to tell him when they were asleep. He kept his gaze on his wife and gave no sign of suspicion.A recent renovation had left Monsieur de Merret with a surplus of plaster, which gave him an idea. Gorenflot, a mason, was sent to wall up the cupboard door with bricks and plaster, ostensibly using the leftover materials. But the mason was to sleep there that night, and Monsieur de Merret secretly arranged to bribe him: a passport to leave the country, six thousand francs for the journey and another six thousand upon fulfilling conditions—going abroad, staying ten years, remaining silent. For Rosalie, he promised ten thousand francs, payable on her wedding day if she married Gorenflot and kept silent; otherwise no wedding gift. Rosalie, too, was bribed with smaller sums: a thousand francs a year if she persuaded Gorenflot to leave a crack at the bottom of the wall.As Gorenflot worked, he broke one of the two panes in the top of the cupboard door to peer in. He and Rosalie saw the face of a dark, gloomy man with black hair and fiery eyes. The poor woman gave the man a nod—an emblem of “Hope.” By four in the morning the wall was completed; Gorenflot was to be kept near and Monsieur slept in his wife’s room.Next morning Monsieur de Merret announced he must go to the Maire for the passport, took the crucifix, and left. Madame de Merret, joyously hopeful, immediately rang for the pick and cried, “We must work. I saw how Gorenflot did it yesterday; we have time to make a gap and build it up again.” Rosalie fetched a cleaver and began demolishing the wall with frantic energy. She had removed a few bricks when, turning to strike harder, she saw Monsieur de Merret standing behind her. She fainted.“Lay madame on her bed,” he said coldly. He had set a trap in his absence—he had only written for the passport and had summoned Duvivier; the jeweler arrived as they tried to repair the disorder. Monsieur de Merret questioned Duvivier about buying crucifixes from Spanish passersby; Duvivier denied it. The husband then confined his wife to her room for twenty days, never leaving, and when the cupboard made noises and Josephine would plead for the dying man hidden there, he silenced her, reminding her of the oath she had sworn on the Cross.When Bianchon finished this account, all the ladies at the table rose, shaken by the last words.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################During the Restoration Archbishop of Besançon frequented Baronne de Watteville’s salon for her piety. De Watteville, a descendant of Watteville, married Mademoiselle de Rupt—arranged 1802, formalized 1815. After her grandparents died, Madame grew more devout and influential.De Watteville worked as a turner and collected specimens; admired for his humility, he accepted his wife's prominence and devoted himself to work. The Rupt house showed their status; Madame’s fortune far exceeded his modest estate. Their ties attracted abbés.At a September 1834 dinner Abbé de Grancey announced a favorable suit; the Vicar‑General credited Savaron, a nearby lawyer of unknown origin, intriguing guests. Amedée de Soulas would later prove significant.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################France and England have long traded cultural influences—what is "English" in Paris becomes "French" in London. Examples range from the English anthem "God Save the King," said to have been composed by Lulli for a French play, to hoop skirts and shifting fashions mocked in Paris after 1815.In the 19th century England contributed terms like "dandy" and "lion," the latter popularized by Alfred de Musset and sparking a trend of stylish young men even in the provinces. In 1834 Besançon’s "lion" was Amedee-Sylvain de Soulas, of Spanish descent, ill at ease in the dull military town. He kept a spare apartment, cultivated an ostentatious appearance, and maintained a young servant, Babylas, as his "tiger," dressing him fashionably while living on scant means. Amedee economized cunningly—often dining out—to sustain a polished façade and worldly tales aimed at impressing the local aristocracy and winning heiress Mademoiselle Rosalie de Watteville. Besançon’s conservative "Colony" used his quirks to seem modern to Parisians. Rosalie, modest and resolute, was fiercely protected by her mother, complicating Amedee’s pursuit. His story shows social ambition, cultural exchange, and provincial constraint.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Madame,” Monsieur de Soulas began while his soup cooled, “a Parisian gentleman took rooms on the first floor of Mademoiselle Galard’s house on Rue du Perron and registered at the Mairie as ‘Albert Savaron.’ He left his card with the local officials.”“The Savaron name is well known,” Mademoiselle de Watteville observed.“He’s a Frenchman and no man’s son,” Amedee de Soulas replied. “If he claims the Savaron arms he must add a bar-sinister.” Rosalie noted that even a bastard of a Comte de Savarus remains noble, but the Baroness cut her off. The Vicar-General went on: in a town like Besançon, Albert was carefully catalogued. He sent cards to judges rather than calling, hired Jérôme, the late Monsieur Galard’s servant who could cook, and kept mostly to himself.“Does he go to mass?” asked Madame de Chavoncourt.“He attends early service at Saint-Pierre,” Monsieur de Soulas answered. “He rises between one and two in the morning, works until eight, breakfasts, works again, walks in his garden, dines, and sleeps early.” He watched Albert from Rue Neuve; his servant spoke with Jérôme; townsfolk provided the rest.The Baroness inquired why the Vicar-General had engaged him. He had been assigned to defend a simple peasant accused of forgery; Savaron proved the man innocent, exposed the true culprits, secured arrests, and later won a merchant’s difficult suit. Monsieur Berryer recommended him, and the Vicar-General’s confidence was justified.“Is he extraordinary?” asked Madame de Chavoncourt.“Certainly,” replied the Abbe de Grancey, who then painted a striking portrait. He first found Savaron in a room lined with law books and furnished simply: an old table, six armchairs, gray curtains and a green carpet. Younger than expected, Savaron wore a black dressing gown with red accents. The Abbe declared him devilishly dressed but magnificent in head and countenance: thick black hair streaked with gray, a round white throat, a forehead furrowed by thought, an olive complexion, a square nose, fiery, eagle-like eyes—passionate yet calm, with a sweet voice that could thunder. He was of average height, neither stout nor thin, with hands like a prelate’s.Subsequent visits found him in a shabby bedroom or having a frugal breakfast in his library. The Abbe sensed something heavy burdening Savaron—a man not merely learned but marked by secret weight. He wondered why such a man left Paris for Besançon and predicted he would make a significant impact.Rosalie, the Vicar-General’s daughter, was captivated. The Abbe’s portrait offered for the first time a figure far removed from the commonplace suitors she’d known. She replayed his words—“What a fine forehead!”—and compared him in her mind to the men at the table. When pressed by her mother, she confessed she had lost her appetite thinking of the prelate-like hands and the window she had glimpsed from her bed, now knowing it belonged to him. “I must see him,” she decided.That night she planned to wake between two and three to glimpse his windows. The sight of candlelight and a man at work while the world slept filled her with a sacred thrill. The matter, she knew, raised the broader issue of educating girls: how much should they know? Too much knowledge forces premature maturity; too little leaves them stunted. Rosalie’s sudden inner stirring—transforming the ordinary into the extraordinary—was a consequence of that precarious balance.The next morning she saw Albert walking in his garden and resolved to enter his house, uncover his mystery, hear his voice, and glimpse his eyes. She embroidered with the absorbed air of someone outwardly idle but inwardly intense. She arranged confession at half-past seven before the eight o’clock service, hoping to meet him.She suggested to her father a grotto belvedere in the garden as a pretext. He delighted in carving larger pieces and liked the conceit; she cautioned him not to tell her mother. She pictured an observatory from which to spy on Albert’s private room. Young girls, she reflected, often contrive elaborate plans to adore from afar.Sunday she dressed carefully and arrived at church before Mass. She watched for the Abbe and positioned herself to see Albert. To her newly curious eye, no man could look unattractive. Albert’s appearance astounded her: solemn, burdened, with a deep, intense gaze like an eagle’s revealing hidden ardor. In a bold moment she stood in his path and exchanged glances; a rush of warmth followed. After confession, Mass ended and Albert was gone. She left convinced the Vicar-General had been right: he was unhappy, an “eagle” in exile. Her desire to know everything about him hardened into resolve.Rosalie masked her absorption with innocent industry and, with a practiced air, supported the belvedere plan. Madame de Watteville approved, inspected columns, and chose a site commanding a view into Savaron’s rooms. Builders were engaged to construct a grotto with periwinkles, clematis, ivy, honeysuckle and Virginia creeper; the interior would be rustic—mirror, ottoman, inlaid bark table—and Monsieur de Watteville suggested an asphalt floor while Rosalie wanted a rustic chandelier. Rumors spread in Besançon about the Wattevilles’ new garden feature and its cost—“a thousand crowns!” exclaimed Madame de Chavoncourt. No one guessed Rosalie’s true motive: a foolish-sounding scheme to peer into Albert’s study.Meanwhile Albert’s legal triumphs were quickly resented by jealous local lawyers. He remained secluded, avoiding society, and continued to win challenging cases, gaining four prominent merchant clients. More importantly, he launched a fortnightly paper, the Eastern Review, funded by forty shares of five hundred francs each from his first ten clients. The Review aimed to promote Besançon as a center of trade and enlightenment to rival Strasbourg and Dijon. Savaron kept his name out of the foreground, letting Monsieur Boucher handle finances while he retained editorial control and a share of the profits.The Review, modestly priced, quickly secured five hundred subscribers. To avoid offending local pride, Savaron gave the literary management to Alfred Boucher, a junior eager to shine, while he subtly guided the content. With two hundred more subscribers, shareholders might see dividends; the editor, for now, remained unpaid. The third issue gained notice among French publications and contained a tale attributed to Savaron that stirred comment in Madame de Chavoncourt’s drawing room and drew ridicule from the town’s more conservative circles.Rosalie, hearing of the Review, persuaded her father to subscribe and keep it in his room—her mother would not have allowed her to read it—so he did. That night Rosalie devoured the tale—the first story she had ever read. Given her sheltered upbringing, it affected her deeply; she was convinced the narrative reflected Savaron himself, a confession of his life and feeling. Others likewise suspected that Albert drew from his own experience in his writing.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In 1823 two young law students left Lucerne to tour the lake of the Four Cantons. Passing Gersau, Rodolphe was so taken with a newly built house and a woman at the window that he stayed while his friend Leopold continued the trip. Leopold was steady and destined for a Parisian notary’s life; Rodolphe, the illegitimate son of a nobleman, had a troubled past and a passionate, impulsive nature. His mother had sacrificed for his education.Rodolphe lodged with a couple and learned the woman was Fanny Lovelace, who had moved there with her elderly father and supported him by translating and writing. He admired her beauty and musical gift. After gaining reluctant permission to visit the Bergmanns’ gardens, he noticed a deaf-mute girl, Gina, of mysterious origins. The Bergmanns guarded Fanny; later he heard her sing and, when he finally met her, an unknown assailant wounded him—Fanny and Gina aided his recovery.During convalescence he learned his mother had died, plunging him into despair, yet he found solace in Fanny. He confessed love, discovered she was married to an older man, Emilio, though she later admitted the marriage had been coerced and he was not as old as he seemed. Their intimacy grew, complicated by Rodolphe’s jealousy—especially when Tito, a former lover, reappeared—and by ambition, duty, and social constraints. They dreamed of freedom, but love, ties, and sacrifice kept them conflicted, the landscape echoing their passions.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For twenty days Rodolphe stayed with the Bergmanns, watching Francesca with a mix of fascination and puzzlement. He saw in her both youthful imprudence and resolute, sometimes resistant character. The old man treated her like a father; her gratitude suggested an innate nobility that deepened the enigma she presented.Those days combined secret joys and melancholy, quarrels as sweet as harmony. Rodolphe grew increasingly captivated by Francesca’s constant tenderness and jealous over trifles. One evening, when she said she wished to leave Gersau for its lack of luxuries, he asked if she valued luxury. She answered passionately, equating a love of luxury with appreciation for art and beauty, while insisting she had endured hardship without complaint. Her playful claim that ordinary people aspire to greatness revealed her charm.Francesca then insisted Rodolphe not accompany them to Geneva, citing gossip and the need to protect her husband’s reputation. She professed love for Rodolphe but demanded secrecy, kissed him, and left him astonished.The next day Rodolphe went to Vevay and Geneva, renting a room in Eaux-Vives and asking after Italian refugees—hoping for a bookseller named Lamporani—but found only Prince and Princess Colonna and Prince and Princess Gandolphini. At Villa Diodati he saw Francesca in an elegant carriage and felt dizzy: the intimacy he had felt collided with her noble rank, and he feared he had been a whim.He left his card with Prince Gandolfini, attended a concert by invitation, and that night his passion intensified and his ambition to rise socially burned. At the concert they met; she was radiant, and he believed she had remained true. Introduced to the Colonnas, she teasingly called herself “Princess Rodolphini,” acknowledged his faithful affection, and he confessed his desire to achieve greatness so she could be proud.Francesca’s life was full of contradiction: born for the cloister, family tragedies forced marriage to Prince Gandolphini to preserve status, yet she prized independence and liberal ideas. Winter in Geneva flew by, but in spring Rodolphe grew restless; her calm clashed with his need for more demonstrative feeling. On a terrace he bitterly lamented others’ happiness, wounding her; finding her in tears, he kissed her. She rebuffed him, asserted her dignity, and ordered him to leave—vowing they would not meet again until Naples—and he complied.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Rodolphe saw Schinner’s portrait of Gandolphini. Francesca persuaded the reluctant artist to paint one for him and a duplicate for Emilio, beginning their indulgence—correspondence.Ambition and love led him to invest all in a venture of deceit that cost three years. Villele ministry's fall ruined him; after months at Villa Gandolphini he turned to politics. His talents won a promised post, but the July 1830 upheaval thwarted him. Only Francesca and God witnessed his brave, unfavored heart. Lovers, pray for him!############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When Mademoiselle de Watteville finished her tale, Rosalie flushed and wept. The novel—an early instance of a man narrating his own love—was her first encounter with that voice and made her jealous, convinced that Francesca Colonna was Albert’s beloved.Despite insecurities about her looks and lack of musical talent, Rosalie resolved to win Albert or at least learn the truth. Her sheltered imagination devised schemes—sneaking into his garden, writing, visiting her father’s land—to force a meeting. From Albert’s window she saw Jerome being helped over the wall by Mariette; Mariette’s plainness explained Jerome’s interest and taught Rosalie servants could bypass her mother’s restrictions.The next morning Rosalie accompanied Mariette to Mass and bargained for access to letters Albert sent by Jerome; Mariette, relieved, agreed. A letter to Leopold disclosed Albert’s noble lineage, struggles, ambition, and steadfast devotion to Francesca. Reading more, Rosalie felt her own inadequacy and the wrong of invading privacy but justified it as proof of love. When she read a letter meant for Francesca confirming Albert’s hopes, she was moved to step back, recognizing the nobility of their bond and longing for such selfless, enduring love.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Rosalie spent the winter of 1834–35 in turmoil; by April, at nineteen, she fantasized about triumphing over the Duchesse d’Argaiolo, nurturing private passion and dark plans. The story is offered as a cautionary example of such women.That winter Albert de Savarus advanced his position in Besançon, pressing for the Chamber’s dissolution and winning backing from a wealthy contractor connected to moderates. He promoted restoring the Arcier aqueduct—arguing Doubs water was unfit—despite local dependence on Roman works seeming odd; the local Review echoed him and “Roman” water became accepted across parties. Hidden interests drove the debate, opponents were dismissed as old-fashioned, and after eighteen months Savarus had stirred the stagnant town and gained influence without broad popularity. He also won lawsuits for local priests and, though he appeared indifferent to personal gain, his ambition sought larger rewards: in October 1834 he aided a struggling merchant with Léopold Hannequin’s funds, helping his election prospects. The Abbé de Grancey, seeing Savarus’s designs, called him remarkable but misdirected.Meanwhile Rosalie resolved to introduce Savarus to the Hôtel de Rupt’s society, initially content to observe. The Wattevilles’ estate, les Rouxey, produced income but was poorly managed by the inattentive Baron and steward Modinier; Watteville’s heirs pressed a dubious claim on the village of Riceys, whose inhabitants long held rights. After the Baron's death, his descendants continued asserting usurpation.In May 1835 Rosalie went to les Rouxey, enchanted by its lake and linking it to love. On a boat her father revealed legal troubles over Riceys; Rosalie urged hiring Savarus to secure the Dent de Vilard, wanting the lawsuit—and Savarus—at les Rouxey to fix her future and create gardens. The Abbé visited Savarus to persuade him to represent the Wattevilles; Savarus hesitated, fearing political compromise. Rosalie even wrote to the préfet exposing his ambitions, but was ignored.As the election neared, influential men unexpectedly nominated Savarus; surprised, he was cautioned by the Abbé to be transparent about his past. Savarus’s passionate speech impressed many, yet the préfet worked to undermine him and the first vote disappointed him. In despair he reflected on Francesca, until the Abbé assured behind-the-scenes support, promised the Rouxey case would be settled, and suggested marriage to Sidonie de Chavoncourt to consolidate votes. Savarus, reluctantly seeing the advantage, prepared to navigate love and politics.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By nine the next evening Madame la Baronne de Watteville’s rooms filled with Besançon’s aristocracy to debate the surprise nomination of a candidate—the daughter of the Rupts, linked to a former Master of Appeals and secretary to a loyal minister of the Elder Branch. Madame de Chavoncourt came with her younger daughter Sidonie; her elder daughter, secure in her relationship, stayed away. The Abbe de Grancey steered talk, probing an elderly statesman about whom the Elder Branch might support; they feared Berryer, noted the Duc de Fitz-James’s Toulouse nomination, and worried that backing Monsieur Savarus could drive Republicans toward them instead of the Moderates.By nine-thirty Albert had not arrived; Madame de Watteville took his lateness as an insult while Madame de Chavoncourt counselled patience. Rosalie, watching, hinted at a possible match between Albert and Sidonie. At ten impatience grew; some gentlemen played cards. The Abbe, uneasy, went to the window where Rosalie had withdrawn and cried, “He must be dead!” The Vicar-General, Monsieur de Watteville, and Rosalie went to Albert’s dark rooms; the servant Jerome said Albert had left in a post-chaise. The Abbe exclaimed ambiguously, “He is ruined! Or he is happy!” and Rosalie’s face betrayed joy.Back in the drawing-room the Baroness announced Albert’s unexplained departure. By half-past eleven only about fifteen guests remained. A servant brought a letter for the Abbe; he read it, recognized the handwriting, folded it away and declared, “She is in love with Albert Savarus!” Rosalie then confessed the letter was from Albert and begged him to keep silent about Albert’s withdrawal from the election, knowing such a withdrawal would destroy any hope of her sister’s marriage to him.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################On her way to Mass Mademoiselle de Watteville learned from Mariette that Albert had vanished. An elderly Parisian had visited Monsieur Savaron; Savaron turned pale, met him for over an hour, left with a large package, hurried to the Prefecture, bought an old carriage with post horses, quit his post, and obtained a passport. His abrupt departure inflamed local prejudice against outsiders. Most soon forgot Albert—except Girardet, the Vicar-General, and Rosalie, who alone knew the truth about the Duc d’Argaiolo’s death.By April 1836 there was still no news. The Baroness pressed Rosalie to marry; the Baron promised the family estate, les Rouxey. A match with Monsieur de Soulas was arranged; Rosalie refused. Tensions rose that summer. Rosalie confessed to the Vicar-General that she had sabotaged Albert’s relationship with Francesca, the Duchess, by intercepting their letters. A clerk acting for Albert later revealed he had entered a monastery. The Vicar-General obtained Albert’s letter: he forgave Rosalie and chose solitude. Urged to reconcile and accept de Soulas, she refused. After the Vicar-General’s death Madame de Watteville married de Soulas. Now of age and independent, Rosalie confronted the Duchess in Paris, publicly admitting her part in Albert’s downfall, and returned to Besançon bearing the heavy cost of love, betrayal, and ambition.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Mademoiselle de Watteville lived alone on her les Rouxey estate—riding, hunting, and improving the land—making her notable in the Eastern provinces. Madame de Soulas, mother of two, looks younger than her years; Monsieur de Soulas has grown considerably older and told young Chavoncourt, “My fortune has cost me dear. To know a bigot, it is unfortunately necessary to marry her!”Known for her eccentric vagaries, Mademoiselle de Watteville visits the walls of the Grande Chartreuse annually, perhaps following the example of a grand‑uncle who once broke monastery walls to gain his freedom. In 1841 she left Besançon, ostensibly to marry though her motive was unclear; she returned after a steamboat boiler explosion on the Loire that cost her her right arm and left leg and scarred her face. With failing health and constant pain she now devotes herself to religious practices at the Chartreuse of les Rouxey.PARIS, May 1842.ADDENDUMCharacters appearing elsewhere in the Human Comedy: Beauseant, Vicomtesse de — Father Goriot; The Deserted Woman; Genovese — Massimilla Doni; Hannequin, Léopold — Beatrix; Cousin Betty; Cousin Pons; Jeanrenaud — The Commission in Lunacy; Nueil, Gaston de — The Deserted Woman; Rhetore, Duc Alphonse de — A Bachelor’s Establishment; A Distinguished Provincial at Paris; Scenes from a Courtesan’s Life; Letters of Two Brides; The Member for Arcis; Savaron de Savarus (Albert) — The Quest of the Absolute; Schinner, Hippolyte — The Purse; A Bachelor’s Establishment; Pierre Grassou; A Start in Life; The Government Clerks; Modeste Mignon; The Imaginary Mistress; The Unconscious Humorists; Tinti, Clarina — Massimilla Doni.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Middle of the story continues, repeated and framed by decorative separators.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Sweetheart, I too am free! I write first, unless you have already written to Blois.Do not be alarmed; my escape from the convent was simple. The conscience that tormented me yielded to gentler reasons: my aunt feared I would waste away, and my mother relented. I am in Paris thanks to you. If you had seen me when I left you, you would have been proud of your influence on my young heart. At the convent we were so intimate—our imaginations fused and made the closed world a playground—that when you left I fell into deep melancholy. I was like a turtle-dove widowed of her mate; death seemed sweet, and I nearly succumbed. To be at Blois, consumed by dread of taking vows and without you, made me ill.My aunt could not comprehend the private life we led, nor how ill-suited the strict routine was for girls of our temperament. She had sacrificed herself for a brother; to expect the same self-denial from us was too much. For a fortnight I suppressed everything, but letter-writing became my lifeline. I began a journal imagining your own, and dreamed of Gemenos as you will of Paris.One morning a lady companion and Philippe, my grandmother's last valet, arrived to take me away. My aunt kissed me and spoke of the pride she saw in me, which might lead to heaven or hell. I kissed her hands and promised not to return unless my heart were broken.At Beaugency I felt stunned. No one was home—my mother at Bois de Boulogne, my father at council, my brother late dressing. Philippe led me to the suite of my beloved grandmother, the Princess de Vauremont, whose fortune had been left to me. Entering the rooms where she had died brought a wave of grief; though she died in 1817, the place seemed recently occupied. It pained me that so little had been done since her death; the panels were empty and marbles broken. Philippe told me the Princess's furniture had been willed to me, and my father was awaiting a law to restore lost property before renovating—an estimate of three hundred thousand livres had been set.At first despair gripped me: had my father meant to send me to the convent, sparing no expense on my household? But memories of the Princess softened me; the rooms preserved her spirit. I set Philippe to polishing and rearranging, giving the old furniture a youthful air.The bedroom and drawing-room, in yellow damask and scarlet and filled with family relics—a Savonnerie tapestry, a timepiece from Maréchal de Saxe, china from Maréchal de Richelieu, my grandmother's portrait—breathed another century and her coquetry. I recalled how she tended her appearance to the end; her drawing-room showed traces of nocturnal visitors, disarranged furniture, card-tables, snuff.When I was sent to the convent in 1816 she bade me go without display, promising I should remain independent and marry whom I pleased. Six months later she died, leaving her will to the Prince de Talleyrand, who hinted through a friend that she disapproved of the vows. I hope to meet the Prince and learn more.Thus, though no one in flesh met me at first, the Princess's presence comforted me, and I resolved to keep our pact: to tell each other the smallest domestic details. Send me every minute picture of your life.October 19th.At half-past five, Rose announced my mother's return. My mother's suite corresponds to mine, and we share a secret staircase. I descended anxious about our reunion, wondering if I should feign affection. Her tact surprised me: she was neither distant nor effusive, but greeted me like a friend and kissed my forehead.She said she preferred my living with family to dying at the convent, and wished to be more sister than mother. I found her charming at thirty-eight—beautiful, with dark-blue eyes, a smooth forehead, a marvelous figure and hands—and I responded kindly. She took me in and said we had guests for dinner, advising I not make my first appearance in society before visiting the dressmaker.My father arrived and greeted me affectionately, calling me his rebel daughter and praising my looks; he predicted pride in my future. My brother, the Duc de Rhetore, arrived leisurely and kissed me; I was conscious of his indifference for not having come to Blois when I was there.I tidied my rooms and began writing to you. Thus ended the return of an eighteen-year-old after nine years at a convent, to a noble household where old ways linger. Tired, I kept convent hours and slept early.November 25th.The next morning the rooms were made ready; Philippe had put flowers in the vases.
