Breakfast was troublesome as convent habits left me hungry early. About one, my father knocked and found me writing. He gave me a purse containing twelve thousand francs as the annual allowance he intended for me, and said my grandmother left me five hundred thousand francs in government stock, yielding about forty thousand francs a year. He had planned to use this to establish my younger brother but now left the decision to me, trusting my good sense. He warned that a Chaulieu daughter must comport herself with pride; any slander might have dire consequences. His frankness pleased me and spared my pride.Philippe spent the morning arranging my transformation: dressmaker Victorine, corset-maker, shoemaker, glove-maker—all called to fashion me out of convent attire. I was impatient to see my new self; even a glovemaker was summoned. At lunch, my mother proposed we go to the milliner; I felt dazzled by this independence—like a blind man who had recovered sight. I began to sense how far I had been from society.My father seemed absent at lunch; he is deeply engaged in state affairs and had refused an appointment as Minister of State, preferring an embassy. At home, family life is fragmentary: my brother and father are often away, my mother devotes mornings to dressing and a busy social calendar of drives, receptions, and evenings at theatres and concerts. We meet mainly at lunch and dinner; otherwise each pursues his own life. I see now why family affection is so rare: in Paris people have no time for private intimacy.Miss Griffith, my governess—recommended by the English ambassador—is a Scotch clergyman's daughter, thirty-six, well-bred and charged to teach me English and chaperon me. She is cautious but accommodating; I quickly perceive she will be governed by me. She advised against being dazzled by society and against being seduced by what most attracts me—her moral lectures are brief and repetitive.I have not yet seen the streets; I must first be made presentable. The Italian opera opens soon, and my mother has a box. I read too much indiscriminately; modern literature often dwells on love—the theme that once obsessed us at the convent. Corinne and Adolphe delighted me. When I asked if I might meet Mme. de Staël, my parents laughed and informed me she was dead.December.My Darling—tomorrow I make my debut. I have rehearsed all my follies, tried on stays, shoes, curls, dress, ornaments—performed a private inspection. Griffith alone witnessed my little play. In short, I have left the convent and been reborn as a frivolous society girl. I may be raw—shoulder lines not yet rounded—but there is strength, health, and a vivid complexion. My features are correct, my hair pale gold with a darker center, my eyes bright and insolent, the mouth expressive. I have grace of gait and voice; my grandmother's manner and voice are in me. If I wished, I can be innocent, witty, or melancholy enough to command admiration.My father has provided me with a trousseau—ribbons, shoes, gloves, dressing-case, scent-box, fan, sunshade, prayer-book, cashmere shawl—and promised riding lessons. I can dance; tomorrow evening I am to appear in white muslin, with a Greek garland of white roses, and I intend to play the simpleton to win the other women. My mother underestimates me and sees me as a simpleton; my brother treats me with polite contempt. I suspect Alphonse harbors secret discontent and jealousy toward our father, which none understand.We are unlike my parents: my father absorbed in public life and pleasures, my mother devoted to her toilette and society. They both know how to wear their parts; the show of unity in the family conceals indifference. It amazes me how Paris life can consume people from nine until the small hours, draining both energy and money. I am newly curious about this spell of society that keeps people enslaved and separate. For now I have sixteen hours a day to think, while those around me barely have minutes for reflection. There is much to learn in this world I sought; I am eager and anxious at once.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Yesterday at two I drove along the Champs-Élysées and through the Bois de Boulogne on a beautiful autumn day that reminded me of the Loire. For the first time I felt I had truly seen Paris; the Place Louis XV seemed a triumph of art. Impeccably dressed, pensive, poised, I rode without a smile returned. Only my father, a duke, acknowledged me with satisfaction; my mother blew a butterfly kiss. Griffith scanned the crowd; a young woman should know where to look.I was furious when a man—probably a carriage-maker—inspected our carriage without seeing me. I had misjudged my appeal; beauty, I found, is common in Paris. Hats were tipped to the less deserving while my mother drew admiration. Determined to understand why, I studied the men: unattractive, shabby imitations of former elegance, their hats like truncated columns, faces worn by thwarted ambition. “And these are the products of Paris!” I exclaimed to Miss Griffith; she called them “most cultivated and pleasant.” I was silent—a thirty-six-year-old spinster’s heart practices tolerance.That evening at a ball I stayed near my mother as she received every compliment; I was merely the excuse for praise aimed at her. Men praised the heat and beauty as though I were freezing and blind; each acted as if seeing me for the first time. Married women outshone me in extravagant dress, surrounding themselves with admirers; a few were genuinely beautiful—my mother among them. A girl at a ball is only a dancing machine.The men disappointed as much here: exhausted, bland, uniform. One striking man failed to move me—he was a commoner, a professor and author, absorbed in himself. I judged intellectuals who keep their pens when they love as treating women like objects; love must displace ambition.I resolved not to quarrel with society and simply enjoy dancing. Overhearing trivial gossip, I felt my ignorance might be a blessing. At home, my mother interrupted my account: “My dear child, it requires as much tact to know when to be silent as when to speak.” Her words revealed lessons even a mother must conceal. Suddenly I was ashamed of my candor. Must I never again express joy? The clash between candor and society’s rules left me sorrowful; already the hedges of my life are flecked with white wool. Farewell, beloved.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################How deeply your letter moved me, especially as our paths diverge: you to Parisian brightness, I to a quiet retreat.At the Castle of Maucombe my room is as I left it, now opening on the Gemenos valley I once saw without understanding. Soon after my arrival my parents took me to dine with M. de l’Estorade, a wealthy, frugal old gentleman. He lost his only son to Bonaparte’s conscription in 1813; the youth evaded the draft, joined the Emperor’s bodyguard, and vanished after Leipzig. The Baron clung to hope and hoarded his income for him. Only later I learned the son had been slowly returning from Russia, Poland, and Germany; his fortunes turned in Berlin where the French Minister helped him home. The Baron, a small Provençal landowner with about ten thousand livres of income, remains obscure in Provence despite these adventures.The combined sum from the late Mme. de l’Estorade’s estate and the father’s savings is roughly two hundred and fifty thousand livres—excluding property—a considerable fortune here. The Baron has just bought a poorly run estate he plans to plant with ten thousand mulberry trees. Now that his son is found, his one desire is to marry him to a girl of good family. My parents approved once they learned Renée de Maucombe would be accepted without a dowry and that her prospective inheritance would be acknowledged in the marriage contract; my brother Jean signed to recognize an advance on his inheritance. Such are the arrangements of Provençal nobles under Bonaparte’s Civil Code, which otherwise forces many girls into convents rather than marriage.That dinner was the first meeting of your sweetheart and the exile. My father-in-law-to-be has no castle, only a simple country house at the entrance of a beautiful valley: unpretentious, four pebble walls and a red-tiled roof, with a typically Provençal garden and a touch of neglect. Still, the house did its best for the dinner, serving us on old silver. The exile—my dear—is emaciated and pale, marked by suffering. At thirty-seven he looks much older, hair streaked with white; he is a little deaf, like the Knight of the Sorrowful Countenance. Despite his ravaged appearance, he seems pleased to be marrying Renée.I have graciously agreed to become Mme. de l’Estorade, bringing the two hundred and fifty thousand livres, on condition that I may transform the grange, La Crampade, into a park; I have asked my father for a water grant to help this. In a month I will be Mme. de l’Estorade, having made a good impression.While you enjoy Paris’s vibrant life, I descend from our shared dreams into humble country existence. I am determined to bring joy to this young man, who knew little youth, going straight from his mother’s arms to war and Siberia. I will take pleasure in improving our little oasis—trees, a hillside park, perhaps a kiosk with glimpses of the Mediterranean, orange and lemon trees—and in tending household affairs, which M. de l’Estorade has promised to leave to me. My father-in-law and the Chevalier share my religious sentiments; provincial dangers are minimal. We will busy ourselves with silkworms and the ordinary ups and downs of Provence, and I shall be cherished by a frail man in the Gemenos valley.You, dear Louise, will provide the romance for me. Tell me of your balls, your dresses, and the men you meet; your other self will always be with you, listening and feeling the excitement. If only I could enjoy some Parisian fun while you managed La Crampade—poor M. de l’Estorade thinks he is marrying one woman; will he discover two?I must conclude—I am foolish only through you. One kiss on each cheek; my lips remain untouched, he has only taken my hand. P.S. I have just opened your third letter. I have about one thousand livres to spend; buy me pretty things we cannot find here or in Marseilles. Think of the recluse at La Crampade; neither family has anyone in Paris with taste to buy what we need. I will answer later. Goodbye, dear.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################VI. DON FELIPE HENAREZ TO DON FERNAND PARIS, September.The head of our house lives. The massacre in the Court of Lions forced us into wearing two faces, but that survival instinct—an “Arab cunning”—probably saved me. Ferdinand’s terror made his acting convincing enough to deceive Valdez; had I not intervened, the Admiral would have died. The Liberals still do not understand kingship. I know this Bourbon; his repeated promises of protection only confirm my mistrust. A true Spaniard need not repeat assurances; too many words usually hide deceit.Valdez sailed away on an English ship. Seeing the cause of Spain fail in Andalusia, I set my escape plan in motion: I sent my steward and coral fishers to meet me on a shore, and while Ferdinand sought to seize me, I was already at my barony of Macumer among brigands who defy authority. The last Hispano-Moorish family of Granada has taken refuge in an African desert; even a Saracen horse grazes on an estate inherited from Saracens. Brigands who once feared me now shield me from the King’s vendetta, proud to defend the Duc de Soria, a Henarez—the first of our line to visit them since Moorish rule.I had hoped to live off the estate’s income, but Quevedo was right: my possessions are nominal, not productive—vast prairies without houses, virgin forests without furnishings. It would take a million piastres and a resident master for fifty years to make them profitable. This must be settled.Watching my noble country reduced to a carcass for monks to exploit moved me to tears and foreshadowed Spain’s grim future. In Marseilles I learned of Riego’s fall. It struck me that my life would be prolonged as unnoticed martyrdom. Is existence worth living when one can neither die for one’s country nor live for a woman? To love and to conquer was our law. Yet my sabre is broken, the palace ruined, the spring dried to sand.Here is my last will and testament.Fernand, this is why I checked your enthusiasm and urged loyalty to the rey netto. As brother and friend I implore you; as master I command: go to the King and claim my dignities, property, office, and titles. He may hesitate, but tell him you are loved by Marie Heredia and she can marry none but a Duc de Soria. The Heredia fortune has long protected me; your proposal will seem to deprive me of a final resource, and the King will willingly hand over my spoils.Marry Marie. Her secret love for you was no secret to me; I have prepared the old Count for your ascension. Marie and I discharged our family duties; everything favors you. You are handsome and possess Marie’s heart. I am an ill-favored grandee for whom she harbors an unconfessed aversion; any hesitation about my misfortunes you will soon overcome. As Duc de Soria you will not regret my predecessor nor lose a maravedi. I will keep my mother’s diamonds for my independence; the gap they leave can be made up with Marie’s jewels. Send them with my nurse, old Urraca—the only servant I shall retain. No one makes my chocolate as she does.During our short revolution my needs were scant and covered by salary. You will find the income of the last two years with your steward. This sum is mine, but a Duc de Soria cannot marry without expenses, so we will share it. You will not refuse this wedding gift from your brigand brother; I mean it so.The barony of Macumer, not Spanish territory, remains mine. Thus I still have a country and a name should I ever re-enter the world.Thank Heaven, the house of Soria is saved!Just as I resigned myself to being merely Baron de Macumer, the French cannon announced the Duc d'Angoulême’s arrival. I must stop here.October.When I arrived I had not ten doubloons. A poor leader finds means to live within calamities he cannot avert: for the vanquished Moor, a horse and the desert; for the frustrated Christian, the cloister and a little gold. My resignation is weariness, not surrender. I am not ready to abandon thoughts of life. Ozalga gave letters of introduction, including one to a bookseller who tends to our countrymen. He found me eight pupils at three francs a lesson. I teach every other day, earning twelve francs—more than I need. When Urraca arrives I will hand my connection to another Spanish exile.I lodge in Rue Hillerin-Bertin with a poor widow who takes boarders. My room faces a small garden; it is quiet, with green trees, and I spend one piastre a day. I relish the calm: from sunrise to ten I smoke and enjoy chocolate, contemplating two Spanish plants—a broom rising from jasmine—colors that thrill any scion of the Moors. At ten I give lessons until four, return for dinner, then read and smoke until bed.I can endure this life of work, meditation, solitude, and modest society for a long time. Be happy, Fernand; my abdication brings no regrets. Five days since my will feel like five centuries. Honor, titles, wealth—as if they never existed.Now, with social pretenses stripped away, I open my heart to you. Though outwardly grave, my heart overflows with tenderness and devotion—sentiments I never gave even to my childhood bride. This is the source of my political zeal: Spain took the place of a mistress and received my homage. Now Spain is lost. Beggared of all, I look on the ruins of my former self and ponder the mystery of existence.Why was life given to this carcass, and when will it depart? Why did a chivalrous race place its virtues in this last offshoot only for the seed not to sprout? What crime in me blocks love? Was I destined to be a stranded hulk? In my soul I see deserts where my ancestors once roamed under a scorching sun. Proud remnant of a fallen race, vain force, love wasted—an old man in the prime of youth—I will await death’s last grace. Under this murky sky no spark will rekindle these ashes. Thus my last words may echo Christ’s: “My God, Thou hast forsaken me!”—a cry of agony and terror few mortals have plumbed.You can now understand the joy I find in living through you and Marie. I will watch you with the pride of a creator satisfied with his work. Love one another well; any discord between you would pain me more than it would you. Our mother foresaw that events would one day fulfill her wishes; perhaps a mother’s longing is a pact with God. She often revealed a heart that rightly belonged to both her sons, yet was given to you alone by irresistible passion. Her spirit will hover above you at the altar. What I have done pleases our womenfolk, the dead, and the King; it is God’s will. Do not resist, Fernand; obey and remain silent.P.S. Tell Urraca to call me only M. Henarez. Do not mention me to Marie. You must be the sole living soul to know the secrets of the last Christianized Moor, whose veins hold the blood of a great family that began in the desert and is now about to die out in solitary exile.Farewell.VII. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO RENÉE DE MAUCOMBEWhat! Married so soon? Engaged after a month to a stranger you barely know? He might be deaf, sickly, insufferable. They want you only to carry on the l’Estorades line; you will be buried in the provinces. Were these the promises we made? If I were you, I’d prefer to sail to the Hyères islands, risk capture by Algerine corsairs, and become a Sultana to stir a harem!You are merely leaving one convent for another. You are a coward and will submit to family life; I will guide you: come to Paris. There we shall set fashion and drive men wild. Your husband may join the Chamber in three years; I know how to work that machine. My mother calls women who manage it “women of fashion.” I will not leave you in your grange!Monday.For a fortnight I have lived the life of society: evenings at the Italiens, the Grand Opera, and balls. Society bewitches; the Opera enchants me. Yet no young man has stirred me; none evokes the passion I feel listening to García in Otello. What jealousies and cries! I speak in a tongue you have not heard, but you know my jealousy.Shakespeare is wretched in some ways: Othello wins battles and leaves Desdemona meekly neglected. I prefer long chivalric trials. A lout who fetches a glove among lions was impertinent—presumably he lost a prize. But I ramble.My father will likely represent the King in Madrid; I shall be part of the embassy. My mother wishes to remain; the Duc de Canalis might be secretary, but the King appoints secretaries. The Duchess stays; Canalis cultivates my mother’s society and limns diplomacy as a hobby. The Duc de Rhetore will remain in Paris; Alphonse is enamored of a ballet girl—how can one love legs? Two ballet girls in a family are more destructive than plague. My second brother is with his regiment. Thus I must act as the Antigone of His Majesty’s ambassador. Perhaps I will marry in Spain; perhaps my father imagines a marriage without a dowry.He teased me at lunch, probing my impressions of young people and society. I answered flippantly about looks and figures. He seemed disappointed. Still, with family, one may speak freely. I find young men self-centered; they don’t sustain attention. Girls are artificial, revealing themselves only in dance. Society’s courtesy is a thin veneer over selfishness—a likely residue of Bonapartist manners.Saturday.I omitted something: love is hidden here; I have seen no trace of the deep, abiding passion we imagined—the constancy, the interchange of happiness and sorrow, the daily renewal. Love, as we pictured it, seems rare; one might live a lifetime without meeting the being who can make one happy. The thought chills me. For days I have trembled at the thought of women’s destinies and begun to see why so many wear sad faces beneath social excitement. Marriage depends on chance. What soil yields the radiant flowers of the soul? Are we or the world at fault?I have seen hundreds of men; none has moved me. No proof of admiration or a sword drawn in my defense would touch me. Perhaps I will return to the convent; remaining in society might make me do foolish things. Music alone has filled me; it has even taken the place of our friendship. At night I walk the Champs-Élysées imagining the man who will awaken me; I meet jugglers and secret lovers instead. I am tempted to ask a happy passerby, “Tell me, what is love?” but I restrain myself and swear to die an old maid. Love is an incarnation; how many conditions must be met? Only God can solve it.My mother is happiest, adored by Canalis. Griffith tells me happiness can blacken other women’s characters; virtue buries savage instincts, but then what becomes of the heart? That is humiliating—no admirer for me yet. I am marriageable, but family honor may deter men; if so, they are cowards.The parts of Chimène in the Cid delight me. Farewell.VIII. THE SAME TO THE SAME, January.Our master is a poor refugee hiding after participating in the revolution crushed by the Duc d'Angoulême—a triumph accompanied by splendid fêtes. Though probably a Liberal and a man of the people, he interests me; I suspect he was once condemned to death. I try to make him reveal his secret, but he has the true Castilian taciturnity: proud as Gonsalvo di Cordova, yet angelic in patience and gentleness. His pride is not irritable but natural, obliging civility through impeccable manners and keeping us at respectful distance.My father praises Señor Henarez’s nobility, teasingly calling him Don Henarez. I once used that name; he looked up, usually downcast, and gave a look that abashed me; he has the most beautiful eyes. I asked if I had offended. He answered in dignified Spanish that religion should teach respect for the unhappy; a jest would be unkind if he had lost everything after Ferdinand VII’s triumph. If he is only a poor language teacher, is jesting not mean? Neither status warrants satire. I begged forgiveness; he bowed, opened my Don Quixote, and sat.The incident disturbed me more than a harvest of compliments. During the lesson I watched him closely; he never looks at me. He seems under thirty, with black hair, pearly teeth, velvet-fire eyes, and a plain, singular look. Spaniards are sometimes called unclean, but this man is meticulously groomed, hands whiter than his face. He stoops slightly and wears smallpox scars that give his ugliness a piquancy. His prominent forehead and shaggy brows lend a frowning, plaintive look, like a sickly child dependent on exceptional care. His natural dignity unnerves the Duke, who dislikes him.When Henarez returned two days after his rebuke, I said—perhaps to show gratitude—that I thought he had left Spain for political reasons. If my father goes to Madrid we might help get him a pardon. He replied that it was impossible for anyone to help him. Pressed, he said both that help was impossible and that he would not accept it—an answer that angered my father and made me drop Señor Henarez.Yet there was something admirable in his refusal: he would not accept friendship as charity. When I spoke to him in Spanish about equality of sentiment between friends, he answered that such equality was necessary and lacking here. I challenged whether he meant equality in feeling or in rank, hoping to provoke. He raised those compelling eyes, and mine fell. He seemed to ask whether my words implied love; the joy, pride, and agonized doubt in his glance moved me. I perceived that with a Spaniard, light advances could grow serious, so I retreated.The lesson ended; he bowed, and his eyes seemed to plead: “Do not trifle with a poor wretch.” This shift from grave dignity to humble vulnerability struck me deeply. It is dreadful to admit, but I cannot help believing there are hidden treasures of affection in this man.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################All is over, my dear child, and it is Mme. de l'Estorade who writes to you. Between us there is no change; it is only a girl the less.Do not be troubled; I did not give my consent recklessly. My life is now mapped out, and the certainty suits me. A great moral power has removed chance from my life. We have property to develop and a home to beautify; I have a household to manage and a husband to reconcile to life. I shall likely have children to care for and educate.Everyday life cannot be cast in a heroic mould, yet I can still explore the infinite within our familiar craft. My humble duties allow for passion; restoring faith in happiness to those who have suffered is a noble task that will relieve the monotony of existence. There is little room for suffering and much good to be done. My love for Louis de l'Estorade does not make my heart race, but there is nothing in him that offends me.My instincts for greatness remain, though concealed and directed to our family’s welfare. The time for a woman to seek admiration is short; I have chosen motherhood: my children will be my gods, this spot my Eldorado. Thank you for all you have sent; please consult the enclosed list. I intend to live with refinement, keeping from provincial life only what adds charm. Solitude never vulgarizes a woman. My father-in-law is delighted and is sending workmen from Paris to renovate the house.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################X. Mlle. de Chaulieu to Mme. de L’Estorade — JanuaryOh, Renée, you have made me miserable. Your beauty, pride, grace, and goodness will be wasted in a dull marriage you will come to despise; your children will be monsters. You seem resigned, neither hopeful nor afraid. Yet what if a glorious morning brings the man who awakens you? At least you will have a friend; you will become the valley’s guardian angel and learn to love its monotony. As you walk with husband and children I foresee letters reflecting the same; I will remain your friend, untouched by social pettiness.Monday. My Spaniard bewilders me: calm, severe dignity, solemn and silent—how odd that a language teacher fascinates me more than nobles or officers. His coldness both amuses and irks me. Yesterday something I said to M. Henarez struck him; he did not answer, and the lesson ended as if he might not return. That would suit me; I do not want a sentimental catastrophe. I’ve been reading Rousseau and find his moralizing passion detestable; Richardson’s Clarissa better captures English women. Love is for me a subjective poem; all written about it is both false and true. Since you will be authority on married love, I feel called to remain unmarried and seek a grand passion. Tell me every detail of your first days with that strange creature called a husband; I will do the same if I ever find love. Farewell, poor martyred darling.XI. Mme. de L’Estorade to Mlle. de Chaulieu — La CrampadeYour Spaniard and you make me shudder. Dismiss him. Everything you describe fits a dangerous adventurer who risks all. He cannot be your husband or lover. I will write more about my married life when my heart is less anxious by your letter.XII. Mlle. de Chaulieu to Mme. de L’Estorade — FebruaryThis morning my father visited, more thoughtful than usual. After flattering me and admitting he’d misjudged my intelligence, he launched into politics: France stands precarious—its Revolution persists in law and thought; the King is head without arms, nobles lack authority over elected men, and the family has ceased to exist—individuals and State coffers have replaced family spirit. He argued a state cannot stand without paternal authority and warned of calamity if our party fails. Half-joking, he asked me to persuade him to sacrifice my inheritance for my younger brother’s estate, then reminded me that a husband would expect a dowry.Your letter made me reflect on the abyss you warned about. I took precautions concerning Henarez, whose gaze unsettles me. I hesitated to tell my father I wished no more lessons, yet find myself drawn to him. His voice thrills me; his manners are simple and genuine. After a lesson he almost reached to kiss my hand, then stopped, terrified. I smiled, understanding the courage it takes for a plebeian to love a noblewoman; his eyes were wet and I felt a strange happiness. He has fascinated me.Friday. He is handsome and speaks admirably, revealing intellectual power and a new world. He told a story of a valet who died for a queen’s glance and I was captive. At a ball M. de Vandenesse hinted Henarez was of noble descent—Duke de Soria, his titles confiscated by Ferdinand. Suspense followed. In our next lesson I suspected him; he denied it, calling himself merely the servant of the King of Spain. His reticence provoked me; I tested him by asking him to translate a letter I wrote describing my ideal lover. He was moved, kept the letter as a remembrance, and left asking to speak with my father. From my father I learned Henarez is now the Baron de Macumer, with property in Sardinia. Two weeks have passed since I last saw him; he has become necessary to me. Sunday at the Italian Opera I felt his fiery gaze; he has a genius for love.XIII. Mme. de L’Estorade to Mlle. de Chaulieu — La Crampade, FebruaryMy dear Louise, I waited to write until I had learned many things for your happiness. The difference between a girl and a married woman is vast. I chose to marry Louis de l’Estorade rather than return to the convent. Marriage terrified me, yet I realized friendship might be the key to a happy union. I proposed we separate marriage from certain marital rights to preserve independence; he accepted. I asked to be left free, promising to attend to his feelings; he was overjoyed, and we married soon after.I am nearly happy now, though I must conceal my full contentment because he is sensitive. For three months we lived much as before; I studied our relationship and saw how my presence changed him. I could bring new life to my husband before giving him children. His happiness brightened our home; even my father became generous. I manage the household with economy—seeking pleasure without excess—and harbor ambitious plans for Louis, hoping to see him in politics. He embraced my designs, showing the depth of his affection. To conclude, here is a letter he wrote expressing his love: he sees me as a treasure, and I feel proud of our relationship.XIV. The Duc de Soria to the Baron de Macumer — MadridMy dear brother, I did not make you Duc de Soria to betray the name. I cannot tolerate your exile while we enjoy happiness. Marie and I will delay marrying until you accept the money Urraca offers. We have prayed for your happiness; I believe Heaven will send you the love you seek. Marie and I will always be grateful to you. Write through the Nuncio. Farewell; take pride in the happiness you have brought us.XV. Louise de Chaulieu to Mme. de L’Estorade — MarchAh, my friend, marriage has made a philosopher of you! Your prudent calculations trouble me. Do you think you can convert me to matrimony with cautious arithmetic? You have made a holocaust of your youth—becoming a miser before your time. If Louis loves you, he will never realize you act from necessity. Love is the first principle of all virtues; I cannot accept your justifying marriage by calculation. You act a man's part; Louis must play the woman! Your letter plunged me into thought; I see now the convent cannot replace a mother. Please, do not turn away from my cries!Monday. Last night I looked at the stars and pictured my Spaniard in a tree by my window. He blew a letter to me through the window, but I burned it unread—it felt improper to read such an offering. At the opera he seemed sad; I learned he had renounced a rich heiress for another man’s sake. I was wretched for burning his letter—what might he have confessed? To be loved in secret, to know a man is devoted—how can I resist? Returning home I reopened my window, hoping he might be there. He was, and I received another letter, which I read and cherished.XVI. The same to the same — MarchI am dressed in white, camellias in my hair. I feel an odd desire to delay my decision and make him wait. What a vision—driving my barouche, seeing him, the thrill. I complimented him on his horse; he said it was a gift from his steward. Henarez is no longer just a simple man; he grows more distinguished. Do my love affairs amuse you? I am a little off my head and must stop.XVII. Mme. de L’Estorade to Louise de Chaulieu — AprilMy angel, you have grieved me. You do not realize the force of the word indissoluble in marriage. Each marriage has laws shaped by place and character: a wife of a leader must resign to his guidance; a wife of a fool must take control. Passion can be admirable when it springs from the heart. We are answerable to God and ourselves for our happiness. A wife’s calculation is necessary to maintain happiness; a sublime hypocrisy may be required to reach the ideal of a perfect woman. I persist in my principles, convinced virtue varies with surroundings. If I wished to make Louis unhappy, I would leave him the helm entirely. I may not have married a man of distinction, but I hope to further him toward it. Five years from now we will see if I was mistaken. Farewell, my dear friend.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Well, my Renee, you are a wonderful woman, and I now agree that we can only be virtuous by cheating. Will that satisfy you? The man who loves us is our property; we may make a fool or a genius of him as we please—though the former is common. You will make yours a genius and keep the secret—two heroic acts, if you will!Ah, if there were no afterlife, how nicely you would be sold, for this is the martyrdom you are choosing. You want to make him ambitious and keep him in love! Child, surely keeping him in love alone is punishment enough.Tell me, to what extent is calculation a virtue, or is virtue calculation? We won’t quarrel; we have Bonald to refer to. We are, and intend to remain, virtuous; still, I believe you, with all your little knavery, are a better woman than I.Yes, I am shockingly deceitful. I love Felipe and conceal it with hypocrisy. I long to see him leap from his tree to my balcony—and if he did, how I would wither him with my scorn! I am frank with you.What restrains me? Where is the power that prevents me from telling Felipe how supremely happy he has made me with his pure, absolute, overflowing love?Mme. de Mirbel is painting my portrait, which I intend to give him. Love animates life; every hour, every trifle matters, blurring past, present, and future. Is it still so at the heights of happiness? What is happiness—soothing or exciting? I am restless, dragged to him despite reason and propriety. At least I can better understand your feelings.Felipe’s happiness rests in feeling himself mine; his aloofness and strict obedience irritate me, as his profound respect did when he was my Spanish master. I want to cry, “Fool, if you love me as a picture, what will it be when you know the real me?”Oh, Renee, you burn my letters, don’t you? I will burn yours. If anyone else read these thoughts, I would send Felipe to silence them; perhaps even kill the owners for security.Monday.How can one fathom the human heart? My father should introduce me to M. Bonald; I would ask him. I envy God, who can read the heart’s undercurrents. Does Felipe still worship me? That is the question.If I ever detect the slightest fall in the respect he used to show me as my instructor, I believe I would have the strength to end it. “Why these fine words, these grand resolutions?” you will say. Dear, I will tell you.My charming father, devoted as an Italian cavaliere servente, had my portrait painted by Mme. de Mirbel. I managed to get a copy for the Duke and sent the original to Felipe with these lines:“Don Felipe, your single-hearted devotion is met by a blind confidence. Time will show whether this is to treat a man as more than human.”It was a rich reward—both promise and challenge—given without fully committing. If his reply had begun with “Dear Louise!” or even “Louise,” he would have been lost!Tuesday.No, he is not lost. The constitutional minister is perfect as a lover. Here is his letter, in substance:Every moment away from you was filled with ideal pictures; my gaze rests on this wondrous ivory—this talisman—your portrait. I gave myself up to complete happiness, yet the thought of displeasing you wracks me. Queen of my life, oh that you could grant me but a fraction of the love I bear you!Doubt wrecked my soul as I swung between belief and despair. A criminal awaiting his verdict is not more racked. The smile on your lips, which alone can calm my dread, haunts me. From birth, no one— not even my mother—smiled on me. The beautiful girl meant for me rejected my heart for my brother. In politics I have been thwarted; the king has long been my enemy; even the Cortes that placed me in power felt like an insult.I have no illusions about my graceless person; to be loved had ceased to be more than a dream when I met you. Devotion alone could excuse my passion. As I look upon your portrait and hear your smile, hope pierces the gloom only to be obscured by doubt. It is impossible you should love me yet; but time, constancy, and depth of affection may win you. If my daring offends, tell me, and I will return to my former role. If you consent to try and love me, be merciful and break it gently to one who has placed his happiness in serving you.My dear, as I read these words, he seemed to rise before me, pale as the night when the camellias told their story and he knew his offering was accepted. These humble words, unlike usual lover’s flourishes, washed over me; it was the breath of happiness.The weather has been atrocious; impossible to go to the Bois without arousing suspicion. Even my mother, who often goes out regardless of rain, remains at home.Wednesday evening.I have just seen him at the Opera; he was another man. He came to our box, introduced by the Sardinian ambassador.Having read in my eyes that his audacity was well taken, he seemed awkwardly conscious and addressed the Marquise d’Espard as “mademoiselle.” A light brighter than the chandeliers flashed from his eyes. He left unsure of his next move.“The Baron de Macumer is in love!” exclaimed Mme. de Maufrigneuse.“Strange, isn’t it, for a fallen minister?” replied my mother.Outwardly I kept my composure, regarding Mmes. de Maufrigneuse and d’Espard and my mother as if they spoke a foreign language, while inwardly my soul sank in waves of intoxicating joy. There is only one word for what I felt: rapture. Such love as Felipe’s makes him worthy of mine. I am the very breath of his life; my hands guide his thoughts. I long to see him clear every obstacle and boldly ask for my hand. Then I would know whether this storm would calm at a glance.Ah, my dear, I stopped here, still trembling. As I wrote, I heard a noise and rose to see. From my window I saw him coming along the wall at the risk of his life. I made him a sign; he leaped—ten feet—and then ran along the road, unhurt. That he thought of my fear moved me so much that I am still crying; I don’t know why. Poor ungainly man! What was he coming for? What had he to say to me?I dare not write my thoughts and will go to bed joyful, imagining all we would say if together. Farewell, fair silent one. I have no time to scold you for not writing; it has been over a month since I heard from you! Does this mean you are finally happy? Have you lost the “complete independence” you were so proud of, which nearly played me false tonight?############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################If love is life’s essence, why do philosophers dismiss it in marriage? Society demands that women sacrifice for family, which breeds marital discord. Anticipating this, men devised defenses against women; without them, their theories might be overturned by love or aversion. Marriage now embodies two opposing forces the law must reconcile. Your letter challenges the foundations laid by the sage of Aveyron, under which I once found comfort. Laws made by men accept conjugal love without passion, allowing women to yield without love’s sanction, reflecting Nature’s emphasis on procreation.Once a person, I now feel like property. I have swallowed many tears alone, aching for a comforting smile. Why are our fates so different? You thrive in lawful love where virtue aligns with pleasure, while I face an uncertain future. You are loved and adored; embrace this noble romance we long dreamed of. Your beauty, refined and spiritual, was made to enchant. Guard your heart’s secret and test Felipe’s love—and, more importantly, test your own. Novelty and the illusion of happiness easily mislead.Remain independent; do not rush into marriage without assurance. In intimate conversation, a gesture or word can reveal hidden depths. You have the courage to explore where others falter. I watch you anxiously from afar; my heart is in sync with yours. Write to me about everything; your letters ignite passion in my otherwise mundane life. I oscillate between despair and hope—perhaps expecting too much. In youth we often believe ideals and reality will align.Seated beneath a rock in my park, I concluded that love in marriage is a fortunate accident, not a universal rule. The philosopher rightly views the family as society’s unit, placing women in subservience as history shows. The solution for me lies in motherhood, which I yearn for to channel my energy and heart. Louis remains cheerful; his love is active while mine is passive. He enjoys the flowers of my affection without acknowledging the toil behind them. I play the devoted mother, sacrificing for his happiness, and that sacrifice gives me joy. I await the rewards of my efforts, hoping motherhood will fulfill my aspirations and compensate for what I surrender.---Dear wedded friend, your letter arrived just as I contemplated a bold step. A strange yearning for the unknown revealed the conflict between societal and natural laws within me. I want to speak with Felipe alone, under the lime trees in our garden. This suits the modern girl I’ve become, my father observes.There is method in my seeming madness: I wish to reward Felipe for his nights beneath my window while testing his reaction to my boldness. If he respects my folly, he may imagine me as his wife; if not, farewell. Meeting him there seems less risky than society’s prying eyes. I longed for your company to share the anticipation. I sent Felipe a tracing of the garden key with a note urging him to stop his reckless behavior, warning that if he fell it would tarnish my reputation. Yesterday I took Griffith to the garden, and after a delicious moment of tension, I saw Felipe approach like a shadow.He was pale with fear; I warned him that if he were caught, suspicion would fall on my family. He apologized and then expressed joy and reverence for me, saying my actions were sacred. I told him my heart would belong only to someone who truly knows it and must prove himself worthy. His tenderness—his low, melodic voice—reminded me of divine love. I dismissed him, aware of the implications.Once again he appeared timid and reverent. I am still fond of him, but calm and in control. Love appears in two forms: one that commands and one that obeys. Can both coexist? Will Felipe ever become my master? Is suspense more pleasurable than fulfillment? Do we exaggerate feelings by letting imagination run wild? How can a woman reconcile imagination’s boundlessness with pleasure’s limits?############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################I am displeased with you. If you did not weep over Racine’s Berenice and regard it as a profound tragedy, we have no connection: forget me, or else you will be M. le Baron de Macumer to me, or cease to exist.At Mme. d’Espard’s yesterday your self-satisfied air disgusted me; your confidence alarms me. You have lost the humility of your first letter and, instead of the absent-mindedness of a lover, you polish epigrams. That is not the behavior of a true believer, who should stand in awe of his divinity.If you do not see me as the very breath of your life, a being above others, then I am less than a woman in your eyes. You have bred a spirit of mistrust that drowns my tenderness. Reflecting on our past, I feel justified in my suspicions. Understand, Prime Minister of all the Spains, that I have contemplated the vulnerable state of our sex. My innocence has lit my path, and I have emerged unscathed.Youth taught me this: deceit and broken promises are often punished, but love answers only to its own law—the victim is both accuser and judge. The most heartless betrayals remain secret, known only to the two hearts involved; the injured cannot reveal them without wounding herself. Love gives its own justice, unknown to the world.I have resolved never to forgive a serious offense; in love, what is not serious? Yesterday you looked like a man who had already succeeded. If that certainty has stripped you of the charming uncertainty that once characterized you, I blame you. I want you neither timid nor presumptuous; I do not wish you to fear losing my affection—that would insult me—yet I also do not want you to treat your love lightly. Your heart must never be freer than mine. If you cannot grasp the torment a single stab of doubt can inflict, beware the lesson I might teach.In a single glance I entrusted my heart to you, and you understood it. The purest feelings of a young girl are yours. My reflections have enriched my mind; yet if a wounded heart seeks counsel from reason, it may ally itself with darker knowledge and daring.I swear to you, Felipe: if you love me as I believe, and if I sense the least decline in the fear, obedience, and respect you have shown, or if the pure flame that ignited my heart grows dim, expect no reproaches. I would not burden you with letters of weakness, pride, or rage. But if I became silent, my face would announce that death was near. I would not perish, however, until I had branded you with infamy and sown eternal sorrow in your heart; you would behold the girl you loved dishonored and lost, condemned to suffering.Do not make me envy the old, happy Louise. I have sought in the Inferno for the most terrible punishment, a mental torture linked with divine vengeance.Yesterday, doubt pierced me like a cold dagger. If my service is too exacting, leave; I would not retain you. I do not need proof of your cleverness—keep your wit for me. Do not present a polished façade to others for flattery. Come to me burdened with scorn or calumny; told that no woman loves you, you will discover the treasures of Louise’s heart.We are truly rich only when our wealth lies so deep the world could trample it without knowing. If you were merely handsome, I would not have loved you. One cause I will reveal: the character and individuality that give meaning to your face are a mystery to all but me. My love transforms you into the most lovable of men; thus I wish to keep your mental gifts for myself. To others they should be as meaningless as your features. Let it be mine alone to ignite your intelligence, as I bring light to your eyes.I envision you as a proud Spanish grandee, cold and haughty, admired from a distance—ruins of barbaric power—rather than a figure of Parisian affability. Your excessive cheerfulness betrayed your love. Had I not restrained you, you would have revealed to Paris’s sharpest salons that your inspiration came from Armande‑Louise‑Marie de Chaulieu.I believe too much in your greatness to think your love merely strategic; yet without childlike simplicity with me, I could only pity you. Despite this fault, you are still deeply admired by LOUISE DE CHAULIEU.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When God sees our faults, He also sees our repentance. My beloved mistress, you are right; I feared I had displeased you without knowing why. Now that you have explained, I adore you all the more. Like the God of Israel, your jealousy is a holy sentiment. Strike me, Louise; the more you chastise, the more contrite I will be, proving my devotion.If my struggle to overcome timidity was invisible to you, will Heaven reward it? It cost me much to show you my true self—what I was before love. In Madrid I passed for a good talker; I wished you to see whatever gifts I had. If that was vanity, I accept the punishment.Your last look unnerved me more than any danger at Cadiz. The thought that I had vexed you and the lack of sympathy between us were intolerable. I wish only to act by your will, think your thoughts, and share your joys and sorrows. The torment lay in a breach of the feelings I believed we shared.“I have vexed her!” I repeated, distraught. Noble, beautiful Louise, your words have illuminated my feelings. If this is chastisement, what sweetness lies in your rewards?To be your servant is happiness enough. You have given me a life I thought lost. I no longer breathe in vain; I have something to devote myself to, even to suffer for you. I remain as when I first offered myself as your bondman. If you were dishonored, my heart would cling to you and seek to heal your wounds and pray for your innocence.Did I not tell you my feelings exceed a lover’s? I will be father, mother, sister, brother—anything or nothing, as you wish. If father and brother sometimes hide behind the lover, they are there still. If you could read my heart when you appear radiant, you would know how free from selfishness my pride is when I hear praises of your beauty. Your greeting feels like God’s blessing, and the joy lingers.Can you be unaware of how you are loved? After seeing you I return to my study; its ornaments pale before your portrait. I lose myself in thought, linking my happiness to verse. Have you never heard the whispers of my prayers reaching you?Through silent contemplation of your face I have deciphered every feature and penned sonnets in Spanish about your dual beauty—perhaps unfit for your eyes, yet constant offerings. Not a moment passes without thoughts of you; my whole being is bound to you, and without you I would ache.Now, Louise, picture the torture of knowing I displeased you without knowing why. My ideal life was cut short; I feared you ceased to love me. Your letter turned despair into joy. Oh, may I hear such chiding forever!Once a child, after falling, turned to his mother and said, “Forgive me,” hiding his hurt to seek pardon. Louise, I am that child, and I remain so. Here is the key to my character, which I humbly place in your hands. Fear not; there will be no more stumbling. Keep tight the chain that binds me, so a touch may convey your lightest wish to your devoted slave, FELIPE.---My dear friend, how can you, who married a worn invalid to mother him, understand love’s shifting drama, where death lies in a glance or a light reply?I reserved one last test for Felipe, to see if his love was as blind as a Royalist’s for his King. He walked with me all night without suspicion, and the next day loved me more, with reverence and humility. He is a true Spaniard, climbing my wall to kiss my hand from my balcony, risking his life.I spoke lightly to my father—the royal ambassador-to-be—about gossip that he was marrying me to an ambassador’s nephew for wealth and title. Your daughter weeps but bows to paternal authority, some say; others think she hides a worldly soul.We planned to go to the gentleman’s box at the Opera that night; M. le Baron de Macumer would visit us there.“Macumer needs a spur,” my father said, smiling.“You mistake Clarissa Harlowe for Figaro!” I answered. “When you see me with my right hand ungloved, you will mark your displeasure at this gossip.”“My dear, you have no more a girl’s head than Jeanne d’Arc had a woman’s heart. You will be happy, love no one, and allow yourself to be loved.”I laughed too much. Conversation drifted: my mother teased that I made light of everything, and my father declared it high time I were married. Two hours later my mother, the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse, and Mme. d’Espard bloomed in the box. I sat aside, unseen, to observe.Macumer arrived and peered at me through opera-glasses. In the first interval the “king of the profligates,” Comte Henri de Marsay, entered with an epigram in his eye and greeted us, then turned to me with a teasing remark about a marriage. Macumer met him with noble scorn, which delighted me. I caught sight of my father ready to act.Felipe showed no fear or doubt. His faith is in me; society’s lies could not touch him. Not a muscle of his face stirred.The two young counts left, and I laughed at Macumer: “M. de Marsay has been treating you to an epigram on me.”“He did more,” he replied. “It was an epithalamium.”“You speak Greek to me,” I said, smiling.My father spoke with Mme. de Maufrigneuse about the scandalous gossip surrounding me; the Duchess and Mme. d’Espard exchanged glances—women scent love like perfume.Having enjoyed Felipe’s response, I signaled him to come to my window by the dangerous route. Later I found him, upright as a statue, studying the light in my room.“My dear Felipe,” I said, “you acquitted yourself well tonight; you behaved as I would have if told you were marrying.”“I thought you would hardly tell others before me.”“And what right have you to this privilege?”“The right of one who is your devoted slave.”“In truth?”“I am, and shall ever remain so.”“But suppose this marriage was inevitable; suppose I had agreed...”His eyes flashed, measuring the danger. A power stronger than passion held him back.“An Arab cannot take back his word,” he said. “I am your slave; my life is not mine to destroy.”I placed my hand on his as I said, “Felipe, my beloved, from this moment I am your wife in thought and will. Go in the morning to ask my father for my hand. He wishes to retain my fortune, but if you acknowledge receipt of it in the contract, his consent will be given. I am no longer Armande de Chaulieu. Leave me; no breath of scandal must touch Louise de Macumer.”He listened, pale and trembling, then cleared the ten feet to the ground in safety. It was agonizing, but he waved goodbye and disappeared.“I am loved then,” I thought, “as never woman was before.” I fell asleep content, my destiny fixed.The next day my father summoned me to his private room; I found the Duchess and Macumer. After civilities I said that if my father and M. Henarez agreed, I had no reason to oppose their wishes. My mother invited the Baron to dinner; we drove in the Bois de Boulogne, and I smiled ironically at M. de Marsay as he passed.Felipe has had his cards reprinted as follows:HENAREZ(Baron de Macumer, formerly Duc de Soria.)Every morning he brings me a splendid bouquet, hidden within which is a letter containing a Spanish sonnet in my honor. To be brief, I send only the essence of the first and last sonnets.FIRST SONNET (essence)I have braved bulls and battles, scaled redoubts, staked life on dice. Yet before your sweet eye my courage deserts me; I tremble, shrink, and lose my nerve like a child with ghosts.SECOND SONNET (essence)I sought sleep to dream of thee, but jealous wakefulness kept me on the balcony. Heaven itself seemed robbed of color and light by thy beauty: the moon and stars appear pale beside you; your brow wears its snowy shine, your eyes hold heaven’s blue.What a gracious way to tell a young girl she fills a man’s life! Only in these ten days have I understood Spanish gallantry.Ah, dear, what is happening at La Crampade? How often I stroll there, inspecting our crops! Do you have news of our mulberry trees and last winter’s plantations? Does everything prosper? While buds open on our shrubs, have they also blossomed in the bosom of the wife? Does Louis continue his madrigals? Do you enter each other’s thoughts? I wonder if your wedding peace is better than the torrent of my love! Has my sweet lady professor taken offense? If so, I would send Felipe post-haste to plead—or worse. Sweet love, my life here is a splendid success, and I want to know how it fares with life in Provence. We have just added a Spaniard with the complexion of a Havana cigar to our family; your congratulations still await.Seriously, Renée, I fear you may be eating your heart out in silence, afraid to cast gloom over my sunshine. Write at once, naughty child! Tell me every detail; whether your “independence” stands erect, has fallen on its knees, or sits comfortably—a serious matter. Can you suppose the incidents of your married life are without interest for me? Often at the Opera, while watching the dancers, I think, “It is half-past nine; perhaps she is in bed. Is she happy? Is she alone with her independence, or has it gone the way of other dead independences?”A thousand loves.---Saucy girl! Why should I write? What could I say? While your life is varied by social festivities and passions, mine is as monotonous as a convent.We go to bed at nine and rise with daybreak. Meals are served with maddening punctuality. Nothing ever happens. I have adjusted; such fixed laws give life stability. I have set rules of dress; it pleases Louis and the good old man. After lunch we walk; when newspapers arrive I vanish to manage household affairs or read—often writing to you. I return before dinner; after, we play cards or receive visits. Thus my days pass between a contented old man, who has done with passions, and the man who owes his happiness to me. Louis’ joy warms my heart.For women, happiness cannot consist solely in satisfying desire. In the evenings, when I relax, I imagine your life, its riches and passions, and wonder what such a stormy preface can lead to. For you, love’s illusions are possible; for me, only the facts of homely life remain. Your love seems a dream.I find it hard to understand why you insist on romanticizing it. Your ideal man must have more soul than fire, more nobility and self-command than passion. You demand sacrifices to be rewarded by them, testing Felipe to see if desire, hope, and curiosity endure. But behind your theatrics lies the altar where everlasting bonds are forged. The day after your marriage, the graceful structure raised by your strategy may fall before the reality that transforms a girl into a woman, a gallant into a husband. There is no exemption for lovers; for them as for ordinary folk like Louis and me, the great “Perhaps” of Rabelais lurks beneath wedding rejoicings.I do not blame your rashness in speaking to Felipe in the garden or spending a night with him; but you play with life, and I fear it may turn the tables. The viaticum of married life lies in resignation and self-sacrifice. Despite your tests, I see that marriage will mean to you what it has meant to me. The greater the passion, the steeper the precipice we have prepared—that is the only difference.Oh, how I wish to see the Baron de Macumer and talk with him! Your happiness lies so near my heart.---As Felipe acknowledged my fortune, my mother warmed, calling me sly-boots and a woman of the world.“But, dear mama,” I protested before the contract was signed, “you attribute cunning to what is truly the outcome of the most unselfish love! I assure you I am not the ‘woman of the world’ you think.”“Come, Armande,” she said, drawing me close, “you did not want to return to the convent or die an old maid. Like a Chaulieu, you recognized the need to build up your father’s family.”I looked at my father and confessed, “I love Felipe de Soria with all my soul. Though this love sprang up without my knowledge, I swear I never yielded until I recognized in him a character worthy of mine.”“But, my dear,” my mother interrupted, “he is as ugly as—”“As anything you like,” I retorted, “but I love his ugliness.”“If you love him, Armande,” my father said, “and can master your love, do not risk your happiness. Happiness in marriage depends largely on the first days—”“Days only?” my mother interrupted. “You had better leave us to talk.”“You are to be married in three days,” the Duchess began, “so I must give you the serious advice every mother owes her daughter. You are marrying a man you love, and there is no reason for pity. I have only known you a year, but long enough to love you. You have gratified maternal pride and shown yourself a sweet daughter. I promise you will always find a staunch friend in me.”“You smile? Alas! it often happens that a mother who lived well with her daughter as a girl comes to cross purposes with her as women.”“I want your happiness, so listen. The love you feel is that of a young girl, natural to us all. Sometimes fate’s choice is not ours. If we cannot love the man family chooses, the fault may lie in both or circumstances. Yet the man chosen may be one we can love. Barriers between husband and wife often stem from lack of perseverance on both sides. Transforming a husband into a lover is as delicate as making a lover into a husband, which you have just proved marvelously successful.“Your happiness is my object. Never forget the first three months of marriage may bring misery if you do not submit to the yoke with the same forbearance, tenderness, and intelligence you showed during courtship. You have indulged in clandestine pleasures. If the culmination of your love begins with disappointment, come and tell me. Do not hope for too much from marriage; it may bring more discomfort than joy. The happiness of your life requires patient cherishing as much as early shoots of love.“If you lose the lover, you will find in his place the father of your children. Sacrifice everything to the man whose name you bear; his honor cannot suffer without involving you. Such sacrifice is wise policy and moral principle. But I say no more.“I fancy you are jealous, and if you knew how jealous I am! But do not be foolish about it. To publish your jealousy is like playing politics with your cards on the table; those who show their hands learn nothing. Whatever happens, we must know how to suffer in silence.”She planned to talk seriously with Macumer before the wedding. I kissed her hand, moved by her words. Her advice combined principle and tenderness; it revived fears that had haunted me at Blois as marriage approached. I needed strength and affection to pass from maidenhood to womanhood. All our conversations returned, and I re-read your letters, feeling a vague sadness.This anxiety had one advantage: it helped me adopt a proper bridal expression. On the day of signing the contract everyone said I looked charming and right. The Mairie ceremony was informal, with only witnesses.I write while they prepare my dress for dinner. We shall be married at midnight at the Church of Sainte-Valere, after a gay evening. I confess my fears give me a martyr-like air that will be admired—I cannot conceive why. I am delighted to see that poor Felipe is as timid as I; society grates on him, and he feels like a bat in a glass shop.“Thank Heaven, the day won’t last forever!” he whispered innocently.The Sardinian ambassador presented me a pearl necklace linked with six diamonds—a gift from my sister-in-law, the Duchess de Soria—along with a sapphire bracelet engraved, “Though unknown, beloved.” I consulted Felipe, saying I would not wear ornaments from anyone but him.He kissed my hand, moved, and replied, “Wear them for the inscription and the sincere feeling.”Here, then, my poor Renée, are the last words of your girl friend. After the midnight Mass we set off for an estate Felipe bought in Nivernais, on the way to Provence. My name is now Louise de Macumer, but I leave Paris in a few hours as Louise de Chaulieu. However I am called, there will always be but one Louise for you.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################I have not written to you, dear, since our marriage nearly eight months ago, and not a line from you! You are inexcusable.We set off in a post-chaise for the Castle of Chantepleurs, which Macumer has bought in Nivernais on the Loire, sixty leagues from Paris. Our servants, except my maid, were there before us; we arrived the next evening after a rapid journey. I slept from Paris to beyond Montargis, my head on Felipe's shoulder. His tenderness moved me; he kissed my forehead twice and looked at me with unchanging passion.At Briare we breakfasted in the carriage, talking as in our old Blois days and admiring the Loire. By half-past seven we entered the beautiful avenue to Chantepleurs, dined at eight, and retired to a charming Gothic bedroom, made comfortable with modern luxuries. Felipe, often thought ugly, seemed beautiful to me in his kindness. He felt like an old friend, though he later confessed to storms of passion hidden beneath a calm exterior."So far, I have found nothing terrible in marriage," I said, gazing at the moonlit park."Why fear it?" he replied. "Have I ever proved false to my promise? Why should I in the future?"His words and glances had mastery and thrilled my heart. I thought of duty and the unfair constraints placed on a woman's delicate feelings. What force seeks to crush a woman's sensitivity under obligation? Love should guard its own rights and bloom freely under its warmth.Oh, my noble Renée! I understand you now. The woman who has not used marriage to legalize her heart's choice has only motherhood left to turn to. When earthly ties fail, the soul seeks the divine.A hard truth: only truly great men know how to love. They balance sensual and spiritual forces; weaker men confuse them. Great souls express instinct with grace, tempering passion with reverence. The depth of feeling follows the caliber of intellect; hence only men of genius possess a woman's delicacy. When mind, heart, and senses unite in rapture, we briefly soar to heaven.This was the philosophy of my first three months of marriage. Felipe is angelic, another self; I can think aloud with him. His greatness surpasses my understanding, and our bond deepens with possession. I foresee years of marriage will only strengthen his affection and our connection. What joy!Pleasure invigorates me; its sunshine becomes part of my being. The intervals between ecstasies are like short nights between long summer days. What happy chance has given me such a destiny? My mother's fears proved unfounded; your fears and mine dissolved.We spent seven and a half months at Chantepleurs like runaway lovers in blissful solitude. One morning, in particular happiness, I reflected on my life and thought of your prosaic marriage. I grasped the meaning of your life. Oh, my sweet, why do we speak different languages? Your marriage of convenience and my love match are worlds apart. You walk the earth, while I soar in the heavens. Love crowned me queen; you reign by duty. My heights are so lofty that a fall would shatter me.But I will not dwell on that. I hesitate to describe the vibrant joy of love's springtime as we know it.For ten days we have been in Paris, staying in a lovely house on the Rue du Bac, prepared by the architect who decorated Chantepleurs. I have been listening to Rossini's divine music, which once soothed my restless heart. They say I am more beautiful now, and I take childish pleasure in being called "Madame."Friday morning.Renée, my dear, my happiness draws me back to you. I feel I can be more to you than ever. I have studied your married life alongside my own, and you emerge as great, noble, and splendid in your goodness. I declare myself your humble admirer and friend. My marriage has been a joy; I would have perished had it been otherwise. And you live! What feeds your heart? I will never mock you again. Mockery comes from ignorance; we jest at what we do not understand. "Recruits laugh where veterans are grave," said the Comte de Chaulieu.I suspect you have not told me everything. You hide wounds; I believe you suffer. From afar I wove romantic theories: perhaps you merely experimented with marriage, and what brought me happiness brought you pain. Your sacrifice seems unrewarded, wrapped in social axioms. If self-denial is noble, what of my joy, sanctified by the church and the mayor's seal? Is it unnatural?For the honor of the law and to complete my happiness, I long to see you content. Oh, tell me you see a dawn of love for Louis, who adores you! I return to this midday blaze, fearing it may consume me.Dear Renée, do you remember how you once said, "I love you so, Louise, that if God appeared to me, I would pray for all your sorrows to be mine"? I take up your prayer, imploring Heaven to grant you a share in my happiness.I have an idea. I suspect you are nurturing plans for Louis de l'Estorade. Very well; help him get elected deputy in the upcoming election. He will be nearly forty then, and the Chamber won't meet for six months, so he will qualify for a seat. You will come to Paris—wouldn't that be enough? My father and my friends will know and admire you. If your father-in-law consents to a family, we could secure the title of Comte for Louis. That would be something! And we shall be together.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My dear Louise, your letter left me dizzy with joy and a little envy. I held it at sunset beneath a barren rock overlooking the Mediterranean—my Louise’s seat, where I hope climbers will one day grow—a place that marks my solitude yet reminds me I am not alone.Reading you stirred the child within me: warning, delight, pain, promise, fulfillment. It convinced me I will one day know laughter and love. I now understand my purpose: to bring forth life. The certainty of motherhood has turned my sacrifices into deep joy, surpassing any personal passion.For you, love reflects Felipe’s ardor; my love will return in my children. Your golden harvest may fade, but my delayed motherhood will be enduring. Love can be a beautiful gem from Nature, but motherhood is Nature’s purest joy. A smile has dried my tears; while love makes Louis happy, marriage has made me a mother, and in that I find happiness.I returned to my white grange. For five months the miracle has been unfolding, yet I remain emotionally detached. My family is excited; I feel cold and hesitant—motherhood is still abstract. Louis was surprised; the father’s role seems slight. My doctor says childbirth is governed by chance, though passion may favor luck. I suffer nausea and odd cravings—half-rotten oranges soothe me.I will stay on the grounds to avoid being seen. I wonder when true motherhood will begin and hope it won’t arrive with suffering. Farewell, my fortunate friend—your love story inspires me and I long for every detail, though rereading it filled me with dread. Pray and do good; religion matters more to me now. God bless you!############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Madame,—My wife asks you to hear first of our joy: Renée has given birth to a healthy boy. We postpone his baptism until your return to Chantepleurs and hope you will come to La Crampade as godmother to our firstborn, Armand‑Louis de l’Estorade.Renée bore much pain with patience; the thought of bringing us joy sustained her. Little Armand, without fatherly exaggeration, is a beautiful infant who resembles Renée in features and eyes—a promising sign. The physician says Renée is out of danger and proves a good nurse; our home is revived by this happiness.Renée has prepared rooms for you; our warm welcome will compensate for any lack of splendour.The birth reconciled my father to heavy sacrifices: he bought two estates, and La Crampade now yields thirty thousand francs a year. He will seek the King’s permission to entail it; your help with the title would benefit your godson.I will follow your suggestions to arrange your reunion with Renée during the Chamber. I am studying to become a specialist; nothing would encourage me more than your interest in little Armand. Come, and with your beauty, grace, and noble heart be a good fairy to our son—you will have our everlasting gratitude.Your very humble, obedient servant,LOUIS DE L’ESTORADE.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Macumer woke me with your husband's letter. Yes, we'll go to Chantepleurs at the end of April. I am delighted to travel, to see you, and to be godmother to your first child. I insist on Macumer as godfather; sharing the role would be unbearable — if you could have seen his look, you would know how dearly he cherishes me.I look forward to La Crampade; I may have a child there too. Can you picture me torn between you and an infant? If anything ever supplanted my place in your heart, I hardly know how I'd bear it — Medea's anger seems less extreme now!So: you have the fruit without the flowers, I the flowers without the fruit. Our lives contrast, yet we have the philosophy to endure. Ten months married is too soon to give up hope.We live happily, as happy people do. Society now calls the Baronne de Macumer prettier than Louise de Chaulieu—love is a wonderful cosmetic. Driving along the Champs-Élysées this January I reflected on how much has changed since last year.If I lack a mother's joys, you can share them with me and I will learn by sympathy. Still, I cannot imagine anything surpassing the rapture of love; often I think it would be better to die at thirty, preserved from later disappointment, than to carry a young heart in an aged body among cold faces. Felipe and I quarrelled about this: I wished him strong enough to let me slip away at thirty so I might pass from one dream to another; he refused and turned pale. This distinguished statesman can be surprisingly childlike. Now that we share such confidences, we often surprise each other.Felipe and I would like to send you a gift — something to bring you joy and remind you of us. Tell me what you would like. I thought of a luncheon service with baby figures, since lunch is our happiest meal. Let me know soon; we must order it.Farewell, dear nursing mother. May you enjoy all the delights of motherhood. I eagerly await your first letter describing your experiences. Some parts of your husband's letter moved me; I will tell my godson how dearly he must love you. Much love, my sweet one.It has been nearly five months since the baby was born, and I have found no moment to write. When you become a mother, you will understand. Please write more often! Share your joys; I am happier than you can imagine.I went to the parish Mass of recovery, supported by both grandfathers. Kneeling before God, overwhelmed with gratitude, the Virgin seemed to bow toward the smiling infant Christ — and I felt blessed as the priest blessed little Armand.How sweet the word "child" is! In late pregnancy I wandered the gardens half-unconscious, haunted by gloomy forebodings; the little one reminded me only by lively kicks. During labour I mustered all my strength; pain and peace coexisted — my body in agony, my soul calm. At the cry of the newborn my suffering fell away.Laying me back felt like paradise despite exhaustion. Facing the child, I terrifiedly cried, "It looks like a little monkey! Are you sure it's a child?" My mother, now my nurse, soothed me: "You've got the finest baby. Don't worry; be a mother." I slept and let nature take its course.Waking from those first days was heavenly. The moment he took my breast was pure joy — the dawn of motherhood. Nursing is a miracle: the child's cries go straight to your heart; his eyes and lips say everything. The father can do nothing to be forgiven if he disturbs the baby. This bond is profound; the joy of nurturing is unparalleled.At five months Armand is the prettiest darling: content, crying little, wrapped in our love. My feelings for Louis have become tender gratitude, perhaps deeper than passion: unselfish devotion that lights the future. The old L'Estorade and his son redouble their care; Louis watches his son sleep for hours and works with new purpose.I grow happier by the hour. A mother's love is enduring and ennobling, combining passion, duty, and joy — a woman's true sphere where self-sacrifice blooms without jealousy. Society reinforces nature's instincts: a mother cherishes the child and learns motherhood's art; without children a woman is incomplete.Hasten, my sweetest, to fulfil your mission. Your happiness will multiply with mine.23rd.I was called away by my godson's cry from the garden. Rereading this, I am surprised at how ordinary it sounds; yet every mother must feel this. I am as happy now as once miserable. This place is my land of promise—the desert is past. A thousand loves, darling. Write to me, for I can now read of your love without tears. Farewell.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Do you know it has been over three months since I last wrote? I must own I failed to answer your last letter, but you won’t mind?Macumer and I presumed your silence meant consent about the baby-wreathed luncheon service; after six months’ work it is bound for Marseilles. When Felipe asked me to inspect it before packing, I realized we hadn’t exchanged a word since your letter that showed the heart of a mother.My sweet, I blame dreadful Paris—what is your excuse? Society here is a whirlpool. Didn’t I tell you one must conform? It drives out sentiment, monopolizes time, and can consume the heart. Moliere’s Celimene is the epitome of the society woman—timeless and apt.Without my armor—my love for Felipe—I should be lost. This morning I told him he is my salvation. Evenings are full of parties; nights my heart heals in passionate love.I dine at home only with friends; I spend one afternoon a week at home—Wednesday. I have entered the social fray, competing with Mmes. d’Espard and de Maufrigneuse; my house is lively. I became fashionable for Felipe’s pleasure. My mornings are his; from four in the afternoon until two in the morning, I belong to Paris. Macumer is an admirable host—witty, dignified, courteous. No woman could help loving such a husband.My parents have gone to Madrid. With Louis XVIII gone, the Duchess obtained my father’s appointment as attaché. My brother, the Duc de Rhetore, now acknowledges me. My younger brother, the Comte de Chaulieu, owes me for spending my fortune to secure him an estate and a marriage with Mlle. de Mortsauf, an heiress. The King will confer the de Lenoncourt and de Givry titles on him to preserve their legacy. Mlle. de Mortsauf, sole heiress of the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry, is expected to inherit over a hundred thousand livres a year.
