My father insisted the de Chaulieu arms be placed on the de Lenoncourt escutcheon, making my brother Duc de Lenoncourt. The young de Mortsauf is gravely ill, so the marriage waits until next winter, after mourning.My father’s plan has justified itself, earning me compliments and softening explanations of my marriage to society. The Prince de Talleyrand, kindly toward my grandmother, is now warm to Macumer. Society that once criticized me now admires me.I now reign where I was once insignificant. Macumer is envied as the husband of “the most charming woman in Paris.” Many women vie for that title; men murmur and gaze. This adulation flatters vanity—I see why women pay dearly for such fleeting privileges. This triumph is intoxicating; I understand how women become selfish and frivolous under admiration. Society’s demands consume your spirit; you give yourself for smiles and jealousy and receive only flattery. You know the cost, yet can’t escape the vortex.Ah, how I crave a true friend! Felipe’s love and devotion are my treasure, and my heart longs for you. We are joyfully preparing to move to Chantepleurs, where we can escape the farce of Paris. After rereading your letter, I realize no fashionable woman in Paris can be a good mother.Goodbye for now, dear. We will be at Chantepleurs for only a week and hope to see you around May 10th. We will meet again after more than two years! How we have changed—we are both matrons now, you in motherhood, I in love.If I haven’t written, it isn’t because I’ve forgotten you. How is my little godson? He must be over nine months now; I long to see his first steps, though Macumer says even precocious infants don’t walk at ten months.We will have delightful gossip and see if a child truly spoils a pattern.P.S. If you reply, address it to Chantepleurs. I’m just leaving.---My child, if you become a mother you’ll find it impossible to write during the first two months of nursing. Mary, my English nurse, and I are exhausted. I resolved to do everything myself—sewing baby clothes, embroidering caps. I am a slave, day and night.Master Armand-Louis eats whenever he pleases, and I must constantly change, wash, and dress him. I love watching him sleep, singing to him, and carrying him about, leaving little time for myself. Society has been to you what motherhood is to me!I cannot express how rich my life has become, and I long for your visit to see for yourself. I fear he will soon be teething, and you may find a fussy baby. So far he hasn’t cried much because I’m always attentive. Babies cry when their needs aren’t met, and I’m vigilant. My heart has expanded while yours shrinks under society’s demands. I eagerly await your arrival, wanting your thoughts on l’Estorade just as you’re curious about Macumer.Write to me from your last stop. The gentlemen want to meet our distinguished guests. Come, Queen of Paris, to our humble home, where love will greet you!---The name on this letter shows my petition has been granted: your father-in-law is now Comte de l’Estorade. I wouldn’t leave Paris until I secured this for you; I’m writing in the Keeper of the Seals’ presence—he has just told me the patent is signed.Goodbye for now.---I am ashamed that my sudden departure will surprise you. But I must be honest: I am horribly jealous.Felipe’s eyes rested too often on you. Your little talks at the foot of your rock tortured me, and I became irritable. Your beauty reminded him of his homeland and Marie Heredia; I can be jealous of the past too. Your magnificent features stabbed me in the heart. I tried to enhance my appearance, but everything paled beside you.Felipe doted on the child, whom I began to resent. Yes, I confess—I wanted that exuberance of life for myself. I saw regret in Macumer’s eyes, and I wept for two nights. I was miserable in your home. You are too beautiful and triumphant for me to endure.You complained of your lot, but L’Estorade is presentable, charming, and likely to become a deputy. His exile has given him a calm, dignified air, useful for a politician. Macumer, too, should achieve high place.Having satisfied myself of your happiness, I went to Chantepleurs, where Felipe must pursue his aspirations. I won’t receive you there without a fine baby to match yours.I know I deserve your scorn. I am a fool, a wretch—jealousy is a poor virtue. I am not angry with you, but I was miserable; you will forgive my escape. Two more days and I might have made a scene.Despite my jealousy, I am glad to have seen you in the pride of motherhood. Even in Marseilles I feel proud of you as a splendid mother.You are born for motherhood; I am not. A good mother and a passionate wife both require intelligence and grace. I watched you and admired you. Your children will be happy, not spoiled, by your tenderness and reason.Tell Louis the truth about my departure, but find a decent excuse for your father-in-law and family. Felipe doesn’t know why I left, and he never will. If he asks, I’ll fabricate a pretext—perhaps that you were jealous of me. Forgive my little fib.Goodbye. I write in haste; the postilion waits.Kisses to my dear godson. Come to Chantepleurs in October: I’ll be alone while Macumer is in Sardinia planning estate improvements. That seems to calm him; sharing plans with me unsettles him. Goodbye!---Dear sister, we were astonished at lunch to learn you had left, especially after your letter. You were happy; there was no reason for such objections. My sentence: you return at my first summons. Your letter didn’t say your next stop, so I address this to Chantepleurs.Listen, dear sister of my heart. My focus is your happiness. Your husband commands respect—not only a grave exterior but a revealed power and a fire in his eyes. He has been Prime Minister and adores you, which makes him a profound dissembler. Extracting secrets from such a diplomatic soul requires skill.You know my role is reason while yours is imagination. I am a simple country vicountess, ambitious for family prosperity. Macumer, the Duc de Soria, has a name, and you, a duchess by right, reign in Paris, where it’s hard to maintain power. You have a considerable fortune, which may double if Macumer develops his Sardinian estates. If anyone has the right to be jealous, it’s not you. Thankfully, we both have generous hearts that place our friendship above pettiness.I know you must feel ashamed of fleeing, but your flight won’t spare you from my words. Read carefully; this concerns you more than Macumer.Firstly, you do not love him as a wife should. In two years you may tire of adoration. You will never see Felipe as a husband; he will always be a lover to you. You do not revere him as a woman should her god. I have studied love, and this is not love.You cannot escape the fate of all queens. One day you will long to be treated as a light-o’-love—to be swept off your feet by a strong man who won’t worship you. Macumer loves you too much to resist or criticize. Sooner or later you will scorn this excessive devotion. He spoils you, as I spoiled you at the convent; you are enchanting, and there’s no escaping your charms.Worse, you are candid; our happiness often relies on social hypocrisies you won’t embrace. Society won’t tolerate a wife’s power over her husband. Convention demands that a man not show himself as lover to his wife, nor may a married woman act as a mistress. You both disregard this.The unpardonable sin in society is to be happy; if happiness exists, it must remain hidden. More importantly, equality between lovers shouldn't appear in marriage, or it undermines social order. It is painful to see a man become a nonentity, and worse to see a capable man reduced to that. You will reduce Macumer to a shadow; your household will lack balance. You will regret it, but by then it may be too late. Women often lack the qualities essential to family life. Macumer senses this; his Sardinia visit suggests he hopes to regain himself.You use the power his love gives you. Your position shows in your gestures and tone. You are the wanton your mother called you! Have you ever heard me contradict Louis? I respect his public authority. If I want to advise him, I wait for private moments; I never assume the role of mentor. If I didn’t present myself as submissive publicly, he would lose confidence. The good we do for others is spoiled unless we erase ourselves so they feel no inferiority. There is sweetness in these hidden sacrifices; it is a triumph when you praise Louis.This is my counsel. Right now, you love Felipe not as a husband but as a reflection of yourself. Your father’s words ring true: beneath your passion lies a selfishness typical of a great lady. How I must love you to speak such bitter truths!If you promise not to tell the Baron, I’ll share the end of our talk. We praised you, and he soon sensed my sisterly love. I ventured, “Louise has never struggled with life; fortune has spoiled her, and she might pay for this if you weren’t there to be father and lover.”“Ah! but is it possible?” he broke off—enough for me. If you had stayed, he would have spoken more freely.Think of the day when your husband’s strength wanes, when pleasure turns to satiety, and he feels diminished before you. The stings of conscience will awaken remorse, painful for you, as you’ll feel responsible and end up despising the man you failed to respect. Remember, scorn can become hatred. You are too noble to forget Felipe’s sacrifices, but what more can he give when he has already given himself fully? Woe to those who leave no desire unsatisfied—women can be insatiable in love.Oh, Louise, change while there’s still time. If you treated Macumer as I treat l’Estorade, you could awaken the hero within him. Wouldn’t you feel pride in using your power to elevate a gifted man, as I do with one of lesser stature?Had you stayed I would have told you this in person, but I know you will read and weigh my warnings. You have everything to be happy; don’t spoil your chances. Return to Paris as soon as Macumer does. The demands of society, which I complain about, are necessary; otherwise you’ll be lost in self-absorption. A married woman shouldn't be too generous with herself; a mother should reserve sacred hours for herself—children should not take her presence for granted.Farewell, my dear jealous soul! Many women would be flattered by your pangs, but I can only mourn, for what is not mother in me is your dear friend. A thousand loves. Make whatever excuse you must for leaving; if you doubt Macumer, I am sure of Louis.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My beloved,—Italy enchants. Macumer arrived and postponed Sardinia; warmly received. Felipe may gain Sardinian embassy in Paris. Address letters to Florence. Week here; Leghorn to Florence; month in Tuscany and Naples; Rome Nov.; Venice fortnight Dec.; back to Paris via Milan, Turin Jan. Like a honeymoon. Forgive scarce letters—absorbed in sightseeing.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dear—Send Marseilles letter from Nivernais; reply at Chantepleurs. Gov may dissolve Parliament—bad for Crown; Louis not forty until 1827. Father will stand, resign. Godson walks. I’ll wean Armand in December; a year suffices. You’ll impress in Italy. Thousand loves.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Your dreadful letter has reached me, forwarded by my steward as I asked. Oh, Renée — I will spare you unnecessary complaint and tell you only what it produced.We had just returned from the ambassador’s reception, where I shone and Macumer was utterly captivated, when I read your harsh reply and sobbed. My dear Arab flung himself at my feet, calling you mad. He led me to our balcony and, speaking Italian like poetry, swore that even if your predictions were true he would not trade one moment of our love for a lifetime of convention—he would choose our love and death over a dull existence with children.While he whispered, a bat’s cry startled us and I fainted. I am better this morning, though that sound haunts me. Upon waking I knelt before Felipe and confessed: “My love, I may be naive, and Renée might be right. Perhaps I love only your love, but this is the only feeling in my heart. If anything about me disturbs you, tell me. I want to follow your wishes.” Macumer was too choked with emotion to answer.I thank you, Renée; your letter revealed the depths of Macumer’s love. Rome is the city of love, where passion should flourish alongside art and faith. In three days we leave Rome after the ambassador’s farewell; in Venice we shall meet the Duc and Duchesse de Soria. If you write, address Paris.P.S. Dear naive child, your letter shows you know love only in theory. Love is a force that yields diverse fruits beyond any theory’s grasp.My father has been elected to the Chamber, my father-in-law has died, and I am near my second confinement; these are the year’s end—I mention them first to spare you the fright of the black seal.Your letter from Rome chilled me: you and Felipe behave like children. Either he is a deceitful diplomat, or his love is like a man’s for a courtesan—lavishing affection while knowing she is unfaithful. Enough. You say I rave, so I will hold my tongue: from our differing fates I draw a harsh lesson—do not love if you wish to be loved.When Louis joined the provincial Council he received the Legion of Honor cross. Nearly three years on, my father asks the rank of Officer for his son-in-law. I would value your influence with the right person, but do not entangle yourself in my father’s affairs. The Comte de Maucombe seeks a marquisate for himself; reserve efforts for me. When Louis becomes a deputy next winter, we will be in Paris to secure him a government post so we may live on his salary. My father sits between center and right; a title will satisfy him. Our family was noble even under King René, and Charles X will likely grant a Maucombe title. My fear is my father using this to favor my younger brother—if a marquisate is dangled, he will focus on himself.January 15th.Ah, Louise, I have been through hell. I tell you because you are like a sister; still, I doubt I shall ever relive those five ghastly days. The word “convulsions” sickens me. Five days felt like five centuries. A mother who has not suffered this cannot comprehend it.The night before, the weather was close; I thought Armand affected by teething. Usually sweet, he grew peevish, crying and breaking his toys. I put him to bed beside me. Toward morning he cried “Mamma!” in a strange voice and twitched. I rose for a drink; as I handed him the cup he was motionless, repeating “Mamma!” in a voice hardly a voice. His hand was stiff. He gulped water convulsively, clung to me, eyes rolling back, limbs rigid. I screamed for Louis.“A doctor! Quick!... he is dying,” I cried.Louis vanished. Armand gasped “Mamma!” then lost consciousness. Veins on his forehead swelled, and convulsions began. For an hour before doctors arrived I held my lifeless baby—my pride and joy. His eyes! I cannot think of them without horror. My pretty Armand was a mere shell—black, shriveled.Two doctors from Marseilles hovered like birds of ill omen. One blamed brain fever, the other called them ordinary convulsions. Our country doctor offered no opinion. “Teething,” said one; “fever,” said the other. They agreed on leeches to the neck and ice to the head. To see a child like that—purple, silent—was horrible. They even cut his curls for the ice. The convulsions returned every ten minutes; the child writhed, white then violet, limbs clattering like wood. This unconscious flesh had smiled and said “Mamma!”—the thought pierced my soul.After four days and nights, the doctors recommended a dreadful ointment to create open sores. Sores on my Armand! Five days earlier he had been playing and laughing. I refused, trusting nature. Louis, who believes doctors, scolded me. But in those moments when the disease mimicked death, I felt that remedy might save him. His skin was dry and rough; the ointment would not work. I wept; the doctors were at dinner!Alone with him, I stripped off the medical appliances, pressed him to my bosom, and laid my forehead against his, begging God to spare him. For minutes I held him, longing to die with him. Then his limbs relaxed; the writhings ceased; the ghastly colors faded. I screamed; the doctors rushed in.“He is saved!” cried the oldest doctor.What music those words were! Two hours later Armand returned to life. I was crushed, saved only by the joy of his recovery. What tortures bind a mother to her child! Must the nails be driven to fasten him to my heart? Was I not mother enough? I who wept at his first words and steps—why these horrors?Now Armand plays and laughs. What causes this dreadful disease in children? I ponder it, especially as I am again with child. Is it teething? A brain disorder? A nervous susceptibility? Our country doctor blames nervous trouble from teething. I would give every tooth in my head to see Armand’s through. One white pearl peeking from swollen gums brings a cold sweat. The heroism with which my angel bore suffering proves he is his mother’s son. I could walk forever with him in my arms!Medical science offers no clear explanation for this tetanus that appears and vanishes swiftly, unpreventable and uncured. One thing is certain: it is hell for a mother to see her child in convulsions. How passionately I hold him now! To have endured this six weeks before my confinement made it worse; I feared for the coming child. Farewell, my beloved. Do not wish for a child—this is the essence of my letter!Poor dear, Macumer and I forgave you upon hearing of your terrible trouble. I felt pain reading of your double agony; there seem compensations in being childless.I write to tell you Louis has been promoted; he may now wear the ribbon of an Officer of the Legion. You are fortunate, Renée—likely to have a little girl, as you wished!My brother’s marriage to Mlle. de Mortsauf was celebrated on our return. Our gracious King, extraordinarily kind, has granted my brother the reversion of the post of first gentleman of the chamber, presently held by his father-in-law, on condition the Mortsauf scutcheon be placed alongside the Lenoncourt’s. “The office ought to go with the title,” he told the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry. My parents came from Madrid for the wedding and will return after the reception I am hosting for the newlyweds.The carnival will be lively. The Duc and Duchesse de Soria are in Paris, which unnerves me. Marie Heredia is one of Europe’s most beautiful women, and I dislike the way Felipe looks at her. Therefore I am extra sweet and affectionate. Every look and gesture says what my lips do not: “She could not love like this!” Heaven knows how lovely I am! Yesterday Mme. de Maufrigneuse said, “Dear child, who can compete with you?”I keep Felipe entertained, making his sister-in-law seem dull by comparison. I am less sorry that a little Abencerrage is not on the way, since the Duchess will likely remain in Paris for her confinement and lose her beauty. If the baby is a boy, he will be named Felipe in honor of the exile. Fate decrees I shall be godmother again.Goodbye, dear. I plan to go to Chantepleurs early this year: our Italian tour was shockingly expensive. I will leave around the end of March to economize in Nivernais. Besides, I am tired of Paris. Felipe and I long for the tranquility of the park, cool meadows, and the sparkling Loire. Chantepleurs will be delightful after Italy; splendor becomes tiresome, and a lover’s gaze is more beautiful than any opera or painting!We expect you there. Do not fear my jealousy. You are free to test Macumer’s heart and expose his doubts. I am utterly indifferent. Since that day in Rome Felipe’s love for me has deepened. He just told me (looking over my shoulder) that his sister‑in‑law, Princess Heredia—his childhood dream—has no brains. Oh, my dear, I am worse than a ballet dancer! If you knew the joy that remark brought me! I pointed out her faulty French—she says “esemple” for “exemple,” “sain” for “cinq,” “cheu” for “je.” She is beautiful but lacks charm and wit. Don Fernand, the Duc de Soria, suits her perfectly: generous but a spoiled child. I am tempted to be mischievous and make you laugh, but I will refrain. Much love, darling.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Jeanne‑Athenais is two months. Your godson says "Matoumer"—he's teething. I keep him away during recovery; doctors insist and I suffer. Don't tell Felipe—she resembles me and may outshine Armand. Father said Felipe looks thin; your letter was shorter and colder. Nurse and baby need me—write long letters.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My dear Renée,For the first time I am truly alone and in tears. I sat beneath a willow by the Chantepleurs marsh—pretty but desolate without children’s laughter. Nearly three years of marriage and no child; your house full of little ones forces me to examine my heart. I would endure any suffering to hold a cherub of my own—an Athenais of my own! I imagine her looks and sweetness, yet you tell me nothing—always discreet as ever. I am sure you share my distress.Each disappointment plunges me into melancholy, from which I compose mournful elegies. When will I embroider caps and sew lace for a tiny head? Will I never hear “Mamma” or have a little despot tug at my dress? My gravel will lie untouched by carriage wheels, my courtyard free of broken toys. I long for a life to watch unfold—another Felipe, dearer still; a son to become my second self. My park and château seem cold and empty. A woman without children feels unnatural; we exist to be mothers. Barrenness is a bleak fate. If I cannot be a mother, I will at least indulge in romantic sorrow.I told my Moor of this and he wept; he cannot bear jokes on his love. Sometimes I dream of pilgrimage to present my longings at some shrine. Next winter I shall consult a physician. I am too angry with myself to write more. Farewell.---A whole year has passed with no letter—what does it mean? I am hurt. Is Louis, who visits so often, a sufficient substitute? It is not enough to know you are well; I want your feelings. Your silence and seclusion in the country while your husband gains Parliamentary honors alarm me. His eloquence has secured favor; he will likely receive a fine post when the session ends. Are you spending your time writing him counsel?Why did you not come to Paris? I could have enjoyed your company for four months. Louis told me you would fetch him and have your third confinement in Paris—what a monstrous Gigogne of a mother! After pestering Louis, I learned his grand‑uncle, Athenais’ godfather, is very ill; perhaps you, as a careful mother, wish to secure a legacy from your husband’s last relative. Rest easy; we are all working for Louis. Martignac will likely place him in the audit department. But if you will not tell me why you hide in the country, I shall be cross.Are you afraid that your family’s political wisdom should seem to center in you? Is it the uncle’s legacy? Do you fear you would be less to your children in Paris? Or are you simply too vain to show yourself in the metropolis while expecting? Vain thing! Farewell.---My dear friend, have you forgotten my two little brown heads, my subjects and tyrants? I could not have dragged a four‑year‑old and an almost three‑year‑old to Paris while expecting again. I would not trouble you or Louis with such tumult. I detest hotel life. When I do come to Paris for the session, it will be to my own house: Louis’s uncle, pleased with his grand‑nephew’s prospects, gave me two hundred thousand francs to buy a house near you, and my mother sent thirty thousand for furnishings. Louis is charged with finding a place; I will do my best not to disgrace you.I appreciate all you have done for Louis at court; yet I do not want him placed too conspicuously. I prefer the Audit Office for steadiness rather than a post that would commit him too much. Our affairs are in good hands; once the steward understands the details, I will support Louis.As for writing long letters—I cannot. One day is like another here: the children are either well or ill, and that sets the tone. In this solitary grange, minutes become hours depending on their health. My delightful hours come only when they sleep and I am no longer needed to rock or soothe them. When they lie beside me, I think, “Nothing can go wrong now.” Yet every mother is haunted by imagined dangers: razors, fires, ponds, the demon of convulsions. We spend our days on a stage of small dramas, tender and terrible.At night I dream of their future, comforted by the watch of their guardian angel. Sometimes Armand calls in his sleep; I kiss his forehead and his sister’s feet and watch their beauty. Once an angel roused me to Athenais’ cradle—her head was too low and Armand’s feet purple with cold. How vital it is to have one’s children at night! A nurse cannot soothe them as a mother can after a nightmare. I have learned to sleep lightly, my ear tuned to every sigh or rustle by their bed.Morning brings their chatter. Nais strains to reach me while Armand climbs up like a little monkey; they transform my bed into a playground and I am at their mercy. The baby tugs my hair and Armand guards my breast like a jealous sentinel. Their antics replace sleep; their laughter repays all care.Then the dressing begins: dressing gown, apron, sleeves rolled up, and Mary’s help in a series of baths and scrubbings. Bath time is perilous—so much crying results from a misjudged temperature or a dropped brush. Amusement is necessary—paper boats and glass ducks—to get them to submit to the tub. I contrive diversions: prayers, scoldings, promises; motherhood becomes a game of cleverness matched to a child’s cunning. Our triumphs are private: Mary and I rejoice when, after a struggle, the children are at last clean and content. Only God, His angels, and perhaps you can grasp the satisfaction of such small victories.English women excel at arranging material comforts; their nurses and systems are admirable. My children will always be bare‑legged in summer and in woolen socks in winter; no swaddling or tight bandages for them. A true mother, though, is never free; the helplessness of many French infants gives nurses latitude their charge’s mothers would not allow. That is why I do not write: besides managing the estate, I am raising two children, with a third on the way.Motherhood demands silent self‑denial and attention to minute details—a mother blows on the spoon for her child’s soup, minces the chop, endures annoyances. No servant or foreign nurse can replace a mother in those daily contests where gentleness must soothe every little grief. Louise, caring for these darlings, engages my whole soul. A crying child condemns the nurse or mother unless pain is natural. With two to tend (and soon three), they occupy all my thoughts—sometimes even you, whom I adore, recede to a memory.Often my dressing is not finished until mid‑afternoon. I distrust mothers whose rooms are immaculate and who always look perfect. Yesterday, in a lovely April, I planned an outing with the children—an epic for a mother. I had laid out Armand’s little black velvet jacket and Nais in white and pink; my heart was in my mouth. Once outside, proud of my work, I admired Armand’s princely air as he helped Nais along the path. Then a carriage approached and in our scramble the children tumbled into a puddle, wrecking my preparations. We had to return and change: a crying baby and a soaked child left me with no thought for myself.At dinner I often accomplish nothing. Feeding two, securing bibs, turning up sleeves, cajoling food—these recurring cares, joys, and catastrophes make me neglect my own tidiness. To write you I have let them cut pictures from novels and arrange my silks; they are absorbed and content.Yet I have nothing to complain of. My children are robust and inventive; they amuse themselves more readily than you might think. A little freedom and a few pebbles, shells, and sand do more for them than many toys. Armand talks to flowers, flies, and chickens; he imitates and admires insects and asks “why” about everything. It is touching to see his curiosity—he even thinks of you, his godmother, as a fairy.Let me not sadden you with all my joys. A poor man once followed us begging—beggars know a mother with a child cannot resist. Armand, innocent of hunger or money, offered the beggar the trumpet I had bought him: “Here, take this!” That moment of spontaneous charity was incomparable. The poor man, moved, kept it, saying, “I too, madame, have had children.” I wept at the sweetness.I shudder at the thought of Armand going to school: I have only three and a half years before public institutions tear him away. The bright flowers of his sunny childhood will be cut by the scythe of education; his gracious candor may be lost. What will they do to the thinking being he is, to the curls I brushed and kissed so often? And you—are you still in love with Felipe? I have no fear of the Saracen. Goodbye; Nais has tumbled, and this letter threatens to become a volume.---My sweet, tender Renée, you will have learned from the papers of the terrible calamity that has crushed me. I could not write for twenty days; I did not leave his bedside. I received his last breath, closed his eyes, kept vigil with the priests, and said the prayers for the dead. The pangs I suffered felt like a just punishment; yet when I saw the calm smile on his lips, his final farewell to me, how could I think I had caused his death?He is gone and I am bereft. To you, who knew us both, what more need be said? I would give my share of Heaven to bring him back even for a second. Will you not come soon and soothe me with such impossible promises? You told me beforehand that he would suffer through me. Was it so? Yes; I had no right to all his love. Like a thief I took what belonged to him and in my frenzied grasp crushed the life out of my bliss. The madness is over, but I feel alone and crushed. Merciful God! what torture can surpass the misery in that single word: alone?When they took him away I lay on the same bed hoping to die. There was but a door between us and I thought I could force it. I was too young for death; after forty days of cruel care and attention, I was slowly brought back to life. Now I sit in the country by a window, surrounded by the flowers he had loved and made flourish, looking at the view he admired. Ah, Renée, how new surroundings wound when the heart is dead. His flowers torment me; to walk in my garden is to walk upon him. When I first ventured out I trembled and could not move.My parents are in Spain, my brothers far off, and you detained in the country. But two angels of mercy comfort me: the Duc and Duchesse de Soria hurried to their brother in his illness and have been everything I could wish. The last nights found us gathered in mute grief around this great man—rare in any age, distinguished by mind and heart. Felipe’s calm resignation was angelic. His last words to me were simple and frank about his will and his love; he blessed me on his lips and spoke of my memory more fondly than any other. His brother forgave him, knowing what love is.I owe my life to my brother‑in‑law and his wife; they wish to take me to Spain. To no one but you can I freely pour out my grief. A sense of my own faults sits heavy: my demands, jealousy, and anxious unrest may have worn him down. My love was dangerous because we were so alike in sensitivity; my careless words pierced him to the heart. You cannot imagine how submissive he was: I only had to tell him to leave me alone, and he obeyed without a murmur. His last breath blessed me, saying that a single morning with me was worth more than a lifetime with another. My tears fall as I write.This is my life now: rising at midday, going to bed early, lingering over meals, standing motionless before a plant, gazing into the trees, finding significance in trifles. I seek shade, silence, and night. I drag through each hour, taking a gloomy pride in surviving them. My park gives me company enough; everything there echoes my vanished joy.If I still live it is because Heaven tempers grief to the strength of those who bear it. Only a woman can know what it is to lose a love that was genuine—one that satisfied spirit and flesh. How rare a man so gifted that worship does not feel debasing! If such fortune befalls us once, we cannot hope to find it again. Men of true greatness, veiled by poetic grace, charm with a high spiritual power—beware of love: it may ruin you and the woman you love. That is my cry as I walk the paths of my wood.And he has left me no child! That love, rich in smiles and tenderness, left no fruit. I feel cursed. Is it that intense, single‑hearted passion bears no children? Is only a marriage of reason blessed with progeny? Such wild questions haunt me.You are the one person whose company I could bear. Come to me; none but you should help Louise in her sorrow. The day I first donned my widow’s bonnet—when I first saw myself in black—I fell back and wept until night. I weep again at the memory. Bring your children; you can nurse your baby here without making me jealous; what is gone is gone, and I will be glad to see my godson. Felipe wished for a child like little Armand. Come, then—come and help me bear this woe.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My darling—I'll be near. Louis stays in Provence seeking re-election. To comfort you: weep—tears bring reunion. He goes to Heaven; duty loosens your bond. Preparations were interrupted when Armand cried, "Godmother, let me see her," making me jealous—he may be your child.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Yes, Renée, it is true: I have sold my house, Chantepleurs, and the farms in Seine-et-Marne — but I am neither mad nor ruined.After settling everything, I have 1,200,000 francs from my late husband’s fortune. I invested 1,000,000 in the Three per Cents at fifty; it now yields 60,000 francs instead of the 30,000 the property produced. My former life was tedious — six months in the country managing leases and impatient farmers, the rest in Paris plagued by delays and legal troubles. For a twenty-seven-year-old widow it was intolerable.Now my fortune is secured with the State. Instead of paying taxes, I receive 30,000 francs every six months from the Treasury, handed to me by a pleasant clerk. If the nation failed I should still be as well off as before, and I have time to save a little capital. Besides, the Comte de l’Estorade is a peer of this semi-republic and a friend at Court, so I have no cause for anxiety.What drove me to these calculations? Love.I must tell you plainly why I acted as I did. I am to be married in a village near Paris. I love and am loved. Forgive me for keeping this from you and de l’Estorade; I wished to spare you both anxiety. You know my jealousy; I thought secrecy kinder.The man I love had 30,000 francs of debts, which I have paid. You may suspect an adventurer, but I investigated him. He has courted me nearly two years. I am twenty-seven and he twenty-three — yes, a wide gap. He is a poet who has lived on very little and spent more time dreaming than making a living. Practical people think artists unreliable; I understand that prejudice.But my love is deliberate, not a caprice. He waited patiently; his love is noble and tender. My beloved is Gaston, the illegitimate son of the beautiful Lady Brandon, who died tragically. He was raised by his brother Louis, a sailor, whose letters once encouraged him; now Louis has vanished and Gaston is often despairing.Gaston believed genius would bring fortune, yet his life has been hardship: failure in literature, mounting debts, humiliation. I met him at Mme. d’Espard’s and felt an immediate sympathy that has become passion. He has talent and pride — qualities that can intimidate a woman — but his love for me seems genuine. He knows nothing of the depth of my feeling.I am confident of our happiness, though I fear losing him or my charms as he remains young. I mean to nourish our love, to be loved for myself. If his affection should wane, I would blame myself rather than him. My nature inclines to be a lover rather than a mother; should disaster come, Renée, I beg you to care for my children as my legacy.I have resolved to marry quietly. I will retain control of my property; Gaston must not know everything. I propose to assign him stock yielding 12,000 francs a year, which I shall place where he will find it on the eve of our wedding. If he refuses it I will break the engagement.I bought a small property near Ville-d’Avray: twenty acres, a little lake, a charming garden, secluded and surrounded by woods. My Swiss-inspired chalet is simple, solid, and hidden; there is a separate service house and a discreet, loyal staff. All my cherished Paris belongings are there; the valley’s tranquility moved me to tears. I paid the architect and insisted on secrecy.In two days I shall be Mme. Gaston. Address future letters to Mme. Gaston, Poste Restante, Versailles. Farewell. Writing this I feel grief and joy together — proof that my love for Gaston is as deep as the sorrow of leaving you.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My Dear Daniel,I need two witnesses for my marriage; please come tomorrow evening with our friend Joseph Bridau. My future wife wishes to live in complete retirement, which explains our infrequent meetings this past year. After the wedding we will be apart for a long time, but I know you will understand. Friendship can endure absence; I shall miss you, yet I shall not see you in my own home. She has sacrificed the closeness of a childhood friend for me, and I must do the same.This proves our love is deep and genuine, founded on mutual understanding, and it gives me immense joy. Yet I carry a painful secret. You know how desperate my finances have been; only through your support have I managed. My wife is wealthy; I am not. I once joked about wanting a rich heiress, but now that it is real I feel a bitter humiliation I cannot shake. That shame confirms my love for her—I do not recoil from it, though it overwhelms me.Otherwise, my dreams have come true. She is the most beautiful and noble woman, with a charming personality and a keen mind. At twenty-seven she is a widow without children, lively yet sometimes melancholy. Despite her aristocratic background, she loves me despite my humble origins. Our love has grown over time; we share a deep emotional connection and avoid the physical aspect of love out of mutual respect. Once she confessed that her marriage had been her regret, revealing the depth of her feelings. You, Daniel, are a giant among men, and you love a remarkable woman, just as I love my Armande.Louise, can it be that you intend a life of wedded solitude after sacrificing your first husband? You seem to court future misery. You are resolved, and the man who won your heart must possess rare charm. But have you forgotten your earlier criticisms of young men when you claimed they had tarnished purity? You are fortunate to believe in happiness, and while I cannot blame you, I must warn you: nature and society conspire against absolute happiness, and I foresee some disaster arising from your boundless joy. I have no objections to your fiancé, but I hope you will describe him to me one day.I have not told Louis about your marriage; it would only invite his ridicule. I will simply say you wish to keep it secret. You are brave to face winter first, and I will be your discreet friend. Your journey into this emotional wilderness is like an explorer’s perilous expedition, though fortunately only a short distance from Paris. I wish you a safe journey and a swift return.What has happened to you, my dear? After two years of silence, Renée has a right to worry about Louise. Is this what love does, scattering friendships like ours? You must admit that, while I am devoted to my children, a mother’s love is expansive and does not diminish other affections. I miss your letters and long to see your face.Regarding our situation, I will be brief. Your last letter mocked our political position and keeping our posts in the Audit Department, which Louis owes to Charles X’s favor. But how could we provide for Athenais and René on an income of forty thousand livres, of which thirty thousand is entailed? We must live on our salary and save the estate’s income for their future. In twenty years we will have saved enough.Louis has been raised to the Peerage and is now a grand officer of the Legion of Honor. He has served usefully in the Chamber and was offered an embassy, which I declined for the sake of our children’s education. I will not leave Paris until René has finished his schooling. We could not remain strictly loyal to the elder branch of the dynasty without jeopardizing our children’s prospects; a mother is not called to be a Decius. In fifteen years Louis should retire on a good pension, and I hope to secure a post for Armand in the Audit Department.I am a happy mother; my children give me endless joy. Armand is a day scholar at Henry IV’s school—I wanted him in public school but could not bear to part with him, so he returns home each evening. He is the same charming boy you loved; his tutor praises his progress. Home education has drawbacks—early exposure to passions and a lack of peer social training—so a mother must shield her children from society’s distractions. Now that I am thirty, I feel my duty fulfilled. Armand, once dull, has lately excelled, winning prizes; René, inspired by him, wishes to attend school too. The three children can be noisy, but I cherish every moment. I study their characters closely: I believe Armand will become an admirable public servant, René a bold sailor. Nais is so dear to me I can hardly see her as separate from myself; I will only give her to a man who truly loves her, though I fear the risk of a poor marriage. Your silence has reminded me of the anguish marriage can bring a mother. Farewell for now, dear Louise, and please write back.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################So, after two years of silence, you wonder why I have written so little. My dear Renée, words fail to express my happiness. We bear everything with ease; we are in perfect harmony. There has been no discord in our feelings, and each day brings its joy. We do not fear monotony; rather we regret that our time together may not suffice for all the expressions of our love. We take more pleasure in each other's company than on the first day, and every hour reveals new reasons to love one another.Every evening we speak of visiting Paris, of the new boulevards and the finished Madeleine, but in the morning we linger over breakfast and take a siesta. Gaston gazes at me as if I were a painting, and we both shed tears of joy at our love. I still play the mistress, affecting to give less than I receive; I delight in the little coquetries of our life. Passion tempered by tenderness is sweet; Gaston, my master, remains a timid suitor at my command.You asked me to describe him, yet it is impossible to portray the man we love without emotion. Society alters men so that the public man can differ from the man of deep feeling. The charming suitor may lack what a woman values, while an unrefined man may possess a profound capacity for love. Our ideal is a man whose outward appearance aligns with his true nature—a grace that cannot be acquired. This ideal haunts every woman's imagination, and in Gaston I have found it.I never knew love could be so intertwined with youth, talent, and beauty. Gaston is unpretentious, moving with effortless grace. When we walk together our bodies seem to fit perfectly, a single figure gliding through the woods. Our thoughts, desires, and words harmonize. Sometimes we walk in silence, listening to the rain and admiring the sunset, feeling as if in silent prayer for our happiness. A timid kiss exchanged in nature feels like an act of worship.We return home each time more in love than before. Such passion between older married people would scandalize Paris; only in the woods can we express it freely.Gaston is of average height, well-proportioned, and agile. He has a fair complexion, dark hair, and a striking resemblance to Louis XIII. His honorable poverty has spared him the vices that ruin many young men. His teeth are magnificent, and he has a robust constitution. His blue eyes, full of tenderness for me, can flash with intensity at inspiring thoughts. He has a remarkably even temperament; I have heard of men burdened with self-discontent, but he is free from such troubles. I have learned that marrying an older man can lead to unequal unions, and I urge you not to let Athénaïs marry one.His smile is a treasure—always fresh, full of unspoken love and gratitude. Our shared experiences are part of our joy; even the smallest details of nature recall our time together. We seem like two editions of the same book, united in thought and pleasure.Despite these delights, life here is laborious. I do my own room, refusing a hired hand to intrude on my privacy. I rise early, bathe in cold water before he wakes, and emerge like a newly opened flower; he appreciates this freshness without knowing the effort. My maid handles daily grooming, and I dress several times a day for Gaston’s delight. We take great interest in our flowers, tending a garden that supplies abundance and pride, and the gardeners sell surplus produce.To keep Gaston's mind engaged, I encourage him to finish plays he began in harder times; I assist him so our winter evenings are never dull. Our servants live easily; they present us as a content traveling couple.I have learned true jealousy—the kind born of love. The thought of him ceasing to love me sends shivers through me. Alas, I am still without a child. I long for the laughter of children in our garden; it pains me to see your lovely brood, for motherhood expands a woman’s heart and I envy your happiness.As I approach thirty, I feel my beauty waning. Though still attractive, I fear aging while Gaston remains young, and often ask him to promise he will tell me if his love ever fades; he swears it will not.Goodbye, dear friend. Shall we let years pass without writing again? Happiness can be monotonous, and perhaps that is why Dante seems greater in the Paradiso than in the Inferno. I am not Dante, only your friend, and I do not wish to bore you. Write to me—your children supply endless stories—while I... No more of this. A thousand loves.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My dear Louise, I have read your letter repeatedly; its tone is childish. Pure love belongs to nature and is distorted by civilization. Society built the family as the foundation of organized life at the cost of individual desires. A married man dedicates himself to his wife, who benefits from his efforts. The dream of being adored suits the young, not a mature woman. Vanity may enjoy adoration, but true fulfillment lies in the life of wife and mother. Return to Paris; fear the loss of ordinary happiness, not hardship.Life’s simple pleasures—bread, air, silence—sustain us. Passionate love exhausts; motherhood’s affections endure and reward. I chose self‑sacrifice and would choose it again. Your life, masked by romance, is rooted in selfishness; I plead with you as I watch your happiness tested. Life seeks balance; excess brings pain or fleeting pleasure. You are ill because you cling to a passion a marriage should steady. The glory of home is calmness and shared life, which you overlook. You, more passionate than tender, would be both wife and mistress, undermining marriage’s sanctity. If you could understand a mother’s sweet self‑discipline—my proud nature softened into gentle melancholy, rewarded by motherhood’s joys—time might restore your reason. Farewell; I despair that my letter, meant to reconcile you with society, only revealed what I see as selfishness.Renee, calamity has struck—doubt is my torment. The day before yesterday I noticed Gaston gone and his mare Fedelta sweating, implying a secret trip to Paris. Suspicion seized me. When he returned he carried me to the parlor; I fled to weep. He teased about mud on Fedelta, reassured me when I asked if I bored him, yet I remained unconvinced. He suggested keeping Fedelta; his responses made me feel inadequate. Later he left with a whip he brought back from Verdier—a small confirmation of my fear.Gaston changed: he worked harder, his drawer was empty, he tore up letters. Jealousy consumed me; I followed him to the post office and confronted him about a letter from Nathan announcing a play’s acceptance. I suspected a rival and investigated. I followed him to Paris and found he visited a woman—his brother’s widow. The discovery shattered me: he had been secretly providing for her and her children. I returned tormented by jealousy and betrayal, still loving him and fearing the truth would destroy me. I even contemplated ending my life.My dear Louise, your last letter alarmed me and I confided in Louis; we involved the police discreetly. Gaston is not the villain you feared. His brother Louis died in Calcutta, leaving a widow and two children; Gaston has been supporting them out of duty and kindness. The woman you mistook for a rival is a mother in need. You should love him more and welcome the widow and children—perhaps adopt the boys. This should bring you peace.Ah, my friend, it is too late for me. My actions have sealed my fate. Tormented by love for Gaston, I cannot bear losing him. I am filled with despair and long for relief. Come, dear friend, and witness my decline.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My love,—Take the children to Provence without me; I must stay with Louise, who has only a few days left. I cannot leave her or her husband, for whom I worry.You know the letter that sent me rushing to Ville d'Avray, gathering doctors along the way. Since then I have not left my dear friend, sitting up every night for two weeks.When I arrived, Louise and Gaston seemed charming and happy, but it was a façade. She whispered, "He must not know; I am dying." Her hand burned and her cheeks were flushed. I kept the doctors in the woods until needed.Alone with me, she collapsed into my arms and confessed. She had deliberately brought herself to the last stage of consumption in a few days—by inducing heavy night sweats and lying in the dew by the lake. Gaston thought it a cold. I rushed to fetch the physicians.After consultation none of the doctors gave hope; they believe Louise will die "with the fall of the leaves." Her strong constitution had prolonged the deception, but a few days of folly had made her case desperate.I have lived in Louise as much as in my own life; the news crushed me. Yet she received it with a strange calm. She told me to stay with her to the end; she would not have sad faces around her and wished to keep the truth from Gaston. "I know I have the strength to do it," she said. "I am full of youth and spirit, and can die standing! I have no regrets. I am dying as I wished, still young and beautiful, in the perfection of my womanhood."She reflected that for Gaston she might have made life miserable: her jealousy had already wounded him, and indifference would have been her death. She felt her thirty intense years equaled much longer lives. For him and herself the end was, paradoxically, happy; for me it was an irreparable loss—"You alone have the right to mourn my death."More gravely she drew a lesson: marriage cannot rest on passion alone. Addressing me she praised my noble way of giving my husband growing affection while her own eager passion diminished. She accepted death with the dignity of one who had known two noble men—one of mind, one of form—and died adored. She admitted perhaps she had thought too little of God, and hoped her spirit would be reunited with the two who made her life a heaven.She warned that her example might tempt others to expect impossible felicities, then, with her characteristic disdain for the social fuss about women's emancipation, declared women weak and foolish to make such clamour; they should yield more to their husbands.She spoke gently, even prettily, until Gaston entered with his sister-in-law, the children, and the English nurse from Paris. Louise greeted the nephews with playful tenderness and played hostess to Mme. Gaston, insisting the chalet be hers. I wrote to the Duc and Duchesse de Chaulieu, the Duc de Rhetore, the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry, and to Madeleine. The next day Louise, exhausted, could not go out; Madeleine, her brothers, and her mother arrived. The coolness caused by Louise's second marriage melted; her family visited daily and duchesses spent evenings at the chalet. Death unites as well as separates; it silenced petty feelings.To the last, Louise remained charming, graceful, witty, tender—a Parisian queen to the end. "I want to look well even in my coffin," she said with that matchless smile, lying on the bed where she would linger for a fortnight. Her room lacked the usual sick-chamber air; medicines and nursing supplies were hidden. She asked the priest, "Is not my deathbed pretty?"Gaston bore the blow bravely but deeply wounded. I would not be surprised if he followed her soon. Yesterday by the lake he said, "I must be a father to those two children," gesturing to his sister-in-law and the boys, and asked me to be a second mother to them and to persuade my husband to accept guardianship. He wore smiles for Louise, but I saw through the façade.August 25th (her birthday). Yesterday evening Louise was delirious briefly, but even her babble was beautiful—a proof that the madness of gifted people differs from that of fools. In a faint voice she sang Italian airs from I Puritani, La Sonnambula, Moïse. We stood around her bed in silence; not one of us, not even the Duc de Rhetore, had dry eyes, as we felt her soul departing. She could no longer see us, yet the charm lingered in the melody.During the night the death agony began. It is now seven in the morning; I had just raised her from bed. A flicker of strength returned; she wished to sit by the window and asked for Gaston's hand. Then, my love, the sweetest spirit we shall ever see on this earth departed, leaving us with an empty shell.The last sacrament had been administered the evening before, without Gaston's knowledge—he had been sleeping during the ceremony. After she was set at the window, she asked me to read the De Profundis in French, facing the lovely scene she had created. She repeated the words after me and pressed her husband's hands.August 26th.My heart is broken. I have just seen her in her winding-sheet; her face is pale with purple shadows. Oh! I want my children! Bring me my children!############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################AT THE MIDDLE OF THE STORY — MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################To Madame la Comtesse Bolognini. Recalling the pleasure of your conversation in Milan, I place this work at your feet, hoping you will shelter it with your name as you once protected the tales of a beloved writer. You have a daughter, Eugenie, whose beauty and intelligence promise a happy childhood, unlike the rigid upbringing of the Eugenie in these pages. Though Frenchmen are called fickle, I am Italian in my constancy and memory; writing “Eugenie” revives the laughter and warmth of our time in Milan. Your devoted servant, De Balzac.CHAPTER I. THE TWO MARIESIn a sumptuous blue-velvet boudoir on rue Neuve-des-Mathurins, two young women—Madame du Tillet and her sister Madame Félix de Vandenesse—weep by the fire. The room belongs to Ferdinand du Tillet, a rich banker who has purchased social standing but lacks true aristocratic ease; both sisters, raised by a strict, pious mother and married without youthful freedoms, find themselves naive about life and love, repressed and unprepared for adult passions.CHAPTER II. A CONFIDENCE BETWEEN SISTERSThey confide in one another: Madame de Vandenesse laments a husband indifferent to her inner life, while Madame du Tillet describes a cold, listless domesticity. Each craves passion and excitement suppressed by society and duty, seeking solace and encouragement from the other.CHAPTER III. THE HISTORY OF A FORTUNATE WOMANFélix de Vandenesse, of noble lineage, married hoping to recreate idealized love, but class duties and restraint cool their relationship; his kindness cannot supply the romantic intensity his wife desires, awakening longings for love beyond marriage.CHAPTER IV. A CELEBRATED MANRaoul Nathan, an audacious literary figure with caustic wit, enters society and attracts Madame de Vandenesse at Madame de Montcornet’s salon. His talent and unconventional manner intrigue her; social position and decorum, however, make any open liaison fraught.CHAPTER V. FLORINEFlorine, Nathan’s long-term companion and a gifted actress, balances affection with practicality—supporting his career while depending on him for protection and status—and grows insecure as his admiration for the countess deepens.CHAPTER VI. ROMANTIC LOVENathan and Madame de Vandenesse bond over art and literature; their encounters become intimate and perilous, leaving her torn between marital loyalty and the lure of passionate fulfillment despite social consequences.CHAPTER VII. SUICIDECrushed by setbacks and despair, Nathan contemplates suicide; a chance meeting with Madame de Vandenesse revives his will, and she resolves to protect and encourage him, countering his self-destructive impulses.CHAPTER VIII. A LOVER SAVED AND LOSTAfter his crisis she becomes more involved, offering strength yet inviting turmoil; secrecy, social risk, and Florine’s jealousy complicate the fragile relationship as Nathan’s fortunes and loyalties shift.CHAPTER IX. THE HUSBAND’S TRIUMPHFélix, sensing his wife’s attachment to Nathan, moves to preserve their marriage, investigate the truth, and shield her from scandal. The characters’ entangled ambitions, loyalties, and desires lead to a dramatic clash between passion and duty.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In April 1813 a luxurious cabriolet brought an anxious, aged father and his beautiful daughter Julie to the Tuileries for a military review by Napoleon—the last before a disastrous campaign. Though her father urged them to leave, Julie insisted on staying; an officer admitted them to a prime spot. The spectacle was magnificent and, when Napoleon appeared, the crowd cheered. Julie was captivated but soon fixed on a young officer on the Emperor’s staff, Colonel Victor d’Aiglemont. A sudden attraction—and her father’s worried caution, warning that Victor was unworthy—set the stage for their attachment despite his objections.By March 1814 Julie and Victor were traveling together, yet she had become frail as his attention shifted to military duty and adapting to political change. Isolated and craving affection, she met Arthur, an Englishman detained in France, whose quiet admiration and gentleness contrasted with Victor’s indifference. Torn between marital duty and desire, Julie’s inner conflict deepened.With Napoleon’s fall Victor embraced the new regime and grew increasingly self-interested; his infidelities revealed his selfishness. Julie, heartbroken and trapped in a loveless marriage, sought solace in Arthur despite moral and social repercussions. Declining in health and spirit, she ultimately resolved to refuse passivity and reclaim her happiness, confronting choices that would define her life. The narrative probes love, sacrifice, duty, and the search for fulfillment.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between the Seine and the Loing, beneath the Forest of Fontainebleau and west of Moret, Montereau, and Nemours, lies a flat, dreary country whose monotonous horizon mirrors its inhabitants’ melancholy. At its center stands the old château of Saint-Lange, once prosperous, now diminished.In late 1820 the Marquise d’Aiglemont, famed in Paris for beauty and wit, arrived at Saint-Lange and astonished villagers unaccustomed to gentry. Rumors of her husband’s gambling in Spain and an impending sale fed local opportunism. The Marquise retreated into isolation, rarely leaving her room and dining only briefly with her daughter. At twenty-six she bore a deep, unacknowledged grief: a former lover had died protecting her honor, and social conventions forbade open mourning. Her respectable marriage offered no consolation; youth and beauty meant little without the true love she had lost.The village priest, M. le Curé, visiting, spoke of the universality of grief and the sustaining power of faith. Initially dismissive, she found unexpected solace in his tale of a father who outlived all his sons and eventually confided in him. She admitted that duty, not affection, governed her relationship with her daughter Hélène, who served as a living reminder of the lost man. Urged to draw strength from duty, she nonetheless struggled with bitterness at the social constraints that stifled her.Spring’s labors revived her somewhat; by October she left Saint-Lange with regained vitality but lasting scars, avoiding the curé’s notice as she sought to move beyond the château’s painful associations. Her quiet struggle exposes the costs of rigid social structures and the burdens placed on women.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At Madame Firmiani’s ball, M. Charles de Vandenesse, a promising thirty-year-old diplomat about to leave Paris for the Congress of Laybach, came to take his leave. Philosophical and ambitious, he found Parisian society superficial. Madame Firmiani introduced him to the enigmatic Marquise d’Aiglemont, also thirty, known for past affairs and marked by delicate beauty and melancholy that contrasted with her dull, opportunistic husband. Vandenesse perceived deep sadness and unfulfilled dreams beneath her calm.Their conversation shifted from banter to feeling, prompting him to seek further visits; she soon occupied his thoughts. Their meetings blended friendship and unspoken desire, forcing him to confront loving a married woman. The Marquise was drawn to him but divided by loyalty to a deceased lover’s memory and social constraints. As their bond deepened, past wounds, society’s expectations, and his ambitions produced joy, anguish, a fateful kiss, and the husband’s intruding interruption—love constrained by duty and longing.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between the Barrière d’Italie and the Barrière de la Santé, the boulevard toward the Jardin des Plantes offers a stirring view: a tree-lined avenue descending into a valley where factories and the brown Bièvre mingle with greenery; Faubourg Saint-Marceau’s squalor set against the domes of the Panthéon and Val-de-Grâce; the observatory’s windows and the Invalides’ lantern punctuating the skyline. To the right, the canal Saint-Martin runs among lime trees and granaries; beyond rise the smoke-streaked slopes of Belleville. When sunlight floods this quarter, tiles gleam and the city seems at peace.One morning, while admiring this scene, I was startled by a sudden kiss from a neatly dressed young woman standing with a handsome man and a charming little boy. A second child, a dark-haired, grave girl of seven or eight, stood apart; her plain collar and scowl hinted at parental preference. As the couple played, the girl’s indifference darkened: when the boy approached with a toy she pushed him; he stumbled and vanished beneath the murky water. I rushed to help, but it was too late. The mother’s frantic grief and the terrible knowledge of her daughter’s action would forever shadow the family.Years later, the Marquis de Vandenesse, now mourning his father, hosted a dinner where a loquacious notary monopolized conversation, ignoring the household’s restlessness and the Marquise’s wish to leave for the theatre. At nine the Marquis tried to hurry him, but the notary persisted. The husband returned with the children, who looked upset; the girl’s eyes were red, the boy fretful. The notary’s thoughtless levity about family dynamics only increased the tension; the Marquise, overwhelmed, sent her daughter away and the Marquis demanded the man leave. The notary later blamed aristocratic whims; his wife, seeing the deeper truth, warned him to stick to business or risk more painful misunderstandings.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################One of Napoleon’s orderly staff-officers, called the General or the Marquis, had spent the spring at Versailles, amassed a Restoration fortune, and taken a country house near Montreuil for solitude. The house, once a nobleman’s retreat, had broad grounds and an entrance that still faced a now-quiet road.One winter evening the family was alone: the servants had gone to Versailles for a wedding, and as Christmas revelers lingered the General grew uneasy when it struck eleven and they had not returned. Outside, wind and distant footsteps broke the deep silence.Inside was a cozy domestic scene: the rugged General by the fire watching his youngest resist bedtime while playing with his sister; the Marquise looking on; their thirteen-year-old absorbed in the Arabian Nights; Helene, striking and thoughtful, at her tapestry. The children’s play contrasted with the older siblings’ more serious reflections, and the General took simple pleasure in the warmth.Suddenly urgent knocks: a breathless, disheveled man begged refuge, secrecy, and water, his hands bloodied from a duel. Despite misgivings the General hid him in a secret room, torn between unease and duty. The Marquise suspected and pressed her husband, who demanded secrecy to protect the family. Helene, curious, peered and met the wounded stranger’s magnetic gaze, feeling an unsettling attraction.As the General fretted, servants returned and gendarmes soon arrived seeking the fugitive. Tension built into a dramatic confrontation that forced the General to confront the moral cost of his choice and exposed the fragility of their happiness.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################It was a June day in 1844 when the Marquise d’Aiglemont, about fifty, strolled in her daughter’s Paris garden, pausing to gaze at the dome of the Invalides and her own grounds. Having lost her two sons, Gustave and Abel, she had given her fortune to her daughter, the Comtesse Moïna de Saint-Héreen. Her devotion to Moïna was profound, a sharp contrast to the faint memories she had of her dead children. Society dismissed the Marquise’s attachment as superstition and overlooked both her past and her coolness toward her daughter-in-law.Despite her sacrifice she was often alone; Moïna was absorbed in social life, and when the Marquise appeared in society she felt eclipsed by the younger woman’s charm. She feared Moïna’s flirtation with Alfred de Vandenesse, whom she regarded with disdain, especially with the Comte away for months. Her warnings were likely to be ignored. A series of small slights had marked Moïna’s growing distance; confrontations brought flippant replies that deepened the Marquise’s sorrow and made her feel disrespected.Overwhelmed, she collapsed on a bench and was carried inside, lifeless. Servants fetched a physician, but it was too late. Struck by remorse as her mother declined, Moïna realized she had taken that love for granted. In the Marquise’s final moments she managed a smile—a last act of enduring love and forgiveness—while Moïna stood hollow with too-late regret.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY. MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In the spring of 1822 Parisian doctors sent M. le Baron Gaston de Nueil to Lower Normandy to recover from an inflammatory illness likely caused by overwork or excess. Seeking rest, light diet, and fresh air, he settled in Bayeux with relatives.After a few evenings with his cousin, Mme. de Sainte-Severe, Gaston met the local elite, who illustrated provincial French types. One was an ancient but impoverished noble family: a patriarch, a resolute sportsman contemptuous of modernity and the new political order; a loud, domineering wife convinced their daughters’ prospects rested on their name. They clung to obsolete customs—nobility without wealth or influence.In contrast was a wealthier, more recent family attuned to Parisian trends: the wife a society woman, the husband a shrewd member of the Departmental Council who managed finances and political gossip. Their Royalism was mild; they engaged with modern life while keeping ancestral ties.The local bishop, a former Vicar-General, mediated between families—respected yet reminded of plebeian origins. Retired officers and courtly-dressed women preserved a veneer of aristocracy; a few wealthy townspeople and ecclesiastics added bourgeois wit. Conversation and judgment were antiquated and immovable; intellectual life stagnated and critics were branded misfits.Gaston, twenty-three, was welcomed after his cousin disclosed an income of eighteen thousand livres from land (his father negotiating more woodland), omitting that his elder brother was consumptive. At first he amused himself sketching their quirks, but weeks of monotony left him weary and trapped; ennui threatened to turn him into one more fossil among the fossils.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################One evening Gaston de Nueil sat between a dowager and a vicar-general in a gray-paneled drawing-room where family portraits overlooked four card tables and sixteen people playing whist. After dinner he absorbed provincial habits—the old cards, threadbare cloths—and their indifference to luxury, which made Paris seem remote.He noticed a shy young lady with red hands, but a casual remark about Mme. de Beauseant stirred him more. An elderly guest revealed that the Vicomtesse, supposedly ill and withdrawn to Courcelles, had a scandalous liaison with M. d’Ajuda-Pinto and, after separating from her husband, was shunned—an exile that intrigued him.Enthralled by the idea of the Vicomtesse as a focus of hope amid provincial stagnation, he feared a direct letter would be refused and resolved to have the Marquis, through M. de Champignelles, arrange an introduction. Champignelles’s message, at first resisted, aroused her curiosity and she agreed to receive him. Excited and anxious, Gaston prepared carefully and went to meet the enigmatic Vicomtesse.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A great fire burned on the hearth despite mild weather, casting a soft light on a young woman in a high‑backed bergère. The Vicomtesse, with graceful movements, stirred the coals, picked up a fallen glove, and motioned to a chair for M. de Nueil. Her charm and poised, fastidious carelessness set her apart from the automata he had met during two months’ exile in Normandy; in her presence he felt transported back to Paris and its sophistication.Mme. de Beauseant had fair hair, dark eyes, and a pale complexion that gave her a regal, almost fallen‑angel air; her hair, braided in a crown, heightened that bearing. Beneath her beauty lay an underlying sadness—traces of past unhappiness and lost love. Alone in her silent salon she seemed cut off from the world, haunted by memories of a once‑vibrant youth. M. de Nueil felt humbled and captivated by this blend of beauty, nobility, and misfortune.When she acknowledged him he felt the weight of his deception. He had come to deliver a message from M. de Champignelles, but consciousness of having deceived her made him flush. Summoning courage he confessed, “Madame, I do not deserve the happiness of seeing you. I have deceived you basely.” She rang for a servant to escort him out; her manner turned icy.As he left he realized that if he went away now he would never return, and the Vicomtesse would think him a fool. He hesitated, then turned back, saying he had forgotten something; the servant followed unknowingly. A half‑sad smile—“Jacques lighted me to the door”—softened her; she invited him to sit. He began to plead greater blame, and she, shifting between playfulness and gravity, complained that she could not act as she pleased, accused him of disrespect, and rejected pity—preferring scorn. She urged him to recognize the impropriety of his conduct and commanded him not to return, fearing gossip.M. de Nueil owned his fault, blaming youthful enthusiasm, exile’s weariness, and an idealized longing for love that led to this foolish intrusion, for which he sought indulgence.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################An impassioned voice warmed the chill solitude with youthful charm. Mme. de Beauseant, moved by its sincerity, found herself captivated by M. de Nueil’s noble countenance—Gaston, in the bloom of youth, seemed the ideal man, unaffected by ambition or vanity. Both were drawn to one another, reflecting on and concealing their feelings. He perceived in the Vicomtesse not only beauty but depth and goodness; she, hearing his youthful troubles, recognized the purity of someone resisting society’s corruptions.Gaston’s approach was instinctive; Mme. de Beauseant’s deliberate—she tested him with opposing opinions. Her wit and grace put him at ease, prompting his exclamation, “Oh! madame, how could any man have left you?” The remark revived her sense of innocence, condemned the world for her past, and offered an absolution that lifted her above former shame.She checked the clock and, smiling, acknowledged the fleeting nature of their encounter. Gaston wished to prolong it; she insisted on preserving her solitary life, fearing a new connection would tarnish her reputation. Candid about a forsaken love, she urged they remain strangers. Torn between admiration and fear of appearing foolish, he confessed his love and offered to heal her old wounds. Moved yet resolute, she refused. He left uncertain, then covertly returned to Courcelles and, after much hesitation, wrote a heartfelt letter laying bare his feelings, awaiting her response while doubts and longing gnawed at him.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################M. de Nueil received a letter from Mme. de Beauseant, sealed with the arms of Burgundy, in which she refused his advances. She explained she had tried to spare him a blunt rebuff: youthful passion would not move her. Though she cared for him, she did not share his love and would not yield to impulse. Nearly thirty while he was just over twenty, she argued, they could not predict how feelings would change with age; vows made lightly might become burdens she could not ask him to bear. Her consolation was in God, not earthly ties; she offered only the friendship of an older woman and urged him to remain at home rather than wreck his life for an illusion.Gaston answered immediately and fervently: if he could cease to love her and accept the ordinary life she suggested, he would deserve his fate; his fidelity could be absolved only by death, and she might take his life if she feared his remorse. When the messenger returned, Mme. de Beauseant was already preparing to leave by carriage, so Gaston followed her to Geneva, convinced that if she truly cared she would choose the freedom of Switzerland.She had rented a small lakeside house. One summer evening he surprised her there; she dropped her book and embraced him as he explained his pursuit. For three years they lived in the villa on Lake Geneva, alone and seemingly in dreamlike happiness: a simple home, beautiful views, and tranquil days treasured together.After the deaths of his father and brother, weighty obligations called Gaston back to France. They kept the villa unsold and bought the family house; Mme. de Beauseant sold her property to buy an estate near Manerville adjoining Gaston’s lands. They lived together, his mother taking the family house, and resumed the secluded happiness they had known. For nine years they enjoyed an almost indescribable bliss, yet the legal obstacle remained: Mme. de Beauseant’s husband, the Marquis, was still alive, so any lawful union was impossible. The dowager comtesse would never accept her, and the Marquise feared the dowager’s influence might one day pull Gaston away. Though tempted to return to Geneva, Mme. de Beauseant would not act in a way that betrayed distrust.The arrival of a young heiress, Mlle. de la Rodiere, and Gaston’s meeting with her disturbed this fragile peace. Mme. de Beauseant answered with a letter revealing pride and inner turmoil: she had not known love until him and had felt no jealousy for nine years, but now she feared, at forty, becoming a burden to his future and suspected his devotion might be pity. Too proud to bind his future to her fading life, she urged him to be frank—if he still loved her he should come, and she would love him more than ever; if he sought freedom she would conceal her suffering and put his happiness first. Learning that her letter had reached Gaston, she was stunned and numb.
