Reading it, M. de Nueil found himself plunged into a painful dilemma of passion, duty, pride, and the prospect of a conventional future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By this time Gaston had nearly yielded to his mother’s entreaties and the practical attraction of Mlle. de la Rodiere, a plain young woman with a forty-thousand-livre dowry. Mme. de Nueil pressed for a respectable match, arguing that Mme. de Beauseant, who had refused many distinguished suitors, should encourage a marriage that might one day bring him eighty thousand livres. Her plea left him torn.Just as his passion for Mme. de Beauseant resisted convention, a letter from her altered everything: he resolved to end their liaison and marry. “One must live a man’s life,” he told himself, though he knew the choice would wound her. Vanity and conscience deepened his sorrow; he felt pity for her and meant to prepare her gently. For a time he planned to sacrifice Mlle. de la Rodiere to soften the blow, marrying later if necessary to honor Mme. de Beauseant’s wishes. He intended a letter to allay her suspicions, though he feared a woman so perceptive might be devastated by mere words.When Jacques delivered the note, Mme. de Beauseant trembled; dread of loss closed in. If he did not come in person, she feared all was lost. As she read, an inner cry declared, “He lies!” She saw the message as calculated, not true. In despair she tore the letter and cast it into the fire, feeling betrayed and abandoned.Jacques then brought word that “Madame la Marquise has left the chateau.” M. de Nueil opened the paper and found, to his amazement, his own letter from nine years earlier in which he had sworn eternal fidelity to Mme. de Beauseant; Claire de Bourgogne had scrawled beneath it, “Monsieur, you are free.” Stunned, he consulted his mother and, within three weeks, married Mlle. Stephanie de la Rodiere.If the story stopped there it might seem trivial, but its notoriety touches those who have known profound love and loss. Mme. de Beauseant never left Valleroy after their parting; she lived in seclusion, rarely seen except by servants, and attended mass daily.Soon after the marriage M. de Nueil grew pensive. A trivial-seeming incident revealed deeper turmoil: returning from a hunt he had Jacques set aside game for the Marquise, then brought home an empty bag while Jacques delivered the spoil to her. His letters to her were returned unopened. One evening, finding her in despair and threatening to throw herself from a window if he approached, he fled terrified. He wrote again—an hour later the servant brought back the unopened note. In despair M. de Nueil retired and shot himself.The tragic end, though at odds with fashionable conventions, seems inevitable to those who have known a soul-connection. Deep love needs time and nourishment; exchanging it for a loveless marriage yields either despair or a cold, material life. A mistress cannot accept humiliation—the purity of her passion demands respect.ANGOULEME, September 1832.ADDENDUMCharacters from other Human Comedy tales: Beauseant (Marquis, Marquise, Vicomte, Vicomtesse), De Champignelles, Jacques (butler), Nueil, Gaston de.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ################################################################ MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ### repeated across decorative lines.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################La Grenadiere is a quaint house on the right bank of the Loire, about a mile downstream from the bridge of Tours. The broad river, dotted with green islands, runs between cliffs terraced for warm-climate fruit; cliffs are crowned with white stone country houses, gardens and vineyards. A church spire marks nearby Saint-Cyr and, further on, the Choisille joins the Loire. The house stands halfway up the hillside, a hundred paces from the church.Typical of Touraine and two to three hundred years old, La Grenadiere is reached by a fissure in the rock that provides steps down to the dike—the embankment that keeps the Loire in its bed and serves as the Paris–Nantes causeway. A gate opens to a narrow footpath between terraces; trees partly conceal the house until the river view widens. A crumbling Gothic archway leads into a small garden hewn from the rock, with a wooden summer-house draped in jessamine and honeysuckle. Trellised vines and pomegranate trees (the source of its name) surround the house; vine-covered steps lead to cellars carved into the rock.The façade has two large windows flanking a rustic door, three dormers in a steep slate roof, yellowwashed walls and green shutters. Inside, a crooked staircase rises to a lobby; right, a dining room with white tile floors; left, a sitting room with saffron walls and exposed walnut rafters. The first floor holds two simple whitewashed bedrooms with stone chimneypieces. A later timber-framed kitchen, with a rustic pump and well, hints the house began as a vintner’s retreat.Two acres of vineyard slope steeply at the back; a vinedresser’s cottage leans against the western gable and stone walls with espaliers maximize the land while neglected corners fill with figs, wildflowers and strawberries. Modest yet dignified, La Grenadiere yields varied grapes, figs, peaches and melons; Spanish broom and Italian oleanders lend an exotic note. The Loire flows below, evening breezes bring a sea-scent, and changing clouds reveal the south bank, Tours and châteaux—an owned retreat for lovers and dreamers where the river’s sounds invite quiet contemplation among perennial flowers and fruit.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################If an Englishman rents La Grenadiere for the summer, he pays a thousand francs for six months, excluding vineyard produce; orchard fruit doubles the rent, and the vintage doubles it again. With its stone staircase, triple terrace, two acres of vineyard, flowering roses, and well-head, it seems priceless. Unsold since its 1690 sale for forty thousand francs, it commands a view over three valleys of Touraine and cathedral towers, its health and beauty beyond valuation.In a bright spring during the Restoration, Mme. Willemsens arrived in Tours with her housekeeper and two children, aged thirteen and eight, and took La Grenadiere—perhaps for its distance from town. She converted the drawing-room into a bedroom, gave the children the two rooms above, and the housekeeper slept in a closet. The dining-room served as sitting and drawing room; furnished plainly in walnut, the house’s charm lay in neatness and harmony rather than luxury.Mme. Willemsens resisted easy classification: her simple dress invited assumptions while her polished manners suggested higher rank. Tall, slender, and melancholy, with dark eyes that sometimes revealed secret anguish and premature wrinkles, she was always in a long black gown and an English bonnet, signaling mourning. Her health seemed fragile; she walked with her children to the bridge of Tours for air and sunsets.She visited Tours only twice—to arrange the children’s lessons—and the lease was signed as Augusta Willemsens, Countess of Brandon, a hint kept within business circles. Curious townspeople found her untouchable; suitors were shy, and she burned her letters. The three tutors admired the close household bond: Louis-Gaston, dark and robust, protective of his younger, almost golden-haired Marie-Gaston. Well-mannered and devout, the boys rose early, prayed, and shared affectionate, intuitive care for their mother, Louis noting small signs of her failing strength.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Mme. Willemsens dressed for her children’s early breakfasts and play so she would be a pleasing sight for them. From ten until three she listened to their lessons, breaking for a midday lunch in the summer-house where they all ate together; afterward the children played while she rested on a sofa, enjoying the Touraine landscape.They roamed the orchard and garden—chasing lizards, studying plants and insects, and returning with eager questions. During lessons Mme. Willemsens embroidered but followed attentively, concentrating on her eldest, Louis, encouraging his ambition and asking little about Marie. She walked the tutor to the gate and candidly inquired about Louis’ progress; tutors answered honestly. Work and play were well balanced, leaving little room for quarrels. Her steady love smoothed difficulties and taught her sons moderation and submission, and she often wept for joy watching them.The garden-house hours were precious and full of laughter; the children spoke English and French thanks to their English nurse, and their mother guided their learning with books about great historical figures to inspire ambition. Little Marie often fell asleep on her knee. Afterwards Mme. Willemsens grew pensive and tearful, confessing fears of dying and leaving them without resources. Louis promised to care for Marie; she said love kept her alive and hoped Touraine’s air would help.Her talk of death made Louis more focused and the household quieter. Annette watched with concern as Mme. Willemsens grew frail but strove to preserve her beauty. By autumn she spent nearly all her time with the children while Louis, encouraged by his mother and tales from a retired naval officer at the bridge of Tours, took long walks, climbed trees, learned to swim, and worked late preparing for the École Polytechnique—his sunburned face showing a new purpose.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When October arrived, Mme. Willemsens could only rise at noon. The Loire’s warmth made the air almost like the Bay of Naples, and the faculty had recommended the area. At midday she sat in the shade with her two children, Louis and Marie, who never left her side. With lessons over they sought a life of closeness: the elder child lying beside her, basking in her affection, while restless Marie gathered flowers. Mme. Willemsens, with tired eyes and languid movements, smiled at her vibrant children—a poignant scene touched by autumn’s melancholy.Eventually the doctor ordered her to stay in her room, brightened daily by her favorite flowers, and her children remained near. One early November day she sat at the piano for the last time, fingers moving with passionate feeling. When the doctor arrived he insisted she stay in bed; she was stunned. After he left she asked Louis to take her to the terrace for one last view. Outside, she watched the clouds, then, anguished, clutched her children’s hands and lamented their uncertain future, fearing how they would judge her life when older. Seeing Louis and Marie kneel beside her, she returned to bed, never to rise again.Days blurred with Annette and Louis keeping vigil. On the fifth day the doctor banned flowers—an ominous sign. One Sunday night she told Louis tomorrow would be her last day, dictating a letter to Lord Brandon and hesitating over the signature before choosing “Louis-Gaston.” She revealed twelve thousand francs saved and urged him to keep it safe. Louis vowed to care for Marie, send her to school, and apprentice at sea to return successful.She received the sacrament the next morning and died surrounded by loved ones. Annette and the vinedresser’s wife prepared the body; only a few mourners attended. Marie cried as they lowered the coffin and a simple cross marked the grave. The children returned to La Grenadiere, then went with Annette to her cousin’s in Tours. Louis entrusted Annette with the papers and money, saw Marie to school, and left to apprentice on a warship. A month later, watching the French coast fade, he embraced life at sea determined to honor his mother, comforted by an old sailor’s words.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ################################################ MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################I have always wanted to tell a simple true story that would terrify two young lovers and drive them to seek refuge in each other’s hearts, like children clinging together at the sight of a snake. Saying so may seem vain.I played a part in this almost ordinary tragedy, so if it fails to engage you, blame me and the mundane nature of reality. In 1819 I was traveling from Paris to Moulins and, for want of money, took an outside seat on the coach. An Englishman, seemingly better off, joined me and we quickly bonded over fresh air and the landscape. Conversation turned to women and love; oddly we agreed a birth certificate meant little—some women at thirty-five to forty were younger in spirit than girls at twenty. We celebrated “the woman of a certain age.” He confessed his mistress was thirty-eight; I loved a woman of forty. Freed by mutual admiration, we traded romantic follies: one had traveled six hundred miles for a brief meeting; the other had risked danger for secret trysts. His countess had once lighted a cigar for him; mine made chocolate and wrote daily. Both were adored by their husbands, whose simplicity lent danger to our pleasure.At Pouilly I observed my new friend closely: well proportioned, dark-haired, blue-eyed, a pale, ill look, shapely hands—a Vicomte with a respectable income. About a league out of Pouilly the coach overturned. He jumped, miscalculated, and was crushed beneath the vehicle. We carried him to a peasant’s cottage where, in agony, he entrusted me with a sacred task: deliver the news of his death to his mistress so she would not learn it from a newspaper. He asked me to retrieve a key worn round his neck; I found it embedded in his flesh. He gave directions to La Charite-sur-Loire for the love letters, then died.At La Charite I took the sealed letters; an old servant fainted. The Countess’s chateau lay eight leagues beyond Moulins and I had little money, yet youthful resolve made me walk fast, eager to reach her before anyone else. Nearing the Chateau de Montpersan, open doors, barking dogs and a servant-maid unsettled me. A playful little girl then led me, calling for her mother, through winding park paths.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################I paused behind a shrub on the avenue, adjusting my shabby clothes so no one would mistake me for a local taxman. I spotted Juliette and her husband among the flowers; at her child's remark her pace quickened. Surprised, she regarded me coolly, disappointment visible. My prepared phrases failed me and her husband approached. I asked if they were M. le Comte and Mme. la Comtesse de Montpersan to size them up. He was a provincial nobleman: large, self-important, badly mannered. The Countess was the opposite: petite, graceful, in white muslin with rose accents, youthful and lively — the very Romantic ideal.I chose diplomacy, took the Count aside with an air of mystery, and requested a private word. He obliged, confident his wife would learn his secrets, and left her to wander. I told him of my traveling companion’s death and of his sincere attachment to Juliette. Concerned for his wife, the Count urged caution; I said I could not approach her without first informing him and stressed the sacredness of the message. Flattered, he allowed me to proceed.As we neared the house Juliette glanced at us with curiosity and disdain — likely at my appearance and our unexpected presence. I recalled her beauty in the sunlit alley and sighed. “Alas, madame, I have made a difficult journey solely for you,” I began; her face turned pale. She asked if he was ill; I said he was but urged her to control herself and revealed he had entrusted me with secrets about her. Pressing for details, she demanded whether he still loved her. I could only nod. Distress overwhelmed her; she cried out and leaned against a tree.“Your husband is coming!” I warned.“Do I have a husband?” she fled.The Count called us to dinner; the Countess was absent. A maid soon reported her missing. Alarmed, the canon of Saint-Denis, the maid and I searched the garden and park. At last we heard a muffled cry from a granary and found Juliette buried in hay, sobbing. The maid and I helped her to her room; I told the servants she had a headache and should not be disturbed. Returning to the dining room, I was surprised the Count still ate: a serious medical condition had left him ravenous, which explained his apparent indifference. The evening ended in quiet; we retired early.Later that night I was awakened by curtain rings. The Countess sat at my bed, pale and drawn. “Is it true?” she asked, trembling. “I don’t know how I can live after this blow, but I want to know everything.” I related the accident briefly and handed her the letters the dying man had entrusted to me. She lamented having burned all his other letters but produced a lock of his hair as a token. I returned it; she wept on my hands, kissed the lock, and fled, crying, “Ah! You love! May you always be happy.”The next morning felt unreal. I searched under my pillow for the letters but found nothing. I spent the day with Juliette, seeing her noble spirit and selflessness. That evening the Comte de Montpersan accompanied me to Moulins and asked me to carry a rouleau of twenty-five louis d’or to a Paris contact. I agreed; the money covered my return. On arrival I appreciated how delicately Juliette had arranged the loan — proof of her deep, thoughtful love. What joy to share this adventure with a woman who cherished me: “Oh, my dear, not you! You must not die!”############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################DEDICATIONTo M. le Baron Barchou de Penhoen.Of all the pupils of the Oratorian school at Vendome, we are, I think, the only two who have since met in mid-career of a life of letters. When we met, you were engaged on your works on German philosophy, and I on this study. So neither of us has missed his vocation; and you, seeing your name here, will feel as much pleasure as I do in inscribing my work to you. Your old schoolfellow, 1840 De Balzac.GOBSECKIt was one o’clock one winter night in 1829–30 in the Vicomtesse de Grandlieu’s salon; two guests remained. A young man took his leave; as his carriage died away, the Vicomtesse turned to her daughter Camille, who pretended to watch a fire-screen while listening anxiously.“Camille,” she said, “if you continue to treat young Comte de Restaud as you have this evening, I shall have to prevent him from visiting. Trust my love and let me guide you. At seventeen one cannot judge the past or the future or social standings. M. de Restaud has a mother, Mlle. Goriot, who squandered her father’s fortune and is of no birth. Though he behaves admirably toward her and his siblings, any family would hesitate to entrust their daughter’s future to him while she lives.”Derville, a family friend who had overheard, interposed: “I have won, M. le Comte. I shall assist your niece.” On being challenged, he said he had read the Vicomtesse’s looks and settled by the fire. Camille’s uncle approached and the Vicomtesse sat between them.“It’s time for a story that may change your view of Ernest de Restaud’s prospects,” Derville said.By birth and fortune the Vicomtesse led the Faubourg Saint-Germain; that an attorney could be intimate with her seemed odd until the facts were known. Reduced after exile to a royal pension and having lost the Hotel de Grandlieu during the Revolution, she owed Derville much. He had discovered defects in the title, pursued legal action, reclaimed the property and obtained judgments and indemnities that raised her income to about sixty thousand francs. Refusing preferment to keep his practice and ties, he became a trusted family confidant.His conduct won esteem and aristocratic clients; he did not exploit his popularity for office. Since Ernest de Restaud’s arrival—especially as Camille showed interest—Derville had visited more. At a recent ball he teased Camille about Restaud’s lack of millions; she defended him, saying he would have wealth when in power. “And if he were already rich?” he pressed. Her blush and abrupt departure convinced him she loved the young man. Since then Camille was unusually attentive to Derville, who approved; her feeling combined respect and gratitude, a sentiment often burdensome to the next generation.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“This adventure,” Derville begins, “recalls the one romantic episode of my life.” He explains that, though an attorney, he once fell under the spell of a man unlike any other: a usurer named Gobseck.Gobseck was a small, sallow-faced, lunar-looking old man with sleek iron-gray hair, little yellow eyes under a shabby cap, thin lips, and a gimlet nose. His voice was low, suave and unemotional. His room matched him—clean but threadbare, with a smoldering fire that never flamed. He moved with the steady economy of a life lived like hourglass sands, hoarding every sensation. Yet at day’s end his money made him gloat: he would rub his hands and, with a silent inward laugh, show a trace of satisfaction. He spoke in monosyllables and seldom showed any expression.Derville lived near him in the Rue de Grès while finishing his legal studies. The house was damp and gloomy, and no one seemed to penetrate the old man’s reticence except Derville, who, through four years of neighborly contact and sober conduct, obtained small courtesies: a light, a book, an occasional invitation to sit in the evenings. Gobseck’s capital was not visible—kept in the Bank—yet he ran about Paris collecting debts personally. He ate cheap dinners, made coffee on a chafing dish, and kept no ostentation in his lodging; he was eighty when Derville later learned more, though Gobseck himself was born about 1740 near Antwerp as Jean-Esther Van Gobseck, son of a Dutch father and Jewish mother. He had one surviving relative, a grandniece Sara, whose death he mentioned only by saying, indifferently, that women in his family “never marry.” The thought of heirs appalled him; the idea of his wealth passing to others was intolerable.Sent away at ten as a cabin boy, Gobseck spent twenty years abroad. The inscrutable lines on his face concealed adventures: panic, sudden luck, squandered chances, encounters with celebrated names and pirates, treasure hunting, and the American War of Independence. Yet he refused to tell details willingly. Sociability was foreign to him; his beliefs, religion, or attitude toward Gentiles remained unknown—he appeared simply indifferent.One evening Derville found him motionless by the mantel, a lamp casting his face pale. Gobseck invited him but said nothing for a long time. Derville pitied the old man as one pities a diseased creature, marveling that while millions were at his command he seemed to possess only the idea of the world—having ransacked and appraised it all yet feeling no claim upon it. Gobseck then surprised him by offering a philosophy of life, showing what pleasures remained for a man like him.Gobseck’s creed was brutal in its clarity: life, after long experience, reduced to one concrete reality—gold. Place, manners, conventions change; the only constant is self-interest, and gold translates into every form of human power. He scoffed at small pleasures and political or literary vanities; only strong emotion or methodical occupation could be called higher happiness—“passion and calm,” or Science and Art. But beyond these, vanity remained and was satisfied only by gold, which can realize dreams and secure time and means for their fulfillment. He professed to have no illusions, having adapted his principles to each man and latitude; he claimed to see the world with the discount taken off, measuring life by utility and profit.To prove his point, Gobseck described a morning’s work. He had only two bills to present that day; the rest he had let stand. He always deducted small amounts for hiring a brougham when he discounted a bill—an absurdity in his view that clients should make him trudge through Paris for a few francs when no man was his master. The first bill, for a thousand francs, was presented by a flashy dandy and bore the signature of a Countess, one of Paris’s prettiest women. Why would such a woman sign a bill? Fear of scandal; fear of a protested bill; stupidity, imprudence, love, or charity—anything might be behind such an act. The second bill, signed by “Fanny Malvaut,” came from a linen-draper near bankruptcy. Those who came to Gobseck had usually exhausted other credit; the first step to his door meant despair.Gobseck set out, making conjectures as he walked. The Countess lived in the Rue du Helder; the girl in the Rue Montmartre. He liked to leave muddy footprints on a rich man’s carpet “not spitefully, but as a reminder of Necessity.” At the Countess’s, a maid said the mistress had returned from a ball at three in the morning and could see no one until noon. Gobseck waited. When admitted, he found a scene of lavish disorder: an Indian shawl thrown over bare shoulders, a loose ruffled gown, an unmade bed, slippers on a bearskin, a crumpled dress and scattered trinkets—flowers, diamonds, gloves—an atmosphere of exhausted luxury and faint perfume. Beneath it all he saw Misery: the marks on a young face, dark circles under the eyes, traces of last night’s excesses. Yet Nature had not been wasted—she was vigorous, alive, a luminous, dangerous beauty that awakened desire.The Countess received him with an ingratiating tone, and Gobseck, feeling the whole power of his role, contemplated the temptation to be lenient; he recalled a past indulgence at Pondicherry where trust had been repaid. But he knew better. At the sight of him drawing the bill, she slipped a diamond into his hand and begged him to go. He took it—the jewel worth twelve hundred francs to him—and left. Outside, flunkeys brushed liveries and groomed splendid vehicles; the contrast between their pomp and the mud on the street struck him as emblematic: the rich stole millions with formalities while scrubbing off a splashed mud to preserve appearances.As he left, a young man arrived—the one who’d presented the bill. Gobseck advanced two hundred francs to him and asked him to tell the Countess he would hold her pledge for a week. The young man’s ironic smile told Gobseck all he needed: he was a heartless gambler who would ruin himself, the Countess, and perhaps more, devouring portions and bringing greater havoc to Parisian salons than any battery of guns. Gobseck read their futures as plainly as if they were open books.He then went to Fanny Malvaut’s lodging. She lived in a neat two-room apartment on the fifth floor, where sunlight softened her fresh, honest face. A sempstress by trade, she showed signs of honest toil: a crucifix and a sprig of green box on her bedstead, and a simple, almost sacred air of industry and solitude. Her chestnut hair and clear blue eyes bespoke rectitude. Gobseck had an instant, contrary tenderness for her: he wanted to ease her burden, to charge her no more than twelve percent to help her business. But he checked himself, suspecting that misplaced generosity often ruins the recipient; benevolence must be tempered by prudence. He thought Fanny would make a good wife, and compared her clean, lonely life with the Countess’s dissipated glamour—yet he restrained the impulse to save her with money.Gobseck asked Derville if he thought it nothing to have the power to peer into human hearts and live many lives by proxy. He described the endless variety of drama that appeared at his door: girls in love, merchants at the point of bankruptcy, mothers hiding sons’ crimes, starving artists, great men whose influence was fading. Their pleas made him shudder; these sublime actors played solely for him. He could read their inmost thoughts without error. Holding the purse-strings meant the power to loose and bind consciences; he could buy the consciences of those who guided ministers and sway men of consequence. He equated his power with Pleasure and Power—the essential engines of social life.He claimed there were ten men in Paris like him—silent, unknown kings—arbiters of destinies. Money set the social machine in motion; gold was the spiritual basis of society. These ten met at the Café Thémis near the Pont Neuf, keeping a Black Book that recorded important bills and instruments on public credit. They were, in effect, casuists of the Paris Bourse, an Inquisition weighing every man of fortune and forecasting with infallible precision. Each of them watched a different sphere—judicial, financial, administrative, commercial; Gobseck watched the eldest sons, artists, high society, and gamblers. From the secrets people unwittingly revealed, from thwarted passion and mortified vanity—excellent detectives—these men learned everything. Sated with experience, they loved power and money for their own sakes.In his bare, chilly room, Gobseck rehearsed how the proudest soldier, the most haughty merchant, the most vain woman come to him on their knees—famous artists, men of letters who may be remembered, all humbled before the lender’s balance. He asked whether Derville still thought there were no delights behind his blank mask. He stretched out his livid face that “reeked of money” and described the intoxicating panorama of human frailty he watched daily.Derville left Gobseck stunned. The wizened man, once merely a neighbor, had become a strange visionary—the power of gold personified. Derville felt a shrinking from life and his kind and could not sleep. The Countess’s image haunted him; ashamedly, he admits she eclipsed Fanny’s gentleness in his thoughts. Yet in the morning, as sleep’s clouds lifted, Fanny’s fresh, innocent face rose before him and remained his thought. He ends uncertain, torn between the glittering ruin of the Countess and the quiet virtue of the sempstress, and troubled by Gobseck’s insistence that, in the end, everything resolves to gold.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Will you take a glass of eau sucrée?” the Vicomtesse offered, interrupting Derville.“I should be glad of it.”“But I see nothing here that concerns us,” Mme. de Grandlieu said.Derville then told his story. After speaking with the old miser Gobseck, he finished his law studies, became a licentiate and then an advocate. Gobseck’s opinion of him improved; the old man consulted him gratis on delicate matters. Derville rose from clerk to head-clerk, moved into his employer’s house, and at last earned 150 francs a month. He went to bid the usurer goodbye; Gobseck showed no sentiment but a look implying second sight. Even after they parted, Gobseck continued to consult him.When Derville’s employer—a pleasure-loving man—fell into financial trouble and sold his practice for 150,000 francs, Derville, the seventh child of a small tradesman from Noyon, had neither money nor connections to buy it. Determined not to beg, he went boldly to Gobseck, who now occupied Derville’s old room and watched callers through a grated window. Derville proposed buying the practice: it now produced about 20,000 francs a year and might reach 40,000 under his care; with a ten-year plan he could pay 150,000 if Gobseck lent the sum.Gobseck weighed him—no guarantees, age under twenty-five—then agreed to think it over. The next morning he consented on his terms: for acquaintances he would take “twelve and a half”—corrected to thirteen percent—interest. He would pay the seller directly, secure the lien, and demanded that Derville give bills for the amount—fifteen bills of ten thousand francs each—payable to a third party unnamed. Puzzled by the need for a birth certificate, Derville heard Gobseck explain, with malicious smile, that youth’s integrity and brains are commodities one can mortgage—after thirty a man’s value becomes uncertain.Further conditions followed: Derville would give the old man legal advice without fees for life so long as it cost no outlay; permit Gobseck to visit him, not live ostentatiously, and see him for dinner on Wednesdays and Saturdays after the “Change” at five. Gobseck promised to recommend him, send him business, and teach him “to know men, and what is more—women!” The miser delighted in investing capital in Derville’s head and watching it prosper.Derville became an attorney, won the suit to recover Mme. de Grandlieu’s estates, and his name spread. Despite the usurer’s enormous terms, he repaid the loan in under five years. He married Fanny Malvaut, whom he loved; through work, shared fortune, and a timely inheritance—one of her uncles left her seventy thousand francs—they cleared the debt. Derville summarized his happiness modestly and returned to the company.About a year after buying the practice he was drawn into a bachelor breakfast lost as the forfeit of a bet. Among the guests was M. de Trailles, the consummate dandy: graceful in clothes and tandem, a master of taste, gambler and gourmand of society, spending perhaps a hundred thousand francs a year with no visible capital. Brilliant but frivolous, Trailles charmed and ruined in equal measure.Derville had tried to avoid Trailles, but Goriot insisted. He described the bachelor breakfast: a display of sumptuousness dissolving into a chaotic orgy—broken glasses, ruined linen, loud jesting, songs, quarrels, smashed bottles and a cacophony of voices—an exhausted Bacchanal. Amid that pandemonium Trailles, cool and calculating beneath an affected drunken air, engaged Derville and by evening secured his promise to introduce him to Papa Gobseck. Trailles painted an urgent picture: a young woman’s reputation and marriage at stake unless fifty thousand francs could be procured that morning.Bound by his word, Derville accompanied Trailles to Gobseck. Trailles, outwardly courtly, was inwardly agitated; on the Rue de Grès he grew nervous, glancing about with sweat on his brow. At Gobseck’s, Derville vouched for him; Gobseck said he had no money except for his own clients’ business. Trailles boasted of partners and foreign revenues, quipping that spendthrifts and usurers were interdependent. Gobseck answered coldly: other creditors—Girard, Palma, Werbrust, Gigonnet—were full of Trailles’s paper and offered it at heavy losses; he would not lend a stiver to a man who owed thirty thousand francs and had nothing.A cab arrived; Trailles left and returned shortly with a woman on his arm—one of Goriot’s daughters, the Countess. Derville watched unseen at the window; he saw the Countess enter Gobseck’s damp room, proud and distressed. She shuddered at the instant she recognized Derville; he felt pity despite her social brilliance and moral failings. Trailles had become her evil genius, ruling her by vanity and other strings.The Countess asked if she could obtain the value of her diamonds while retaining the right of repurchase, handing over a jewel-case. Derville explained the sale with faculty of redemption—a transfer allowing recovery by paying a set sum. The Countess breathed freer; the Count feared Gobseck would undervalue the stones.Gobseck, impassive, took his magnifying glass and inspected the jewels. Derville never forgot the look that came over the old man—an unnatural glitter as he rose and went to the light, holding the diamonds close to his toothless mouth, as if to devour them. He handled bracelets, sprays, necklaces and tiaras like a child or doting miser, letting the light draw out every fire. That flash of greed, the only time Gobseck’s heart seemed to open, shocked Derville and left a lasting impression.Earlier, Gobseck had mocked Trailles’s ruin and refused to be fooled. Now, with the jewels, his mood changed to triumph; he rejoiced at outwitting rivals Werbrust and Gigonnet and at the prospect of laughing at them that night. Yet after his brief ecstasy his face reverted to the pale, impassive mask of the usurer. The sight of the Countess—beautiful, suffering, and controlled by Trailles—registered in him only as the value of her jewels.Derville reflected that Trailles had become the Countess’s tormentor: an angel-faced youth whose charm masked moral ruin. In Gobseck’s room they stood as before a judge; the usurer peered at them like an inquisitor, measuring guilt and bargaining worth. The bargain Gobseck had struck with Derville—interest, anonymous bills, visits, unpaid counsel—illustrated a system where personal ties and youthful promise were commodified, and financial life depended on shrewd calculation and a cold knowledge of men’s weaknesses. Gobseck’s power came not only from capital but from this almost scientific mastery of human needs, which he used to acquire dominion and to profit emotionally as well as financially from the commodities people brought him—practices, promises, or jewels.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Fine stones! This set would have fetched three hundred thousand francs before the Revolution. What water! Genuine Asiatic diamonds from Golconda or Visapur. No one in Paris but Gobseck can appreciate them. In the Empire they’d have cost two hundred thousand more.”Gobseck’s eyes shone as he handled the case, torn between rapture and calculation. The Count wanted a loan of one hundred thousand francs for three years, offering the diamonds as security; the Countess insisted they were hers. I warned that, as a married woman, she could not legally alienate property without her husband’s consent, so a deed of purchase would be void. Gobseck shifted tactics—possession being as good as title—and offered eighty thousand francs if they left the stones with him.The Count’s pride flared; the Countess was stupefied; Maxime, her lover, blustered. Gobseck, unmoved, produced a cheque for fifty thousand francs on the Bank of France and thirty thousand in protested drafts—and even two pistols to underline his temper. Cornered, they accepted. Gobseck exulted at having obtained the diamonds “tolerably cheaply”; his ferocious delight in possession chilled me.Soon the Count burst into Gobseck’s room, furious. Gobseck replied with implacable logic: if the Countess could sign bills, she could engage in business; whether the jewels were the Count’s could not be proven without dragging the wife into court and exposing her. Our dispute became a legal contest: perhaps in equity the Count had a claim, but legally the buyer could plead good faith. I advised the Count to compromise—pay to redeem or secure a redemption clause—since litigation would be ruinous and humiliating. With my help the Count signed an acknowledgment of receipt of eighty-five thousand francs, including interest, in consideration of Gobseck’s promise to return the stones.Months later the Count, fearful and agitated, asked me to draw papers to transfer his estate to Gobseck as a simulated sale, Gobseck to hold it in trust and later restore it to the eldest son. He distrusted his son’s loyalty and feared the Countess’s influence. I warned that such a counter-deed risked disinheriting other children; he wept and agreed to provide for them in the instrument. The deed was signed but not delivered to me.Gobseck dined at my house and hinted the Count was dying and that only he and I were trusted. I resolved to visit the Count, fearing for the counter-deed. At the Count’s lodgings I found the Countess—pale, anxious—playing with her children and watching the death-chamber from an adjoining salon. She feigned devotion; some thought her penitent. Her motives were mixed: maternal anxiety, fear of destitution, some remorse, and a calculating will to recover the family fortune.Suspecting her of seeking the counter-deed, I took precautions. I sued the Count for an amount ostensibly due Gobseck so that upon his death I could attach the estate and secure the deed; I bribed a servant to warn me when the Count was gravely ill. Money governed these manoeuvres at the bedside.For two months the Count lay prostrate in squalor, disdainful of care. One morning, in a lucid moment, he summoned Ernest and entrusted the boy with a sealed packet addressed to me, begging him to carry it to the post and keep the secret even from his mother. Ernest promised. Back with his mother, he was pressed until he refused to repeat what his father had said. Furious, the Count confronted her. He accused her of filling his life with trouble and corrupting their son; she begged for mercy and vowed to expiate her sins if the children could be spared. He, exhausted of affection, called her a bad wife and mother; she fainted. At midnight he died.Gobseck and I arrived soon after. The house was in chaos. In the death-chamber and adjoining salon the Countess had been ransacking drawers—mattress overturned, papers and furniture strewn. A paper bearing the Count’s arms lay at her feet; in panic she had thrust some documents into the grate and was burning them when we entered. I snatched an addressed paper from the hearth: it was to me. Confronted, the Countess was near collapse, torn between shame and fear. Gobseck, cold and triumphant, reminded her that he was the legal owner of what her husband had sold. He asked if I would judge him; I could not deny that under the law he had rights. I left her beside her husband’s corpse while Gobseck declared himself trustee of the estate.From then on Gobseck managed the property with the hard efficiency of a usurer turned landowner—leasing the mansion, repairing mills, planting timber. He became, in all but name, lord of the manor, showing no softness. When I suggested he help young Ernest, Gobseck scoffed: adversity is the best teacher; let the youth fend for himself. My attempts to move him to honor the Count’s intentions and provide for the children met his icy thrift.Time passed. Gobseck grew feebler but retained his iron will. He kept his rooms exactly as always, receiving clients even from his bed. During financial reorganizations after France recognized the Haitian Republic, his knowledge of claims made him influential; he devised agencies, took slices of large transactions, and accepted gifts—plates, snuff-boxes, game—without ever parting cash. He remained as thin, his avarice concentrated to a point.Then Gobseck died. Summoned by his old porter, I found him kneeling by the grate, muttering bequests and naming people—half delirious, half lucid. At the hearth, amid cinders, I dug and found metal: coins, receipts, tucked papers—proof of the vast hoard he had amassed. I ordered the justice of the peace to seal everything at once.The rooms revealed the scale and eccentricity of his thrift: one chamber reeked of putrid pies, moldy fish, and shells among chests of tea and coffee; others were packed with furniture, silver-plate, vases, pictures, books, and curios—many likely presents or pledged goods. Jewel-cases bore ciphers and coats of arms; linen and weapons lay in confusion. Every item had been argued over, bartered, haggled for. Some bargains remained unsettled; dealers balked at Gobseck’s stingy terms. The stench and clutter bespoke a miser’s final degradation.There was talk of Gobseck’s intended bequests—an absurd address for an heiress known as “La Torpille”—but the immediate issue was the Restaud estate: the counter-deed, the burnt fragments, the tormented Countess, and young Ernest’s uncertain inheritance. Despite Gobseck’s harshness, Ernest would, in time, inherit a fortune sufficient to marry Mlle. Camille and provide for his mother and siblings—assuming the legal knots could be untangled and the provisions enforced.Thus closed the arc of transactions, betrayal, and legal scrimmage: Gobseck’s cold mastery of money altered an aristocratic family’s fate; the Count’s plans were thwarted by rage and sickness; the Countess’s frantic attempts to save her children degenerated into crime; and a miser’s death revealed treasures amid squalor. The outcome promised both restitution and new trials as the rightful heirs navigated tangled transfers and the old usurer’s secreted wealth.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Monsieur de Manerville, a respected Norman who married a wealthy Bordeaux heiress, became attached to her Gascon estate, the château de Lanstrac. He fled the Revolution for Martinique in 1790 and returned to find Lanstrac well managed by Mathias, a progressive notary’s clerk. After Madame de Manerville died in 1810 the old man grew miserly and deprived his only son, Paul, of means.Paul returned from college in 1810 and endured three years of strict paternal control that stifled his character. Physically brave, he lacked the will to oppose his father and so became timid in society; confined by the mansion, he failed to connect with peers and felt out of place at his father’s royalist gatherings. To escape monotony he practiced fencing and riding and read novels.His father’s death freed him: he inherited substantial, well-kept estates but retained an aversion to Bordeaux and Lanstrac. He invested in consols, traveled, served at embassies, and through indulgence and poor management depleted much of his fortune. Resolving to return as a country gentleman, he felt compelled to marry well, believing his foreign experience refined him. De Marsay warned marriage brings ridicule, responsibility, and loss of individuality; Paul insisted he sought emotional companionship and was ready for family duties, while de Marsay remained skeptical.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Despite earlier doubts, the Comte de Manerville returned to Bordeaux in winter 1821 intent on his plans. Restoring the family mansion and his Parisian manners won him a place in Bordeaux’s royalist society; an old marquise called him “the pink of fashion,” a jibe from liberals and a compliment among royalists.Paul de Manerville enjoyed the attention. Charming, witty, attentive to women, with a touch of foppery and fastidious appearance, he preferred to receive happiness rather than bestow it. Young and averse to dull pursuits, he nonetheless felt provincial life demanded marriage and domestic variety—an inclination sharpened by a secret love for Mademoiselle Evangelista, Natalie.Years earlier an affluent Spaniard, Evangelista, had settled in Bordeaux; his Creole wife, of an illustrious Spanish family, sustained their social standing with extravagant tastes funded by her devoted husband. After Evangelista’s death in 1813, Madame Evangelista, now a wealthy widow with a beautiful daughter, continued living lavishly even as her fortune waned. By Paul’s return Natalie was striking but unmarried, her prospects dampened by her mother’s extravagance and gossip that only a prince could afford her. Sheltered and accustomed to adoration, she knew little beyond privilege.Six months after Paul’s arrival they met at a high-society event and were mutually charmed. Madame perceived his interest and hoped he would become her son-in-law; Paul pictured Natalie as his future wife, reassured by childhood knowledge of the Evangelista fortune and unconcerned by provincial warnings. Society soon treated him as her suitor; royalists deemed the match prudent. Madame, forty and still striking, saw Paul as a path to Paris, while Paul felt flattered.A year later the courtship was assumed though neither pressed actively. Natalie’s reserved naivety intrigued Paul, who rationalized marriage would secure happiness and position. The narrative closes by noting that marriage negotiations—especially contracts—are pivotal in French society and will shape their future, foreshadowing forthcoming social and personal complexities.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In winter 1822 Paul de Manerville sought Natalie Evangelista’s hand through his great‑aunt, the Baronne de Maulincour. The baroness reported Natalie’s dowry would be her patrimony; her mother would contribute nothing, and advised Paul to consult notary Mathias. Paul visited Madame Evangelista, who admitted she owed Natalie a sum and could not pay; fearing he would withdraw she summoned her notary, Solonet, who promised to conceal the shortfall and arrange a favorable contract. Mathias and Solonet met—Mathias cautious, Solonet pragmatic. While Paul and Natalie shared moments, the notaries argued about selling Madame Evangelista’s house to cover debts. They compromised: Madame Evangelista would entail her property, securing Natalie and shielding Paul. Marriage could proceed, but tensions lingered, foreshadowing further conflicts of love, ambition and money.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Elie Magus, then in Bordeaux, answered Madame Evangelista’s summons expecting to discuss jewels for Comte Paul’s fiancée, Mademoiselle Natalie. Instead he valued Madame’s necklace and earrings at 250,000 francs and the “Discreto” at 100,000, warning that selling them would mean a heavy loss.That appraisal sparked staff rumors and society gossip that the marriage was unstable and Madame was selling her house. Notaries denied the claims, but public skepticism—fueled by envy of the Evangelistas’ wealth—persisted; Paul organizing a hunting party only heightened speculation. Madame de Gyas relayed the tales to Natalie and her mother, who dismissed them as self-interested.To counter the rumors and flaunt her means, Madame planned the fête of the camellias to coincide with signing the marriage contract. As the day neared she worried the contract endangered Natalie’s fortune. At the signing—guests festive, Natalie radiant, Paul enamored—Madame learned the notaries’ warning that if Paul died first Natalie would inherit only a fraction. Determined to protect her daughter, she turned from careless spender into a shrewd strategist bent on reclaiming the family fortune and influencing Paul, setting up future conflict.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The fête had briefly distracted Paul from his anxieties, which returned that night. He feared Madame Evangelista might have deceived him without Mathias’s intervention, but comforted himself that in two days Natalie would be his and their finances settled.Unbeknownst to him, his future had shifted. The most astute woman in his life had become an adversary, contemplating withdrawing her interests from their shared future. He failed to perceive the cunning of his Creole mother-in-law—part European intellect, part tropical passion—awakened by the prospect of losing her daughter. With a day until the marriage, Madame Evangelista realized she could shape the couple’s life: Natalie trusted her implicitly, and a single conversation might alter their fate.Seated on Natalie’s bed the next morning, she recalled their harmony and warned that conjugal love can fade unless carefully managed. She urged Natalie to establish authority early, suggested her own presence could complicate the household, and proposed leaving to avoid conflict. She advised feigning obedience while subtly asserting dominance and outlined tactics: keep a social life, avoid constant proximity, preserve allure and independence, and keep Paul entertained.Overwhelmed, Natalie accepted continued contact but agreed separation was necessary. When Paul arrived, Madame Evangelista offered to sell her jewelry for the couple; Paul, moved, accepted. The notary Solonet reported potential buyers for her property, and Natalie consented to the sale. Madame Evangelista resolved to step back from Parisian life and support them from afar. At the midnight wedding she played the grieving mother, extracted promises from Paul to secure her influence, and sent the newlyweds to Paris, where Natalie immediately began to assert her authority—setting the stage for a complex marriage.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Five years later, in November, Comte Paul de Manerville, cloaked and somber, arrived at his old friend Monsieur Mathias’s house in Bordeaux. The retired notary was absent; Paul waited in the late Madame Mathias’s room until evening. When Mathias returned, he showed concern and embarrassment over public notices of Paul’s ruin, blaming Paul’s marriage to Natalie Evangelista for the downfall while admitting relief that the estate titles were preserved even though the usufruct was lost.Paul, altered but calm, confessed he had left Natalie without her knowledge and planned to sail to Calcutta to rebuild his fortune. He no longer considered himself a count and intended to go into commerce under another name. He explained enormous debts from lavish living and gifts to Natalie, the sale of assets, and heavy borrowing from usurers, which produced the catastrophe. Mathias doubted prospects—especially reliance on de Marsay—but the next day a bill of exchange from de Marsay confirmed some funds. Still, Paul remained determined, vowed to return wealthy, and boarded the ship.At sea Paul, tormented by memories and seasickness, opened letters. Natalie begged to help, pledging her jewels and promising to economize and live at Lanstrac with her mother to pay his debts. De Marsay’s letter revealed the full extent of ruin and exposed Paul’s mother-in-law’s profiteering. Confronted by love, treachery, anger, and remorse, Paul sailed away uncertain of the future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Railroads threatened to make many industries obsolete and to transform travel around Paris; the once-common "coucous" of 1830 were already fading by 1842, surviving mainly at suburban spectacles. In 1820 many famed districts still lacked regular stage-coach service, so local short-route vehicles remained essential.The Touchards, father and son, held a near-monopoly on travel within about forty-five miles from their rue du Faubourg-Saint-Denis base, but local coucous vigorously competed on shorter routes. Parisians’ appetite for the countryside let small, familiar operators—often innkeepers who knew the roads, undercut prices, bent rules, and earned local loyalty—thrive. These coucous offered speed and convenience that challenged the Touchards until omnibuses later eclipsed them by carrying more passengers more efficiently.One lucrative contested line ran from Paris to Beaumont-sur-Oise; despite Touchard efforts to cut fares and improve equipment, stops like Saint-Denis and Saint-Brice guaranteed demand, and rivals even reached Beauvais while the Touchards extended to Chambly. By contrast, the road to Isle-Adam, prized for quarries and its picturesque terminus, discouraged speculation; only two coaches served it, giving Pierrotin and his colleague a virtual monopoly that locals often preferred.Pierrotin, in his forties, had taken over his father’s coucou after leaving the cavalry in 1815. He married an innkeeper’s daughter, expanded the business, and combined intelligence with military precision—dress, bearing, and a tendency to regard passengers as cargo. He tried to modernize: ordering a larger coach while keeping an inherited yellow coucou (built for six but often overloaded) and a noisy four-wheeler that could hold seventeen though limited to fourteen. He paid taxes only on the coucou, using a permit for the larger coach to skirt regulations.On an autumn Saturday in 1822, outside the Lion d’Argent, Pierrotin fretted over scarce passengers and a thousand-franc debt, mulling a loan from Monsieur Moreau, when a footman sought discreet passage for the incognito Comte de Serizy. Pierrotin seized the chance, hoping the fare would ease his burdens; the footman’s insistence on secrecy heightened his sense of responsibility for a smooth journey.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur Huguet de Serisy descends from the eminent President Huguet, ennobled under François I; the family arms and motto reflect modesty and estate. His father was president of parliament before the Revolution. The count became councillor of State at twenty-two, noted for persuasive diplomatic memoranda. He remained at Serizy during the Revolution, sheltered by his father; after his father’s death in 1794 he joined the Council of Five Hundred. After 18th Brumaire he won the First Consul’s favor, reentered the Council of State, and reorganized a chaotic department, helping shape much of Napoleon’s administration. In 1806 he married the widow of a prominent Republican general, improving his fortune. By 1814 ill health forced his resignation; though Napoleon mistrusted him, he kept rank. Louis XVIII later trusted him as a cabinet minister. During the Hundred Days he stayed loyal to the Bourbons at Serizy; after Waterloo he resumed duties as privy councillor and vice-president of the Council of State, influencing affairs without seeking acclaim. His strict routine and many honors, including the grand cross of the Legion of Honor, belied an unspoken sorrow.He was passionately attached to his wife; work was his refuge from recurring heartache. He treated her with respect and freedom, yet her frequent ingratitude wounded him.A pressing dispute concerned the Presles farm, leased by tenant Léger from burgher Margueron and soon to be sold. The count instructed his notary to buy it to end conflicts. His steward Moreau—saved by the count’s father and later spared by the count—was appointed to manage Presles but became lax and corrupt, secretly dealing with Léger. Madame de Reybert lodged detailed accusations; the count, at first doubtful, grew uneasy and sent his notary with her. A routine purchase thus turned into an investigation of intrigue and possible betrayal by trusted servants.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Pierrotin left the Café de l’Echiquier he saw Madame Clapart and her son at the Lion d’Argent searching for him. In a faded black gown and old shawl, she was anxious but dignified, entrusting eighteen-year-old Oscar—travelling alone for the first time—to Pierrotin’s care. Oscar, in a short-sleeved coat and patched trousers, resented her fussing about his gloves and trip to Monsieur Moreau.Pierrotin knew them from errands for Moreau, a discreet patron who had helped the family and secured Oscar a scholarship. Once Madame Husson, Madame Clapart had been ruined by her first husband’s suicide and later married Clapart, a meager government clerk; his coldness strained the household and aggravated Oscar’s faults. Madame Clapart’s cling to past glories and overprotection fed Oscar’s vanity and entitlement, prompting Moreau to propose testing his character by placing him with the steward.As they prepared to leave, two well-dressed youths mocked Oscar, deepening his humiliation and desire for independence. Boarding heightened his self-consciousness: a shabby young artist and his apprentice, Mistigris, bantered bohemianly, while the Comte de Serizy, travelling incognito as a bourgeois, altered the coach’s tone—Pierrotin even rearranged seats to oblige him, drawing complaints. The departing coach thus presented a cross-section of social types, with Oscar’s struggle over identity and self-worth quietly simmering.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Those who travel by public conveyance usually begin in silence, broken only when the most talkative passenger starts a conversation. National types are sketched briefly: Englishmen taciturn, Germans somber, Italians cautious, Spaniards seldom using public transport, Russians hampered by poor roads; France, by contrast, is lively, especially among the lower classes and bourgeois.Georges, a clever, lively young man, quickly sized up his fellow passengers, deemed himself superior, and resolved to amuse himself at their expense. As the coach descended from La Chapelle to Saint-Denis he toyed with identities, finally posing as a Russian prince or a soldier returned from the East. When Mistigris complained about dust, Georges joked and steered talk toward the Levant, claiming service under Ali Pacha of Janina and inventing a plague scare that even made the count have the driver stop. He asserted he’d fought in 1813 and at Waterloo and praised Napoleon, spinning tales of Egypt and a supposed harem.At Saint-Denis the party paused at a tavern famed for its cheesecakes, where the count—suspicious—found a portfolio identifying Georges as Crottat’s second clerk, casting doubt on his stories. Still, Georges paid for treats and ordered drinks, impressing Père Léger and Oscar and cultivating importance. He continued with vivid accounts of Eastern oddities and harsh pacha taxation, and capped his performance with a dramatic Dalmatian romance—Zena, a jealous pirate, poison, and a narrow escape—that captivated the travelers. Laughter, banter, and drink warmed the coach: admiration mingled with the count’s skepticism as the journey became a weave of tales and a performance of identity.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Pierrotin’s coach stopped at Saint-Brice about half past one to rest the horses. The innkeeper greeted the travelers by name and they disembarked. Georges, showy and boastful, called for a hearty breakfast and twirled his cane; Père Léger, a sturdy farmer, agreed to “break a crust.” Georges produced a straw cigar case and offered cigars to Oscar and Schinner. Oscar, flattered but awkward, puffed ineptly and became the butt of teasing: Schinner smoked with ease, Georges fumbled, and Mistigris joked about Oscar’s discomfort. Oscar watched Georges’s generosity with thinly veiled resentment, mentally tallying the bills he suspected the painter was burning through.Georges proposed a bottle of Bordeaux with Père Léger, and the innkeeper supplied gossip: François, conductor of Touchard’s coach, had said Monsieur Margueron was invited by the Comte de Serizy to dine at Presles that day. Père Léger laughed and outlined his plan to beat aristocratic patronage: he intended to buy Les Moulineaux cheaply—using freshly minted coins and buying at once before the count could act. The innkeeper calculated returns and called the estate a sound investment; Père Léger explained he’d taken Moreau as partner and, riding royal favor, had secured the property. The scheme sounded shrewd enough even if the count intervened.The count—listening—grew uneasy at their lack of discretion. When Pierrotin stepped out to water the horses, the count drew him aside, reminding him of a bribe: ten louis to keep quiet. Then he raised it: if Pierrotin would conceal his identity and stay with the horses rather than enter the inn, the count would give him a thousand francs next day for a new coach. He instructed Pierrotin not to speak of the matter and to wait with the horses. Pierrotin returned a curt reassurance and hurried the travelers on, insisting he must deliver fish by three to a big dinner in Stors.Père Léger suggested they harness the horse Pierrotin meant to sell into the cabriolet so they could breakfast leisurely and overtake him. To their surprise, Pierrotin rebridled and set off; Schinner and Mistigris walked. Georges kept up talk about the hostess and the landscape; Oscar, stung by being called “the little young man,” defended his dignity: he had not been to the East, his family intended him for diplomacy, and his mother ran their household. His companions ridiculed his pretensions, tutors, and vocabulary.Pressed to name his tutor, Oscar named the Abbé Loraux, vicar of Saint-Sulpice, and hinted at family influence that might help the abbé’s rise. Pushed further, he declared he was bound for the Château de Presles to visit the Comte de Serizy and claimed intimacy with the count’s son. His confident talk about the Serizys drew the count’s attention.Seizing a chance, the count questioned Oscar, saying he sought information on Monsieur de Serizy to support a claim he intended to press with the American government. Oscar advised him to approach the count’s wife rather than the count: the count, he said, was madly in love with her while she secretly despised him. He described the count’s disfiguring skin disease and humiliating remedies—sulphur and steam baths, even an iron box—portraying a humiliated man, and recommended the Marquis d’Aiglemont, an old admirer of Madame de Serizy, as a better route to influence.The young man’s ease in speaking of such intimacy shocked the party. Schinner scolded him for airing friends’ affairs in public; Mistigris professed indifference to tale-bearing. Georges pronounced a maxim: in a public coach one should know how to talk without saying anything. When the conversation turned to the Comtesse de Serizy, the count interrupted sharply, forbade disparagement, and asserted his friendship with her brother, the Marquis de Ronquerolles. He vowed that speaking ill of the countess would not go unpunished, hinting at ministers, decorations, and acquaintances with Presles’s architect. He asked Pierrotin to stop at the next inn so he could step out.Once clear of the others, the count had Pierrotin show him aside in the yard and asked about the talkative young man. Pierrotin explained the boy was the son of Madame Husson of the rue de la Cerisaie, whom he often supplied with fruit and game from Presles. Pierrotin had driven the youngster only once and thought he might be the prince of Maffliers; the boy had told him to leave him near Maffliers, not go on to Isle-Adam. Satisfied, the count rejoined the party. Père Léger and the others exchanged awkward glances, feeling exposed; the three young men fell silent. Mistigris quipped about suffering for loose talk, and Georges reminded Oscar that public conveyances required artful speech.They rode into the Carreau woods in a heavy hush; the count noted their quiet as if led to the scaffold. Oscar, striving to assert himself, declared he was Oscar Husson and would be famous within ten years. The companions teased him, and the count refined his name to Husson de la Cerisaie, lending his boast a veneer of dignity. The coach descended toward Presles.Before disembarking, the count delivered theatrical farewells: the colonel should make peace with the king; Schinner’s fame was assured; Oscar needed no protection since he possessed “the secrets of statesmen;” Père Léger was urged to seize the Comte de Serizy firmly. Asking Pierrotin to set and fetch him at the same spot next day, the count left, provoking bewildered speculation about his identity. Père Léger guessed he was the count who’d leased Franconville. As they neared the château gate, Georges warned Oscar to be wary if the man truly were the Comte de Serizy—a chill reminder of his mother’s warnings. Pierrotin called out: they had arrived at Presles.Some of the young men feigned familiarity, but Georges, not wishing to reveal his destination, pretended he would go on to Les Moulineaux. Père Léger then triumphantly disclosed he was the farmer at Les Moulineaux, and Georges was forced to admit it. Oscar left his packages with the steward and plunged toward the château, guided to the little gate by Père Léger. Georges slipped away when he realized Père Léger truly owned Les Moulineaux. Pierrotin, having obeyed the count’s injunction to keep the matter secret for now, drove in with swagger, deposited packages, and handed a small portmanteau to the concierge’s wife. Mistigris and the painter took rooms in the château itself—astonishing Oscar.The scene closed on Pierrotin’s contentment: he had kept the count’s secret and now expected the promised reward. He anticipated a thousand francs and the splendid new coach he long desired. The coach rolled on, the passengers dispersed through the estate, and the social ambitions, pretensions, and private bargains that had surfaced settled back into the polite surface of conversation, continuing to move unseen beneath it.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Oscar hid and watched Monsieur Moreau emerge from the guard-room in a blue overcoat with a riding whip. Moreau greeted the painters Mistigris and Bridau—sent by the architect Grindot—told the concierge to show them to their rooms, invited them to dinner at five, and mentioned hunting on the estate.Leading Oscar through the park, Moreau sent his son Jacques ahead to tell Madame Moreau of their arrival. Stern but kindly, he urged Oscar to learn riding and hunting despite inexperience. Oscar’s mother had allowed only a two-week stay because Madame Moreau was present, yet Moreau seemed intent on extending it.Their steward’s house, attached to the chateau and built before the Revolution, stood in a large garden and held a salon, dining room, and a modern boudoir furnished to Madame Moreau’s taste. They lived largely on estate provisions. Madame Moreau, once a maid, retained an air of elegance, courted the appearance of the chateau’s mistress, and patronized villagers. She bore a grudge against the Reyberts for reminding her of her origins and hoped the visiting artist would enhance her social standing.On the painters’ day, Madame Moreau dressed elegantly but the Paris coach brought only Mistigris’s packages, disappointing her. When Mistigris and Bridau arrived she professed a love of art; they saw her pretension and humored her. A maid then announced the count’s unexpected arrival. Flustered, Madame Moreau hurried; Oscar was scolded for his appearance, and a joking remark by Mistigris heightened the awkwardness.The count, angry to find Moreau absent and having overheard remarks implying disloyalty, confronted him. Moreau’s explanations failed; distraught at the loss of his position and the harm caused by Oscar’s careless words, he returned home. Estelle, furious, grasped the gravity. Moreau demanded an explanation from the silent, ashamed Oscar, dragged him back to apologize, but the count dismissed the boy and doubted his promise. Humiliated, Moreau took Oscar home to face uncertain consequences for the family.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Moreau wrote to Madame Clapart, distressed that Oscar had revealed Madame de Serizy’s secrets to an incognito count; the count sent Oscar home, costing Moreau his stewardship at Presles and jeopardizing the family’s prospects. At the Clapart house Monsieur fretted about Oscar’s character while Madame defended him; when Brochon returned with Oscar and Moreau’s letter Madame fainted. The next day Oscar was sullen; Madame reminded him of Moreau’s past support and their precarious situation, urging discretion, hard work, and prudence, but he remained indifferent. She appealed to Uncle Cardot, a modest self-made businessman, who kindly praised Oscar and offered to fund his legal studies, advising integrity and discipline. Hope revived. Moreau later said he’d begun speculating in land and could place Oscar as a law pupil, again insisting that disciplined study was essential; Madame urged Oscar to show gratitude and heed these admonitions.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Ten days later Monsieur Moreau brought Oscar to Maître Desroches, a young solicitor in the rue de Béthisy who had recently opened a roomy but inexpensive office. Desroches, twenty-six and raised in poverty, recognized Oscar’s situation; the boy’s gaunt look and curt manners intimidated him. “We work here day and night,” Desroches declared amid a mountain of papers, and he placed his head clerk, Monsieur Godeschal, in charge of Oscar’s training so the youth would not lose time on the way to law school and would master the Code. Oscar was lodged in a small attic beside Godeschal’s room; Desroches plainly intended to make him a capable clerk.For two years Oscar lived at the office under exacting, petty work. Under Godeschal’s strict oversight he kept a monk-like schedule: rising at five to prepare, running errands, studying the Code under rigorous tests, returning to office tasks after classes, and subsisting on plain meals. His social life was minimal—monthly breakfasts with his uncle Cardot and Sundays with his mother—interrupted only by occasional outings Moreau could arrange. His efforts at elegance met his superiors’ disapproval.Godeschal counseled a modest wardrobe and frugal living—insisting a good clerk should not spend more than seven hundred francs a year—and lived those principles. He showed practical humanity: when a colleague failed, he discreetly guided him to resign rather than face public dismissal.By twenty, Oscar was the third clerk, performing the second’s duties without pay but receiving room and board. His legal knowledge surpassed many licensed graduates and he began arguing cases in chambers, yet he felt constrained, envying well-dressed young men and yearning for freedom and pleasure he habitually suppressed. Godeschal, aware of these struggles, tried to shield him and sometimes paid for outings; Moreau supported him but could not connect as Godeschal did. “How is he getting on?” Moreau asked. “Too much vanity,” Godeschal replied, suggesting Oscar’s talents might suit the bar rather than a clerkship.In November 1825 a new clerk, Frédéric Marest, arrived—well-to-do and aiming for the magistracy. Practical jokes lightened the office: Godeschal kept a fictitious reception register filled with absurd entries. When Marest read it and then spoke of consulting a cousin who was a head clerk about his welcome, Godeschal saw his inexperience. Despite office camaraderie, Oscar’s inner conflict persisted as he navigated professional pressure and youthful expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next day Oscar recognized Georges Marest, now head-clerk to notary Hannequin, who greeted him and recalled past mischief involving a peer of France that had led to Georges’ transfer. Georges invited Oscar and the other clerks to the Rocher de Cancale and to an evening with Madame la Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos, promising high society. Desroches, head of the office, interrupted to task Oscar with fetching an important legal document from the Palais and warned him to beware of Derville. Eager to prove himself as second clerk, Oscar accepted.After Georges and Oscar left, Godeschal sought details about the Marquise, but Frederic kept up a ruse, calling her a wealthy widow from Mexico. In fact she was Mademoiselle Agathe-Florentine Cabirolle, a celebrated danseuse at the Gaîté, supported by Père Cardot, who had taken in her and her mother and financed her career as she prepared for a possible Opera engagement. With new means, Georges actively pursued her.The clerks dressed up; Oscar’s mother, Madame Clapart, hoped for his advancement and warned about Georges. At a lavish breakfast the intoxicated clerks went to Florentine’s opulent salon. Oscar, charmed, joined a vingt-et-un game with Florentine and the actress Fanny Beaupré, quickly losing the money meant for the document. Florentine offered him a thousand francs; he accepted, then lost everything and sank into despair and stupor. The party continued into the morning while Oscar slept in the boudoir, unaware of the consequences.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next morning a loud noise wakes clerk Oscar Husson. His uncle Cardot scolds him for late-night revelry; Florentine, a danseuse, reveals Oscar as Cardot’s nephew and urges him to help. Oscar has lost gambling money belonging to his employer, Monsieur Desroches, and, ashamed, begs Cardot, who reluctantly lends 500 francs as a final favor.Mariette sends a note and money; fellow clerk Godeschal rushes to secure documents for Desroches. Angered, Desroches plans to dismiss Oscar despite Godeschal’s defense and his blaming of Georges Marest; Desroches half-jokes that Oscar might try law. At home, Clapart torments his wife over their son’s failures; Madame Clapart defends Oscar. Oscar confronts his father asserting independence but is ignored. Determined to take responsibility, he faces conscription. Clapart hopes the army will discipline him; Oscar draws a number and, three days later by the Comte de Serizy’s influence, is drafted into the cavalry.Madame Clapart turns to religion. In the army Oscar applies himself, rises to lieutenant-colonel, and years later — a decorated man — stands with his mother outside the Lion d’Argent. They are changed; Georges Marest is a ruined shadow.
