Honorine is pious—she attends church and prays—but she has never confessed or taken Communion, anticipating what a priest would say. She will not return to me; the horror I inspire overwhelms her, though I have never harmed her and have always been kind. Even if I was hasty in teaching her, is that a reason for her implacable determination? Her silence is absolute: she has never told Madame Gobain who she is; the housekeeper cannot advocate for me. The others know nothing; they live in awe of the Prefect of Police and respect the power of a Minister. Thus it is impossible for me to penetrate her heart. I have no means of action; any violence would ruin me forever.How can I argue against reasons I do not know? Should I write and have my letter copied and delivered, risking a third removal, which cost me fifty thousand francs previously? The last purchase of a house had been made in the name of my second secretary. The poor man, unaware of my vigilance, was caught opening a private box; I intimidated him into selling the house and dismissed him. If I did not feel my noblest faculties satisfied by this divine fatherhood—if I did not drink delight in every pore—I might think myself a monomaniac. Sometimes at night I hear the jingling bells of madness and dread the violent transitions from feeble hope to complete despair. Recently I thought of the tragic ends of Lovelace and Clarissa Harlowe; if Honorine were the mother of my child, she must return under her husband’s roof.I have such faith in a happy future that ten months ago I bought one of the finest houses in the Faubourg Saint-Honoré. If I win her back I will not let her see the room from which she fled; I will place my idol in a new temple where life will seem entirely new. That house is being transformed into a marvel of elegance and taste. I was inspired by an anecdote of a poet’s extravagant purchase for an actress; great passions share such impulses. I love like a poet—like an emperor!From the vehemence of my speech you might imagine a burning physical passion, but it is nothing compared to my worship for her soul, mind, and heart. By retrospection I now see the graces of Honorine’s mind and heart I overlooked in happiness. Day by day I appreciate my loss, discovering gifts in that capricious and proud young creature who has grown strong under poverty and the cowardly shock of desertion. That heavenly blossom is fading in solitude and hiding. The law we discussed cannot compel love—seizing my wife by force would be triumph over a corpse. Religion has little hold on her; she craves its poetry but does not heed its commands. I have exhausted mercy, kindness, and love; I am at my wit’s end. Only one chance remains: the cunning and patience of bird-catchers who finally trap the wariest birds.When M. de Grandville’s indiscretion revealed my secret life to you, I regarded it as fate’s decree. Have you affection for me to show romantic devotion? I replied with a question about danger: my first secretary had tried to open my deed box; my second might fall in love with my wife. Can anyone put his hand in a brazier without burning it? The Count answered that he would send me well-gloved. It would not be his secretary who lodged in the Rue Saint-Maur but his distant cousin, Baron de l’Hostal, a lawyer high in office—an appearance of respectability.Shortly after, Madame de Courteville and her daughter called; the Count had family ties on his mother’s side. A young woman of twenty might suit the Count, but he did not propose that match. Instead, three weeks later, I moved into the gardener’s cottage, repaired and furnished by Paris workmen and money. I was as much in love as the Count could desire for security. Would a young man of twenty-five play this part faithfully for his friend’s cause? I confess I relied on my uncle’s advice; the Count had authorized me to confide in him if needed. I engaged the garden, devoted myself to horticulture, and worked frantically—obsessing over varieties of flowers as part of my disguise. The Count dictated the tragi-comedy enacted in the Rue Saint-Maur. Each night, after Honorine went to bed, Octave, Madame Gobain, and I held council; Octave lived only for that hour.For two months I worked in the garden without ever seeing the little house where my fair neighbor lived; I had not even inquired if I had a neighbor. Her garden front was divided from mine by a paling, her cypress trees already four feet high. One morning Gobain warned me that a neighbor intended to build a wall between the gardens. My curiosity burned to see the Countess—my wish nearly extinguished the budding affection I had briefly felt for Amélie de Courteville. Building a wall would close off fresh air from Honorine’s garden and turn her plot into a narrow alley. The cottage, once a summer villa, imitated a trellis with flowers up to the second floor—a charming, fragile place. My wall would cut away three-quarters of it.When Gobain informed the Countess that the florist wished to be left in peace, she replied she would speak to him herself. The next day as I walked the path I saw the curtain parted and a face peering out. Gobain announced me as the eccentric neighbor. I had dressed as a countryman—gray flannel trousers, wooden shoes, shabby coat, peaked cap, hands soiled with mold—to strengthen the part. The Countess did not shrink.It was early May and the air was pure; seeing Honorine, I understood Octave’s passion. Her pallor was of a particular white, with pink flushes at emotion. Sunlight through the acacia gave her a pale gold halo. Her brown eyes were tender and lively, framed by long lashes; a glance could enchant. Light-brown hair framed a high brow; her mouth was voluptuous; her head bore a stamp of nobility. Slight but not thin, she embodied mignonne—pliant, ready to be petted, yet proud and imposing. Her walk had a proud carriage, and even humble people made way. Gay and tender, haughty and commanding, she combined contradictions and remained childlike. She could be as implacable as an angel if hurt.The violet perfume she wore made sense of the Count’s adoration: she inspired devotion that demanded chivalrous sacrifice. Many women study their expressions; in her it flowed from a nature that went straight to the heart. If I sketch her it is because her soul and exquisite heart are central to this tale, and you would blame me had I not done so.I was about to forget my ridiculous role as half-crazy rustic when I asked her, with assumed gravity, if she loved flowers. She replied she made artificial flowers—after growing them she imitates them, as a mother paints her children—an occupation that proved she was poor. I pretended to be a gardener with an interest in blue dahlias and blue roses; I argued that blue is the color of superior souls. I proposed open railings to unite our gardens. She, who loved solitude and independence, allowed me the right to enter her garden anytime and urged I be treated as a neighbor with whom she would keep good terms. With a leap I cleared the paling.For a fortnight I seemed indifferent; by late May on a lovely evening we walked on opposite sides of the paling and exchanged civil words. I leaped the fence and joined her. She led me into the house—a charming sanctuary of eighteenth-century taste: frescoed dining-room, tasteful staircase, faded but elegant drawing-room, a bedroom, dressing-room, and workroom used as a library. The kitchen lay below. Everything harmonized with the woman I had come to love.Exquisite simplicity reigned. Her little house was like a box of 18th-century art; but occasionally a bitter smile hinted at deeper sorrow. In days we reached intimacy born of proximity and her conviction of my indifference to women. A look would have spoiled everything; I kept my feelings masked. Honorine saw me as an old friend and treated me with pity rather than coquetry. She soon allowed me into her pretty workshop, full of books and curiosities—a boudoir where elegance softened the vulgarity of the tools of her trade. She had poetized manufacture.Perhaps making artificial flowers allows a woman to remain herself: she can chat, laugh, sing and still work. Honorine had an artist’s sense in arranging petals and shades; white china saucers held colors; a cabinet with drawers held molds; a Japanese bowl held the paste; a Venetian glass held the living model she strove to imitate. She pursued difficult tasks—tiny corollas, wildflowers—moving like an accomplished pianist. Her fingers seemed fairy-like in their deft economy of action.I could not tire of admiring her; she displayed the genius of a painter in bold attempts, copying faded flowers and yellowed leaves to create poetic crowns suitable to women’s moods. She explained a fanciful botany of feelings—flowers for bacchantes, for bigots, pensive flowers for bored women. She employed me to stamp leaves, cut material, and prepare wires. My affected desire for occupation made me useful. We talked as we worked; when idle I read to her to keep my role. My gloomy, misanthropic persona amused and matched her antipathy to love; she was content with a companion on her desert island.I spared you the shame I felt as I feigned wounds in my heart to excite her compassion. Her renunciation of love, her dread of happiness, she proclaimed with vehemence. These happy days convinced me that a woman’s friendship can be superior to her love. She resisted any trap of sentiment; mulish obstinacy is common among women, and she was stubborn.One evening, playing the cynic, I argued that women, once they have inspired passion, hold men sacred forever, while women forget. She rebuked my generalization and denied being like those who give themselves to successive loves. She declared herself a nun, seventy-two years old in spirit, not a woman. Her disgust for life made her invulnerable. She often spoke of the angel Gabriel singing in her heart; if rich she would work still to prevent herself from soaring on “many-tinted wings.” She found some solace in her flowers but sometimes they failed to occupy her. She felt some great event impending; her life seemed about to change.After three months of this diplomatic struggle between my assumed melancholy and her proud indifference, I told the Count I could not coax her out of herself; the shell must be broken. The Count left me free to act.One Saturday in July, she gleefully showed me five gold pieces she had earned for her flowers and caps. It was late, a moonlit evening. The room smelled of perfumes; the Countess jingled the coins that Octave’s accomplices had provided as part of the ruse. She said she earned her living by amusing herself and felt transports of pride every Saturday. I suggested the image of a pretty child running among the flowers calling her “mother,” and her reaction was immediate and extreme: she suffered an attack—what she called “a cramp of the heart.” Madame Gobain tended her; I walked the path weeping and doubting whether I should abandon the bird-catcher’s role I had assumed. Gobain returned saying I cried like a child, and the Countess, roused by the danger of misinterpretation, wore a wrapper and came down to me.“You are not the cause of this attack,” she said, and when I asked about her troubles she declared her heart a poem laid before God. I threatened, lightly, that I could know all her secrets by tomorrow. She defiantly challenged me to be curious and told me to reveal what I discovered and the means by which I discovered it, for the preservation of the small happiness she so dearly bought depended on my discretion.“I defy you,” she said; she wanted me to find out who she really was. I warned that if I learned anything carelessly, others would be informed, and then—she would fly “on wings” to the New World. I interrupted that Paris is a place where those who must work can hide. She persisted: find out who I am. I promised to tell her what I might discover the next day and pleaded she not hate me. She replied coldly, almost imperiously, that only two words would suffice to destroy her peaceful fraud and she must be allowed to keep her carefully arranged retreat. After a few more words, she dismissed me abruptly.Thus the Count’s scheme had reached a crucial point: I was to be the Count’s instrument, the gardener-spy and companion, someone who could win Honorine’s confidence without revealing his identity. The house’s delicate balance—Honorine’s love of flowers, her pride, her vow of solitude, and the secret charity that sustained her—would be the stage for whether pity, cunning, or patience could redeem what force and authority could not.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“The battle is to-morrow, then,” I said, trying to smile and appear indifferent. Octave shared Honorine’s agitation; the Count and I walked until two in the morning like generals before a decisive engagement, aware that victory might hinge on a single instant. For Octave and Honorine the true drama was interior—located in the spiritual world of lofty souls.The next evening I called at ten and entered Honorine’s white-and-blue bedroom, the refuge of this wounded dove. The Comtesse, wrapped in grief, seemed ready to speak but fell silent at my respectful manner. I told her plainly she was not living by choice in her house but under Count Octave’s protection: M. Lenormand the usher was only a front; the seclusion, money, and shelter all came from him. Her disappearance had been plausibly explained; her husband had even hoped she had been saved from a shipwreck. He had hidden her as she hid herself, obeying her for seven years.The Countess trembled with defiance that later yielded helpless tears. She had believed herself free; marriage weighed on her like a prison. She declared she would not return—if forced she would go somewhere none could follow. When I asked if she meant suicide she denied it but insisted she had powerful reasons to refuse Octave. I urged her to confide in my uncle and me—both impartial, devoted advisers. My uncle, a priest but not a pedant, would listen and try to find a solution: if she were a victim a misunderstanding could be cleared; if guilty expiation might be sought. I promised friendship and refuge if she proved she could not and ought not to return, offering peace, solitude, independence, and to see her only by her consent.She answered that no man could understand her conscience—“like circles of Dante’s hell.” Another man, though unworthy, had had all her love; for her there could never be two loves. The cup of happiness once broken could not be refilled. She refused to be a prostitute to Octave, whose generosity she esteemed and respected but could never love. Expecting tenderness from him would be impossible; every time their eyes met she would see her sin reflected. To return would be a snare: she feared killing him by coldness or provoking suspicion, and could not bear to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of a child conceived in rapture and nursed for seven months; other children would draw tears in their milk. For her there was no situation under his roof that did not hide a precipice. Religion offered solace, but she lacked the strength to endure that particular suffering. She had chosen between a hell in which God did not forbid blessing Him and another under Count Octave’s roof.I asked where she would go. Could a woman at thirty, beautiful and with undiscovered powers, live unprotected in the wilderness where I could hide her? I reassured her the Count—who had not shown himself at her house for nine years—would not go anywhere without her permission; she had his devotion, a guarantee of tranquillity. I urged her to speak with my uncle, whose moral influence equaled a minister’s. I left at midnight; Honorine seemed calm but depressed, harboring a secret purpose I could not divine.I found Octave a few paces away in the Rue Saint-Maur—drawn by irresistible attraction, he had left our agreed spot. On hearing of my visit he fretted: “What a night my poor child will go through! Suppose I were to go to her now—what if she saw me suddenly?” I warned that she might throw herself from a window; she was one of those Lucretias who could not survive such violence. He countered that passions change like a tempest on a lake; tonight she might as well rush into his arms as she might throw herself out. He joked of opium to sleep away the torment.Next day Gobain brought a letter: Honorine had gone to bed at six after taking a chemist’s draught and fallen asleep. He read her anguished letter aloud. She acknowledged conscience condemned her: conscience is God’s interpreter to man. If she did not reconcile with Octave she would be damned; civil law condemned her to obey; if her husband did not reject her the world would deem her pure. Legally, religiously, and socially she ought to return; it would be cruel to deprive him of happiness, children, or to blot his name. Her sufferings and repugnance she admitted were selfish and ought to be sacrificed to family. She should be a mother; a child’s caresses might wipe many tears. God, law, and society were in accord.Yet she declared she could not live false to herself. She would become “white and innocent again” only in death. Not obstinacy, she said, but a nature that could not renew a passion once consummated. “A young bride is like a plucked flower; a guilty wife is a flower trodden underfoot,” she wrote. She could not love Octave: every time their eyes met she would see her sin reflected; his generosity would measure her crime. Neither esteem nor sacrifice could survive. She feared she would by coldness kill him or provoke suspicion. She could not bear to be treated as chattel, to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of her first child; religion might offer solace but she lacked energy for that suffering. She had chosen between a hell in which God did not forbid blessing Him and a hell within Octave’s house. She closed asking me to tell her where to find the solitude, peace, and silence I had promised.After copying the letter I went to the Rue Payenne. Anxiety had conquered the opium’s power; Octave paced his garden like a madman. I gave him Honorine’s letter and urged him to answer; he must reassure modesty with experience, for it is harder to restore conviction than to seduce ignorance. He cried out with joy on reading her plea: “She is mine!” Ecstasy and torment possessed him; he would act. I visited other friends and, comparing different women, reflected that some inspire perfect fidelity while others demand extraordinary generosity. Life teaches that marriage excludes passion and family cannot be founded on tempests of love. Having dreamed impossible passion and tasted its torment, I saw modest reality and took a manful resolution.When I returned to the Count to announce his relations’ arrival I set a condition: if he succeeded in bringing Honorine back, she must never see me again and must never know Maurice was his secretary. “Never mention my name to her, or all will be undone.” I asked Octave to get me a diplomatic post abroad instead of a home position; I did not want to marry Amélie and sought to be sent away. He understood and pressed my hand. We agreed on evening arrangements at Honorine’s.That August evening the Countess sat under an arbor in loose white muslin with blue bows. She did not rise but signaled me to sit. “Is not life deadlocked for me?” she asked. I told her it need not be if she chose differently. She accused me of being a traitor sent by her husband. I explained I had forwarded her letter through my uncle and would bring Octave’s reply. I insisted she must read his answer in the presence of my uncle and me; there is a law, divine and human, that even hatred must obey: do not condemn without hearing the defense. She consented.My uncle’s entrance into the little Pompadour drawing-room at ten calmed the Countess. He handed her Octave’s letter. In it Octave said that if she had trusted him and read a letter he had written five years earlier she would have spared five years of privations; he admitted faults and ignorance at the marriage’s start and confessed he had been punished for audacity. He begged mercy, saying he so valued her susceptibilities he would not bring her back to Rue Payenne where he could not bear to see her. He was decorating another house and hoped to conduct not a wife secured by ignorance but a sister who would allow him to press a fatherly kiss on her brow. He promised not to force passion on her until he felt sure of his strength to leave her free. She could live as under legitimate protection, unapproachable, occupied as she pleased, with fortune for good works. If she longed for absolution it would wait on her pride; he would not compel her by ecclesiastical terrors or legal bidding; he wanted her happiness and would not take peace except by her consent. If she persisted in condemning him to loneliness he would yield.Honorine folded the letter, pressed it to her bosom, and, soothed, said she would avail herself of the Count’s permission to remain where she was. My involuntary cry betrayed that I was more than a messenger; she looked at me with mischief and pressed my hand at the door. I replied that I would leave tomorrow. Six days later I departed with an appointment as vice-consul in Spain. After I established myself, Count Octave wrote: if I were happy he would not write; he had entered a new life of suffering, grown young again in desire but governed by a diplomat’s prudence.When I left, Octave had not yet been admitted to the pavilion in Rue Saint-Maur, though a letter promised permission. He waited more than a month and finally called. Gobain stalled with flattery—“Madame had to dress”—raising Octave’s hopes. Once admitted, he spoke tremblingly: he told her he had no crime but being in love with his wife and proposed she no longer work, offering certificates worth twelve thousand francs each—an offer that was freedom more than money. She took them, languidly, and understood the gift. She conceded and said she would be seen as often as he liked.Two months of resumed habit revealed her true nature; it was a delicate springtime of love giving Octave ineffable bliss. Yet when he proposed she go to England to resume rank and life in their new residence she feared leaving their experiment. “Why not live always as we are?” she asked, and he submitted, wondering if she were making an experiment. She eventually agreed to return as his wife when he wished but warned that submission had dangers: she could be resigned but could not give the love he wanted. She loved his soul enough to die in his service—an expiation—and knelt, sobbing she might not die.For two months he struggled. His heart was too full; he sought a friend and cried, “What shall I do?” I did not answer that letter. Two months later newspapers announced the Comtesse Octave had returned on an English vessel, restored to her family after contrived adventures. When I moved to Genoa I received formal notice of the birth of a son to the Count and Countess; I held the letter for two hours on the terrace bench. Two months later, urged by Octave and friends and broken by my uncle’s death, I agreed to marry.Six months after the Revolution of July I received a final letter concluding the story. The Countess wrote that she was dying though already a mother—perhaps because she was a mother. She had played the part of wife well; she had deceived her husband; she had had happiness as genuine as an actress’s tears. She was dying for society, family, and marriage as early Christians died for God. She did not know of what she was dying; she tried to find out but could not. She was not perverse; she suffered from the malady of memory. She could not twice feel that love which identifies a woman with a man. To the last moment she cried in the confessional and to her husband, “Have mercy!” but there was none. She died with stupendous courage, wearing a cheerful mask while the invisible rival—her dead child—came every day to claim her. She was rent and smiled; two children existed, but the elder, the dead one, would triumph.She told me she had said this before: the dead child calls her. The intimacy of marriage without love degraded her soul every hour. She could only weep or dream when alone. Social duties and care of her child left no moment to renew herself as she could in solitude. The constant need for watchfulness starved her heart. She lacked tender lips to cool her burning eyes; her soul acted a part and so she died. The doctors, who discovered her secret, assigned a plausible complaint as cause. Octave believed in her apparent adoration; she feared he might follow her. She begged me to be her little Count’s guardian; she had placed a codicil expressing her wish and told me not to produce it unless needed. She asked me not to mourn; she had been dead these many days if Saint Bernard was right that where there is no love there is no life.The Consul closed the reading by locking his pocket-book. Guests asked about the Count. The Consul-General had seen a white-haired man, an old man of forty-five, pass through Genoa in search of health and amusement; the man had placed his will in the Consul’s hands and appointed him guardian of his son. He suspected the truth of Honorine’s death and that suspicion was killing him. At the steamboat Octave had entrusted his will and looked down at the Mediterranean, pondering why human nature sacrifices exquisite beings to fugitive pleasures. He confessed he was consumed by remorse. In the Rue Payenne he had been dying of joys not had; now in Italy he would die of joys he had had. He wondered where the discord lay between two equally noble natures.A profound silence followed. The Consul then asked the two women present, “Was she virtuous?” Mademoiselle des Touches turned the question on men: are they not equally wrong when they take a young girl to wife while cherishing an angelic image and then compare us to unknown rivals? If marriage were based on passion such criticism might be fair—but such marriages ruin both sexes. Others recalled such passions devastating men and driving women to nunneries or suicide. Camille Maupin and others agreed that such exceptional souls find peace only in solitude or religious life. As the guests dispersed along the seashore, they lingered on the rarity of Honorine: perhaps the most extraordinary intellect, a pearl whose tragedy was that life alternates pain and pleasure and that to ask Dante’s Paradise of life is to demand a luxury nature resists. Those great souls still existed, they agreed, though to live by such intensity often meant sacrifice beyond the world’s comprehension.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ########################################################################## MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################“HULLO! There is that old Box-coat again!” cried the lawyer’s clerk Simonnin, leaning from a window and flinging a breadcrumb at a stranger crossing Maître Derville’s courtyard.“Come, Simonnin, don’t play tricks… However poor a client may be, he is still a man!” Boucard, the head clerk, scolded, bent over a bill. Simonnin, a saucy thirteen- or fourteen-year-old, and the other clerks, including Godeschal, mocked the shabby visitor as “old Box-coat”; Godeschal suggested a prank, saying Derville took clients only at odd hours.The office, cluttered, dim, smelling of food and musty papers, buzzed with laughter. The weary stranger asked for Maître Derville but was brusquely told to return at one in the morning. When he insisted he was Colonel Chabert, reported dead at Eylau, they treated it as a joke. Boucard finally silenced the room, his sternness contrasting with their irreverent gaiety.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At about one in the morning Colonel Chabert knocked at Maître Derville’s door. The porter said Derville had not returned, but the head clerk—preparing papers—explained Derville preferred to work at night and that the Colonel was only the third late caller.When Derville arrived he was struck by the man’s thin, pale appearance and scars suggesting past trauma. The stranger claimed to be the Colonel presumed killed at Eylau; though Derville and the clerk suspected madness, Chabert insisted on telling his story.He recounted Eylau: gravely wounded and buried among the corpses, he had miraculously survived because his horse’s body shielded him. Rescued by a woman, taken to a hospital, and after months of recovery he found he had lost everything—especially proof of identity. Without documents his attempts to reclaim his life met disbelief and ridicule; he wandered a beggar and spent two years confined in a mental institution. Upon release his wife, the Countess Ferraud, had remarried and borne children.Sensing the gravity, Derville offered to help: to obtain necessary documents from Germany, gave a small sum, and provided a letter to a notary. Overwhelmed with gratitude, Chabert voiced a longing for vengeance and a refusal of obscurity; the meeting restored him a fragile hope.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################About three months after the interview, in Derville’s room, the notary Crottat—who had advanced half-pay to Derville’s eccentric client—demanded the six hundred francs be refunded.“Are you amusing yourself with pensioning the old army?” Crottat laughed; his sarcastic tone revealed he was a young man who’d recently taken over after his chief fled in bankruptcy. Derville shrugged, joking his philanthropy would not exceed twenty-five louis.Noticing a letter on his desk, Derville had his clerk Boucard translate. It was from Berlin: the requested documents would arrive, they were legally stamped, witnesses to the recorded facts still lived in Eylau, and the woman who had saved Comte Chabert was alive in Heilsberg. “This looks like business,” Derville said, and questioned Crottat about Chabert’s estate and the rogue Roguin.“Let’s call him the unfortunate Roguin,” Crottat scoffed, then recalled the papers he’d copied as third clerk. Rose Chapotel, wife of Hyacinthe Chabert, had married without a settlement, so property had been common. The personalty had been about six hundred thousand francs. Before his marriage Colonel Chabert had made a will leaving one-quarter to the hospitals and another quarter to the State; the will was contested, forcing a sale that left the widow with the portion originally bequeathed to the treasury. Roguin later absconded with large sums—reports mentioned eight hundred thousand francs—and the net available to Chabert could be no more than three hundred thousand francs. “You lawyers can be clear-headed despite accusations,” Crottat added.Derville then sought the Colonel. Chabert lodged in the Faubourg Saint-Marceau with Vergniaud, an old quartermaster-turned-dairyman. The coachman refused the unpaved street, and Derville found a dismal, ill-built house: trodden-earth floor, damp walls, a straw bed. Yet when Chabert appeared, smoking his pipe, he retained the military calm of a man unshaken by hardship.“Why did you not write to me?” Chabert asked. When Derville questioned his lodgings, Chabert replied that good folks had fed him for a year and that, having a little money, he could not leave them. Derville told him the Heilsberg papers were expected and that his savior was alive; Chabert, frustrated at the thought of money he did not yet possess, threw down his pipe.Derville explained the legal complexities: proving identity would demand long, costly proceedings, complicated by the bigamy question since Comtesse Ferraud had remarried. Opponents would insist on inquiries, translations, and foreign formalities—years and expense. Overwhelmed, Chabert despaired; Derville reassured him the outcome might be favorable and proposed a friendly suit to annul both the death certificate and the subsequent marriage. He offered to send a power of attorney and to provide financial help. Trusting Derville completely, Chabert agreed.As Derville left he met Louis Vergniaud, who asked for a loan to improve the Colonel’s lodging. Derville agreed but insisted Chabert should be the one to help his friends. He then planned to visit Comtesse Ferraud.Madame Ferraud, formerly Madame Chabert, had turned her share of the husband’s fortunes into substantial income; her second marriage to Ferraud, an ambitious man and son of a former councillor, was socially secure and brought her influence. At the Hotel Ferraud she was surrounded by luxury when Derville told her plainly that Colonel Chabert was alive. She laughed until his serious tone silenced her. He warned her of the legal and social danger if Chabert proved his identity and hinted Ferraud might not support her if his name and fortune were threatened. The Countess, shaken, asked whether Chabert still loved her and began to consider compromise. Derville left confident he had gained the upper hand.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A week after Derville’s visits, Colonel Chabert — restored in appearance and bearing, wearing his Legion of Honor ribbon — arrived at Derville’s office to meet his wife, Madame la Comtesse Ferraud. She appeared youthful and composed; sensing their tension, Derville separated them and proposed a memorandum: Chabert would formally acknowledge his identity in exchange for financial support. The Countess, alarmed at public exposure, resisted.When Chabert returned he confronted her, forcing the painful truths of their separation into the open. She feigned surprise and tried to leave; Derville pressed her to stay. She then took Chabert to her country house in Groslay, confessing that she loved another man, Comte Ferraud, and sought to avoid scandal. Though hurt, Chabert showed some understanding. She treated him kindly while privately scheming to secure her position, writing to Delbecq to quash his claim. Discovering this betrayal, Chabert ultimately sacrificed his identity to protect the Countess and their children, renounced reclaiming his name, and disappeared. Months later Derville found him a poverty-stricken vagabond in a courtroom; still dignified, he scorned society’s indifference and refused charity.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In June 1840 Godeschal accompanied Derville to Ris. Near Bicêtre they found an old pauper under an elm—shabbily clad with a carved, grotesque look. Derville recognized him as Comte Chabert, whom his pious wife, Comtesse Ferraud, had likely placed in the workhouse to bury their past.Two days later Chabert, drawing in the sand, refused his name and called himself “Hyacinthe,” an institutional number who even envied a condemned man. Godeschal gave him snuff; Chabert hailed them “brave troopers,” waved his stick and cried “Vive Napoleon!” A fellow pauper recalled Chabert’s impressing a Prussian officer with wartime recollections.Derville reflected on Chabert’s fall—from foundling and Napoleonic soldier to inmate—and on the priest, doctor, and lawyer confronting betrayal, greed, and moral decay. Godeschal admitted similar disillusionment and longed for a quiet country life. An addendum lists Balzac’s characters and cross-appearances.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Bianchon, a physician schooled in theoretical physiology, first trained in surgery under the celebrated Desplein. Desplein excelled diagnostically and operatively but left no lasting school; his declared atheism—strengthened by dissecting corpses without sensing a soul—shaped his outlook. Critics pointed to contradictions: eccentric habits (alternating lavish and indifferent dress) and a mercurial temperament that ranged from kindness to harshness, provoking mixed judgments. Horace Bianchon, a poor but loyal and cheerful student, won Desplein’s mentorship and entry into practice and Parisian society. Anecdotes—Desplein rushing to aid a destitute water‑carrier and secretly attending and founding a Mass for twenty years—reveal a skilled, skeptical, compassionate, paradoxical man.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Some time passed before Dr. Bianchon, despite their friendship, found an opening to ask about a pivotal episode in Desplein’s life. Meetings rarely allowed private talk, but seven years later, after the Revolution of 1830, Bianchon saw Desplein at Saint-Sulpice; they attended mass together.“Can you explain your sudden piety?” Bianchon asked. Desplein first deflected—outwardly religious but inwardly skeptical—and criticized politicians. Pressed about the mass he had founded, he spoke plainly: nearing life’s end, he could trace its beginnings. Pointing to a building in the Rue des Quatre-Vents where he had lived two desperate years, he recalled hunger, poverty, isolation, an irritable temper that drove away friendships, no money for books or medical training, and a solitary struggle to rise from destitution.He described meager meals, tireless work without sympathy, and how the well-to-do could not grasp his plight, asking why he had incurred debt as if it were voluntary. Early hardship taught him to expect selfishness and envy, and to see how mediocrity sought to undermine excellence.A turning point came at the depth of his poverty, when, having been stranded by a porter who demanded payment for a relative’s linen, he considered enlisting. His neighbor Bourgeat, a humble water-carrier, changed everything: though poor himself, Bourgeat paid Desplein’s debts, secured lodgings, devoted his savings and understanding to Desplein’s future, and supported him selflessly. Bourgeat was nurturing, proud of Desplein’s success yet seeking no recognition. When Desplein became house surgeon he left Bourgeat, who remained proud; later Desplein bought him a horse and a barrel, fulfilling a dream. Bourgeat, angry that Desplein had spent money on him, was nonetheless delighted and never claimed credit, though Desplein felt he owed him everything.When Bourgeat fell ill, Desplein cared for him until he died in his arms, leaving him all he had. A man of strong faith, Bourgeat’s wishes were honored with masses; the mass Desplein founded was a tribute to that faith. Desplein said he would give anything for such belief. Bianchon, who had witnessed Desplein’s final days, could not say whether the great surgeon died an atheist; he wondered whether Bourgeat might have opened Heaven’s gates for Desplein as he had opened the doors to the earthly temple they shared.PARIS, January 1836.ADDENDUMThe following characters appear in other stories of the Human Comedy: Bianchon, Horace; Father Goriot; César Birotteau; The Commission in Lunacy; Lost Illusions; A Distinguished Provincial at Paris; A Bachelor's Establishment; The Secrets of a Princess; The Government Clerks; Pierrette; A Study of Woman; Scenes from a Courtesan's Life; Honorine; The Seamy Side of History; The Magic Skin; A Second Home; A Prince of Bohemia; Letters of Two Brides; The Muse of the Department; The Imaginary Mistress; The Middle Classes; Cousin Betty; The Country Parson.In addition, M. Bianchon distrusted her; Rastignac, dazzled by her beauty and rank, believed she could raise his standing and settle his debts. Bianchon warned it was a mistake, likening her to a “one-eyed horse” and, as a physician, dismissed fashionable women as often insincere—a polished façade. Rastignac brushed this aside, insisting a woman of fashion could be an ally. Bianchon agreed to introduce him to his uncle, Judge Jean-Jules Popinot, though he doubted the rigid judge would help. Next morning Bianchon visited Popinot’s house in a decayed quarter—a building of faded grandeur. Popinot, unkempt and unambitious, was a supernumerary judge: devoted to justice, kindly and firm, able to elicit confessions without force, yet frustrated by the system. Bianchon reflected how hard it would be to persuade such an uncompromising man.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Twelve years earlier, during the 1816 famine, Popinot was named President of the Commission Extraordinary to distribute food in his neighborhood. A prominent lawyer and criminal judge, he had long recorded legal outcomes without probing causes; direct contact with the destitute awoke a deep compassion that gradually made him a protector of the poor, likened to a modern Saint Vincent de Paul.The change was incremental: one act of charity led to another until Popinot became central to local benevolence—preventing crime, finding work, and offering discreet assistance while living largely unknown to Parisians. He converted a storeroom into a modest parlor with benches and chairs, kept meticulous records of donations and requests, and relied on his aide Lavienne to manage pawn tickets and visit the worst districts. The parlor filled from dawn with women, children, and the needy; its occupants, even criminals, respected the household. Popinot spent mornings tending the poor, afternoons on legal practice, and evenings on judicial duties, which honed his ability to see hidden virtues and detect wrongdoing. His modest inheritance, his late wife’s savings, and a restrained judge’s salary forced frugality, especially given his charity; despite his service he lacked the Legion of Honor.His routine was disrupted when the President of the Second Chamber, at the Marquise d’Espard’s request, sent Popinot to examine the Marquis d’Espard for a Lunacy Commission. Bianchon visited Popinot’s Rue du Fouarre parlor—Popinot in a threadbare nightcap, ledger in hand, Lavienne organizing petitions—and watched him interrupt an urgent matter to help a woman sustain her grocery while her husband was jailed for debt. By midmorning Popinot had aided over two hundred people and the room was emptied. His home—dust, scattered papers, his late wife’s belongings, tokens of gratitude—revealed a divided life. Bianchon urged him to accept the Marquise’s hospitality; though magistrates normally avoid such ties, Popinot agreed, considering the Marquis’s mental state and its family implications.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next evening at nine Bianchon climbed his uncle’s dusty staircase and found him bent over a complicated judgment. Because the tailor had not sent the coat Lavienne ordered, Popinot wore his old stained coat; Bianchon straightened his cravat and buttoned it, though the judge’s habitual movements soon revealed a gap between his waistcoat and trousers. He noticed the absurdity just as his uncle entered the Marquise d’Espard’s room.A brief account frames the interview. For seven years Madame d’Espard had been a Parisian fashion-figure. After separating from her husband in 1815, the thirty‑three‑year‑old Marquise—mother of two boys, fifteen and thirteen—kept her vogue by elaborate hair, cold baths, and strict diet; emotionally detached, she admitted neither love nor hatred and gladly parted from M. d’Espard when he took the children, whom she saw as obstacles.With an income of 26,000 francs she lived quietly at first but by 1820 re-emerged socially, hosting gatherings and supplanting other prominent women. She cultivated ties with duchesses and countesses and turned her drawing‑room into a discreet political hub. Admired by ambitious men, she was vain and instrumental in relationships—using intimacy as influence while avoiding compromise.Rastignac sought an alliance; Popinot’s visit confirmed the household’s opulence. He probed the Marquise about her separation; her crafted story and extravagant living clashed with claims of hardship, suggesting self‑serving motives. Warned that the court would scrutinize her, she tried to deflect. Afterwards Bianchon and Rastignac feared her influence while Popinot resolved to investigate, summoning Madame Jeanrenaud.Next day Madame Jeanrenaud arrived—stout, unrefined, candidly concerned for M. d’Espard. Her straightforwardness intrigued Popinot. As he traced D’Espard’s finances, he realized the case was more complex and would require careful, thorough examination.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Those who have matured know how trivial incidents can shape major events: Popinot’s minor illness delayed a crucial meeting when the judge, feeling unwell, stayed home instead of visiting the Marquis d’Espard, losing a day that echoed larger historic delays.Before following the lawyer and his clerk to the Marquis’s residence, consider the father’s home life, which his wife’s petition branded as mad. In old Paris some buildings still show permanence. The house on Rue de la Montagne-Sainte-Genevieve where M. d’Espard lived was such a stone structure: once grand, now weathered and partitioned, partly given over to a printing establishment. He occupied the ground floor—likely for his children because the garden was unusually large—and paid modest rent in a depressed market. Dating from Henry III–Louis XIII, the house still bore traces of former glory, including a perron and the garden.The Marquis furnished his rooms in rich browns, creating a quiet, harmonious atmosphere. Few visitors were admitted; the family life was secluded. The household comprised the Marquis, his two sons, a long-serving cook and a man-servant whose discipline kept order. To shield his children he avoided tenants, inviting gossip: neighbors called him mad and his servants untrustworthy, and his frugality and cash payments fueled rumors of ruin. Noble by birth, the family’s dignity clashed with egalitarian neighbors. The Marquis, about fifty, had a distinguished, somewhat cold air—eccentric in appearance and frugal in dress. His sons, Comte Clément de Négrepelisse and Vicomte Camille d’Espard, were handsome; the elder, nearly sixteen, favored fashion. The Marquis concentrated on broad education—mathematics, Eastern languages, and history—eschewing what he deemed unproductive studies.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The day Popinot planned to question M. d’Espard fell on a Thursday holiday. Before their father rose, the two brothers were already in the garden; the younger begged to visit the shooting-gallery and pestered Clément, who yielded. Their noisy quarrel, full of roughplay the Viscount encouraged, roused the Marquis’ laughter and drew neighbors’ jests that he seemed mad to let his children fight. When the boys were called, Clément hesitated but obtained permission from Camille.Through the house people whispered of the Marquis’ supposed madness. When Popinot arrived near noon with his clerk, the portress led him to the third floor and reported that the Marquis had made his children fight that morning and had laughed when the younger had bitten the elder until he bled; she added that the Marquis rarely showed himself. The muddy landing and grimy banisters lent the gossip an appearance of truth and made Popinot, though impartial, receptive.They entered an outer office where an old clerk sat amid shelves of publications; a grating separated a private room and a cash-desk revealed the enterprise’s commercial side. A shopman opened the inner room to show the Marquis: a venerable, simply dressed old man wearing the Cross of Saint-Louis, seated at a desk. Popinot explained he was a judge on the Inferior Court of Appeal for the Department of the Seine, charged with examining M. d’Espard in response to a petition by the Marquise for a Commission in Lunacy. The Marquis reddened and trembled, then steadied himself.Surprised to have had no notice, the Marquis listened as Popinot explained that the law presumes those subject to such a commission may lack reason and that the Court must verify the petitioner’s claims. Popinot urged him to answer fully and discreetly, especially about delicate accusations involving Madame Jeanrenaud—widow of a bargemaster—or her son Colonel Jeanrenaud, for whom the Marquis was accused of having dissipated his fortune and used influence at Court. The Marquis feared that explaining would wound his family’s honor, but Popinot promised confidentiality and common courtesy if a misunderstanding were found. Freed from reserve, the Marquis spoke with clarity and passion.He began with his marriage, which had brought heavy expenses forcing him to borrow. He could not afford a steward; most title-deeds had been taken to Paris by his father, who feared scrutiny. The family name was Negrepelisse; the title d’Espard came through marriage in Henri IV’s time. An ancestor had lost the town of Negrepelisse in the religious wars and been ruined by property destruction; later relative regained favor and fortune but a stain of blood and disgrace remained. The Marquis had long been determined to erase that stain.He discovered his grandfather had obtained the Negrepelisse lands by means tied to the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes: a Protestant merchant named Jeanrenaud was arrested and hanged, and the land passed to his ancestor by iniquitous law. The merchant’s family had the rightful claim. Unable to endure the disgrace, the Marquis sought the heirs. He eventually found M. Jeanrenaud living in poverty, reduced to a lieutenant under Napoleon. Convinced their rights were incontestable and they lacked the means to sue, he offered to pay the lands’ value at the time they were taken—eleven hundred thousand francs—payable at his convenience and without interest.To effect restitution he lived frugally for years. When he suggested to Madame d’Espard that they move to the country and live on half her income, she treated him as mad; her coldness convinced him she would have condoned his ancestors’ crimes. Appalled, he resolved to leave her in control of her fortune once debts were settled and to take charge of educating their children himself, determined to raise them as gentlemen. He invested prudently, repaid debts sooner than expected, and had recently completed the eleven hundred thousand francs payment, making restitution without damaging his children’s prospects. These were the reasons for the payments to Madame Jeanrenaud and her son.When Popinot asked if Madame d’Espard knew his motives, the Marquis answered yes. Popinot, moved, excused his clerk and inquired about other allegations—particularly that the Marquis was engaged in a business unbecoming his rank. The Marquis summoned the Comte de Nouvion, explaining that he used the rooms as offices for publishing the Picturesque History of China by a reputable firm, not as a private residence. He showed Popinot his ground-floor apartment: a simple, touching harmony that convinced the judge a madman would hardly create such beauty. The pleasant weather and garden further belied lunacy.The Marquis outlined his plan: remain until his younger son finished studies, then travel through Europe with the children to perfect their languages and manners. His China project, with Comte de Nouvion as partner and the Abbé Grozier—Keeper of the Books at the Arsenal—as his tutor, would be an honest literary enterprise. They had two thousand five hundred subscribers—twelve hundred in France—the work would cost about three hundred francs per set, and he hoped the venture would yield a hundred thousand francs to fund his children’s education and pleasures; the Comte might earn six to seven thousand francs a year. Twelve years of retirement had cost him his standing at Court, but he believed he had purified his family’s past and secured a noble future for his children.At that moment the two boys burst in, robust and sunburnt from riding. Their greeting and obvious devotion confirmed Popinot’s impression of a devoted father. When Clément admitted that their mother avoided speaking to them publicly, a cloud passed over the Marquis’ brow. Popinot felt deep sympathy: “If anyone is mad,” he thought, “it can only be the children who are a little crazy about their father, and the father who is very crazy about his children—but that kind of madness rings true.”Madame Jeanrenaud then appeared, bypassing the manservant’s protests. Firmly and courteously bowing, she declared she had come too late because the criminal judge was there; she and her son were of one mind to return everything—their honor demanded it. “They must be as stupid as pans without handles to call you a lunatic,” she added. The boys clung to their father, alarmed.Popinot intervened: legally the payments were due but had been prompted by the Marquis’ scrupulous honesty; the law did not require descendants to repay confiscations centuries old and a Commission in Lunacy is for those who act without reason. Here the remittances sprang from the highest motives; she could keep the money without remorse and let the world misinterpret a noble action. Popinot declared he would rather see the Marquis crowned than subjected to a Commission. Madame Jeanrenaud applauded, and the Marquis and judge exchanged a humane, private understanding. Popinot advised the Marquis to counteract any influence his wife might be under and then left, impressed by the scene.The next morning Popinot, after writing his report, went to the Palais de Justice. As he prepared to don his gown, the President called him privately and, invoking propriety—“Caesar’s wife must not be suspected”—asked Popinot to step aside from the case as a personal favor, citing the Keeper of the Seals’ suggestion to avoid any appearance of conflict. In recompense, the President would secure the Cross of the Legion of Honor for him. M. Camusot, a newly appointed judge, was named in Popinot’s place. Popinot, ironic but acquiescent, bowed and withdrew.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY repeated and framed by hash-line separators.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Dedicated to Lieutenant-Colonel Periollas, as a testimony of the author's esteem.Have you felt unease and sadness in the crowded galleries since the revolution of 1830? Balzac contends the true Salon has been buried under an excess of artists. Where a strict selection once showcased the finest works and provoked debate—making reputations like Delacroix and Ingres—exhibitions now overwhelm viewers. Once limited displays have become floods: individual works lose impact, shows close quickly, and the Salon’s role as arbiter of taste has degenerated into a tumultuous bazaar. Great artists are obscured by mediocrity, and talk of unrecognized genius grows when the Academy’s discerning judgment wanes.Amid this swelling catalogue stands Pierre Grassou, nicknamed “Fougeres.” In 1832 he lives in a cramped rue de Navarin apartment with a small studio near Montmartre. Tidy and careful, Grassou is a genre painter who avoids historical subjects, producing easel paintings suited to his modest means.One December morning the dealer Elie Magus, who habitually bought Grassou’s works cheaply, proposes a commission: portraits of the bourgeois Vervelle family, eager to enter the art world. Magus hints the daughter’s dowry—and even marriage—could secure Grassou’s future. Startled, Grassou nonetheless sees a chance to establish himself.Balzac traces his past: trained under Servin and Schinner, mocked for mediocrity, rejected from the 1819 Salon, advised to quit, yet he persisted, selling a refined piece to Magus for little. Grassou’s tale embodies many contemporaries’ struggle: ambition constrained by limited talent, hopeful striving, and the market’s power over artistic merit.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For days Pierre Fougeres waited near Elie Magus’s shop hoping his painting would attract attention; when it disappeared after a week Elie said he had sold it and would buy more if Fougeres produced Flemish interiors, anatomy lessons, and landscapes. Overjoyed, Fougeres felt validated and missed the ironic skepticism a more cynical artist might have noticed.He ran into his friend Joseph Bridau but, absorbed in painting, could not enjoy the evening. At home he sketched, bought supplies, hired models, and after two months produced four canvases. Schinner and Bridau called them derivative; Fougeres retouched them and sold each to Elie for twenty-five francs, barely breaking even. He noticed a strange mist over the paintings in the shop but dismissed it.Over seven years he developed competent, unremarkable work that paid modestly. At the 1829 Exhibition, a prison scene inspired by Gerard Dou drew attention—even from Charles X—and Madame bought it and commissioned more. His success rested more on imitation than innovation, yet his modesty, perseverance, punctuality, and steady output (about two hundred paintings by thirty-seven) won bourgeois buyers and supporters.After the July Revolution he lived frugally, managed money with notary Cardot, and aspired to the Institute. A commission from the wealthy Vervelle family introduced him to their daughter Virginie; the family admired his Legion of Honor cross and began to see him as a suitable match, blending personal and professional hopes.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When Fougeres began painting Mademoiselle Virginie, Grassou had become part of the Vervelle household, frequenting his neat studio. Near the sitting’s end Joseph Bridau burst in, disheveled, begged five hundred francs for creditors, and criticized Grassou’s style; Madame Vervelle feared the portrait ruined, but Grassou reassured her.He accepted an invitation to the Vervelles’ Ville d’Avray country house, where lavish hospitality and fawning praise treated him like a celebrated artist. In their gallery Vervelle boasted a three hundred thousand franc collection—many canvases Grassou recognized as his, long sold to dealer Elie Magus. He hinted he’d sold them for much less; Vervelle offered to double his daughter’s dowry if he proved it. The revelation raised Grassou’s standing; he later finished and gifted their portraits.Grassou now earns about twenty thousand francs, aided by his wife’s six-thousand-franc dowry, charges five hundred francs per portrait, and holds the Legion of Honor. Yet he remains insecure, craving Academy recognition and secretly swapping weaker works for famous ones; his mediocrity, softened by generosity, makes him oddly sympathetic.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ######################################################################## MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ############# MIDDLE OF STORY. ############################################################################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In some provincial towns a cloistered melancholy hangs over certain houses; life seems frozen except for a solitary figure at a window. Such an air brooded over a house in Saumur, down a steep, little-used street of pebble pavement and three-century-old buildings. These wooden houses, prized by antiquarians, show great carved oaken beams, slate façades, worn window-sills with neglected flower-pots, and heavy studded doors bearing Protestant marks or forgotten bourgeois insignia—so that the history of France seems recorded in wood and stone.Beside a dilapidated dwelling stands a country gentleman’s mansion; its stone arch faintly shows armorial bearings eroded by the revolutions since 1789. Ground floors are not modern shops but plain dark rooms where goods lie exposed and a girl knitting will call her parents to serve customers. The local climate governs commerce: merchants, wine-growers, and coopers watch the weather anxiously; on Saturdays vineyards call their owners away, streets fall silent, and neighbors observe one another.The most melancholy mansion belonged to Monsieur Grandet. He began life as a master cooper in 1789, married a rich wood merchant’s daughter, and with savings and her dowry bought prime vineyard land at the sale of church property. He became protector of local nobility and supplier to Republican armies, invested shrewdly in vineyards and estates, and served as mayor in 1806. That year he inherited three fortunes, adding vast vineyards, farms, and an abbey, making him among the region’s wealthiest men.Townspeople speculated about his riches; his knack for timing sales made him admired and feared. By 1816 his fortune was estimated near four million francs, perhaps as much again in cash. Frugality ruled him—he spent only on necessities. Plain, stout, round-faced, and stuttering in long conversation, he concealed calculation and kept his wife dependent. Few entered his house; among them the young magistrate C. de Bonfons, who hoped to win Grandet’s daughter Eugénie. The town’s social life centered on competition for her, chiefly between the Cruchots and the des Grassins. In early 1811 the Cruchots gained advantage: when Froidfond was sold they negotiated its purchase for Grandet, who paid in gold—an ostentatious display that confirmed his wealth and influence throughout Saumur.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“The house of Monsieur Grandet” is a cold, silent dwelling above the town, sheltered by ruined ramparts. The tufa entrance, with weathered pillars and an arch, looks jail-like; above it a crumbling bas-relief of the four seasons is topped by a plinth where plants, even a small cherry tree, have taken root. The solid oak door, split and bolted with iron, has a small rusted grating that opens onto a dark vault and a garden enclosed by damp rampart walls.The principal ground-floor room serves as antechamber, salon, office, boudoir, and dining room. Paneled wood, exposed beams, two street-facing windows, a white stone chimney crowned by an old brass clock and a greenish mirror, and faded tapestry chairs create a modest comfort. Closets and buffets stand in the corners; a card table with a barometer and two pastel portraits of Madame Grandet’s ancestors sits between the windows. Red gros de Tours curtains—rare luxury—contradict Monsieur Grandet’s frugality. A straw chair lets Madame Grandet watch passersby; a work table and Eugenie’s armchair occupy the rest.For fifteen years the household has lived by strict economy: fires allowed only on set dates, the foot-warmer reused through cold months. La Grande Nanon, servant for thirty-five years, earned merely sixty francs a year but managed an annuity. Unattractive yet indispensable, she ran the domestic affairs with tireless loyalty; Grandet’s rare favors were treated as monumental largesse. During the hard 1811 harvest his relation to Nanon combined dependence and domination; she remained devoted out of gratitude and necessity.Nanon’s kitchen contrasted with the bare upper floors—immaculately clean though she slept in a tiny closet. On a November evening in 1819, when she lit the season’s first fire for Eugenie’s birthday, local notables arrived. Grandet maintained the ritual of giving Eugenie a gold coin while Madame Grandet presented a new dress. Guests, notably Madame des Grassins and her son Adolphe, exchanged gifts and compliments that masked rivalry, each subtly angling for a share of Grandet’s wealth. He watched with possessive pride until a loud knock abruptly interrupted the evening.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Nanon lit a candle and opened the door as Monsieur Grandet followed. His wife called anxiously; the players hesitated, and Monsieur des Grassins suggested leaving. A young man arrived with a porter and trunks. Grandet brusquely sent his wife back to the game and stepped out to speak with the newcomer.The players stayed but did not resume. Madame des Grassins asked if he came from Saumur; her husband said Paris. The notary checked the hour; Abbé Cruchot inquired if the gentleman was young. Eugénie noticed Nanon’s absence; others speculated the newcomer might be kin. Madame Grandet urged them to continue, aware of her husband’s irritation.Grandet returned alone; the traveler entered to polite bows. Madame Grandet offered warmth, but Grandet, absorbed in a letter, showed little interest. The stranger, Charles Grandet, thanked Eugénie and, at twenty-two, displayed Parisian polish: a hunting costume, several waistcoats, a gold toilet set, and—despite instructions to travel modestly—a private coupé to flaunt his means.His elegance contrasted with the provincials. The shabby Cruchots were taken aback; locals responded with mixed admiration and disdain. Eugénie, enchanted, longed for the world he represented and seized the chance to prepare his room, using her birthday as an excuse to persuade Nanon to add small refinements.Madame des Grassins and Abbé Cruchot vied for Charles’s attention, revealing local ambitions. Grandet, having read a letter about his brother’s impending bankruptcy and charged to protect the son, masked his emotion and offered Charles a modest room. As guests left, the Cruchots and des Grassins resolved to unite against any attachment between Eugénie and her cousin and to manipulate the situation to their advantage.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When the four relatives were left alone, Monsieur Grandet told his nephew Charles it was too late for business and set the household schedule: breakfast at eight, light lunch at noon, dinner at five. He professed poverty despite appearances, insisting his fortune came from hard work. He told Nanon to bring candles for Charles; Charles at first refused but accepted one so worn it looked like tallow.Grandet led him through a shabby house, avoiding the cold stairwell. Charles’s expectations dwindled: the staircase and decor were rundown, and he wondered why his father had sent him here. On the landing he noticed a walled-up door to Grandet’s office — the secret room where Grandet did business and hoarded wealth. Across from it sat Eugenie; after a brief goodnight she and her mother retired. Grandet showed Charles to his attic room, warning him to call Nanon because the dog could be dangerous. Nanon brought a warming pan, which Grandet dismissed.Alone, Charles surveyed the surroundings and questioned his father’s intentions. Eugenie, meanwhile, rose with a new sense of longing. She admired herself but felt inadequate beside Charles; the garden and courtyard now charmed her and she yearned to impress him.She asked Nanon to prepare cream for Charles’s breakfast, but Nanon said they needed Grandet’s permission. He quarrelled with Nanon over supplies, revealing his stinginess, and grudgingly gave flour and butter for a galette. Later in the fields Grandet met Cruchot, boasted about land and poplar sales, and when told gossip that Charles might court Eugenie he exploded: he’d sooner throw her into the Loire than let her marry her cousin, shattering Eugenie’s hopes.Back home news arrived of Grandet’s brother’s suicide, a disaster for Charles. Breakfast was heavy with grief; Eugenie wept while Grandet remained indifferent. Risking his anger, Eugenie and Nanon conspired to give Charles a better breakfast, and her affection for him deepened.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After two hours’ work Eugenie prepared a simple breakfast, breaking the family custom of a standing midday meal. She set the table and placed an armchair for her cousin Charles, anxious about her father’s reaction; Madame Grandet promised to take responsibility if he returned.Charles arrived in Parisian fashion, cheerful despite a hard journey. He rarely ate before midday but welcomed the meal and joked about wanting a partridge. His charm captivated Eugenie; she admired his elegance and felt a flutter of affection. Their conversation showed the contrast between his luxury and her modest upbringing. When Nanon brought eggs, Charles praised Eugenie and showed her his ring; their fingers briefly touched. The mood shifted over coffee when Charles called it outdated, and the women grew anxious at the thought of Monsieur Grandet’s return.Grandet entered suspiciously, noticed the breakfast and especially the sugar, and demanded its source. Charles asked for sugar for his coffee; Grandet rebuffed him. Then Grandet revealed devastating news: Charles’s father had killed himself, leaving him bankrupt. Charles broke down; Eugenie and her mother felt deep compassion while Grandet focused coldly on the financial fallout, insisting Charles go to the West Indies. Eugenie offered help; Grandet agreed to fund the journey. Alone with her feelings, Eugenie resolved to share Charles’s sorrow—her life irrevocably changed.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################About four o’clock a sharp knock startled Madame Grandet. Grandet burst in, rubbing his hands. “Wife,” he announced, “I’ve trapped them all! Our wine is sold! The Dutch and the Belgians have gone. I made a deal for my vintage at two hundred francs a puncheon, half down. He paid me in gold; here are six louis for you. In three months, wines will have fallen.”News of the sale spread through Saumur. Eugenie, excited, asked if he had a thousand puncheons. When he confirmed, she calculated the sum and pleaded, “Then, father, you can easily help Charles.” The name of his neglected nephew stung Grandet. He scolded Eugenie for presuming, insisted any duty to Charles would be discharged on his terms, and threatened to send her to the Abbaye des Noyers with Nanon if she meddled.Madame Grandet reported that Charles had not come down because he was weeping for his father. Grandet, momentarily bewildered, retreated to his secret room to consider investing the proceeds. His thinning forest had already brought six hundred thousand francs; with this sale his total reached nine hundred thousand. Tempted by visions of twenty percent returns, he settled on a more modest eight, imagining that in two years he might withdraw a fortune in gold.Nanon’s knock summoned him to dinner. He agreed to the eight percent plan, murmuring that hunger would bring his nephew from grief. At table he learned Charles refused to eat; Nanon approved—“So much saved.” When Madame Grandet suggested mourning, Grandet mocked the expense: “What will you invent next to spend money on? Mourning is in the heart, not in clothes,” and told her to buy her mourning out of her six louis; a hat-band would suffice. Eugenie sewed in silence while he ran through calculations. The town buzzed about the sale; Nanon’s spinning and Grandet’s repeated maxim “Nothing should be wasted” filled the house as he lost himself in arithmetic and plans.That night Grandet, preoccupied with present gain and dismissive of any future life, revealed how money shaped his laws, politics, and morals, bargaining with conscience and religion and finally growling, “The devil take your good God!” He visited Charles’s dark room to berate him gently in his own miserly fashion: “Well, nephew, you are in trouble… I am a good uncle to you. Come, take courage! Will you have a little glass of wine?” He scoffed at a wax candle’s theft. Madame Grandet and Eugenie, listening from the landing, fled in fear.Alone afterward Madame Grandet prayed for him. Eugenie, meanwhile, lay restless; her concern for Charles swelled into a personal disturbance. In the early hours she rose, found him asleep in an armchair exhausted by grief, and, touched by his quiet, watched him with a feeling she scarcely understood. He woke, apologized; she counseled him to go to bed and fled, ashamed and secretly happy—an innocent boldness that marked a turning point.Next morning Grandet arrived distracted but outwardly affectionate. Though he seemed softened, he had spent the night calculating schemes to enlarge his fortune and dominate others; that day he contrived to restore his brother’s honor without disbursing a franc, by manipulating public opinion in Paris. In his absence Eugenie found tender pleasure in caring for Charles. She prepared his breakfast with self-forgetting devotion and brought it to his room to spare him the shame of eating before her. The warmth and attention of the provincial household—so different from the indifference Charles had known in Paris—moved him. Eugenie’s innocence and genuine affection drew him closer; they shared an unspoken understanding.Life went on around this quiet intimacy: Grandet continued his calculations, Nanon spun, and later Grandet invited the Cruchots to dinner. Yet beneath the household routine, Eugenie’s tenderness and Charles’s gratitude suggested changes that money alone could not govern, and hinted at the complex obligations that would shape their lives.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Saumur showed more interest in the dinner Grandet gave the Cruchots than in the previous night’s sale of his vintage, which had offended the wine‑growers; Grandet, who prided himself on outwitting others, cared little for their judgment.When the des Grassins learned of Guillaume Grandet’s failure and death, they came that evening to offer sympathy and to learn why he had invited the Cruchots. At five, Monsieur C. de Bonfons and his uncle the notary arrived in their Sunday best. The meal was sombre: Grandet grave, Charles silent, Eugenie mute, Madame Grandet barely speaking. Afterward Charles went off to write a letter.Once the women left, Grandet revealed the cunning that earned him the nickname “the old dog.” Affecting a practiced stutter, he controlled and frustrated his guests while steering talk toward bankruptcy. The president of the commercial court explained that a respected merchant facing insolvency might have a receiver appointed and that liquidation could differ from outright failure; sometimes affairs could be settled without the courts—if the merchant did not file and no creditor appeared, family or heirs could liquidate. Debts could also be bought at a discount so that, if all titles were acquired, heirs would owe nothing. The notary urged this plan to save Grandet’s name.Grandet, mindful of reputation and his nephew’s prospects, feigned ignorance of Parisian tricks. The president offered to go to Paris to negotiate and suggested Grandet provide some assets. The des Grassins then declared they would honorably liquidate the brother’s debts; their banker and Madame des Grassins volunteered to handle Paris. Favoring the banker, Grandet confided a separate plan to buy government bonds and asked for discretion; they agreed to meet the next day.As word spread of his supposed generosity, the town forgave the vintage sale. Behind closed doors Grandet ordered Nanon to keep silent and secretly set out, in the night, to sell gold in Angers. Eugenie, worried for Charles, thought she heard a cry but was distracted by seeing her father and Nanon carry a heavy keg; Grandet told Nanon to tell his wife he had gone to the country and hurried away.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“My father has gone,” Eugenie thought as she listened from the top of the stairs. The house lay hushed; the carriage rumbled away and a cry from her cousin’s room pierced the silence. A thin thread of light from the doorway lit the old staircase.“He suffers!” she cried, running up. Charles sat in an armchair, head bowed, a pen fallen from his hand; his breathing was labored. On the table were sealed letters to tradesmen—signs he was settling affairs to leave France—and two open letters. The greeting “My dear Annette” struck her to the core. Rooted in place, shame and curiosity wrestled; curiosity prevailed. She adjusted his head, kissed his hair, and read.Charles’s letter to Annette explained that misfortune had ruined him: his father had killed himself, their fortune was gone, and he was penniless. He could not remain in Paris, where society would scorn a ruined man. He would not ask Annette to give up comforts to keep him; better to part forever. He planned to seek fortune in the Indies or America—perhaps as a sailor—and might return aged and altered; he urged Annette to keep the memory of their happiness yet accepted that he could not demand fidelity, for marriage and new ties might become necessities. The letter ended abruptly, as if his hand had left the page.Eugenie excused the coldness of the words, reading passion into them; “My dear Annette” became sweetness to her ear. She mourned his misfortune and thought, “I have gold; I will give it to him.”The second letter, to his friend Alphonse, asked Alphonse to settle his affairs: sell possessions, collect what could be recovered, keep his horse Briton if possible, press builders to delay delivering an unpaid carriage, and pay off debts, including six louis lost at cards. It showed a practical, calculating mind, intent on avoiding dishonor and salvaging what remained.Overcome with compassion and admiration, Eugenie ran to her room and opened the old oak cabinet where she’d hidden a red velvet purse—a grandmother’s relic. Inside were coins: foreign gold pieces, rare rupees—about five thousand eight hundred francs. Joy and resolve filled her. Secret love for her cousin and pity dissolved propriety. She returned upstairs at once, candle and purse in hand.Charles woke, stunned. Eugenie, trembling, confessed she had read the letters and then offered him her savings without hesitation.
