She called herself a poor girl who wanted nothing, a sister helping a cousin. Kneeling, she implored him to accept her gold as proof that he respected her and was generous. Tears fell from Charles onto her hands; moved, she poured the coins onto the table. “You will be rich,” she cried; “this gold will bring you happiness; someday you shall return it to me—are we not partners?”He could not refuse. Rising, he kissed her and revealed he would reciprocate not with money but with an object he prized: a leather dressing-case containing in a hidden drawer two portraits by Madame Mirbel, set with pearls—his mother and father, who were Eugenie’s aunt and uncle. He declared the case more precious than life and begged Eugenie to be its guardian. If she guarded it, and in the worst event his fortune vanished, the pearls and gold in the case would repay her; yet he demanded that it ultimately be destroyed to prevent its passing into other hands. This exchange of trust marked a turning point; Eugenie looked at him with her first deep, womanly affection. He kissed her hand, calling her an “angel of purity,” and vowed that feeling and sentiment must be everything between them henceforth.They exchanged gentle words and good-nights. Eugenie, blushingly practical in her joy, suggested Charles keep the treasures in her old cabinet. He sighed in his mourning but kissed her hand; as she closed the door he murmured the ruin of his fortunes. “What matter? My father is rich; I think so,” she answered, naively. He scoffed; she spoke of family estates, and he dismissed them as poor farms. The exchange was sweet and foolish: beneath his black, Charles began to wear roses.Next morning, before breakfast, Madame Grandet found Eugenie and Charles together in the garden. He remained sad; the three women of the house, respectful of his grief, allowed him the morning while they tended chores and awaited tradesmen. Around five, Monsieur Grandet returned from Angers, unexpectedly jubilant, having made fourteen thousand francs on an exchange of gold. He had left his groom at Angers to look after the exhausted horses.“I am hungry,” he proclaimed. Nanon scolded him for not eating. Shortly Des Grassins arrived to take Grandet’s orders. Grandet barely registered his nephew’s presence, absorbed instead in his financial success and in baffling others. Des Grassins, the banker, told him gold fetched high prices in Angers and spoke of sending theirs; Grandet curtly refused, hinting at giving his old friend respite from wasted exertion. A whispered consultation ensued; they glanced often at Charles. Des Grassins offered to go to Paris and asked Charles if he had commissions. Charles declined stiffly; Des Grassins rebuked him for the curt reply, reminding him of family ties. Charles, pale, kissed his uncle and withdrew. Eugenie watched her father with secret admiration for his mastery over affairs.Grandet dismissed Des Grassins with a jocular “decoy those people; lead them by the nose,” and resumed his armchair, asking Nanon for black-current ratafia. His excitement made him fidget and sing snatches of a ditty; the women exchanged uneasy glances at his towering contentment. He spoke philosophically about money: “You have no sooner put your lips to a glass than it is empty! Such is life.” His cheerfulness came with small generosity and an undertone of calculation that made him formidable.The evening passed under a mood blending domestic routine, latent anxieties about affairs, and the quiet bloom of a new intimacy between Eugenie and Charles sealed by her sacrifice and his trust. Thus the house resumed its hum: tradesmen, chores, and the unspoken commerce of feelings and fortunes. Eugenie’s selfless devotion and Grandet’s thrifty triumph—passion and calculation—had begun to intertwine, shaping the family’s future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next morning the family breakfasted in a private, intimate mood. Grief had drawn Madame Grandet, Eugenie, and Charles together; even Nanon felt sympathy. The old man, absorbed in thrift and intent on sending Charles away to avoid expense, remained aloof, busying himself with farm work while Madame Grandet watched over Eugenie and Charles as their relationship quietly deepened.For Eugenie, love grew from their shared secret into a simple, childlike tenderness: soft glances, gentle words, and small consolations. She delighted in soothing Charles; he, hardened by the world, found in her a pure, sincere attachment and cherished their modest life. While Grandet worked in the fields, Charles savored the company and virtues of Eugenie and her mother. Eugenie surrendered to their love without foreseeing the looming separation.Three days after the des Grassins left, Grandet took Charles to the civil court to renounce his claim to his father’s estate. Charles signed powers of attorney, obtained a passport, and sold his belongings. Grandet bought many of these items—valued at nearly a thousand francs—and insisted on paying for Charles’s passage to the Indies. Charles gave his mother’s thimble to Madame Grandet, who was deeply moved.They wore mourning for a Requiem Mass for Guillaume Grandet. Charles shared letters from Paris and assured Eugenie in the garden that his affairs were in order and his effects would be sent to Nantes, urging her not to tie her future to his uncertain fate; still they kissed. On the day of departure Grandet packed Charles’s baggage, and the family saw him off. After he left, Eugenie kept his room unchanged, plotted with Nanon, followed his voyage on a map, and confided her constant love only to Nanon.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Two months later, Eugénie and her mother were consumed by the secret linking them: Charles filled their thoughts, and Eugénie even searched her aunt’s portrait for his features. One Sunday Madame Grandet discovered the truth about the gold Eugénie had given Charles, and panic ensued.“What will you say to your father on New Year’s Day when he asks to see your gold?” Madame cried. They spent a morning in dread, missed high Mass, and feared the coming confrontation. Madame, ill-prepared for winter and terrified of her husband’s wrath, suggested consulting Monsieur des Grassins in Paris, but Eugénie said it was too late.On the cold morning of January 1, 1820, Grandet—unexpectedly cheerful after receiving a large sum from investments—boasted at breakfast, then demanded the return of the gold. Eugénie said she no longer had it; he accused her of deceit and threatened confinement on bread and water. She defended her independence and stood firm in her love for Charles. Nanon, fearing for her, vowed support and that evening secretly brought a hare pâté, deepening their bond against Grandet’s harshness.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For months the old wine-grower tended his ailing wife while never mentioning their daughter Eugénie, who remained confined to her room by his orders. Madame Grandet’s health declined as her husband grew harsher and even began to err in business. Townspeople learned Eugénie was given only bread and water, with Nanon secretly bringing extra food; the community condemned Grandet, and Eugénie’s gentle attendance at Mass drew sympathy.Eugénie’s chief sorrow was her mother’s deterioration, which she often blamed herself. Madame tried to comfort her and urged reconciliation, begging her husband to restore their daughter, but he stayed indifferent as she weakened.In spring Madame confided in the Cruchots, who were appalled and discussed legal consequences; Eugénie overheard but, fearing scandal, begged silence and resolved to obey her father. A visiting notary warned Grandet that Madame’s death would make Eugénie’s inheritance a threat to his control, and he began to soften. Returning to reconcile, he found them admiring a gold dressing-case from Charles; after Eugénie threatened self-harm he relented, let her keep the case, scattered coins, and promised kindness. Madame died peacefully in October 1822, urging Eugénie to seek happiness in heaven and fearing for her in a selfish world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The day after her father’s death Eugenie felt newly attached to her childhood home, where memories of suffering—especially her mother’s death—moved her to tears. Her father’s sudden tenderness before he died—taking her to breakfast and regarding her kindly—made the household suspect his mind was failing. At the mourning dinner Maitre Cruchot revealed Grandet’s plan: to avoid dividing property or paying taxes on supposed funds, he needed Eugenie to renounce her rights so he could retain control. Confused, she asked for the deed; Grandet, visibly moved, instead urged her to trust him and accept a monthly allowance of a hundred francs. She agreed and signed away her inheritance the next morning. A year later he had not paid; when reminded he awkwardly offered jewels.Over the next two years Grandet trained Eugenie to run the household and manage finances, instilling his frugality. By the third year she was mistress of the house and a monotonous life continued for five years. Her sadness was obvious though silent; her only companions were the Cruchots and their whist-playing circle.In 1827 Grandet, eighty-two, suffered a stroke. Eugenie devoted herself to his care until his uneventful death, during which his fixation on hidden wealth persisted—his last words urged her to look after his riches. Afterwards, with only her loyal servant Nanon, she learned from Maitre Cruchot that she had inherited seventeen million francs. Despite the fortune, Eugenie’s thoughts turned to finding her cousin Charles. She secured an annuity for Nanon, who married Antoine Cornoiller and became housekeeper; the estate and farmers remained unchanged. At thirty Eugenie had known little joy: a childhood marked by her mother’s suffering and an unfulfilled love for Charles, absent for seven years. Mourning and others’ matchmaking—Cruchots, Bonfons, Madame des Grassins and the Marquis de Froidfond—met her indifference, which was misread as interest.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles prospered in the Indies. His early ventures brought six thousand dollars, and he learned that human trafficking was the most profitable trade. He went to Africa, bought enslaved people, combined that traffic with other lucrative goods, and aimed to return to Paris wealthy and restore his social standing.Experience hardened him. He lost his moral compass, came to believe one society’s crimes might be another’s virtues, and became ruthless—engaging in exploitative trades, customs fraud, and buying pirate-captured goods to resell. Memories of Eugénie faded; he grew contemptuous of his family and treated Eugénie like a creditor. To conceal himself he used the alias “Shepherd.” By 1827 he returned to Bordeaux on the Marie Caroline with gold-dust worth nearly two million francs, intending further profit at the Paris mint.On the ship he met Monsieur d’Aubrion, a court-connected gentleman whose family had fallen on hard times. Madame d’Aubrion sought a wealthy husband for her plain daughter and promised Charles help obtaining a title and income through marriage—an alliance to elevate both families. Charles, intoxicated by ambition and urged on by Annette, entertained the match.When Des Grassins asked him to settle his father’s debts, Charles refused. In August Eugénie received Charles’s trembling letter: he recounted his success, announced the pragmatic engagement, and declared conjugal love a delusion. Devastated, she recalled her mother’s warning and resigned herself to a life of prayer and acceptance.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Eugenie returned from the garden uneasy, avoiding the corridor haunted by memories of her cousin. That day the parish priest—aligned with the Cruchots and Monsieur de Bonfons—was urged to speak to her about marriage. Unhappy, Eugenie confessed she wished to withdraw from the world and live only for God; the priest cautioned against abandoning societal duties, especially to the poor.Madame des Grassins, driven by vengeance, revealed that Charles Grandet had come back from the Indies and was engaged to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion, producing a letter that outlined Charles’s situation and his father’s bankruptcy. Eugenie, stunned, returned the letter unread. Tormented, she asked the priest whether remaining a virgin after marriage would be sinful, then secluded herself in her father’s secret room. In a charged evening she asked Monsieur de Bonfons to stay—taken as a pledge.Privately, Eugenie told the president she could offer only friendship and asked him to settle her uncle’s debts for her hand. He agreed, went to Paris, paid creditors, and informed Charles—whose initial excitement turned to shock when he learned of Eugenie’s fortune. The president married Eugenie; she lived simply, devoted to charity. Widowed soon after, she remained pious and independent, supported by servants Nanon and Cornoiller, her life illustrating the tension between wealth, duty, and personal fulfillment.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################To Mademoiselle Anna Hanska: the author addresses a young girl tenderly, noting the incongruity of dedicating a melancholy tale to one who brings household joy, yet insists sorrow must sometimes be spoken of, perhaps to prepare her gentle hands for future need.On an October morning in 1827 a sixteen-year-old boy in plain clothes stood in a small square of Lower Provins, noting working mills and modest bourgeois houses. He found the white stone house he sought—recently restored, with gray blinds and neat balconies—the neatness of a retired petty shopkeeper. As sunlight lit a calico curtain, the boy broke into a Breton ditty that recalled Brittany’s bittersweet turns. Pierrette, a frail girl in a garret, answered him with a signal; he revealed a yellow broom-flower. He was Brigaut, a childhood companion who spoke of settling nearby. Pierrette, alarmed, urged him away when a grotesque old maid below—given to sharp observations and scandalous reading of harmless scenes—appeared.Alone, Pierrette reflected on her precarious past: her mother, born a Demoiselle Auffray, came from a tangled family and a father who remarried, leaving a confused inheritance; her mother later married Major Lorrain, who died in battle and left them poor. The Lorrains could not care for Pierrette, and she was sent to live with her uncle and aunt, the Rogrons, in Provins. Unaware of the full intricacies and consequences of her lineage, Pierrette’s fate is increasingly shaped by these relatives, setting the stage for ensuing drama.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Père Rogron, an indulgent, ruddy, short, fat innkeeper in Provins, and his unattractive, selfish wife lived brutishly on modest earnings. After selling the inn they moved into a dilapidated house inherited from old Auffray, Pierrette’s grandmother. Their children, Sylvie and Jérôme-Denis, were neglected, ill-bred, and coarse-voiced; Rogron sent them to Paris under a doctrine of harsh, minimal support.Sylvie apprenticed on rue Saint-Denis and rose to forewoman before becoming independent; Jérôme became a head-clerk. They pooled savings to buy a retail shop, the “Family Sister.” Though modestly successful, fierce competition and their severity made them notorious for stinginess and harsh treatment, causing high clerk turnover. Sylvie, shrewd but plain, displayed a pleasing front to customers and a sour private persona. While mourning their father they received news of cousin Pierrette’s inheritance; intent on preserving their wealth, they refused to take her in, delayed answering, and later left for Provins four years before Brigaut’s arrival.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A petty shopkeeper returns from Paris; his Parisian airs quickly fade. The Rogrons use new wealth to pursue bourgeois status—remodeling their house to match the wealthy and dabbling in politics. Their renovations advertise ambition as they vie for the town’s elite. Madame Tiphaine advances her husband by hosting gatherings; the Rogrons lack polish—Sylvie learns card games while Rogron remains awkward. Lavish dinners fail; invitations stop and ridicule spreads. Sylvie’s faith that money buys status collapses; she grows frugal, and they sink into ennui and resentment for their old trade. Bringing cousin Pierrette worsens their neglect and ostracism. Bitter, they court the lower bourgeoisie, exploited by opportunists like Colonel Gouraud and lawyer Vinet. The story shows provincial hierarchies and social codes trump wealth in determining influence.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After her grandparents were hospitalized, Pierrette—ashamed to live on charity—felt relieved when wealthy relatives were found. Childhood friend Brigaut, the son of her mother’s friend, gave her sixty francs for the trip to Paris; she accepted without thanks, showing their closeness. He often visited and ran after the coach in tears. Penniless in Paris, a conductor briefly paid for her hotel; a kinder one took her to Mademoiselle Rogron’s, where her cousins received her with astonishment.She arrived in Breton dress with a small bundle; Sylvie and Rogron were startled. Pierrette, shy and overwhelmed, spoke little. Sylvie and the maid Adèle showed her a modest room that felt luxurious, then immediately fretted over her lack of clothes and resolved to buy a wardrobe.In Brittany she had known affection and freedom; with the Rogrons she found coldness, criticism, and obsession with appearances. Reproved for dirty shoes and treated as a burden, she suppressed joy and grew passive. Sylvie forbade visits and pursued social ambitions with Vinet and the colonel. As her cousins plotted for status, Pierrette was confined and transformed from a lively child into a shadow, enduring harsh, self‑interested care rather than love.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At the start of a new phase with her wealthy cousins, Pierrette sought a goodnight kiss but Sylvie turned away coldly, making her cry. When asked why, Pierrette said her rich cousin was not as kind as her poor grandmother; Sylvie retorted the grandmother had taken money while the cousin would leave her some—Pierrette wanted affection, not wealth.Sylvie then accused Pierrette of mischief—breaking a watch, disobeying orders—and Pierrette left ashamed. Rogron demanded punishment, Madame Vinet urged schooling, and the colonel thought instruction might quiet her. A writing master was hired; ink stains and scattered materials were blamed on Pierrette and she was harshly told to earn her keep. Rogron’s severity and Sylvie’s indifference deepened her isolation; curiosity stifled, she grew ill and her complaints were dismissed.Only Adèle secretly showed kindness. Plans to escape to grandparents in Brittany ended after a death and Rogron’s appointment as guardian; he secured a promise of eight thousand francs from Pierrette’s grandmother. Preparations for her first communion brought Monsieur Habert, whose fervent instruction gave Pierrette solace and led her to abandon escape plans and embrace faith. The Rogrons refused a convent school; later, introductions to the Chargeboeufs and Bathilde’s interest in Rogron prompted Sylvie to make Pierrette a servant. Without Adèle’s protection, Pierrette faded from a vibrant child into a silent shadow while her cousins remained oblivious.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Before recounting the domestic drama Jacques Brigaut's arrival would incite, it is necessary to explain how he came to Provins, since he plays a significant yet passive role.Brigaut fled the Rogron house, shaken by Pierrette’s alarming decline from lively girl to one consumed by illness and sorrow. From the Upper town he watched her home, torn by concern and his own feelings. Hoping to better himself for her, he briefly went to Paris to learn a trade, returned after a fortnight, and found work as a journeyman cabinetmaker so he could stay near Pierrette. He suppressed his feelings while trying to learn more about the Rogrons; Madame Frappier disclosed their dubious acquisition of Pierrette’s grandfather’s property. He often saw the frail Pierrette at the market and near the church, where she signaled him to remain hidden, fearful of some danger.Pierrette fretted over changes since Brigaut’s return but did not perceive the Rogrons’ internal strife—chiefly between Sylvie and Mademoiselle Habert (Céleste), rivals for advantageous marriages. Sylvie, forty-two, feared aging alone; Céleste, about ten years younger and urged on by her priest brother, actively sought a husband. A political shift encouraged local Liberals and sharpened social competition. Sylvie, alarmed, consulted a doctor whose discouraging prognosis deepened her anxiety; Céleste saw opportunity and urged delay to eliminate a rival.Vinet, a lawyer eyeing Bathilde’s advantage, manipulated the colonel into courting Pierrette while secretly scheming to divert the Rogron fortune. The colonel’s attentions inflamed Sylvie, who—after overhearing Brigaut serenade Pierrette—mistakenly suspected the colonel, accused Pierrette, and threatened her when she remained silent. Under heavy labor, emotional strain, and relentless scrutiny, Pierrette’s health waned. Brigaut offered brief comfort, but love, jealousy, and ambition became entangled, setting the stage for the tragic events to come.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For a month Rogron had stopped bringing the Constitutionnel to Gouraud, who now called himself and lingered to gossip and walk with Sylvie. Eager to see the colonel, Sylvie dressed her best; when Rogron excused himself, she was left alone with Gouraud.Noting her solemnity, he asked after her cousin’s health. Gouraud was uneasy: Vinet’s rising influence over the Rogrons threatened to sideline him. As a half-pay officer he feared Vinet’s control of their plans, Rogron’s infatuation with Bathilde de Chargeboeuf, and even suspected Vinet might be steering Rogron toward Pierrette to benefit Vinet and Bathilde.When Sylvie tentatively mentioned Pierrette, Gouraud denied any attachment, preferring a more mature wife. Relieved, Sylvie concealed her aims and, sensing her interest, Gouraud declared he would marry her. She began planning but remained wary of Pierrette.At dinner Sylvie chastised Pierrette for a stain yet dressed her nicely, suspecting collusion. Bathilde arrived resplendent; guided by Vinet she treated marriage to Rogron as a stepping stone. Tensions rose—Bathilde’s condescension and Vinet’s remarks sharpened rivalries. At cards Pierrette’s innocent remarks drove Sylvie to anger; Pierrette left in tears. That night Sylvie brooded on men’s duplicity. The next day Pierrette’s painful fall met Sylvie’s blame, revealing a widening rift.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The week ended in torment for Pierrette under Sylvie’s escalating cruelty. Brigaut, whose arrival Sylvie hid, sustained Pierrette with memory and arranged secret letters: one morning he slipped a note while pretending to buy fruit and asked her to lower a string from her window so they could exchange notes nightly.Pierrette received his ardent plea to escape and, despite difficulty obtaining writing materials without waking Sylvie, replied at midnight. Their correspondence brimmed with hope and plans, which only increased Sylvie’s jealousy. Under closer watch Pierrette’s health declined; she fainted one evening, alarming the colonel and others while Sylvie dismissed it as a ruse. When Pierrette cried for help and Sylvie tried to seize the letters, a violent struggle ensued and Pierrette was hurt, including an injured hand.At Sylvie’s height of fury, Madame Lorrain—urged by Brigaut’s urgent appeals to come to Provins—arrived, found Pierrette traumatized, sought medical care, and vowed to secure her safety. As Pierrette recovered, her bond with her grandmother and hope for a life with Brigaut renewed her determination to reclaim freedom and love.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At nine o’clock Monsieur Martener told Monsieur Tiphaine about Pierrette’s mistreatment by the Rogrons. Tiphaine brought in Auffray, a notary and relative; longstanding enmities between the Vinet and Tiphaine factions—and damaging truths about Madame Tiphaine’s family—gave the dispute political charge. Seeking to weaken the Rogrons, influential royalist opponents, Tiphaine consulted the public prosecutor. He, Auffray, and Martener arranged to petition the courts to make Auffray Pierrette’s guardian so a Family Council, backed by medical testimony, could remove her from the Rogrons’ control.By midday the town buzzed after neighbors heard Pierrette’s cries. Doctors Bianchon and Brigaut examined her and confirmed abuse. Vinet, the Rogrons’ lawyer, warned of criminal exposure; Sylvie, initially evasive, later admitted jealousy and the truth. Vinet proposed a marriage announcement to quiet the scandal; under pressure the Rogrons signed a contract that served their political interests. He then filed a petition accusing Brigaut and the widow Lorrain of abducting Pierrette, recasting his clients as victims.The hearing was postponed and interest grew. Pierrette, cared for by her grandmother and Brigaut, was condemned by the physicians. When the court reconvened, Auffray’s medical report led the judge to bar the Rogrons from her until the Family Council met. Relatives voted to remove Rogron as guardian and appoint Auffray; a civil suit for neglect and abuse followed.Pierrette’s health declined. Martener and the Auffray family grew fond of her; a March 1828 operation offered little hope. She forgave her cousins and soon died. Her crowded funeral saw the Rogrons absent; Brigaut made her coffin. Though community support impeded the Rogrons and Vinet suffered backlash, they largely retained political standing. Only a few—Martener, Brigaut, and like‑minded friends—remembered the reality of Pierrette’s suffering. The account closes on questions of justice, accountability, and her lasting impact.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Dedication: To David, Sculptor—Balzac thanks sculpture’s permanence compared with other fame.In autumn 1826 Abbé Birotteau, short, corpulent, about sixty and subject to gout, was caught in a rainstorm returning from a friend’s and hurried across the deserted Cloister behind Saint‑Gatien cathedral. He hated wet shoes but was in high spirits after winning at cards with Madame de Listomère and ambitious for a long‑desired canonry, so absorbed that he neglected his umbrella.The Cloister, now the narrow Rue de la Psalette, held one house owned by the devout old maid Mademoiselle Gamard, who kept it through the Restoration by boarding priests. She took only two lodgers: for twelve years Abbé Troubert and the late Abbé Chapeloud. When Chapeloud died, Birotteau—who had long admired the rooms—succeeded him. Chapeloud’s death left him both bereaved and secretly relieved: he inherited the tasteful library and furniture he had envied. Becoming Gamard’s boarder and securing the canonry seemed to promise the comforts and status he desired. Settled in, he enjoyed these attentions, but his obsession with preferment returned.One rainy evening he found the door locked; his servant Marianne explained Gamard had assumed he was already inside. Small omissions—no slippers, no fire—pricked his vanity and felt like disrespect. Simple and lacking worldly tact, he failed to see these lapses as deliberate. Chapeloud had handled Gamard’s temperament with shrewdness; Birotteau’s guilelessness made him an easy target. Over eighteen months Gamard’s hopes to cultivate a salon were thwarted by his indifference and evenings out; she grew quietly vengeful, using coolness and petty neglects as the first signs. Absorbed by hopes of preferment, Birotteau confronts symptoms, not causes—mistaking personal slights for mere oversight. The contrast between the two priests shows how small domestic grievances, unacknowledged ambitions, and provincial social maneuvering escalate into a deeper conflict, a prologue to a fuller study of pride, naiveté, and intrigue.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next morning Birotteau woke absorbed in thoughts of a possible canonry, briefly putting last night’s ill omens aside. He rang for Marianne, who did not appear; before he could ring again Abbé Troubert entered after knocking. This monthly visit was expected. Troubert noted the absent fire, called for Marianne, and asked after Birotteau’s health and the canonry. Unaware Troubert resented Madame de Listomère for not inviting him, Birotteau described his efforts to obtain the post.The two abbés contrasted strikingly: Troubert was tall, thin, serious, with a wrinkled, sarcastic face; Birotteau plump, round-faced, and cheerful. Troubert’s calm exterior masked a dulled intellect some blamed on subservience to Mademoiselle Gamard; Birotteau’s joviality made him popular. Neither seemed likely to rise far—Troubert had smoothed his ambitions into piety and, at fifty, showed failing health that might still win vicar-general; Birotteau’s amiability kept him comfortable. Abbé Chapeloud quietly opposed Troubert, who nonetheless treated him respectfully.Troubert borrowed the Register of Bishops. While carrying the heavy books downstairs they met Mademoiselle Gamard, who sharply scolded Birotteau for cluttering her dining room. Breakfast was tense; Gamard complained of being disturbed, and Birotteau, oppressed by her bitter, abrupt manner, escaped to the garden and then to church. On returning he found he had missed dinner—Gamard had set the clock ahead to avoid waiting, a petty protection of her propriety.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Eight days after the affair began, Abbé Birotteau discovered that Mademoiselle Gamard’s hostility—visible since the candlestick-and-clock incident—was a six-month plot against him. Previously oblivious, he felt suffocated by her malice and, absorbed in minor worries, confided in no one.His life became torment. Anxiety damaged his health, so he turned to Abbé Troubert. Embarrassed to complain, he described Gamard’s provocations; Troubert listened with restrained amusement and advised indulgence, blaming her on ill health.Seeking respite, Birotteau stayed at Madame de Listomère’s country house, unaware Gamard could exploit his absence. After ten days, Gamard’s lawyer Caron reported she was alarmed by his leave and demanded clarification to avoid disputes. Back among friends, speculation grew: Listomère hinted Troubert coveted Birotteau’s apartment; an elder warned this was only the start; Mademoiselle Salomon said the vicar-general was ill and Troubert might be appointed. Friends urged Birotteau to yield if it aided advancement.Caron pressed him to sign away his residence; under pressure, he signed. He later learned he had surrendered goods Gamard claimed as compensation; she coldly produced the agreement. Crushed, he fled; friends intervened. Monsieur de Bourbonne found the deed exploitative and urged suit. After debate they agreed to support legal action, though Bourbonne warned many would desert a bitter fight. An appraisal exposed Gamard’s fraud, and as proceedings opened gossip and political divisions intensified against the powerful alliance of Gamard and Troubert.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A few days after the quarrel between Birotteau and the old maid, Baron de Listomere—expecting promotion to corvette captain—received a letter warning of possible retirement. Alarmed, he rushed to Paris; the naval minister dismissed his fears, but inquiries produced a document confirming the retirement, pending the director’s signature. Desperate, the baron consulted his deputy uncle, who explained that the minister believed the baron had sided with Radicals in Tours and offended the influential Abbé Troubert. His uncle urged reconciliation with Troubert as vital to the baron’s career.Fearing political fallout, the family followed Monsieur de Bourbonne’s counsel to appease Troubert and distance themselves from Birotteau. The baroness pressed Birotteau to withdraw his suit against Mademoiselle Gamard and leave her house; he agreed and asked for Chapeloud’s portrait. Gamard fell ill amid gossip blaming the suit. The baroness’s meeting with Troubert was a duel of insinuations; he later returned with Gamard’s refusal to surrender the portrait, which the baroness accepted.Gamard died, leaving her fortune to Troubert, who rose to Bishop of Troyes. Birotteau mourned alone, later exiled to a distant parish—his fate exposing the moral costs of ambition and clerical power.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################To Monsieur Charles Nodier, member of the French Academy.Dear Nodier, this book uncovers actions hidden behind domestic doors where supposed divine intervention often replaces human justice. Its moral is about family and motherhood and the harms that follow when paternal authority—the traditional tribunal for domestic wrongs—is weakened. A loving mother cannot take the place of patriarchal power. The story defends indissoluble marriage and warns against unchecked selfishness in a society that exalts wealth and success while the law is impotent. I hope such a society will return to Catholic moral guidance through a different education.In “Scenes from Military Life” I will portray noble self-sacrifice and expose war’s corrupting influence on those at home living as if on a battlefield. You have observed our times keenly; I entrust this tale to your name as a shield from criticism.Your sincere admirer,De Balzac  Paris, November 1842.THE TWO BROTHERSCHAPTER IIn 1792 the townspeople of Issoudun disliked Dr. Rouget; gossip blamed him for his beautiful wife’s unhappiness, though their household concealed its true nature. Madame Rouget, née Descoings, had been frail; she bore a son, Jean-Jacques, and ten years later a daughter, Agathe.Rouget’s conviction that men must display strength caused much female suffering. The Descoings—former wool merchants turned investors in forfeited estates—were rich and miserly. Descoings had gone to Paris, failed as a grocer, and been driven by love for a widow; by 1792 he seemed prosperous. Expecting his wife’s death, Rouget sent Agathe to Paris to secure Descoings’ favor and a share of the inheritance.Agathe, beautiful like her mother, provoked a quarrel between Rouget and their friend Lousteau, who left town. Madame Rouget, learning her daughter had gone, foretold she would never see Agathe again; her health then declined, worsened by her son’s neglect.Agathe’s arrival in Paris coincided with Descoings’ imprisonment for imprudent political remarks during the famine and his execution with the poet André Chénier—an event that struck the community. Agathe later married Bridau, a ministry secretary, with Rouget’s approval; Rouget hoped for profit. Bridau, devoted to Napoleon, rose by work and promotion but died of overwork in 1808 just as another advancement was imminent.After Bridau’s death Agathe received no letters from Issoudun. Her mother died shortly before the birth of a second son; her father died around the Emperor’s coronation; Jean-Jacques remained absent. Madame Hochon warned Agathe she would inherit nothing unless she granted power of attorney to her brother; Agathe hesitated. Bridau’s prudence left only a modest income, and by 1809 Madame Descoings—still fond of theater and fine food—had secretly gambled on lotteries and concealed debts. Devoted to her children but limited in judgment, Agathe sold possessions and liquidated investments to settle the debts—a decisive turning point in their lives.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################One of the bleakest stretches of Paris was rue Mazarin between rue Guenegard and rue de Seine, shaded by the high gray walls of the college and library given by Cardinal Mazarin. There, on the third floor of a damp, cold house, lived a poor widow, Madame Bridau (Agathe). Opposite were the Institute buildings where aspiring artists, the rapins, worked amid the tumult of examinations; from her windows Agathe could see their cages and the students’ uproar. To brighten her room she defied police regulations and set boxes of earth on the sill to make little gardens.The shallow house, reached by a winding staircase, held three rooms: a dining room, a small salon, and a bedroom, plus a kitchen and two single rooms. Agathe had chosen the flat because it was cheap—four hundred francs a year—near her sons’ school and in a neighborhood she knew. The interior matched the building’s dreariness: yellowed wallpaper, unglazed tiles, and only essential furniture. The salon contained an Aubusson carpet given to her and a mahogany sofa bearing a pastel portrait of her late husband, Bridau, which captured his noble essence; a vivid Napoleon hung above a card table.After Bridau’s death Agathe’s life narrowed. She filled the room with two large birdcages—canaries and Java sparrows—and her space was littered with millet, chickweed, and cat hair, signs of grief and neglected care. She preserved everything belonging to her husband, even wrapping his last pen with a note. Seized by sorrow, she seemed to lose her sense of womanhood and devoted herself entirely to her children.Agathe lived for her sons and worried ceaselessly over their finances. The Emperor provided three hundred francs a year for each child, scholarships supplemented this, and her aunt Madame Descoings assigned three thousand francs from her annuity to help; the Descoings themselves lived modestly on twelve hundred francs a year. Distrustful of the future, Agathe scrimped and saved—she set aside three thousand francs from her pension—hoping to secure some inheritance for her boys.The setting shaped her sons’ destinies. Philippe, the elder, resembled his mother and possessed a bold demeanor; enchanted by military life, he petitioned the Emperor, entered Saint-Cyr, and rose rapidly to be a captain by nineteen. Joseph, three years younger and more like his father in untidiness, took to drawing after seeing a caricature in the Tuileries. He sneaked into the Institute, won the students’ respect, and found encouragement in the sculptor Chaudet, who defended art as a calling despite Agathe’s fear of hardship. As politics shifted, Philippe returned home a decorated officer while Joseph, his scholarship withdrawn, sought to earn a living by art with Madame Descoings’ encouragement. Agathe’s preference for Philippe became more apparent, yet Joseph remained devoted; both pursued their callings under the constant love and anxiety of their mother.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In 1816 Joseph persuaded his mother to let him convert the adjoining garret into an atelier, partly financed by Madame Descoings. Unlike the two widows, who treated painting as a trade, Joseph embraced it passionately—installing a skylight and chocolate-colored walls and continuing studies under Gros and Schinner—while living frugally to support the family.Political unrest in France implicated Philippe: he was arrested, released for lack of evidence, and lost his military pay. Though friends urged exile, Agathe resisted his serving a foreign power; nonetheless Philippe joined General Lallemand in the United States to help found the ill-fated Champ d’Asile. Agathe equipped him with ten thousand francs; by late 1817 she had adjusted to reduced means. In 1818 the notary Roguin absconded with funds meant for Madame Descoings, and Philippe soon sent a bill for a thousand francs, having lost everything abroad. Agathe blamed herself, while Madame Descoings continued support.When Philippe returned he was hardened—self-centered, drinking and gambling, swaggering and neglectful. He refused Bourbon service, sought to impress Mariette, and lived off others. Gerard and Giroudeau proposed journalism; a cashier’s post at a newspaper, obtained through Agathe and Madame Descoings, brought only temporary relief. Joseph, increasingly overshadowed and unappreciated, watched family finances and relationships fray under the weight of Philippe’s choices and maternal indulgence.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The colonel, a voracious guest who never paid his way, continued to criticize the food while the widows—reluctant to press him—covered the household expenses. His mother and Madame Descoings hesitated to remind him of obligations; a year passed with no enforcement. “Well, he is happy,” his mother said; “he has a place.”Mariette, encouraged by Vernou’s feuilleton, made a sensational debut at the Porte-Saint-Martin alongside Begrand. A wealthy general, acquainted with Philippe and Giroudeau, became her impresario—a sign of the protective solidarity around fashionable folly. Bixiou soon told the household that Philippe, the cashier, was in love with Mariette, the rising ballet-dancer. The widows were appalled—Agathe’s piety condemned all stage women, and both feared the ruin such attachments bring. Bixiou tried to reassure them that Mariette might be taken by higher-ranking admirers.Philippe, partly motivated by financial hopes, proposed; Mariette refused, perhaps suspecting his motive or valuing her freedom, and aimed for the Grand Opera. Philippe began to spend his days at the office, the theatre, and with Mariette. After her Opera debut in January 1821 she attracted a distinguished duke. Passion drove Philippe to dip into the funds he managed—by May he had taken eleven hundred francs. Mariette left for London to seek patrons while the new opera house was readied; to her Philippe was a stepping stone.Compelled to give her up, Philippe returned to his mother’s apartment unable to imagine life without the pleasures he had tasted. Suicide crossed his mind—not merely because of the deficit but because he could not bear the loss of Mariette’s company. He went to Joseph’s studio and, pale and subdued, asked how long a portrait would take, saying he wanted to leave his mother a likeness. Pressed, he confessed he had taken money and could not live with the dishonor. Joseph urged repayment to save his honor; Philippe, despairing, fled. Joseph informed Madame Descoings and Agathe, who were distraught.That day Philippe calmly submitted accounts, inventing a story that he had retained the money for safekeeping, then went gambling—winning at first, then losing everything. Returning home, he accepted his mother’s pleading—“Promise not to kill yourself, and all shall be forgotten”—and feigned gratitude. The next day Agathe, shaken, entertained Desroches’s counsel: sign a transfer of funds and petition for Philippe’s reinstatement in the army to preserve honor. Desroches’s tact eased some fears, but Agathe’s faith was broken.Philippe resumed a life of leisure—gambling, drinking, growing careless and contemptuous toward women. Joseph, gaining recognition as a painter and receiving Madame Descoings’s hopes, began noticing financial discrepancies and suspected Philippe. He and Madame Descoings planned to catch him; when Joseph found that Philippe had indeed taken money from him, he felt betrayed. Despite Madame Descoings’s plea for sympathy, Joseph remained wary of Philippe’s corrosive influence on the family’s future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Despite careful nightly gambling, Philippe was sometimes “cleaned out” and began stealing from his family—pilfering from his brother Joseph and from Madame Descoings and Agathe. After a distressing dream in which he robbed her, Agathe pleaded that he ask for money instead of stealing. Within two weeks her savings were gone; she took worsted work for twenty sous a day, spent sleepless nights waiting for Philippe, and endured his drunkenness and mood swings.When Madame Descoings suggested new clothes, Philippe said his mother had no money, that he’d mortgaged his pension, and admitted borrowing from Giroudeau as a “debt of honor.” Agathe excused him while Joseph criticized his neglect. Philippe sank further into debauchery and gambling.Denied money by Joseph, Philippe guessed where Madame hid her savings, and, expecting her to be out buying Christmas treats, broke into her room and found twenty napoleons in the mattress. He gambled the sum, winning heavily at first—amassing over 150,000 francs by night—but then lost everything. The next morning he awoke in despair, having ruined his family.At Christmas dinner Philippe was absent; Madame, who had pinned hopes on a lottery stake, discovered her savings gone and fainted. The concierge confirmed Philippe had been in the apartment. Confronted, he claimed he’d taken what was owed him; a fight with Joseph ensued and Madame Descoings suffered a stroke. She later refused Joseph’s money, missed the lottery, and died. Philippe, indifferent and urged by Agathe to rejoin the army, accepted her reluctant help and left. Agathe, heartbroken, prayed and resigned herself to the loss of the son she once loved.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By February 1822 Madame Bridau had been relegated to the attic above the kitchen of her former apartment, recently occupied by her son Philippe. Seeing his mother reduced, Joseph refurbished the garret, furnished it better, and began taking Agathe daily to a table-d’hôte that cost nearly a hundred francs a month. Alarmed by the expense and urged by her confessor, Agathe accepted a seven-hundred-franc-a-year post at a lottery office that demanded long hours.She adjusted to the routine—preparing Joseph’s breakfasts, mending his clothes, keeping the studio tidy, and assisting his work despite no artistic training—and their bond deepened through these small cares. They often spoke of Philippe: Agathe feared his recklessness, while Joseph, convinced Philippe prospered in New York, dismissed her worries.Joseph visited Philippe at a newspaper; Philippe invited him to dine, but Joseph stayed with his mother. Agathe, hopeful, decorated the studio and prepared an elaborate breakfast for a sitting; she hid and watched the brothers together. Philippe later treated them to a sumptuous dinner and bragged of plans and bohemian friends, vague about money. He then mistook a valuable Rubens copy for an original and sold it cheaply—devastating Joseph and breaking Agathe’s heart.Two months later Philippe fell ill and destitute; Agathe at first refused help, then gave what she could. He was implicated in a conspiracy and arrested; Agathe fainted. Joseph consulted Desroches, who urged securing the family inheritance and collecting evidence of undue influence, planning a holiday visit to Issoudun. A discouraging letter from Agathe’s godmother about an unhelpful brother arrived; Agathe and Joseph set off for Issoudun, leaving Philippe to face the consequences and their estrangement unresolved.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Issoudun, one of France’s oldest cities noted by Caesar and Rigord, yielded in recent excavations by M. Armand Peremet a likely unique fifth‑century basilica built from stones of a Roman temple to Isis. Its name—Isis plus “dun,” a religious‑military site—signals ancient Celtic origins.In the Middle Ages Issoudun was powerful: Richard Coeur‑de‑lion built a tower above the basilica, and the town later sheltered Routiers and Cottereaux mercenaries used by Henry II against his son Richard. Its prominence faded. In the 1830 insurrection some eight thousand vine‑growers resisted government measures, burned tax offices, and asserted autonomy.In the fourteenth century the town held 16–17,000 people, prospering on wool and making blankets and gloves; by Louis XIV it enjoyed some elegance. Today it numbers about twelve thousand, including suburban vine‑growers, but the economy—especially wine—suffers from outdated practices: producers resist modernization, make poor wine, and squander potential prosperity. This stagnation shows in civic attitudes: local leaders opposed a proposed road linking Paris and Toulouse, fearing higher food prices and preferring tradition to progress.Socially, the loss of a competitive nobility and a complacent bourgeoisie left governance in the hands of interrelated families and a weak clergy. Disaffected youth formed the “Knights of Idleness,” led by the charismatic Maxence Gilet—rumored an official’s illegitimate son—who staged mischievous hoaxes from a tavern run by Père and La Cognette. Issoudun’s archaeological importance and past grandeur stand in sharp contrast to its modern decline; the Knights express the youth’s frustration and thirst for life.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Joseph and Madame Bridau traveled from Orléans to Issoudun, the Knights of Idleness staged a prank. An old Spaniard, a former prisoner of war, left his empty cart at the foot of the Issoudun tower. Maxence, arriving at the Knights’ rendezvous, proposed, “Here’s Père Fario’s cart. Let’s get it up on the embankment first, and then decide.” In an hour the cart was taken apart and hoisted piece by piece to the embankment like artillery; once reassembled, the Knights, hungry and thirsty, returned to Mère Cognette’s to await the Spaniard’s astonishment.Their amusements varied; they did not prank nightly but met for hunts, the autumn vintage, and winter skating. The twenty youths made Max their idol. François Hochon and Baruch Borniche, grandsons of Madame Hochon, treated Max as a cousin and depended on him for money and adventures their grandfather denied them.That night, after twelve to fifteen bottles of wine, François and Baruch sat beside Max at a dim table. Baruch warned, “You are threatened in your stronghold.”“My grandmother got a letter from Madame Bridau—her goddaughter—saying she and her son are coming here. She’s preparing two rooms for them,” he said.Max, thirty-four and soldierly, was outwardly confident but unsettled. Someone remarked that Rouget’s sister would secure the property when he died, threatening Max’s future. Max bristled at the nickname given to Flore Brazier, Rouget’s servant-mistress, and for the first time spoke openly of her. His friends knew to tread carefully. He explained his position plainly: he spent money and treated the others because of Flore’s influence and her hoped-for claim on Rouget’s estate.“You think I plan to marry Flore when Rouget dies, and that these Parisians threaten my future?” François asked. Max admitted the worry but asked for limited help—“within the limits of our fooleries”—not murder or poisoning. If the Bridaus succeeded and Flore had nothing, he would be satisfied; he loved her enough to prefer her to Mademoiselle Fichet, the richest heiress in Issoudun whom Goddet courted. Moved by his frankness, the Knights cheered their loyalty. Max toasted, half in jest, to each having a mistress like Flore and told them to be ready when Père Fario returned for his cart.At half-past three the Knights slipped away. Max returned to place Saint-Jean, masking his anxiety with the concealment learned in Cabrera. Rouget’s property yielded forty thousand francs a year; Rouget’s savings—accumulated over seventeen years and lodged with notaries—amounted to vast sums that could not be liquidated quietly in gossipy Issoudun. News of legitimate heirs unnerved him. “That fool of a girl never mentioned her sister and nephews in all these seven years!” he muttered. His priority: get rid of the relatives, then secure the property. Exhausted, he let himself in with a pass-key and went to bed, hoping clarity in the morning.The narrative then traces how Flore became known as the “Rabouilleuse” and the quasi-mistress of Jean-Jacques Rouget’s household. One September day in 1799 the doctor found a captivating little girl by a brook, tattered but spirited; she claimed to be from Vatan and to be catching crabs for her uncle Brazier. Struck by her looks, the doctor took her in, fed and clothed her, and at first encouraged study. Town gossip colored perceptions of impropriety, though for five years Rouget enjoyed educating her without scandal. Born in 1787 amid revolutionary upheaval, Flore grew pragmatic rather than traditionally virtuous. As Rouget neared death, his refusal—prompted by cynicism and wounded pride—to leave her anything set the stage for the inheritance struggle now threatening Max’s position and Flore’s hopes.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Jean-Jacques Rouget did not mourn his father’s death, though Flore Brazier did. The old doctor had made his son miserable after Jean-Jacques came of age, yet he had given Flore the material comforts valued in the country. After the funeral, when Fanchette asked Flore what would become of her, Jean-Jacques showed animation for the first time.At seventeen Flore retained the beauty that had drawn the doctor, though she was growing plump like other countrywomen. Jean-Jacques hesitated to speak; the memory of his father’s death and benefactions weighed on him, and Flore was puzzled by his silence. When the doctor died Jean-Jacques was thirty-seven: timid, submissive, and childlike. His torpor left him passionate but lacking confidence, awkward and unattractive, preferring solitude. Too late the father recognized his son’s temperament, wanted him to marry but feared losing control of property, and tried to instill avarice in him.Secretly in love with Flore, Jean-Jacques took pride in the instruction his father had given her, hoping it would make her easier to win. Flore, unaware, waited for him to declare himself, but he remained silent. After three weeks of uncertainty, a night when Flore found him crouched outside her door seemed proof of his love; their conversations, halting, revealed his growing affection. Ten months later he brightened, cared for his appearance, and townspeople speculated. Fanchette, the disapproving cook, left; Flore learned to cook and manage the house, surpassing her.Over nine years Flore gained control: familiar yet commanding, she became dominant while the childlike Jean-Jacques grew dependent. By 1815 she was at the height of her beauty; in 1816 she fell for Maxence Gilet, a penniless officer. Pressing Jean-Jacques about his wealth and empty rooms, she had Max invited. Jean-Jacques, fearing loss, consented. With Max in the house Jean-Jacques found conversation and companionship, while Flore shirked duties, hired staff, refined her appearance, bought Max a horse, and brought in a servant. The household shifted: Flore and Max became central and Jean-Jacques receded, increasingly dependent.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When Madame Bridau returned to Issoudun to claim her threatened inheritance, Jean-Jacques Rouget had become semi-vegetative. After Max’s financial aid, Flore raised Rouget’s standard of living, feeding him from Vedie’s kitchen; yet he grew weaker and utterly submissive, his devotion to Flore the only force in his life.Max’s apartment displayed his taste and devotion: provincial comforts, colored tiles, elegant papering and mahogany furniture. A rush matting muffled footsteps so Max could return late without waking anyone — not even Rouget, who remained ignorant of his nocturnal activities.One morning Flore discovered Max just returned and learned Rouget’s family were coming to Issoudun, likely to evict them. Flore vowed confrontation; Max urged caution. Before they could plan, Baruch announced that Fario’s cart had been stolen. Max led the crowd to the tower embankment where, to everyone’s surprise, the cart was on top. Amid laughter Max theatrically tossed it down, humiliating Fario and turning the episode into town gossip.That evening François warned Max the Bridaus would arrive. Max decided they should not appear hostile and proposed letting the Hochons handle driving them off. Flore, furious at the news, punished Rouget by withdrawing affection; Vedie urged him to placate her. Max advised Rouget to be polite while retaining his will, and he and Flore plotted to secure Rouget’s wealth with the Hochons’ help.The Hochons were modest and frugal; Madame Hochon would receive her goddaughter Agathe while granddaughter Adolphine eyed Joseph Bridau. News of the Bridaus split Issoudun: cautious bourgeoisie on one side, Max’s zealous Knights of Idleness on the other. The Fario incident and the town’s division showed how fragile reputations and power were, and how a few calculated acts could determine the coming inheritance struggle.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Agathe and Joseph arrived at the Messageries-Royales in Issoudun at three. Though tired, Madame Bridau felt revived by familiar sights. News spread fast; Madame Hochon, bereft for years, embraced Agathe like a daughter. Agathe met the cold Monsieur Hochon and the household: the young men Baruch and François, and shy Adolphine, who noticed Joseph. The stingy Hochon remarked on Joseph’s recent hospital stay and hunger; the youths were struck by his looks. Agathe proudly introduced him as “my son, the painter.” Baruch rushed off to spread word, stressing Joseph’s striking appearance.The town gossiped, likening Joseph to a brigand and sneering at artists; many planned to swarm the Hochons that evening. In modest attic rooms Joseph contrasted his Parisian artistic life with the provincial drabness. Dinner revealed the family’s frugality—plain bouilli, hard eggs and scant accompaniments—which Joseph, used to better fare, found disappointing but tried to accept. Agathe learned her childhood home had been sold to the Borniches; Madame Hochon braced for visitors.That evening the women gathered in the salon. Agathe and Madame Hochon exchanged life stories and hard-won counsel, repeatedly interrupted by callers eager to meet the Parisians. Joseph stayed aloof, studying visitors with a critical eye that made them uneasy; when the house quieted, the two women resumed their heartfelt talk.Elsewhere the Knights of Idleness plotted mischief: Max proposed using rats to sabotage merchant Fario and drive the Bridaus away—without harming the Hochons. Next morning at breakfast Joseph glimpsed his brother Jean-Jacques Rouget, dishevelled and accompanied by the conspicuous Flore Brazier. Joseph admired Flore; Agathe feared her influence. A letter from Jean-Jacques invited Joseph to dine; the Hochons, suspecting Flore, were uneasy. Gritte reported the Rouget household’s ostentation; Monsieur Hochon urged Joseph to observe and speak frankly about his nephews. With rival schemes and simmering tensions, a fraught family reunion loomed.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At four o’clock Joseph crossed from the Rouget to the Hochon house, where Kouski announced him. At the large table he exchanged light banter with Rouget, Flore, and Maxence Gilet, admired the house and Maxence’s looks, and suspected a triangular household. Baruch and Francois arrived; Flore suggested a pre-dinner walk. While she prepared, Joseph eagerly examined paintings Rouget said came from the Descoings, bought during the Revolution. Ecstatic, Joseph was shown more by Maxence upstairs. Flore urged Rouget to give the paintings to Joseph, arguing Rouget didn’t care for them.Rouget offered the pictures—without frames—and Flore proposed paying Joseph four thousand francs to make copies. Overwhelmed, Joseph accepted. Word spread through Issoudun and boosted Flore’s standing. Dinner was splendid; Joseph, somewhat tipsy, left at eleven. Rouget, heavily drunk, was carried to bed. Max and Flore, left alone, agreed it was better to befriend the Bridaus than antagonize them and planned to send the pictures to Joseph the next day.The next morning the paintings leaned against Rouget’s wall. Agathe, Joseph’s mother, visited to thank Rouget after Hochon warned her the Bridaus were influencing him and urged her to cultivate Flore. Agathe was warmly received; Rouget was ill. Flore feigned deference while hinting Rouget would remember her in his will.Meanwhile the local troublemakers, the Knights of Idleness, released rodents into Fario’s grain store, causing havoc. The Bridaus tightened their hold: Flore claimed Rouget was too weak to walk and bought a shabby carriage that drew attention. Rouget signed a power of attorney giving Max control of his investments, while Flore handled transactions. Fario suspected Max and began gathering evidence.Convinced the paintings were valuable, Joseph sent them to Desroches in Paris for appraisal. Agathe warned him to stay quiet, but the news leaked and searches for old artworks spread through town. Desroches and Schinner advised keeping Agathe in Issoudun, warned about Max, and suggested Rouget might already have a will—making the inheritance a struggle between Flore and the Church. Disturbed, Joseph decided to return to Paris. He bade Rouget farewell, resolved to return the pictures later and rely on his talent. The Hochon family suspected Max and Flore had manipulated Joseph; Agathe defended him, but Hochon warned that returning the paintings might jeopardize any chance of inheritance.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between midnight and one the Knights of Idleness fed the town’s dogs; their expedition lasted until three. After supper at Cognette’s, Max, turning from rue l’Avenier into Grande rue at half-past four, was stabbed by Fario, who had lurked in a recess, fled toward Vilatte, and wiped the knife on his handkerchief. Fario then washed the handkerchief in the Rivière forcée and returned to his lodgings at Saint-Paterne, where his new watchman later found him in bed.Max fell, crying out and alarming Lousteau-Prangin and young Goddet, who ran up the street calling for help; the townspeople, used to false alarms, stayed in bed. Max had fainted. He recognized Fario but, on regaining consciousness and seeing the wound was not fatal, decided to exploit the incident: “I think I recognized that cursed painter!”Lousteau-Prangin hurried to his father, the judge. Max was carried home by Cognette, Goddet, and others. Goddet, suspicious of Max’s words, asked Gritte if Joseph Bridau was in bed; she replied that Joseph had left at half-past four, restless all night. The revelation shocked the group, and they entered Rouget’s house to find Max in a bloody shirt.Joseph had spent the night anxious about how the townspeople would view him, fearing they might think he had robbed his uncle. He longed to flee to Paris and confront Max; at dawn he walked to the poplar avenue toward Tivoli to steady his nerves.Max plotted revenge. After Goddet examined the wound and found it serious but not fatal, news spread and a crowd gathered at the place Saint-Jean. Max whispered to Flore that they could use the incident to rid themselves of the Parisians: pretend he was dying and have Joseph arrested. Flore, feigning tears, spread the story that Joseph had attacked Max out of jealousy over paintings. Incited by her, the crowd demanded Joseph’s capture; the mob grew hostile.Joseph was spotted returning home and nearly seized by the mob. Gendarmes intervened and, despite his protests of innocence and his alibi, took him to the Palais for safety. Magistrates kept him in custody while verifying claims. Max then recalled the attacker had worn list shoes while Joseph had left in boots and wrote urging Joseph’s release. Witnesses confirmed Joseph’s alibi and he was freed. He returned to his mother’s relief; the ordeal strengthened their bond.The townspeople celebrated the departure of the Parisians; Max and Flore were pleased. Joseph and Agathe returned to Paris; Joseph’s spirits improved, while Agathe fretted about her son Philippe, whose punishment followed: five years’ surveillance and exile to Autun, later modified by Desroches to residence in Issoudun. Urged to reclaim his inheritance from Uncle Rouget, Philippe, resolved to restore the family fortunes and confront Max and the Rabouilleuse, prepared to return to Issoudun and departed after a brief dinner.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################On All-Souls’ Day, November 2, Philippe Bridau registered at the Issoudun police station and was told to stay on rue l’Avenier. The town was uneasy after news that a banished officer linked to the painter falsely accused in the affair had arrived. Maxence Gilet, recently recovered, had converted Père Rouget’s mortgages into cash, alarming many. After consulting notary Héron—who felt Rouget’s heirs should thank him for preventing Gilet’s profit—Monsieur Hochon decided to protect the Bridaus, long resentful of Gilet and afraid of losing property.Gilet and Flore Brazier welcomed Philippe, who arrived in ragged clothes but declared he would not beg from Rouget; his blunt pride impressed them and put Gilet on guard. Philippe visited the Hochons, candid about his poverty, and won some over with charm. He asked Hochon for secrecy; Hochon supplied names of officers hostile to Gilet—Mignonnet and Carpentier. Befriending them by revealing knowledge of the conspiracy against the Bourbons, Philippe integrated into the local military circle while hiding his true aims.He cultivated a respectable image—dining with officers, taking fencing and shooting—while probing Gilet’s plans. Fario told him about the Knights of Idleness; Philippe suspected Gilet and Flore intended to secure Rouget’s income and flee. He urged Rouget on walks to break Flore’s hold, alerted Hochon that Gilet sought power of attorney, and together they agreed to watch Rouget. Gilet proposed going to Vatan to secure the money and marry; Flore, wary of Philippe’s influence, reluctantly agreed. Tensions rose as Philippe’s determination to protect his family and restore their honor hardened, setting the stage for a town showdown.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Philippe walked arm in arm with his uncle Rouget along the boulevard Baron, he outlined a ruthless plan to settle the struggle over Rouget’s fortune: Colonel Bridau must confront Captain Maxence Gilet, and if necessary Philippe would remove Maxence to make Flore obedient again. Philippe told Rouget bluntly that Flore loved Maxence and wanted Rouget out of the way; he urged Rouget to refuse to sign the power of attorney demanded before December third, warning that document would allow the others to sell Rouget’s assets and flee to Paris. Rouget recoiled at Philippe’s proposal to take Maxence’s place; Philippe replied that fear and force were the only means to handle women.Philippe instructed Mignonnet and Carpentier to arrange banquet seating so he could force a public confrontation with Gilet, despite warnings about Gilet’s dueling skill. At home Rouget found Kouski in tears: Flore had left with Vedie, and Rouget’s search failed. Gilet, urged by Captain Potel to face Bridau, felt pressure and aimed first to secure Rouget’s fortune by transferring assets to Flore to block Philippe. Philippe planned to counter, using Baruch to secure the power of attorney for Rouget while distracting Gilet.Rouget began a threatening letter to revoke his will in Flore’s favor unless she returned; Philippe encouraged it. Town gossip swelled. When Flore returned, Philippe asserted control and warned her; Rouget clung to hope. By morning the town speculated on the duel, and Philippe, confident in his banquet plan, believed it would decide Rouget’s fate and his own.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Around four the next afternoon officers of the old army gathered at the place du Marché in Issoudun for a coronation banquet, waiting for Colonel Philippe Bridau. Conversation turned to Max’s recent dismissal from old Rouget’s house; only Potel and Renard defended him. Max arrived to a cold reception—contrasting with Philippe’s warm welcome—and resolved to confront him.At dinner Philippe toasted the Grand Army and others followed. Mignonnet toasted those who had tried to restore the Emperor’s son; Max, whose reputation carried weight, rose with an ironic wish to meet again next year. Tension mounted as Philippe and Max exchanged barbs; Philippe stayed composed while Max grew angrier. The duel was set for the next morning with sabres and Goddet, a former army surgeon, to attend.That night Monsieur and Madame Hochon urged Philippe to pray; he declined their relic. The next morning both men appeared composed, but Philippe’s superior stance intimidated Max. Max attacked, was quickly disarmed; Philippe paused to let him recover his weapon. They resumed, exchanged fierce blows; Philippe was wounded but delivered a fatal stroke. Max died, ending his ambitions and the Order of Idleness, much to the town’s relief.Flore Brazier, devastated, fell ill. Philippe, recovering, wrote plans to secure his inheritance and manage Flore, intending to use her. By February 1823, persuaded that marriage would secure her future, Flore accepted a match he had arranged, with a substantial dower and life annuity. Agathe rejoiced, but family lawyer Desroches warned Philippe’s cunning would block Flore and Joseph from Rouget’s estate. Once married, Philippe asserted control—demanding Rouget’s power of attorney, using Flore to run her uncle’s affairs while watching her—and, backed by local endorsements, was reinstated as lieutenant-colonel.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Lieutenant-Colonel Bridau (Philippe) returned to Paris with his aunt and the ailing Rouget, whom he took to the Treasury to transfer his uncle’s income to himself. The old man and the Rabouilleuse were soon absorbed into extravagant Parisian life; Rouget overindulged after a supper with the dancer Lolotte and died, said to be of indigestion.Madame Rouget prospered in Paris, watched by Mariette at Philippe’s behest. In October 1823 Philippe settled his uncle’s estate at Issoudun, amassing 1,600,000 francs (aside from valuable paintings held by Monsieur Hochon) and entrusting the money to Mongenod and Sons. He married, legally securing his wife’s fortune if she died childless, bought her a furnished apartment and secretly acquired a grand hôtel on rue de Clichy, lavishing money on renovations.The rise of Charles X helped the Duc de Chaulieu’s circle; Philippe supplied information about past conspiracies and was made lieutenant-colonel in the Duc de Maufrigneuse’s regiment and ennobled Comte de Brambourg. Ambitious, he sought further honors—aidede-camp to the Dauphin and a general’s rank—distancing himself from old friends and planning a marriage to Mademoiselle Amélie de Soulanges.Meanwhile Joseph Bridau, an impoverished artist supported by his mother Agathe, suffered criticism while Philippe flourished. Agathe, deceived by appearances, begged Philippe for help; his cold reply hastened her decline and death, buried by Joseph alone. Joseph found solace among friends; when Flore, Philippe’s wife, later fell into destitution, Joseph and Bixiou aided her and plotted to expose Philippe’s abandonment. Philippe’s fortunes collapsed in the July Revolution of 1830; Joseph ultimately inherited his estate, succeeded in art, became Comte de Brambourg, and remained humble, reflecting on the family’s tragedy.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY CONTINUES HERE — MIDDLE OF THE STORY.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################DEDICATIONTo Madame la Duchesse de Castries.CHAPTER IThe commercial traveller epitomizes a modern shift from mere material commerce to intellectual influence: he links provincial isolation to metropolitan ideas, equalizing products and importing Parisian innovations by persuasion. Self-educated, a mimic and rapid judge of character and solvency, he moves between village and capital with a surface knowledge of the world. One such man is Gaudissart—an acrobat of commerce in an olive-green overcoat and striped shirt—who made his fortune selling hats by charm and rapid talk. Jovial and adaptable, he read audiences and opportunities, sometimes out-trumping diplomats abroad.After 1830, when ideas became commodities and Saint-Simonian thought and speculation spread, Gaudissart switched from hats to selling life insurance and periodicals. Quick to seize commissions, newspapers sought him to sell subscriptions; his employers applauded his skill at transforming chatter into profit.CHAPTER IIPreparing to travel in mid‑April after a week immersed in Saint‑Simonism and finance, Gaudissart resisted tempting hat commissions and set off, joking with Jenny the florist about furnishing her room with his gains. Bragging of his expected earnings from insurance—especially the lucrative "Children’s Journal"—he stopped in Blois, then continued to his assigned towns.CHAPTER IIIIn Vouvray he ran into provincial resistance. The Tourangians were witty, satirical, and hostile to Saint‑Simonism. At the Soleil d’Or inn, Monsieur Vernier, aware of Gaudissart’s fame, resolved to humble him and sent him to Margaritis, a reputed lunatic.CHAPTER IVMargaritis, obsessed with wine and newspapers, rambled incoherently. Despite Gaudissart’s efforts to explain life insurance, the conversation devolved into absurdity. Two women listened amused while Vernier watched, intent on exposing Gaudissart’s vanity; mockery and unpredictability made any sale impossible.CHAPTER VWhen the innkeeper revealed the setup, Gaudissart, insulted, demanded satisfaction. A duel loomed, then—after townsfolk and the innkeeper intervened over breakfast—the quarrel was patched up. Gaudissart left Vouvray mortified but wiser: commerce needs not only eloquence but the discernment to recognize when persuasion is futile, and he resolved to return to Paris and his usual work.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################To Monsieur le Comte Ferdinand de Gramont.MY DEAR FERDINAND,—If these lines survive, accept this token of gratitude. You—Hozier, Cherin, King-at-Arms of these Studies of Life—have enriched the "Human Comedy" with your heraldic designs. Your complete manual of French Heraldry, learned in feudal spirit and symbolism, has greatly influenced me.Your old friend,  DE BALZAC.On the skirts of Le Berry lies Sancerre, a hilltop town above the Loire flats, fed by fertile alluvium though troubled by drifting sand. Its port, Saint-Thibault, ships wine and goods. By this story suspension bridges at Cosne and Saint-Thibault have replaced ferries, improvements from the House of Orleans after the 1830 upheaval.Within the Great Ramparts steep, narrow streets center a wine trade rivaling Burgundy and supplying Paris; nearby villages such as Fontenoy and Saint-Satur recall the vineyards. Once militarily significant, Sancerre declined as trade moved to the Loire’s right bank: its 3,500 inhabitants now trail Cosne’s 6,000, reversing fifty years. It still offers fine views, pure air, and a civic, mostly Calvinist populace not given to Puritanism.Notables include Horace Bianchon and journalist Etienne Lousteau. Municipal life is dominated by a few large landowners who manipulate elections; a locally hatched plot to break them failed through jealousy.
