A proposed remedy—to unite behind a single champion for the next election, one of Sancerre’s two deputies in Paris—was championed by the borough’s Superior Woman, whose ambitions reflect her interests and past.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Around 1836 Sancerre had a locally famous figure called the Superior Woman, long overlooked but now attracting notice. Two Sancerrois prospered in Paris then—Etienne Lousteau and the physician Bianchon—and the influence of George Sand fostered a sentimental “Sandism” that revealed many previously hidden superior women.The Superior Woman lived at La Baudraye, a town-and-country house near Saint-Satur belonging to a diminished branch of the Milaud family. The family’s fortunes rose and fell: an ancestor converted at the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes and was rewarded under Louis XIV; later generations suffered reverses, though one member obtained a military commission and left descendants favored by Louis XVI.In 1800 an ex–farmer-general returning from exile bought La Baudraye. His son, Jean Athanase Polydore Milaud de la Baudraye, frail and blamed on his father’s excesses, was raised by his noble mother. On inheriting the fief and a collection of autographs, Polydore lived frugally, enlarged vineyards (1802–1815), hesitated to press Parisian debts during the Restoration, tried and failed to monetize the collection, and returned home.In 1823, at forty-three, he sought an heir and married Dinah Piedefer, a beautiful, educated woman from a reduced ex-Calvinist family who converted to Catholicism. Though the Archbishop of Bourges saw promise and tried to arrange matches, families feared her beauty and intelligence; Polydore’s bluntness offended the Cardinal but he married her.Polydore pressed debtors in Paris, made a bargain with the Duc de Navarreins—forgiving debts for a tax-collector post—then obtained lucrative appointments and became Royal Commissioner, buying Anzy in 1827. Dinah transformed La Baudraye—gardens, interiors, refined company—while keeping her husband’s financial dealings private. Her beauty, intellect, Paris correspondence, and demand for conversation set her apart; provincial women resented and isolated her even as men admired her. Their story shows how exceptional qualities attract both admiration and exclusion.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By the end of 1825 Dinah de la Baudraye was criticized for socializing chiefly with men. In defending herself she made a rash remark to a false friend—“I would rather have my bowl empty than with anything in it!”—which only backfired. Childless after five years of marriage, she invited further ridicule.Monsieur de la Baudraye was a frail, gently yielding man with thin legs who sometimes wore false calves and favoured eccentric dress. He seldom contradicted Dinah and allowed her to dominate conversation. Admired for beauty and intelligence, she drew gentlemen from nearby towns, relishing attention and delivering prepared speeches from wide reading.Her charm protected her from much censure; her husband often excused himself from long expositions to walk or mind business. She championed the romantic arts, collected antiques, and filled their home with Renaissance treasures, which impressed visitors and supplied material for her lectures. Her attempt to found a literary circle in Sancerre degenerated into idle socializing—an evident disappointment—while admirers, including magistrates and a priest, proved easily diverted. When Dinah turned twenty in 1826 suitors still courted her; sheltered by her mother and admirers, she maintained a seemingly virtuous but gossipy domestic life, which Monsieur used to secure favors.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dinah Piedefer, proud and academically accomplished, married the seemingly frail Monsieur de la Baudraye at seventeen, wondering why and what she would gain. At first he surprised her: he let her use five acres for pleasure and financed house improvements so she could redecorate. She struggled to reconcile his stingy reputation with this generosity, but after his second trip to Paris the illusion vanished; he imposed a strict monthly allowance, paid her mother a yearly sum, and insisted on caution because their winegrower income was uncertain.Her attempts to provoke jealousy met only steadiness; he remained unyielding. Frustrated, she felt trapped in La Baudraye and Sancerre, craving a more ambitious life. His purchase of the Anzy estate and application for an entail briefly revived hope, but their short reconciliation only highlighted the clash between Dinah’s energy and his rigid routine.Over six years she grew provincial, losing individuality—wardrobe simplified, elegance gave way to conformity. A meeting with Anna, now a sophisticated Parisian, left her despondent. Turning to poetry, her "Paquita la Sevillane" gained local notice and revealed talent, yet provoked anxiety about exposure. Despite literary effort, Dinah remained caught between dreams of fame and a suffocating routine that bred lasting resentment.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After the Revolution of 1830 George Sand’s fame prompted many towns to celebrate local women writers. Monsieur de Clagny exploited this trend by publishing two hundred copies of poems by "Jan Diaz," a young deceased poet; some sold regionally while others were lost en route to Paris. It soon emerged that Jan Diaz was a pseudonym for Madame de la Baudraye—Dinah—who was quickly hailed by provincials as a rival to George Sand. Local, often partisan praise magnified work that might otherwise have been overlooked.Long feeling unrecognized, Dinah sought fame to fill unmet ambitions. The late Abbé Duret had warned her about her husband’s coldness and vindictiveness; she resisted until discovering his incapacity for love. As her reputation grew, domestic life felt more constraining and public sympathy deepened her despair. After a confrontation she vowed to stop writing and withdrew to Sancerre, where her absence was keenly felt.Jealous local rivals and Clagny’s electoral defeat added to her disillusionment, masking a deeper longing for freedom from marriage. At the Château d’Anzy she hoped to meet influential figures. When Lousteau and Bianchon arrived she hosted them, frankly denouncing provincial monotony, the stifling of creativity, and Paris-centered culture. Her intelligence, wit, charm, and vulnerability captivated Lousteau; she collected their autographs as tangible links to the literary world. Dinah’s story exposes the tension between ambition and domesticity and a woman’s struggle for identity in a restrictive provincial setting.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At five the next morning the household at the Chateau d’Anzy woke for a day’s shoot arranged by the young Monsieur de la Baudraye, an ostentatious plan to reclaim twelve hundred acres. The Parisians in his company—Gatien Boirouge, Lousteau, Bianchon, and Monsieur Gravier—were up and gossiping. Gatien asked why the Public Prosecutor, Monsieur de Clagny, had not joined them; Gravier said court duty, but Gatien suspected Clagny had taken a place on the bench and might be with Madame de la Baudraye (Dinah). The talk slipped into mockery: Lousteau and Bianchon derided provincial life and Gatien’s anxiety, calling it vanity and gossip.At a copse Gravier left to pick a ravine path; the others lingered debating whether to wait. Gatien fretted that if Dinah and Clagny were together they might watch one another for a chance to act—he feared Clagny’s seductive eloquence and Dinah’s Parisian ambitions. Lousteau and Bianchon treated such intrigues with amused disdain, but Gatien insisted he could read a small town’s women—secrets did not survive there.Lousteau proposed a prank: at dinner they would tell lurid tales of betrayed wives and vengeful husbands to watch Dinah and Clagny for reaction. Bianchon agreed that theatrical stories might reveal expressions; Gatien favored entrapment, recalling a Parisian editor’s wife who frightened her husband with tales of lovers boiled alive. They resolved to be diplomatic by day and provocative by night.At dinner Lousteau shone. He produced an album of melancholic verses signed by himself—an elegy that won Dinah’s sympathy. Clagny, offended, took refuge in backgammon. Dinah stitched for a supposed charity and replied wryly when taunted about needlework and marriage. Jests about adultery and morality flew: Clagny criticized Parisian writers’ treatment of illicit love; Lousteau defended literature with poets and symbols, provoking the prosecutor.Conversation widened into paradoxes: history and courts often condemn pioneers—Socrates, Jesus—and institutions persecute what they cannot assimilate. Lousteau teased that religion and state are fragile before new ideas; Clagny called it blasphemy. The company touched nerves about justice, passion, and revenge. Gravier half-joked he’d side with a betrayed husband; Lousteau mused on the sublimity of trusting husbands and the balance of trust and jealousy. Dinah’s quiet sympathy for victims tormented into crime stirred unease: human justice poorly judges private tragedies.Lousteau recounted a Nodier anecdote about a Chevalier feigning escape from a jealous commandant; no one reacted. Bianchon followed with La Grande Bretèche—the Spanish tale of a grandee walled into a cupboard to starve by a jealous husband—stunning the guests. Gravier added a Spanish adventure of illicit deliveries, betrayals, and murder. Clagny reminded them the Gazette des Tribunaux frequently reported horrors rivaling romance.The Parisians watched Dinah and Clagny closely, hoping grim tales would elicit a telltale emotion. That night Gravier proposed a country trick: affixing hairs in wax on Dinah’s and Clagny’s door locks to detect nocturnal comings and goings. The doctor, Lousteau, the tax receiver, and Gatien crept barefoot to press hairs into wax seals and agreed to inspect them at five.At dawn the seals were unbroken—same wax, same hairs. Lousteau declared they had been chasing a will-o’-the-wisp and admitted the matter was more complex. He told Bianchon that if Dinah were truly virtuous she deserved the effort; the joke turned to admiration. Lousteau sought Dinah in the garden and confessed he had plotted the evening’s stories to rouse emotion and test both her and Clagny. He reassured her innocence was proved, criticized cold men, and revealed a softened taste for a certain perfection. Dinah, seeing through him, replied that he sought pleasure not love, but showed sympathy for his vulnerability. For a moment Lousteau’s bravado softened.He and Bianchon spent the day entertaining Dinah with anecdotes and wit. By sundown sixteen guests had arrived; Dinah prepared the drawing-room proudly. When a footman brought letters and a packet of proofs, Lousteau found a shabby romance used as packing and turned its brigands and absurdities into a mock-critical performance. The improvised critique delighted the assembled provincials; Dinah enjoyed the attention and wit. Thus the evening became a stage where Parisian cleverness dazzled provincial complacency, while the sealed-door mystery remained unresolved.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For three days Dinah’s imagination was stirred by her two Parisian visitors. Lousteau watched her distraction, eager to captivate her. A ruse about Bianchon’s papers in Anzy sent Gatien off to fetch them, leaving Dinah alone with Lousteau and Bianchon. They mocked the locals and debated her unhappy circumstances, making Dinah defensive.Crossing the Loire, Bianchon told her she had shut herself to love but now needed it, warning that her current life would render her unattractive; Lousteau half-jokingly defended him. At Cosne Dinah, insisting they disembark, walked briskly with Bianchon, who suggested Lousteau loved her though he was frivolous; he counseled cultivating Lousteau, who could bring beauty and happiness. Under Bianchon’s scrutiny Dinah felt vulnerable.When Gatien returned, Bianchon blessed the party. Lousteau then declared his love; Dinah resisted, asking if she was merely a passing pleasure. Gatien’s arrival complicated matters: Lousteau urged her to feign resistance to save his honor and, recklessly, crushed her muslin dress, leaving her disheveled. Dinah, humiliated, sent Gatien for the papers and retreated indoors; Lousteau followed, begging forgiveness.After a tense confrontation between Lousteau and Gatien, and Dinah’s silent change into a velvet gown, Lousteau vowed to stay near her and renounce Parisian vices. His charm began to breach her reserve; she laughed, their intimacy deepened in the gardens, and they exchanged a pledge before he returned to Paris. Dinah accompanied him to Cosne and later praised him at La Baudraye for showing her true love. Her other admirers, including Monsieur de Clagny, were left to reckon with their feelings; Dinah made Clagny a confidant, lavishing affection while he remained devoted.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Back in Paris, Lousteau soon forgot the happy days at the Chateau d’Anzy. He lived by his pen in an age when writing—after the rise of the bourgeoisie—had become almost servitude: constant production, or the appearance of it. Writing daily for a newspaper and several literary magazines, he lacked artistic pretensions and ranked among Paris’s literary hacks. Deprived of advancement, he had become a journalist.His life, nonetheless, was not unpleasant. He socialized with blue‑stockings, aspiring actresses, publishers, and authors, kept theatre boxes, and relied on review fees to cover minor expenses. After ten years he still believed a monthly income of five hundred francs would make him rich, though he actually earned seven to eight hundred a month and rented well‑furnished ground‑floor rooms in the Rue des Martyrs for twelve hundred francs. His finances peaked and dipped—January, April, July, and October were especially tight—because he spent freely on cabs, meals, and gifts for lovers, leaving him perpetually broke.Weary at times, he joked about marriage with friends like Nathan and Bixiou. He had a mistress, Madame Schontz, proud of having a writer as a lover; she liked teasing friends about relationships. Dinah lingered at the edges of his life: he read and burned one affectionate letter, discarded another unread, and by December her letters piled in a drawer. Madame Schontz opened hampers Dinah had sent and suggested a dinner; Lousteau sent a clever but insincere note, convinced it would charm her.Then Madame Schontz hinted at a marriagable opportunity: a young woman whose lover had died, a notary father eager to restore her position with a quick marriage and substantial dowry. Pressed by promises of comfort—and warned about a strict mother‑in‑law—Lousteau met the notary, flattered him, and was introduced to the Cardot family. At his first dinner he noticed Félicie, shy and grateful, and saw Madame Cardot regard him as a man of promise. Buoyed by this reception, Lousteau felt a new purpose: stability, respectability, and financial security.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Madame Cardot, having borrowed the illustrated Gil Blas to inspect the prints, went with her daughter Félicie to Lousteau’s bachelor rooms to judge her future son‑in‑law. Her sister‑in‑law Madame Camusot had already been uneasy. In January 1837 they took a cab; the porter was away, but his daughter handed them the key after Madame Cardot slipped her a gold coin.Around noon Lousteau, returning from breakfast, was startled when Dinah, his former lover, arrived by coach with a child, Pamela, and revealed she was a mother. He tried to dismiss her, but she stayed; Pamela announced that his mother‑in‑law was inside, and Lousteau discovered Madame Cardot and Félicie hiding. Dinah declared her love; Lousteau, fearing the arranged marriage he faced, began to imagine using her to escape it.Madame Cardot openly disapproved and left. Dinah trusted Lousteau and admired the apparent elegance of his rooms, oblivious to his poverty. Pamela borrowed dinnerware from Madame Schontz, who warned Cardot would not pursue the match. Bixiou argued provincial women often fail in Paris and marriage secures legitimacy, but Lousteau insisted he preferred Dinah to any fortune. When Madame Schontz later learned Cardot had married Félicie to another, Lousteau’s respectable prospects vanished.Having lost a stable future, Lousteau and Dinah grew entangled; their domestic life slid into the provincial romance he once mocked, leaving both trapped in dependency and illusion, unequipped for hardship.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################That winter in Paris transformed Madame de la Baudraye as October had in Sancerre. Etienne introduced Dinah to city life: theater, dressmakers, society. Lousteau, vain and ambitious, stripped away her provincial modesty and she quickly adapted, becoming intelligent, well read, and able to hold her own among sophisticated women. Their passion was intense, and Dinah became both muse and partner, though Lousteau sometimes saw her as a useful asset and toyed privately with the idea of deceiving her.When Dinah became pregnant she withdrew from public life but first attended a Nathan premiere, where Lousteau’s abandoning her to mingle left her exposed and humiliated beside an old acquaintance, Anna Grossetete. Clagny’s warning that the liaison threatened her reputation stirred nostalgia and regret despite her resolve.After the child’s birth Dinah devoted herself to motherhood and household management, pawning jewelry to support Lousteau’s career. His financial irresponsibility and laziness soon surfaced: he failed to write regularly, accumulated debts, grew indifferent, and sought comfort elsewhere. A second child increased Dinah’s burdens and his detachment.The turning point came when her estranged husband, Monsieur de la Baudraye, reappeared seeking a power of attorney for an inheritance. Clagny—now Attorney-General—negotiated an allowance for Dinah and protected her interests. Confronted with betrayal and loss, Dinah realized her sacrifices were unrewarded and resolved to reclaim her identity and seek a future based on mutual respect and commitment rather than mere passion.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################From that day on Etienne lived in luxury and Dinah could hold her own among Paris’s best-dressed women. Lousteau, however, pretended ruin to friends, blamed Madame de la Baudraye, and painted himself as helpless before Dinah’s desperate love—while remaining unfaithful. Monsieur de Clagny, distressed by Dinah’s disgrace, warned her of betrayal; she acknowledged it and, when asked if she still loved Lousteau, cried that she would lose her soul for him, leaving Clagny speechless.Gradually Dinah saw Lousteau’s true nature. She described him to her mother as a lazy, pleasure-seeking poet she could not hate because she felt responsible—convinced she was his only support. Her mother deplored her suffering; Dinah replied, “I will be a mother to him,” accepting compromises to live with shame. Hoping to retain his affection, she resolved to emulate Madame de Pompadour. His insincere, theatrical devotion eroded her dignity, yet she clung to the hope his love might return and found a bitter consolation in being his plaything.She often rehearsed the tragedy Le Dernier Jour d’un condamné, picturing their separation, and even contemplated suicide in her garden, but remained devoted. Influenced by the novel Adolphe, she vowed not to become like Ellenore—refusing public reproach or tears.Meanwhile Monsieur de la Baudraye schemed to restore his fortune by manipulating his wife’s inheritance—reducing its apparent value, pocketing the difference, and investing in land. He convinced himself he owed Dinah nothing for now and imagined a noble life in Sancerre, seeking title and social prominence.By early 1842 Dinah felt reduced to Lousteau’s reserve, dressing in mourning as penance; her mother worried and sought intervention. With Clagny’s help Madame Piedefer urged Dinah to consider leaving Lousteau; Clagny negotiated, assuring that her husband might allow her to keep the children and return respectably to Paris. Unknown to Dinah, Monsieur de la Baudraye was buying a luxurious Paris residence to polish his image, inviting her to live with him and promising support for their children’s education.As a Countess preparing for social prominence, Dinah paid off debts in May 1842 and invited Lousteau to dinner. She announced their separation calmly: she still cared but insisted on parting. Lousteau accused her of ruining him; she refused to accept blame and left both liberated and heartbroken.In her new life she made her home a model of comfort, spending beyond her husband’s expectations. He admired her efforts but was unaware of her inner turmoil. Charming and fashionable, she nevertheless missed past passion and struggled to connect with other women. When Clagny revealed Lousteau was living with another woman, Dinah threw herself into society seeking recognition. Her appointment in March 1843 to collect donations for charity restored her public image. Summers in Sancerre found her mothered by a husband proud to present their children; by June 1844 the family’s status was on display.Throughout these changes Dinah maintained composure, balancing roles as mother, Countess, and woman shaped by sacrifice—yet torn between love and ambition, public success and private longing, an unresolved tension that defines her journey.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Across France there were many Chevaliers de Valois; the Alençon chevalier is this tale’s subject. Though sharing the name, these men mostly did not know one another and accepted the Bourbons after the Valois left no male heir. The Alençon chevalier was an elderly, tall, thin gentleman whose Parisian youth and romances were cut short by the Revolution.Well regarded among the local aristocracy, he was famed for manners, wit, and anecdotes of Louis XV’s reign. A regular at social gatherings, his charm and discretion made him a favorite. At fifty-eight he claimed fifty, preserving a youthful air despite a prominent nose and a pale face that reddened on one side—his excuse for the flush and appetite was a “hot liver.” Seemingly frail but vigorous, he kept immaculate though worn clothes and lived modestly in a rented room above Madame Lardot’s laundry, dining out on a small annuity. Beloved for card-playing and conversation, he mingled with all ranks; rumors persisted of a secret marriage to a Césarine.One spring morning Suzanne, a clever laundry girl who dreamed of Paris, interrupted him with news. The chevalier, touched by her ambition, advised her paternally to attract Monsieur du Bousquier, a wealthy suitor. Du Bousquier, once prosperous but sullied by Revolutionary dealings, sought to marry rich Mademoiselle Cormon to restore his fortunes. His brusque opportunism and the chevalier’s elegance set up a rivalry—along with Suzanne’s aspirations—that exposes class, gender, and the pursuit of happiness in a changing world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Madame Granson, a widow on a modest pension with a small property, struggled to support her son Athanase, who added a meager salary from the mayor’s office. Their poor provincial home shaped their lives and morals. Athanase, a pale, introspective twenty-three-year-old, secretly loved his cousin Mademoiselle Cormon, whom he believed wealthy. What began as a practical hope to secure a better life for himself and his mother became genuine affection, complicated by social expectations and the awkwardness of loving an older woman. Madame Granson, aware of his feelings, saw the match’s advantages despite his lack of fortune and waited patiently.The arrival of Suzanne, a poorer young woman, changed things: she felt an instant bond with Athanase but, driven by ambition, chose deception to escape poverty. After a brief meeting with Madame Granson she left town with money and new clothes, to the community’s regret. As Athanase prepared for a dinner with Mademoiselle Cormon at his mother’s urging—unaware of all her plans—Suzanne departed for Paris, seeking to remake her life while Athanase remained in Alençon, weighed down by unfulfilled dreams and provincial pressures.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In many second‑class French prefectures a local salon gathered influential—but not elite—figures: lesser nobility, clergy, and magistrates who kept a formal distance from the highest echelons. These salons, hosted by prominent locals, wielded notable influence, preferring practical concerns like land acquisition, resisting Parisian fashions, and treating gossip as communal judgment. Young women appeared seldom, usually to seek validation.In Alençon the upper classes met at the house of Mademoiselle Rose‑Marie‑Victoire Cormon, an unmarried heiress living with her uncle, a former grand‑vicar. Though of middle‑class origin, her family’s service to the nobility and advantageous marriages gave her a veneer of aristocracy. Her well‑kept house, dating to Henri IV with a prized location, courtyard, flowerbed and period furnishings, signaled historical standing. Reception rooms—a portrait‑hung salon for cards and local talk and a provincially charming dining room—served a circle of about 150 mostly mature, locally rooted people who seldom traveled.Wednesdays drew larger dinners where modestly dressed women conversed familiarly about gossip, land disputes, and judgments of aristocratic behavior. Cormon never married: political upheavals of 1788–1815 thinned suitable matches, and fearing a fortune‑marriage she tested suitors. Her longing for love—notably for Athanase Granson—grew with age into disappointment. Pious and prudent, she became a local object of ridicule; aborted hopes from returning soldiers left her yearning and despair, revealing the harsh limits placed on women by class expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################To complete Mademoiselle Cormon’s household: she, the Abbé de Sponde, and loyal servants Jacquelin, Josette, and Mariette who ensured the uncle and niece’s comfort. Jacquelin, short and ruddy, had served twenty-two years—waiting at table, grooming the mare Pénélope—and loved Josette, a thirty-year-old maid whom Mademoiselle would dismiss if she married him; the couple quietly saved, hoping Mademoiselle’s eventual marriage would free them to wed. Josette, plump and practical, supposedly ruled her mistress. Mariette, the cook of fifteen years, prepared local delicacies.Childless, Mademoiselle poured maternal care into Pénélope and centered her life on the household, her uncle, and religion, becoming exacting and fixated on trifles—linens, meal times, minor ailments. Each season she visited Prébaudet to inspect the apple trees, leaving with a farewell dinner that was an Alençon event. Town gossip about suitors, especially the rejected M. du Bousquier, surfaced there; her awkward defense of him over rumors involving Suzanne unexpectedly won laughter and admiration, leaving her to ponder her place amid love, ambition, and social expectation.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Mademoiselle Cormon, with Josette, set off for Prebaudet unaware that events would hasten her marriage. The morning after she arrived, Jacquelin brought a letter from her uncle, the Abbé de Sponde: Monsieur de Troisville, a grandson of the abbé’s friend, wished to stay in Alençon and needed hospitality. Alarmed and excited at the prospect of a match, she hurried back to town despite the rain to help receive him.Their arrival provoked town gossip; Josette and Jacquelin managed the household while Mademoiselle Cormon fretted over her old-fashioned home and appearance, even sacrificing prized liqueurs to impress. Monsieur de Troisville arrived distinguished and flattering, and she felt an immediate attraction as their dinner conversation warmed.The mood changed when the Chevalier de Valois and other guests arrived and the viscount casually revealed he was married. Devastated, Mademoiselle Cormon fainted and was carried to her room by Du Bousquier amid public speculation. Determined to recover her standing—and with both Du Bousquier and the Chevalier courting her—she accepted Du Bousquier’s proposal on condition of a public, agreed-upon sham engagement. The town buzzed as the announcement approached, leaving her triumphant yet uncertain.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The news of Mademoiselle Cormon’s marriage shattered Athanase Granson, though he concealed his turmoil. He first learned of it at Chief Justice du Ronceret’s while his mother played cards. Long nourished by hope and shaped by an Imperial fatalism, he slid into indifference and despair; his mother noticed only a pale, distracted son and blamed work.Athanase wandered the Sarthe countryside, finding a bitter solace in neglected beauty. He withdrew from politics and society, becoming a figure of little interest to townspeople who could not fathom his pain. Meanwhile Suzanne, inspired by romantic tales, returned to Alençon resolved to help Athanase financially, though they never met. The town buzzed with gossip about the match’s legitimacy. When the marriage contract was signed and the wedding held, ominous signs—most notably a priest accidentally reading from the De Profundis—set an uneasy tone.The union lacked celebration; the couple retreated to Prebaudet. Du Bousquier set about modernizing the estate, alarming locals protective of provincial customs. Consumed by grief, Athanase ultimately took his life, leaving a letter his mother misread as proof of independence rather than despair. His suicide stunned Alençon. Madame Granson mourned privately; only the Chevalier de Valois disclosed Athanase’s unrequited love, deepening the town’s sense of tragedy.Madame du Bousquier returned to Alençon and had a tense, silent encounter with Madame Granson. Seeking to avoid scandal, Madame Granson arranged a discreet burial. Over time the marriage became both gossip and cautionary tale: socially advantageous yet personally barren. Du Bousquier, outwardly successful and ambitious, was a domestic tyrant; his wife, childless and unfulfilled, performed duty while inwardly resenting him. The Chevalier’s vendetta and wider political changes widened the rift around them. Suzanne’s purchase of a small memento from the Chevalier’s estate symbolized lost connections. Madame du Bousquier’s life thus embodies the painful collisions of love, duty, and social expectation.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################To Baron Von Hammer-Purgstall, Member of the Aulic Council, Author of the History of the Ottoman Empire.Dear Baron, your encouragement for my “History of French Manners in the Nineteenth Century” led me to associate your name with my work. As a representative of studious Germany, your approval lends weight to my efforts. Your devotion to research has sustained me in writing on modern civilization. Receive this token of respect from your sincere admirer and friend.DE BALZAC.In a small French town the Hotel d’Esgrignon houses the old Marquis Charles Marie Victor Ange Carol. The name began as a jest but suits a family proud of its ancient Northman lineage. Proud provincial nobles, the d’Esgrignons refused to bow to kings or commoners: marriages without dowries, refusals of titles, and a stubborn local reverence persisted despite neglect by court and state.The Revolution threatened them: the Marquis hid, lands were sold, and Mlle. d’Esgrignon, through the steward Chesnel, rescued some property. After the Terror the Marquis returned to a ruined castle; the old hotel lay demolished. Chesnel used the family’s last funds to buy a modest new residence, wryly naming it the Hotel d’Esgrignon.Around 1800 émigrés returned. The Marquis married the Baron de Nouastre’s daughter, who died in childbirth leaving a son. Reduced circumstances and grief deepened. When a contractor proposed to Mlle., she and her brother were insulted; Chesnel, who had negotiated, was blamed. More than a servant, Chesnel was confidant, manager, and protector, torn between fidelity and the house’s decline. Politically the family stayed royalist while the bourgeoisie rose and rivals like du Croisier sought to undermine them. By 1822 the Restoration brought little relief; finances remained strained, but the “Collection of Antiquities” salon endured as a testament to their bygone ideals and legacy.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The Marquis neglected the estate, and most considered it fanciful that the d’Esgrignons could regain their former glory. Many pinned hopes on Victurnien, the handsome heir, marrying into nobility to restore prestige, but the family lacked the political connections, finances, and continuity—fractured over forty years—to do so.Only Chesnel, the notary, saw this clearly. He understood the social changes after the Revolution and gently urged the Marquis to adapt; the old noble, however, clung to belief in fate or divine revival.Victurnien, indulged by his father and aunt, never faced opposition; spoiled, selfish, and reckless, he was further inflamed by the Chevalier’s romantic tales of past grandeur. Surrounded by flattery and exempt from restraint, he came to regard himself above common people.In town his behavior alienated locals; his escapades brought legal troubles Chesnel discreetly managed. Companions encouraged his arrogance, while Du Croisier, a rival, sought to exploit him. Victurnien fell into gambling and frivolity, sustained by Chesnel’s sacrifices. Sent to Paris with high hopes, he found the court indifferent; nevertheless he adopted the elite’s extravagant lifestyle. His charm won him society’s doors and the infatuation of the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse—despite her debts—while his own liabilities mounted. Friends egged his spending; Chesnel kept propping him up. The tension between pretension and ruined finances foreshadows a downfall, exposing the dangers of vanity, excess, and the illusion of inherited status.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By the end of 1823 the Kellers had secretly advanced Victurnien two hundred thousand francs; he had also taken two thousand crowns from Chesnel and written flattering, deceitful letters to his father and aunt. Only du Croisier knew, and he delighted in both ruining and dishonoring the d’Esgrignons as Victurnien’s debts grew.Du Croisier’s first move targeted Chesnel, who lived modestly in the Rue du Bercail. Calling one afternoon, he produced a bill for two hundred and twenty-seven thousand francs, demanding payment or threatening exposure. Chesnel, able to raise money only by mortgaging his lands, was forced to sell; du Croisier bought the property within two days, leaving Chesnel with only his practice and house and consumed by fear for Victurnien’s future.Chesnel dreaded telling Mlle. Armande, who had just received a flattering letter from Victurnien about travels with the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse and remained enchanted. The news shattered her; she saw they had given him too much freedom. The Marquis, though he had read Victurnien’s letter, was unaware of the crisis. Chesnel and Mlle. Armande silently resolved to save the young count; she set off for Paris—pretending a medical visit to avoid gossip—with Chesnel’s last bag of louis to confront the Duchesse.Du Croisier ensured Victurnien would be cut off, writing that the Kellers would advance no more without security. Cornered, Victurnien rejected suicide and plotted to flee with the Duchesse, forging a bill for three hundred thousand francs which the Kellers advanced unknowingly. Du Croisier, learning of the transaction, realized his scheme was succeeding.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################When Victurnien got the money he handed it to Mme. de Maufrigneuse, who locked it away and persuaded him to make one more visit to the Opera. He nearly confessed everything to Chesnel but, despite misgivings, stayed at the Duchess’s request, held by her enchanting look.The next day he returned to the Hotel de Maufrigneuse intending to flee, only to find the Duchess resolved to face the storm rather than run. Torn between admiration and despair, Victurnien recognized the depth of his love. After their quarrel Diane’s coldness left him distraught; her angelic indifference to his plea for forgiveness intensified his anguish and clouded his judgment.Worse followed: a warrant was issued for his arrest. Josephin urged him to go to the Hotel du Bon la Fontaine, where Mlle. Armande waited. Reeling, he and his aunt plotted to hide and slip into Chesnel’s house, unwittingly walking into a trap.Chesnel, preparing to sell his practice to M. Lepressoir, was devastated by Victurnien’s plight and even considered taking the blame for the forgery. He resolved to confront the creditor, du Croisier, who relished his ascendancy over the d’Esgrignon family. At du Croisier’s gathering news of the arrest spread and debate about the trial flared.Chesnel tried to influence the magistrate, M. Camusot, and enlisted Mme de Maufrigneuse’s help. Negotiations with du Croisier proved thorny—he demanded money, political favors, and recognition. Chesnel offered a large sum and even contemplated perjury, but du Croisier’s pride resisted compromise. The looming trial thus became emblematic of a wider clash between aristocracy and bourgeoisie, with love, honor, ambition, and Chesnel’s loyalty at stake.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Blondet, a competent judge, was eclipsed by his passion for gardening—especially pelargoniums—which consumed his time and ambition. His marriage to a much younger woman, who had an affair with the local prefect, further clouded his career. Though he had a legitimate son, Joseph, Blondet preferred his illegitimate son, Émile, and after his wife’s death sent Émile to Paris with little support.Blondet planned to leave his estate to Joseph, disinheriting Émile. Émile, however, prospered in Paris with his mother’s family and the patronage of Mlle. de Troisville, gaining literary recognition unknown to his absorbed father. Joseph was being groomed for the bench and mingled with the Blandureaus; Michu, a young lawyer, often outshone older judges.A shrewd prosecutor pursued a politically motivated forgery charge against Victurnien d’Esgrignon at the President’s urging to benefit du Croisier. The Duchesse de Maufrigneuse and Chesnel funded Victurnien’s defense; the trial exonerated him and exposed du Croisier’s plots. The d’Esgrignons preserved their honor, though Victurnien’s future remained uncertain amid shifting loyalties and ambitions in changing France.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Produced by John Bickers, and DagnyTHE LILY OF THE VALLEYBy Honore De BalzacTranslated by Katharine Prescott WormeleyDEDICATIONTo Monsieur J. B. Nacquart, Member of the Royal Academy of Medicine.De Balzac thanks the doctor whose science once saved him and honors the friend of his daily life by inscribing this work among the stones of his literary foundation.ENVOIFelix de Vandenesse to Madame la Comtesse Natalie de Manerville:At your insistence I yield and tell you my past. Women we love may make us ignore common sense; you have the right to demand my history even though I resist. You have guessed the phantom that haunts me; you require its confession. In obeying you I overcome a deep reluctance and hope my confidence will increase your love. Until we meet, Felix.CHAPTER I. TWO CHILDHOODSWhat genius, nourished by tears, will one day write the truest elegy for childhoods starved of affection? A life whose tender roots find only stone at home moves more surely than any public tragedy. Such was my childhood.From infancy I endured alternating neglect and intermittent scrutiny. Left with a nurse in the country for three years, I returned to a household dominated by indifference; the servants pitied me, my siblings, encouraged by a favorite and fearing our mother's temper, often persecuted me. Regular childhood alliances that shelter a child were absent in my case. I was frequently punished for my brother’s faults and forbidden to prove my innocence. These injustices did not corrupt me but hardened me: constant expectation of new blows bred sullen resignation and a premature pride that tempered the ill effects of neglect.Sometimes my mother spoke of educating me herself; the idea chilled me, and I blessed the neglect that let me wander in gardens, watch insects, and fix my mind on a star. Once, crouched beneath a fig-tree absorbed by a star, I was falsely accused by our governess, Mademoiselle Caroline, of tampering with a garden faucet and severely punished; still, the star remained mine. Forbidden pleasures grew more desirable, and that glowing point in my imagination sustained me through boyhood.Charles, five years my senior, was the household favorite—handsome, robust, indulged. I, puny and sickly, was sent at five to a town school as a day pupil with only a scanty lunch. The contrast between my hunger and my classmates' plenty wounded me deeply. Boys taunted me over rillons and dainties I did not possess; a cruel trick—the promised slice withdrawn—left me ashamed and resolute. Once I was stoned; my mother heard and only lamented my supposed dangerous temperament. Rejected by family and schoolmates, I learned to distrust myself.At twelve I went to Pont-le-Voy, an Oratorian school for dull boys, where for eight years I lived a pariah’s life. My three-franc monthly allowance barely covered necessities; I could not afford games and was ostracized except by humiliating flattery of the rich. I took refuge under a tree with borrowed books and thought. When I won prizes for composition and translation and my parents did not attend the theatre, I burned my crowns in tears, feeling the deeper ache of being forsaken.The sense of injustice drove me to religion. At first communion I sought consolation in prayer and martyrology; youthful zeal fed sublime visions that strengthened my imagination and made me sensitive to inner life—qualities that later tempered my talent. At fifteen my father, uneasy about the Oratorians, placed me in a Paris institution, the Lepitre Academy. There neglect continued: meager pocket-money, strict discipline, and social restrictions. Monsieur Lepitre, given to Bourbon sympathies, contrived a small subvention but not enough.In Lepitre’s house the porter Doisy smuggled comforts for better-off pupils; resisting him was heroic for a hungry boy. My parents' arrival in Paris once promised relief: my brother planned to take us to the Palais-Royal and to a theatre. But I owed a hundred francs to Doisy; my mother, infuriated, refused indulgence and reproached me with future ruin. Sent back to Lepitre, I lost the dinner and the play—the first meeting with my mother after twelve years ended in reproach.At nineteen, still under Lepitre's guardianship but longing for Paris, I grew desperate. My aunt, the Marquise de Listomère, ceremonious and cold on the Ile Saint-Louis, would not offer money and shamed me for my youth. Several attempts to slip to the Palais-Royal were thwarted by unlucky coincidences: Lepitre’s coach, my mother’s unexpected arrival, the post-chaise that brought her. My family's anxieties—political and moral—kept me restrained.Study was both refuge and peril; absorbed in books I delayed the normal stirrings of youth. In Tours my mother’s coldness crushed me; on a journey home I threw myself at her feet, begged for affection, and was mocked as playing at "comedy." At Blois I once went to the bridge intending to throw myself into the Loire but could not commit suicide. Back at home I found only the slight mercies of my sisters and poor clothing. My mother's ironical speeches and punctilious sense of duty made affection an unreliable thing.Yet Tours had public excitement. The Duc d'Angouleme passed in triumph; the town celebrated, and I longed to attend a ball in his honor. Despite my mother's mockery about the request, secret preparations were made: a bottle-blue coat, silk stockings, and a frilled shirt—my first finery. The ball overwhelmed me with heat, music, a crush of people, the military band and cries for the duke. Initially the spectacle disgusted me, but then a strange ambition stirred—an appetite for glory and power over the masses.Among the crowd I saw a woman who altered me. Misjudged as a sleepy lad, I leant against a woman's shoulders I took for a mother; she rebuked me with a single word, "Monsieur!" Tears sprang to my eyes; the look that followed—noble, angry, forgiving—awoke in me a passion I had never known. She moved with queenly grace, carrying a purity and brightness that transformed my life: my star had become flesh and blood. From then the sky seemed bluer and I sought her everywhere in Touraine.My mother, seeing my melancholy, sent me for a few weeks to Frapesle, a château on the Indre, hoping country air would restore my health. For a young man newly in love but ignorant of the object's name or station, this was opportunity. Walking the countryside, I scanned every house and old tower as if she might live there. The landscape—moors, woods, the valley of the Indre—enchanted me; beauty seemed intrinsic to the air and matched my inner mood.From a walnut-tree where I later loved to pause I first saw the castle that housed the woman I sought: a white spot among vines, cambric fluttering in an arbor. The castle, Clochegourde, belonged to the Comte de Mortsauf and his wife, a Mademoiselle de Lenoncourt—an ancient, proud, secluded house with limited fortune and fixed habits. My host, Monsieur de Chessel, knowing the count and countess, suggested we call; his teasing revealed how transparent my feelings were.Clochegourde was modest but tasteful: a façade of five windows, terraced gardens, acacias, vines, neat outbuildings. From the road the ground floor seemed an upper story; a tourelle and carved crest testified to lineage. The house fit the valley like a jewel; approaching it quickened my heart with foreboding and delight.We were announced after the bell for breakfast; the count was at Azay but the countess, whose voice I had recognized at the ball, welcomed us. My agitation nearly betrayed me; she returned to her embroidery and spoke with a calm dignity that both concealed and revealed feeling. Monsieur de Chessel explained my fatigue; Madame de Mortsauf offered restoratives and gentle courtesy. Her glance, at first severe, softened when she saw my distress. Her invitation to dine, accepted, seemed a promise: if I pleased her, my introducer might keep favor with the count.Madame de Mortsauf, reserved yet radiant, enchanted me. At dinner she spoke of harvests and local affairs; her cadence, the brevity of her glances, the rare luminous intonations moved me more than artifice. I prolonged conversations and accepted reproach merely to hear the melody of her voice and breathe her presence.Up close she retained the luminous appeal that had struck me at the ball. Her hair was fair, her brow broad and thoughtful; her eyes green tinged with brown, usually wan but capable of sudden gleams. A Grecian nose and graceful lips gave her spiritual beauty; her figure combined strength with softness. She seemed both mother and maiden—grave from suffering, yet childlike in feeling. Her movements, her rare glances, her plain dress formed a charm that made men dream: virginal purity and moral elevation without ambition.More than features, it was the constant emanation of her soul—tenderness to family, cheerfulness in serenity, resignation amid storms—that impressed me. Clochegourde’s salon, panelled and modest, matched her spirit: gray paint, porcelain vases, cambric curtains, an embroidery frame. From its windows the valley unrolled—Pont-de-Ruan, château d'Azay, Sache—images that nourished my ideas and changed my mind. Many of my earliest thoughts in science and politics seemed first to live there, in that composed atmosphere.When Monsieur de Mortsauf returned I watched him closely. A little girl, Madeleine, frail and pale, entered and clouded the mother’s brow; Jacques, her brother, was similarly weak. The children’s frailty suggested family sorrow; Madame de Mortsauf’s health and spirit bore the cost. Monsieur de Mortsauf, about forty-five but aged by care, offered a polite, uneasy courtesy; he seemed a man whose distrust sprang from inability to understand rather than malice. Learning my name and family connections, his manner warmed—a respectful recognition of lineage in a man of old family.Thus began days at Clochegourde that would shape me. The valley, the house, the maternal presence, and that first vivid encounter set in motion a passion combining gratitude, reverence, and the hunger of first love. My lonely childhood had prepared me to adore with an intensity that neglect can create. The domestic world of the Mortsaufs—humble and dignified—provided the soil in which my youthful devotion took root.I learned the subtleties of Madame de Mortsauf’s manner: the economy of her speech, the weight of her silences, glances scarce as jewels. Her conversation, even about trivial matters, carried an undertone of feeling revealing depth. Her benevolence and austere tenderness drew me where admiration and self-sacrifice mingled. The simplicity of the household—the neat salon, embroidery, the garden paths—spoke of moral discipline and inner warmth.The contrast between modest means and noble air left an impression: pride without display, ancestral dignity humbled by circumstance. This image of noble poverty deepened my reverence. In the strangeness of my past—neglected by my mother, punished unjustly, driven inward by study and prayer—the spectacle of such domestic devotion opened me like a flower. Clochegourde, its valley, and above all its countess became the scene and cause of a passion that would shape my destiny.Our visit ended with impressions words cannot fully render: Madame de Mortsauf’s presence, the music of her voice, her discreet tenderness for the children, and the quiet dignity of the home remained with me. I left with mingled hope and helplessness—hope from an attentive hour in her presence; helplessness because I was too young, too dependent, and too ignorant of the world to win her beyond the simple worship of a distant admirer.Thus closes the account of my early years to the point where life after twenty begins under influences both tender and decisive. From neglect to the sanctuary of the valley, my character was formed—partly by solitude and hard study, partly by the sudden appearance of a woman whose moral and physical beauty became my guiding star. The meeting at the ball and at Clochegourde introduced an affection equal parts devotion and pain, opening the way to all that followed.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Though ten years of emigration and farm life had altered his body, the Comte de Mortsauf still bore traces of nobility: chivalric loyalty, steadfast convictions, honest politics, but ignorance of France’s practical affairs. He was the upright type who, unable to be useful to his party, might hurt it; ready to sacrifice his life rather than his money. At dinner I noticed his flaccid cheeks and furtive looks at his children—signs of a burdened mind and a frail constitution he had passed on. He blamed himself but refused to be judged; his harshness, born of self-awareness, magnified the misery his faults had brought the family. When his wife returned with the children, unhappiness settled over the house.After the conversation shifted he introduced me to his wife and, with curious intimacy, revealed family details I had not known. He asked my age; the countess, surprised, inspected me with maternal curiosity and hope for her children. Some barriers between us dissolved. She said, “If study has made you ill, the air of our valley will soon restore you.” The count, however, denounced modern education—burdening children with mathematics and sciences, aging them prematurely—and urged a return of education to religious hands. He added later, explaining his political stance, “I shall always distrust men of talent,” which clarified why he had refused to support a promising candidate.He proposed a tour of the gardens; a brief interruption from Madame—revealing his desire to control the household—preceded a remark about Monsieur de Vandenesse’s arrival from Tours and Monsieur de Chessel’s escort of him on foot. I sensed then that navigating the count’s royalism required care. The manservant announced dinner; Monsieur de Chessel offered his arm to Madame de Mortsauf, while the count took mine.The dining room, though unpretentious, had charm: white tiles, varnished floral paper, family silver, mismatched china, flowers on the table. Dinner was modest but plentiful. I sat beside the countess, served her wine, and felt a profound connection. A kiss had already bound us in a secret that filled me with joy and shame. I lavished affection on the dogs and engaged the children, hoping to be accepted. Any gloom would have jeopardized my place in their lives.Dinner passed in a haze of happiness: I reveled in being under her roof, ignoring her apparent reserve and the count’s polite indifference. Love, like life, has an adolescence, and I was content in my feelings. Foolish remarks, born of passion, went unnoticed; even she seemed oblivious to love. I returned across the Indre enchanted by moonlight and the sounds of nature; that evening marked a turning point—past struggles clashed with newly experienced joy.Approaching Frapesle I saw Clochegourde and a small boat tied to a tree—Monsieur de Mortsauf’s fishing boat. Once out of earshot, Monsieur de Chessel congratulated me on the warm reception I had received, telling me I had stepped into the count’s heart at once. His praise lifted me, though I sensed underlying bitterness.Monsieur de Chessel—actually Durand—had abandoned his father’s merchant name to adopt an old parliamentary one. Ambition led him to claim a count’s title under Louis XVIII, a title his children enjoyed without understanding the sacrifices. He was petty and envious; his recent royal favor made him ostentatious, yet he was kindly to me in a paternal way I welcomed after years of neglect. His ostentation annoyed some neighbors while Mortsauf, with limited means, struggled to maintain his estate. Their relations were polite but never intimate.Jealousy did not fully explain the Comte de Mortsauf’s isolation. His education had been shallow; emigration interrupted the deepening of his learning, and long idleness followed. Hopes for a restored monarchy kept him from seeking work; life’s trials eroded his spirit, natural gaiety faded into moroseness, and he became inclined to hypochondria. Hidden love affairs further harmed his health and prospects.After twelve years he returned under a decree allowing emigrants back. Crossing the Rhine, he cried “France! France!” like a child calling for its mother. Once wealthy, he came back impoverished and stripped of influence; his name offered no protection. His rigid opinions and past made him vulnerable to ridicule. He eventually settled in Maine on a forgotten farm that provided some stability. The Lenoncourt family, having lost fortune, welcomed him; he stayed with them while preparing a home. Mademoiselle de Lenoncourt accepted his proposal and lived with her aunt, the Duchesse de Verneuil, who profoundly influenced the countess’s devout character. The duchess’s death cast a shadow over their early married years, though Saint-Martin later visited Clochegourde to correct his works.The birth of the count’s first child, Jacques, brought despair: the doctor pronounced the child unviable, a truth the count concealed from his wife. Subsequent births confirmed his fears and deepened his sense of failure. The countess, sensing her husband’s turmoil, devoted herself to his care, accepting his miserliness and mistrust and guiding him toward what was right—often at the expense of her own happiness. She resolved to remain at Clochegourde, fearing that leaving would harm their children. Her efforts masked his incapacity and allowed him some peace in her presence.My own understanding of Clochegourde deepened as I spent time there. One evening I found the countess watching a sunset; we acknowledged a shared sorrow, only to be interrupted by the children. She welcomed my visits, seeing in me an amusement for her husband. I often played backgammon with the count, enduring his harshness. To keep peace I learned to balance the game—letting him win at first, then evening the score—an unspoken strategy the countess recognized with a grateful look that filled me with joy.Once, after an intense game, the count erupted in rage. I waited in the garden; when she joined me she revealed her husband’s condition and begged me to forget the scene. “Promise me to bury the recollection,” she implored, asking only for my word of honor. I pledged to do all she wished, though I could not change my opinion of him. She asked that I respect her freedom—never take her hand unless she gave it—and to refrain from undue warmth. As I departed I felt a profound connection, pledging my love in silence.In the days that followed small moments deepened my affection. I resolved to support her, be a source of strength, and honor the bond we had formed. Our shared sufferings had forged a rare, precious understanding; beneath the stars I vowed to cherish and protect that love through life’s trials.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################This scene occurred on a Tuesday; I had waited until Sunday and had not crossed the river. Much had happened at Clochegourde during those five days. The count received his brevet as general of brigade, the cross of Saint-Louis, and a pension of four thousand francs. The Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry was restored as peer of France, reclaimed two forests and his place at court; his wife recovered unsold property that had once been imperial crown lands. As a result the Comtesse de Mortsauf became an heiress: her mother arrived at Clochegourde with a hundred thousand francs saved at Givry—the dowry the count had never demanded despite earlier poverty. With his own savings he bought two neighboring estates producing about nine thousand francs a year. Their son would inherit the grandfather’s peerage; the count thought to entail the estate upon him without harming Madeleine, and the duc could likely help secure a good marriage.These events calmed the count. The duchesse’s presence at Clochegourde affected the neighborhood. I reflected gloomily that her caste spirit, hidden from me by my love for Henriette, now revealed itself. Who was I—poor, insignificant, with only courage and faculties? On Sunday morning, seated in the private chapel with Monsieur and Madame de Chessel and the Abbé de Quelus, I stole a glance toward the duchess’s pew and saw her with her daughter, the count and his children, at prayer. Henriette’s broad straw hat did not tremble; her apparent unconcern at my presence bound me to her more than our past. She prayed with such abandonment she seemed a religious statue; it moved me profoundly.After mass vespers followed. Madame de Chessel proposed going to Frapesle rather than cross the Indre twice in the heat. Monsieur de Chessel offered his arm to the duchess, Madame de Chessel took the count’s, and I offered mine to the countess, feeling her arm against mine for the first time. Walking through the woods of Sache, where light filtered through foliage, sensations and proud thoughts overwhelmed me; my heart beat violently.“What is the matter?” she asked.“I have heard of your good fortune,” I said. “Will your new dignities change you and lessen your friendship?”“Change me!” she cried. “Oh, fie! Another such idea and I shall—not despise you, but forget you forever.”I looked at her in ecstasy.“We profit by new laws which we have neither made nor sought,” she said. “We are neither place-hunters nor beggars; neither Monsieur de Mortsauf nor I can leave Clochegourde. By my advice he declined the command at the Maison Rouge. We are content my father should have it. This forced modesty has benefited our son. The king—whose household my father joins—will favor Jacques’ education. He must represent both Lenoncourt and Mortsauf. I can have no ambition except for him; he must live and be made worthy. Who will keep him safe in Paris? My friend,” she added, voice breaking, “be some day his guardian and sponsor. Go to Paris; if your family will not support you, ours—especially my mother, who rules society—will help you. Put your desires into a noble ambition—”“I understand,” I interrupted. “Ambition will be my mistress. But I will not be rewarded for obedience here by favors there. I will go and make my own way. From you, I would accept everything; from others, nothing.”“Child!” she murmured, pleased.“Besides, I have taken my vows,” I continued. “I am resolved to bind myself to you by ties that can never be broken.”She trembled and stopped.“What do you mean?” she asked.“First tell me how you wish me to love you.”“Love me as my aunt loved me,” she answered. “I gave you her rights when I permitted you to call me by her chosen name.”“Then I must love without hope and with absolute devotion. I will enter a seminary, become a priest, and educate your son. Jacques shall be myself in his own form; political conceptions, energy, patience—I will give him all. I will live near you; my love, enclosed in religion, can never be suspected of evil. I will consume myself in the flame and love you with a purified love.”She turned pale. “Félix, do not bind yourself in a way that might be an obstacle. I should die of grief. Do you think despairing love a life’s vocation? Wait for life’s trials. Marry neither the Church nor a woman—marry not at all—I forbid it. Remain free. You are twenty-one—My God! can I have mistaken him? I thought two months sufficed to know some souls.”“What hope have you?” I cried.“Accept our help, rise in life, make your fortune, and you shall know my hope. And,” she added as if whispering, “never release the hand you are holding at this moment.”She bent to my ear as she said it.“Madeleine!” I exclaimed. “Never!”We neared the wooden gate into Frapesle’s park. At that instant the thought of the count’s death flashed through me, and I said, “I understand you.”“I am glad of it,” she answered, surprised.Her purity provoked tears of admiration mingled with the bitterness of passion. I thought she did not love me enough even to wish me liberty; love that recoils from crime seemed limited. She did not love me, I thought. To hide my pain, I kissed Madeleine’s hair. “I am afraid of your mother,” I said.“So am I,” she answered playfully. “Don’t forget to call her Madame la duchesse and speak of her in the third person. Young people have lost polite manners; you must learn them for my sake. It is good taste to respect women, no matter their age, and to recognize social distinctions. The respect shown to established superiority guarantees what is due to you. You have drunk the milk of the Revolution in your academy; but as you advance, you will find crude principles of liberty powerless to create happiness. Before considering what an aristocracy ought to be, my common sense tells me societies can exist only through a hierarchy. Be on our side—especially now,” she added, laughing, “when it triumphs.”I was moved by her combination of political feeling and affection. In defending her husband she foresaw criticisms I might make of the servility he showed her mother. Monsieur de Mortsauf, king in his castle, had dignity; yet I was astonished at the obsequious distance he put between himself and the duchess. Humiliated by his degradation, I understood the martyrdom of generous women yoked to petty men. I treated the duchess with respect because of my youth; where others saw a duchess, I saw Henriette’s mother, and that sanctified my homage.At Frapesle the count presented me to the duchess, who examined me coldly. Madame de Lenoncourt was fifty-six, wonderfully preserved, erect and imposing, with a hard face and courtly manner reminiscent of my own mother. I behaved well; as I passed the countess she said softly, “You are perfect.”The count took my hand. “You are not angry with me, Félix, are you? If I was hasty, pardon an old soldier. We shall probably stay for dinner, and I invite you on Thursday, the evening before the duchess leaves. I must go to Tours tomorrow. Don’t neglect Clochegourde. My mother-in-law is an acquaintance I advise you to cultivate. Her salon will set the tone for the faubourg St. Germain. She has all the traditions of the great world and immense social knowledge.”The count’s changed fortunes softened him. The duchess was not patronizing; Monsieur and Madame de Chessel accepted the invitation. She questioned me about my family and, learning that the Vandenesse in diplomacy was my brother, grew almost affectionate. She told me my great-aunt, the old Marquise de Listomere, was a Grandlieu, and that by a provision of the Charter my father was once more Marquis de Vandenesse.“I am but one thing, the serf of Clochegourde,” I answered.The Restoration’s transformations bewildered our generation. To me, the revolution meant nothing; the least word from Madame de Mortsauf was the single important event. I was ignorant of councils and politics; my sole ambition was to love Henriette better than Petrarch loved Laura. This indifference made the duchess take me for a child. A large company assembled at Frapesle, and to a young man unused to the world, seeing the woman he loves admired by all is intoxicating. The count, delighted, seemed almost young; his wife hoped for change; I amused myself with Madeleine, whose cleverness made others laugh. It was a happy day; a word, a hope awakened in the morning and illuminated nature. Seeing me joyous, Henriette was joyful too.“That happiness smiling on my gray and cloudy life seems good,” she said to me the next day.I spent that day at Clochegourde; the count left early for Tours. A serious disagreement arose between mother and daughter. The duchess wanted the countess to move to Paris, where she promised a place at court and where the count might accept a position. Henriette, concealing her husband’s incapacity and her own sufferings, opposed leaving Clochegourde because its air calmed his excitements and soothed the children’s health. She had persuaded him to go to Tours so as to hide his condition from the duchess. I alone knew Clochegourde’s dark secret.Henriette saw that the duchess cared little for Jacques and Madeleine—a painful discovery. The duchess brooked no opposition; she used sweetness, then coldness, then sarcasm to force compliance. In those ten days Henriette endured the struggles a young woman must face to assert independence. The mother and daughter had nothing in common; the duchess could not imagine the reasons Henriette stayed. Tortured and wounded, Henriette loved the one who did not strike her, who would build a shield around her. I was happy when she rested upon my heart; she told me of her troubles. Day by day I learned what she meant by “Love me as my aunt loved me.”“Have you no ambition?” the duchess asked me at dinner.“Madame,” I replied, “I have enough in me to conquer the world; but I am only twenty-one, and I am all alone.”She looked at her daughter in astonishment. The duchess’s visit meant constraint. The countess begged me to be cautious. The Thursday of ceremony was wearying, but the duchess returned to court and Clochegourde resumed its ordinary life.My quarrel with the count had made me more at home; I could go there at all times. I clung like a climbing plant to the beautiful soul that had opened the world of shared emotions. Our fraternal marriage, based on trust, grew firmer; Henriette enfolded me with maternal care, while my love for her burned when away. I loved her with a double love—seraphic in her presence, searing when absent. At my age I was not yet man enough to torment her; she feared harm to her children and sat beside them when her husband allowed her a little rest. The mere sound of ardor shook her; she needed veiled love—support mingled with tenderness—the devotion she gave others. This brought languor and secret immolation. Her conscience and self-devotion awed me; the piety that bound her virtues filled the air with spiritual incense. She gave me only the back of her hand to kiss, never the palm, as though limiting sensuality. No two souls ever clasped each other with such ardor while remaining unbound. Later I understood why: in youth we love the whole woman—her children and interests—as our own.I became part of the household and knew infinite sweetness—the privilege of being the mistress’s friend in a strange family. Servants treated me kindly; the children divined my affection and were discreet. True passions bloom more charmingly in barren soil.But I also saw Monsieur de Mortsauf’s failings: perpetual scolding about nothing, complaints of imaginary ills, an inward dissatisfaction that demanded victims. On walks he chose the way yet complained whichever way we went; at home he blamed others. He took pleasure in touching his wife’s sensitive chords. Sometimes he fell into gloomy silence that alarmed her and brought tender attentions. Like a spoiled child, he let her serve him as she did the children. I discovered his petty tyranny in household details and realized how it stifled the family, hindered management, and impeded improvement of the estate. The countess had been noble in describing these daily struggles. My admiration for her rose above my love; I yielded to be a voluntary victim to come nearer her. She allowed me a place beside her and permission to share her sorrows; eager to die in her arena, I became a repentant apostate seeking heaven with my brethren.“Were it not for you, I must have succumbed under this life,” Henriette said when the count had been more venomous than usual.He had gone to bed. Henriette and I stayed under the acacias with the children playing in the setting sun. Our few words revealed mutual thoughts; when language failed our silence served, and our souls seemed to enter one another. We luxuriated in pensive languor and asked by a look, “Among all our days on earth, will there be one for us?”Despite the evening’s poetry, desire crept over me. After three months of repression I could not accept my assigned fate. I took Henriette’s hand and caressed it. She withdrew at once; tears from my eyes fell and she offered her hand to my lips.“You must know,” she said, “that this will cause me grief. A friendship that asks such a favor is dangerous.”Then I lost control; I reproached her, spoke of my suffering and of a slight alleviation I dared request. I told her that at my age the soul had a sex and that I could meet death but not silence my cries. She forced me to silence with a proud glance. Ever since the gate of Frapesle, when I had taken her hope to be born from a grave, I had shunned the thought of staining her soul. She said she could never be wholly mine; if I could not be to her somewhat as her old confessor—less than a lover yet more than a brother—she must never see me again. She could die and offer her sufferings to God.“I gave you,” she said, “more than I ought, so that nothing might be left to take, and I am punished.”I calmed her, promised never to cause pain, and to love her as an old man loves his youngest child.The next day I came early; there were no flowers in her gray salon. I rushed into the fields and vineyards to make two bouquets; as I gathered them it occurred to me that color and foliage have meanings, and by melody awaken memory. I enlisted Jacques and Madeleine and arranged two bouquets on the portico steps: white roses and lilies with blue flowers intermingled, symbolizing two purities—the child and the martyr. The countess discerned the sentiment and gave me a poignant glance, reward enough. I pressed Père Castel’s theories into service and recovered that lost science: the language of flowers. What charm in expressing our sensations through these daughters of the sun! I communed with the flora, seeking varieties that would complete my messages.Twice a week I pursued this poetic enterprise, walking long distances to find flowers, taking pleasure in the world of feeling rather than form. Nature offered scenes rising to high moral conceptions: heather with dew like diamonds, mossy rocks like savage cries, anemone pulsatilla like my pure idol alone in her valley, sheets of water with floating life, a cottage garden as humble existence’s image, a forest path like a cathedral nave. I gathered these images and poured them into bouquets.In September and October no bouquet cost less than three hours’ search. These floral symphonies expressed what no declaration could; my baffled desires found expression as Beethoven finds it in notes. In their presence Madame de Mortsauf was my Henriette. She looked at them often; they nourished her spirit. These neutral pleasures soothed me: abstinence exhausts, and a few crumbs of heavenly comfort keep one from disaster. Sometimes I found her standing before the bouquets, lost in agitation; she would lift her head and murmur, “Ah, how beautiful!”Throughout the month I often saw her at the window when I arrived. If I was late, she wandered on the terrace and said she had come to meet me and tend her youngest child.Backgammon had ended; the count’s purchases occupied him in inspecting his new properties. He was neither arrogant nor offensive, and our walks together were mutual joys. Once, the count, wishing to know what we talked about, asked; Henriette answered with words having a double meaning that satisfied him. Madeleine laughed. Our spiritual union continued.The days passed with new joys. The grape harvest—the vintage—began in late September. The vintage brings abundance and hilarity: coopers and carts, girls and panniers. Classes are equal for the day; everyone shares the harvest. Jacques and Madeleine, who had never seen a vintage, were full of delight. We went to Villaines for baskets; none of us were to eat too many grapes. Jacques made me swear to stay at Clochegourde. The day in the vineyard was glorious: the children’s chatter, their racing about, the mother laughing with the joy of youth; she passed a hand round my neck and tapped my cheek—the only caress she ever gave me. I filled my basket and emptied it into the vat, moving slowly in regulated motion that kept my spirit free. I found the pleasure of external labor that regulates passion; I learned monastic discipline.For the first time in weeks the count was neither surly nor cruel. His son’s rosy face rejoiced him. He had promised a dance in front of Clochegourde to celebrate the Bourbons’ return. As we returned, the countess leaned on my arm and whispered, “You bring us happiness.”To me, burdened with knowledge of her sleepless nights and cares, those words were more than any other woman could give.“The terrible monotony of my life is broken; all things are radiant with hope,” she said. “Oh, never leave me! Be the elder son, the protector of the younger.”This is not romantic; in youth passion restrained by obstacles can fill the crater of a volcano with pure water.We had another fête. The countess, wishing to accustom her children to practical life, gave Jacques the produce of the walnut trees and Madeleine that of the chestnuts. Gathering chestnuts and walnuts, seeing the children take pride in their small granaries, made charming scenes of innocent happiness amid autumn’s fading colors. Jacques’ walnuts suffered from rain; I advised him to hold back and sell later. His father urged prudence; the mother rejoiced to see Jacques listen. These homely scenes attached me ever more closely to Henriette.I learned how, over ten years, the countess had changed cultivation—rotating crops and dividing the estate into four farms on share leases. She induced the count to cultivate a model farm and rebuild two farmhouses to relet for money rents afterward. Fearing to die before her husband, she sought to secure his income and her children’s property. Fruit trees planted in the last ten years were bearing; hedges and elms grew well. With the new purchases and farming system, Clochegourde—divided into four farms—could bring in forty thousand francs a year, not counting vineyards and woodlands. The new road to Chinon would attract tenants. The countess wished to invest fifteen thousand francs in each new estate to improve houses for better rents; this required thirty thousand francs, which she and her husband argued about. The fear “If I die tomorrow, what will become of them?” drove her. The count objected, but common sense won.
