That day we went to the Cassine and the Rhetorière to decide on buildings; she spoke gently, confident of success. A carrier wanted a large farm on the new route; another honest man was ready to take la Baude; the soil would attract tenants. In two years Clochegourde might yield twenty-four thousand francs. Gravelotte in Maine was rented for seven thousand a year; his pension was four thousand. Later additions might let her go to Paris and arrange Jacques’ education.With what feeling she said “Paris!” I knew she wished not to be separated from her friend. I told her I would finish my education and work to be Jacques’ tutor. She looked grave.“No, Félix,” she said. “That cannot be, any more than your priesthood. I thank you as a mother, but as a woman who loves you, I can never allow you to be the victim of your attachment. Such a position would be a social discredit to you. My father would never forgive your taking such a humble place; he would refuse you his protection. You must accept society as it is; never fly in its face.”“Love,” I whispered.“No, charity,” she said, controlling tears. “This wild idea enlightens me as to your character; your heart will be your bane. I shall claim the right to teach you sometimes. Let my woman’s eye see for you. From the solitudes of Clochegourde I will share in your successes. As to a tutor, do not fear; we shall find some good old abbé, my father will devote sums to the boy’s education. Jacques is my pride; he is eleven years old.”At the Cassine Jacques, Madeleine, and I followed her like children. I left to speak with Martineau and saw how beloved she was. A laborer told me she was good and knew our hardships and treated the people well. I gave that man all the money I had.A pony arrived for Jacques; the father, an excellent horseman, would accustom him gradually. The morning of the first lesson, with Madeleine shouting and all the household clustered round, was a maternal festival. Jacques wore a blue collar embroidered by his mother and rode fearlessly. The count smiled for the first time in months; the mother burst into tears and leaned on my arm, “I think I have never suffered. Do not leave us today.” The child jumped into her arms; I and Madeleine arranged bouquets for dinner. She said, “The fifteenth of October is certainly a great day for me. Jacques has taken his first riding lesson, and I have just set the last stitch in my furniture cover.”“Then, Blanche,” the count laughed, “I must pay you for it.”He offered her his arm; an open carriage her father had sent awaited with two English horses. As we returned, she whispered anxiously, “I am too happy; happiness is like illness—it overwhelms me; I fear it may vanish.”I loved her so passionately I was jealous. She asked me what my eyes said; I told her. She was more touched than by any gift, then poured comfort into my heart. “Love me as my aunt loved me,” she said. “If I take your life, must I not be grateful?”“It was time I finished my tapestry,” she added. “Men find occupations a resource against troubles; we women need physical exercise to control inward sufferings. Each stitch rocked my thoughts; to every stitch I confided my secrets. While doing my last chair I thought much of you. What you put into your bouquets I have said in my embroidery.”Dinner was lovely. Jacques offered his garland to his mother with grace. In the afternoon we played backgammon; the count was charming. They accompanied me to Frapesle in the tranquil twilight. It was a day of days for the poor woman.Soon the riding lessons became a source of contention. The countess feared the father’s harsh reprimands; Jacques grew thin and wore dark circles. I advised him to tell his father when tired, but the measure failed, and the old huntsman took over. Angry reproaches resumed. Then the cost of the Cassine and Rhetorière buildings exceeded estimates by half. The news, given first to Monsieur de Mortsauf instead of to his wife, sparked a quarrel that grew bitter until the count’s malady displayed itself.That day I had gone from Frapesle to search for flowers with Madeleine. Returning, I could not find her and heard cries in the house. Madeleine ran out: “The general,” she cried, “is scolding mamma; go and defend her.”I entered the salon unseen. Monsieur de Mortsauf was in a violent tirade.“Never marry, Félix,” he said. “A woman is led by the devil; the most virtuous would invent evil if it did not exist; they are all vile.”He harked back to old troubles, cursed the new farming system, declared he could have been twice as rich had he managed Clochegourde differently. He sprang about, displacing furniture; then stopped, moaned his marrow was on fire, and accused his wife of ruining him.“Yes, Blanche,” he cried, “you are my executioner; you are killing me; you are a monster of hypocrisy. She is smiling! Do you know why she smiles, Félix?”I kept silence.“That woman denies me all happiness; she is no more to me than to you, and yet she pretends to be my wife! She fulfills none of the duties of laws, human and divine; she lies to God and man. She puts all her art into keeping me away; she has made me mad through privations; she is killing me by degrees, and she thinks herself a saint and takes the sacrament every month!”Henriette wept bitterly, answering only, “Monsieur! monsieur! monsieur!”His words burned me with shame for him and anger because they struck at chastity and delicacy—the warp and woof of first love.“She is virgin at my expense,” cried the count.At this the countess cried, “Monsieur!”“And what mean you by your imperious ‘Monsieur!’?” he shouted. “Am I not your master? Must I teach you that I am?”He advanced, face white, eyes savage. Henriette slid from her chair to avoid a blow, fell full-length, and lost consciousness. The count stopped bewildered. I took her in my arms; the count opened her bedroom door. We laid her, still dressed, on the bed. When she revived she motioned to unfasten her belt; the count found scissors; I gave her salts and she opened her eyes. The count left the room, ashamed. Two hours passed in silence. Henriette’s hand lay in mine; she pressed it but could not speak. Once she rose on her elbow and whispered, “Unhappy man!—ah! if you did but know—” then sank back. The memory of past sufferings and the present shock threw her into convulsions I calmed by an instinctive magnetism. I smoothed her hair—the first time I touched it—and sat looking at the simple room: chintz curtains, a crucifix, her aunt’s portrait, the children’s drawings with locks of hair. What renunciations of luxury for such a woman—the chamber of a married nun, filled with holy resignation. Painful as the sight was, it exposed hidden, irreparable woe.When the children and Manette came in I left. The count waited for me; he seemed to seek me as mediator. He caught my hands, exclaiming, “Stay, stay with us, Félix!”“Unfortunately,” I said, “Monsieur de Chessel has a party, and my absence would cause remark. But after dinner I will return.”He left the house with me and took me to the lower gate.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As I read the letter I felt a maternal warmth under my fingers that contrasted sharply with my own mother's coldness; the countess had feared I might break down if I opened it in Touraine. Soon after I reencountered my brother Charles, haughty and distant; his condescension made me awkward, though my deep love kept me from feeling foolish. Childhood sorrows and solitude had sharpened my sense of affection, and since returning from Clochegourde I could better recognize genuine attachment.I often visited the Duchesse de Lenoncourt, where Henriette's name was seldom spoken. Only the old duke, whose welcome eased my shyness, seemed aware of our bond. Just as I began enjoying society again, the events of March 20th forced a change. At Henriette's advice I followed my brother to Ghent with the Duc de Lenoncourt. The duke’s amiability protected me when he perceived my loyalty to the Bourbons; he introduced me to the king, who valued my steadfastness. A letter from Madame de Mortsauf told of Jacques' illness; her distress compelled me to help. Putting rivalry with my brother aside, I left court to serve the sovereign as a reliable messenger, eager to return to Clochegourde.After carrying out the king's orders I evaded Bonapartist authorities, travelling through the Vendée to Clochegourde. Jacques was better; the count welcomed me, and in the salon I met Henriette. Her surprise and joy were immediate: she prepared my room, the count saw to my food, and at dinner we learned of Waterloo and Napoleon’s flight. More than the political news, I noticed Henriette’s tenderness—she had even built an ice‑house for me—and absence had only intensified my love.Henriette spoke of long nights at Jacques' bedside, prayers and exhausted vigilance, and the count’s harshness that made her words fall flat. Still she clung to duty, urging her children to respect their father and managing their farm with practical wisdom she sometimes called divine insight. She encouraged me to be available to the king and pursue opportunities in Paris. The tension in her marriage haunted us both; she feared the count’s behaviour might harm the children and hesitated to assert herself. I urged firmness.When the count fell ill we alternated caring for him, which drew us closer; his slow recovery, however, revived his difficult ways. Henriette repeatedly sacrificed her own happiness for her children. Eventually I returned to Paris, faithful to her. Our letters revealed her love and burden — Jacques' relapse later intensifying her pain. Throughout, her resilience and maternal devotion shone, and my loyalty grew with her suffering; our love remained a rare, guiding light I hoped would endure.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################During a deeply emotional period I met a remarkable woman at the Élysée-Bourbon salons: immensely wealthy, of noble lineage and married to a distinguished British peer. Her beauty, grace, and wit made her the idol of Parisian society; she ruled with a firm yet charming demeanor.The English, distant and reserved, live by strict laws and rigid virtues, producing an Englishwoman who, though delicate and charming, is trapped in hypocrisy and compelled to maintain appearances; her passion is intense but constrained. You may envy these women, but they are complex and convinced that love alone suffices.My liaison with the Marchioness of Dudley became notorious. At a time when my better feelings were subdued, I was drawn to her; my fidelity to Madame de Mortsauf only increased her desire. Lady Dudley craved the chase, and my resistance inflamed her passion. Her mocking remark—“I am wearied to death of these turtle-dove sighings”—should have warned me.Men have fewer means to resist women than women have to escape men. Social codes keep men from appearing harsh; women are expected to be coy. Though I loved Madame de Mortsauf, I was not immune to Lady Dudley’s beauty and rank. Her attention stirred me; she often hinted she would be my mistress. After one beguiling evening she made her intentions clear, provoking scandal in the British aristocracy.Seemingly fragile, Lady Dudley possessed a strong will and remarkable courage—she could outpace any horse—and pursued her desires relentlessly. By contrast, Madame de Mortsauf was nurturing and expansive; her love was spiritual and boundless. Lady Dudley embodied a materialist Englishness: compelling physically but leaving an emptiness I did not feel with Madame de Mortsauf.Lady Dudley’s jealousy sought to erase Madame de Mortsauf from my heart. My letters to Madame de Mortsauf went unanswered, and when I returned to Clochegourde her coldness revealed the harm my infidelity had caused. Her beauty was dimmed by sorrow; the count, unaware of the cause, welcomed me, while she remained distant. The count confessed his fears and ill health; I was tormented that I had contributed to her decline. Seeking forgiveness, I was rebuffed. Torn between Lady Dudley’s passionate allure and Madame de Mortsauf’s devoted love, I was left to confront the true nature of love and sacrifice as the weight of my actions pressed upon me.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################I know nothing more destructive than the wit of an Englishwoman; it carries the pompous certainty with which the British mask their prejudices. French wit is lace; English wit is corrosive. Arabella wielded her power like a sultan, cutting down rivals with words.She spoke to me in a mixture of passion and flattery that sought to erase the impressions left by Henriette’s dignified love. “My angel,” she said, arguing that her love was not a crime but pious, that God created beauty to inspire adoration, and that the real sin would be not to love me. She contrasted her weakness with another woman’s strength, declared me above moral laws, and closed by claiming me forever. Her speech was designed to seduce and to denigrate Henriette as prudish and unworthy of my devotion. She demanded assurances, wept at the thought of losing me, and, after extracting pledges, dried her eyes and released me to return to Henriette if that was my will—because loving me by choice would prove my love truer.Under Arabella’s influence I went back to Clochegourde. In the moment, I did not feel the falsity of my position; refusing would have let Lady Dudley triumph and carried me to Paris. But leaving then was fatal to my genuine love. I realized, belatedly, that any truly loving woman would rather see her lover die than know him happy with another; my departure made Madame de Mortsauf’s illusions burst.When I arrived that morning she was pale and sleepless, distance between us like a precipice. Henriette’s illusions were broken; she refused to look at me, denying me the light that had warmed me for six years. She treated me with a kind of brotherly care—ostentatious attention that humiliated me. Our long intimacy yielded nothing now; we had nothing to say, hiding intolerable pain from each other. Henriette called herself my sister; our forced silence intensified my shame. I cursed the pleasure Arabella had given me and wished Henriette would demand my blood. I could not attack Arabella before Henriette; had I mentioned her, Henriette would have despised me. After five years of intimacy we could not bridge the gulf.Her manner grew cold; I resolved to leave. At the grove marking the boundary of the estate she stopped me. “Farewell, my friend,” she said, laying her head on my chest and embracing me. She declared she could foresee the future; she had seen me turn my back on her. She asked me to take the place of the mother she might lose, to care for Jacques and Madeleine. Startled by the fire in her eyes, I ordered her to swear to live and to obey her doctor. She misread me, cried that she would do all I asked, and, at my seeming submission, I kissed her eyelids. The look we exchanged erased Arabella’s memory. For a moment I was once more the lover of an angel and the plaything of two irreconcilable passions: one virtuous and selfless, the other ostentatious and corrosive.Yet I remained with Arabella in Paris, perhaps hoping the count’s death would restore me to Henriette, perhaps seduced by the corrupting spirit of the court. Arabella and I grew intimate quickly; we discarded conventional prudence and displayed our union. She wanted to exhibit me to Paris as her “sposo,” keeping me under her roof by flaunting our liaison. At first this life enthralled me; then despair set in. I saw my life bound to a path that offended Henriette’s principles and advice. From then on I lived in a rage that ate at me. My letters to Henriette, expressing the moral malady I lived, did her infinite harm; she answered only once, pleading she wished my happiness even at so many treasures lost. But I was not happy; happiness leaves no room for comparisons. When the first ardor cooled, character’s asperities reasserted themselves, and natural antipathies resurfaced.I began to examine Arabella critically. She lacked the qualities of mind that make Frenchwomen so transforming in love. A Frenchwoman, when truly in love, becomes mother, adviser, doctor; she adorns converse and action with coquetry and self-abandonment, making love a grace that varies and enlivens itself. Arabella, by contrast, lived two lives: passionate in private, cold in public. She could set her love aside like a garment; society could govern her feelings; fashion often reigned over pleasures. Where Madame de Mortsauf’s smallest movements were constant caresses and faithful signs of devotion, Arabella’s mind existed for the world and for sarcasm. She loved to wound and jest; she exhibited her affection but kept an impenetrable English reserve in public. Her ambivalence—ostentation plus dignity, love plus coldness—wounded me. In private she mixed tenderness with cutting sarcasm; in public she preserved a composed, ambassador-like aloofness that outraged my heart.The contrast extended beyond manners to inward generosity and outward domestic feeling. Henriette studied my prospects, engaged ardently in my permitted ambitions, and took hold of my life with modest ardor. Lady Dudley showed only the reticence of an acquaintance; she never inquired into my affairs and separated self-interest from love. She was prodigal for herself but not generous in the way that nourished a man’s life. To save me a pang, Henriette would have labored for me in secret; Lady Dudley would not. French luxury expresses the man’s spirit and reproduces his ideas between lovers; English luxury, initially alluring, proved mechanical and cold. The thousand attentions at Clochegourde expressed care and soul; Arabella treated them as servants’ business. Her candid selfishness, her theatrical crimson draperies over an iron nature, gradually revealed a stony rock where my tender seeds were wasted.When weariness at Arabella and longing for Clochegourde’s serenity possessed me, I received a blow whose echo has not ceased. While I was working in the king’s cabinet the Duc de Lenoncourt entered and told the king that my poor daughter—Henriette—was dying. I begged leave at once. The court’s cold tone, the king’s ironic look when we crossed paths, and the pomp that seemed to drive over the dead body of my beloved—all this provoked bitter reflections on the world’s insensitivity. On the road beyond Tours I met Monsieur Origet, who told me that Henriette was dying of inanition: her stomach had rejected nourishment for forty days. He explained that her illness was essentially grief made physical—an inertia of an organ as necessary as the heart. This accusation pierced me.I hurried to Clochegourde filled with remorse. Jacques, Madeleine, and the Abbe Dominis knelt at a wooden cross; the old huntsman stood apart. The whole house was dumb with grief. The count, white-headed, alternated between despair and accusation. The abbe explained how, for months, Henriette’s suffering had increased despite medical care; the mind must be healed before the body. Her words had grown bitter and contradictory, torn between wanting to live and wanting release. Despair gripped me as I approached the house.They warned me not to enter unprepared; the countess was greatly changed. Within, her room had been given a tenderness of decoration. She sat in white before the fire, flowers around her; her face had a greenish pallor, hollow eyes, and features altered by prolonged suffering, but still a forehead in proportion and, at times, inward fires of feeling. She knew she had been changed and met me with bitterness and irony. When I took her hand, she spoke of death as the end of love and reproached me with coming to surprise her in such a state. She alternately hoped to recover and then disparaged life. She kissed me, clung to me, and fainted; the doctor noted it had been forty-two days since she had eaten or drunk. The abbés and the doctor feared for her; they gave her opium to ease pain and prepared for sacraments. At moments she seemed to rally in a strange composure, speaking of life to come and Italy, yet the danger was constant.Cast into stupefaction and self-reproach, I hid alone to suffer. I asked whether I ought not to have led the simple, faithful life she had proposed. Had she not asked me to become great to avoid shameful entanglements? Made more acute by the sight of her children’s aversion, my self-condemnation deepened. Madeleine avoided me, returning within the house with a look of hatred; Jacques sat expressionless, his handkerchief stained. The children seemed to perceive their mother’s murderer and recoiled.At sunset, the countess fell into a long, opium-assisted sleep that seemed at once annihilation and a triumph of the soul. Her suffering face cleared into a white, heavenly beauty that made those present kneel and weep. The abbes read the signs as angelic presence. The doctor said this sleep was the hour without pain and that they should administer the last sacraments. At nine o’clock she awoke with gentle eyes and the beauty we had known. Madeleine cried that life and health would return; Henriette, smiling, said she would yet live in her daughter. She tried to absolve, to reconcile, to be tender toward me. She asked me to stay for her last scene and to respect her weakness. In confession she uttered words that pierced me: though she had been a faithful wife, she feared she had sometimes failed and had given an outside friendship more affectionate care than to her husband. She wanted his blessing with a clean heart. She begged him to grant as a last wish a pledge she had written—making me her adopted son, a token of affection, and asking my friend to secure her husband’s happiness.These final sacrifices unveiled depths of tenderness and forgiveness that dissolved my egotism. Henriette had, in death, shown a greater charity than I had imagined. Her last acts—burning letters, arranging her affairs, composing a testament of love—were meant to place me under the obligation of taking charge of the household and of Madeleine’s welfare. She surrendered to death with dignity, pressing my hand, asking me not to be absent from the least pain in which I was concerned, and reminding us of the valley where she would rest.From that dreadful day, my world dimmed. Those who die are sometimes buried within us: Henriette’s soul entered mine; when any good was done by me thereafter, it felt like her act. I ceased writing; my recollections were too bitter. Henriette’s letter and her last wishes indicated a way to redeem myself: to live at Clochegourde with Madeleine and consecrate myself to her. But first I had to face Madeleine’s feelings.On the terrace, when the count fetched his daughter at my request, I appealed to Madeleine for a sisterly welcome, urging her not to expel me from Clochegourde in her mother’s name. She answered with cold dignity that she knew my life and thoughts and that she would rather fling herself into the Indre than ally herself to me. In her voice was a resolute hatred. She refused my plea that my life was bound to that place and that her mother’s memory should preserve me there. Her young, searching eye had discovered her mother’s secret. Her hatred, perhaps augmented by a sense of complicity, was implacable.Thus the house I loved turned against me. Madeleine condemned me without weighing cause or victim; the edifice of my happiness lay in ruins. Henriette had been known fully only to me; her nobility had no historian to measure its depths. Mothers, husbands, and friends seldom fathom the inner life; many families shelter a Henriette unknown to the world. When Madeleine withdrew, I left, my heart broken, and departed toward Paris along the Indre.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY — repeated, separated by hash dividers.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################At the story’s outset Angoulême still used old wooden presses tied to Parisian paper mills; “the press groans” is literal: leather ink-balls and hand-inking prevailed. This antique mechanism, beloved by Jérôme-Nicolas Séchard, drives the tale.Once a journeyman pressman, Séchard survived the Requisition of 1793, became sole worker after his master’s death, obtained a master-printer’s license from a Representative of the People despite being illiterate, and bought the business cheaply. He partnered with the noble Comte de Maucombe, who helped print decrees while avoiding official notice. By 1795 Séchard hired a compositor—an Abbé who later became a Bishop—and, though uneducated, learned to run the shop: tracking costs, buying paper when prices fell, becoming his own landlord and acquiring the old printing house. His avarice grew.He had one son, David, schooled in grammar and Paris, who excelled and finished without burdening his father. The firm held a regional monopoly on official prints, threatened when the Cointet brothers sought a second license. Now in his seventies and given to drink since his wife’s death, Séchard presents a bloated, cunning figure. He greets David warmly yet presses business like both heir and rival. The long-established shop—workroom, vine-grown yard, inky kitchen runoff and cramped, unchanged living quarters—reflects his stinginess and relentless pursuit of profit.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The sitting-room, partly modernized by the late Mme. Séchard with powder‑blue wainscoting and Oriental paper, was sparsely furnished—lyre‑backed chairs, bare arched windows and unfinished plans—Mme. Séchard having died before completing it. Jérôme‑Nicolas Séchard, practical and profit‑minded, left it as it was.He brought in his son David, pointed to a valuation of the printing plant and had him read the inventory. David scoffed at the old presses and tossed the paper; Séchard, affronted, led him downstairs, demonstrated the presses’ efficiency and argued their superiority to newer machinery. David doubted the high price for the type; Séchard insisted it was nearly new from M. Vaflard’s foundry. The inventory totaled thirty thousand francs, including license and goodwill. David, recalling his father had once bought the business for ten thousand francs, balked.Séchard pressed the point: the license brought steady income from advertisements and work, and attached to the valuation was a partnership deed—Séchard would rent the premises to David for twelve hundred francs a year and reserve a room for himself. Cornered, David calculated the license and goodwill’s worth and, thinking he might manage the debt, accepted the deal.Next day Séchard had the apprentice move David’s belongings to the attic, leaving him three empty rooms and no funds to pay wages. When David asked for help, Séchard refused, saying he had already paid his share; he dismissed any claim on Mme. Séchard’s fortune as long and costly to pursue. Resolving to work, David accepted his situation. Séchard then spoke of a “treasure”—Marion, a capable country girl who ran many tasks—and departed for his Marsac vineyard, fretting over the thirty thousand francs and visiting intermittently as the Cointet Brothers gained ground.The Cointets, allied with the clergy, monopolized the market and smeared David as a Liberal because of his father. They offered to buy the printing house; though Séchard at first urged David to keep the paper and negotiated what he called a protective price of sixty thousand francs, David was ultimately forced to sell, marking the Séchard establishment’s decline. Séchard grew detached.David’s fortunes worsened when Lucien Chardon, an old school friend ruined by his father’s death, came seeking work and became a printer’s reader. Their friendship deepened—Lucien dreamed of being a poet, David of studying natural science; Lucien recounted his father’s schemes for refining sugar and making paper, which intrigued David. But these ambitions distracted them from the shop’s needs; while they planned, the Cointets consolidated their advantage and Séchard & Son scraped by, the workshop and household falling into disrepair.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################One day in May 1821 David and Lucien waited by the window until the men went to dinner. Uncomfortable in the office, David led Lucien outside beneath the vines, where sunlight made their contrasts plain. David was robust, swarthy, weathered—a man of humble origins with a melancholy reserve. Lucien was graceful and beautiful, with a refined profile and striking blue eyes; delicate in appearance but ambitious, shrewd, and willing to use dubious means to rise.Both were low in social standing yet nourished loftier ideals, which made them bitter when reality intervened. Lucien, impetuous and adventurous, usually led; David, stronger but more introspective and cautious, admired him and often yielded, content to play the ordinary man. For three years their friendship thrived on literature and art: they read voraciously, worked hard, and dreamed of fame as escape. When David brought a volume of André de Chénier, the verses comforted them with kinship and hope. Their reverie was broken by Cérizet, who brought a stranger to print a monograph. The visitor mentioned Mme. de Bargeton—whom Lucien admired—and Lucien, flushed, confessed his love. David warned of social barriers; Lucien hoped love could bridge them and vowed not to visit Mme. de Bargeton without David’s welcome, a pledge showing loyalty and pride.Walking through Angoulême, Lucien reflected on the gulf between his origin and Mme. de Bargeton’s world. The city, perched on a crag, was split between an aristocratic upper town and a commercial lower town; the nobility clung to old values and scorned merchants, while the bourgeoisie resented that disdain. Despite modernization, the hierarchy remained rigid, breeding tension as the middle class pressed for recognition. In that climate Lucien’s ambition was daunting: a druggist’s son was an outsider, yet his talent, charm, and Mme. de Bargeton’s artistic tastes gave him hope. David’s admiration was mixed with concern; Lucien’s determination—and his pledge to include David—encapsulated their bond as love and ambition unfolded against entrenched divisions and shifting fortunes.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################M. de Bargeton descended from a Bordeaux alderman ennobled under Louis XIII, but after his grandfather “the Waster” squandered the fortune, by 1789 the family held only entailed lands and a house in Angoulême. In 1805 he married Marie-Louise-Anaïs de Nègrepelisse (Naïs), whose once-prominent family lived modestly: her father eked out a living on his wife’s estate, farming and distilling brandy.Taught languages and music by the Abbé Niollant, Naïs developed independence and a haughty air; her father’s thrift and provincial seclusion reinforced her refusal to submit. Seeking a husband who wouldn’t rival her intellect, her father chose the well-bred but limited Bargeton. At thirty-six to his fifty-eight, she accepted, planning social ambitions and a move to Paris. Instead, a meager income and petty provincial life stifled her, producing theatrical affectations. Longing for love, she adored an Empire hero who died, leaving her hardened—until the charming, ambitious M. du Châtelet arrived, unsettling the town and awakening her hopes and scandal.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Political opinion was loud and exaggerated; the Quotidienne showed tepid loyalty, Louis XVIII was mocked, and society’s women were awkwardly dressed and ill-spoken. In the provinces, lingering nobility and Bourbon loyalty lent provincials misplaced pride.Châtelet sought solace in society because of Mme. de Bargeton. Flattering many women and embodying what their husbands lacked, he endured insolence, courted the clergy, indulged the queen of Angoulême’s tastes, and circulated new literature. Mme. de Bargeton—Naïs—was enchanted by the Bourbon revival yet longed for Parisian genius; Châtelet spoke of a promising young poet in Angoulême.That poet, Lucien, unaware of the stir, was praised by his headmaster and expected a warm reception. His sister Éve secretly supported him—saving for new clothes and coaching him in aristocratic manners—leaving him proud but anxious.At the Hôtel de Bargeton the house was less grand than imagined, but Naïs embodied the poetry he admired. She admired his beauty and diffidence; mutual attraction promised intimacy. Châtelet, sensing a rival, asserted influence and claimed Naïs, but Lucien continued to visit, at first flattered, later resentful.Naïs cultivated him as patroness and confidante: she encouraged his literary ambitions, introduced him to music and books, and revealed vulnerabilities. A portrait of a dead lover stirred jealousy; Lucien declared his feelings and their liaison advanced. When it became public the town buzzed. Châtelet tried to undermine him; Naïs defended the idea that genius can rise from humble origins, hosted salons, and urged Lucien to adopt the noble name Rubempré and seek courtly patronage. Exhilarated, Lucien’s egalitarian ideals gave way to social ambition; in a passionate letter he begged Naïs’s help for a friend, revealing his conflict between love, artistic sincerity, and pursuit of success.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Lucien descended from Angoulême he worried whether the great lady was angry and if she would receive David. He sensed the vast gulf between a queen and her favorite and knew David could not easily cross that social terrain. He did not yet understand that receiving David might ruin Mme. de Bargeton: for the powerful many sins are pardoned, but breaching social law by associating with lower classes was unforgivable.Lucien’s aristocratic instinct warned him. Noble bearing did not guarantee noble feeling; David lacked Lucien’s graces and would be ridiculed. Regretting his impulsive letter, Lucien foresaw the harsh sacrifices success demanded and preferred to win by his own efforts. Inspired by Napoleon’s rise, he resolved to pursue ambition without patronage, ashamed of his father’s shop as an obstacle.At home M. Postel reminisced about family achievements, stoking Lucien’s resentment. In the shop Lucien found Mme. de Bargeton’s reply: she would help David but revealed prejudice toward lower status. Lucien read it as triumph, convinced David would shine in her circle. Eve noticed his elation and feared his priorities were shifting.David, feeling unworthy, declined the invitation, affirming his commitment to being a printer and the social distance between them. His humility moved Lucien, but ambition began to eclipse gratitude. Before Lucien’s first public reading, David and Eve shared a quiet, suggestive moment. At the reading Lucien met the awkward M. de Bargeton, whose limited intellect underscored the social dynamics Lucien must navigate—setting the stage for future conflicts.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################There was talk of nominating the mute M. de Bargeton for deputy, but Lucien had only briefly met him and could not fathom him. Reclining and silent, Bargeton observed everything with a cool reserve that awed Lucien; imaginative, Lucien treated him like a sphinx and behaved deferentially. “I am the first comer,” Lucien said; Bargeton’s dry reply made him self-conscious. Lucien tried to converse—guessing Mme. de Bargeton was dressing, fretting over the room and his attire—but Bargeton’s curt answers unnerved him.The Baron du Châtelet arrived next, inspecting Lucien and dismissing his poetry as vaudeville. Mme. de Bargeton soon appeared, elaborately charming; Châtelet’s compliments pleased her while she scarcely acknowledged Lucien.Guests followed in a parade of types: the Bishop and his Vicar-General; Mme. de Chandour and her eccentric husband, Amélie posing like a rival queen; the solemn M. de Saintot (Astolphe) with austere Élisa; M. de Bartas seeking praise for singing; the artist Alexandre de Brébian and his wife; fashion-obsessed Lolotte and Fifine; the Comte de Senonches with Zéphirine, striking despite ill health, and Francis, whose solicitous attentions to Zéphirine provoked comment. The wealthy Marquis de Pimentel and the Rastignacs drew envy beneath Mme. de Bargeton’s flattery. The Prefect and the General came last, along with a nervous country gentleman bearing a treatise on silkworms. Mme. and Mlle. du Brossard hovered, pressing social ties; M. de Séverac spoke of silkworm nurseries.Throughout, Lucien felt scrutinized and diminished—often called M. Chardon rather than M. de Rubempré, a stinging slight. When he read, announcing newly discovered Chénier, his first poem Le Malade met mild applause; L’Aveugle fell flat amid impatience. Encouraged by Louise’s glance, he finished with Chénier’s Iambes, which finally secured attention though many missed the meaning. During the break Zéphirine sent Francis to inquire about the poems, and whispers about Lucien’s origins—Amélie’s remarks about his noble name and printing past—fueled gossip. Absorbed in the poet’s beauty, Lucien remained largely unaware of the mounting tension.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Lucien sought to immortalize his love for Mme. de Bargeton with an ode. He presented it before her; she fretted. The audience was unimpressed: the Baron called it ornate but empty; the women feigned admiration while whispering sarcasm. Francis spread a rumor that his mother wrote the verse; the Bishop’s praise only intensified aristocratic scorn. Overwhelmed, Lucien misread faces and felt alienated.Later Mme. de Bargeton withdrew, consoling him and romanticizing suffering as the path to greatness, urging him to turn pain into work. Meanwhile Eve and her lover David debated his aims: David feared Lucien couldn’t survive high society; Eve hoped to support him. David proposed; Eve accepted despite inequality, and David vowed to support him despite his father's objections. Lucien left resolved to win Mme. de Bargeton’s love and rise above society, yet torn between ambition and reality.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In Angoulême, a Bishop’s epigram and Mme. de Bargeton’s rejoinder made Lucien a local celebrity; in Beaulieu he was flattered and envied. He told Louise of his sister Eve’s impending marriage; she replied with passionate affection, praised his reading, and promised a poetic refuge. Buoyed by family and friends, Lucien’s pride grew. He teasingly asked Louise to be his Beatrice; she vowed future love and urged him toward greatness. He confided about David and Eve’s attachment; Louise said their bond transcended family ties. When M. de Bargeton arrived and invited him to dinner and a reading, Lucien—now styling himself M. de Rubempré—enjoyed intimacies, patronage, and hopes of a literary breakthrough. Eve and David postponed their wedding to support him, though David feared Lucien’s coming change.Their closeness drew Châtelet’s attention; in the gossip-driven town, innocent acts were misread. Provincial constraints thwarted Lucien’s declarations, Louise considered fleeing, and Châtelet sowed doubt. An outburst seen by Stanislas sparked rumor; gossip spread and Châtelet seized it. Mme. de Bargeton defended her honor and pressed her husband to act; the insecure M. de Bargeton nonetheless confronted Stanislas modestly and won respect, setting the stage for a confrontation that would further entangle lives and expose the town’s fraught mix of love, honor, and social expectation.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Châtelet, suspecting Naïs’s involvement in a late-night visit to the bedbound M. de Bargeton, asked if Bargeton wished to confront Stanislas. Bargeton, relieved at having a mediator, was brought to Amélie’s room for what could be a ruinous showdown. Châtelet warned Stanislas to be decorous; instead, Bargeton demanded a retraction of remarks about Mme. de Bargeton and M. de Rubempré. Cornered, Stanislas accepted a duel and chose pistols.The duel altered the household atmosphere: guests divided blame, Châtelet remained composed, Stanislas was shaken, and Amélie fell ill. At breakfast Mme. Chardon brought grim news—Stanislas had been shot in the neck and nearly killed after accusing Bargeton of compromising Mme. de Bargeton. Lucien defended her innocence.Lucien then received a note from Louise urging him to avoid her home because of the duel’s fallout and to spend the day with her. David, preparing for his own marriage, suggested Lucien curb his visits to Mme. de Bargeton. Visiting her later, Lucien found her and her husband with M. de Nègrepelisse watching him. Louise confided she planned to take Bargeton to Paris to obtain a post and contain the scandal, and she pressured Lucien to join her there. He hesitated because of his sister Eve’s impending wedding; after a heated exchange and her fainting, he promised discretion and to be ready to leave the next night.At home Eve and their mother fretted over wedding costs. David agreed to endorse a loan and assist; Lucien’s belongings were secretly sent to Paris. Châtelet sent someone to follow Mme. de Bargeton. At dawn Lucien left with David, pretending business, as Mme. de Bargeton’s carriage neared—Lucien torn between hope and dread.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################MIDDLE OF THE STORY — narrative's central section, tale's core; repeated as heading.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################
